Laws 673 to provide that the trial of a misdemeanor in certain demographic areas be before a single judge where the maximum authorized term of imprisonment was six months or less. According to the court, the legislature reduced the maximum period of imprisonment from one year to six months for all but 27 specifically designated Penal Law class A misdemeanors. The court noted that a defendant who was accused of violating a "non-designated" class A misdemeanor was entitled to a jury trial only when he was believed to be a "second crime offender" and the District Attorney filed a second crime offender information before trial. The filing of that information, per the court, entitled a defendant to a trial by jury. The court held that § 340.40(2) was constitutional. The court held that the legislature was presumed to have gauged the locality's social and ethical judgments on the seriousness of the offense and weighed public opinion and history. The court also held that there was a strong presumption that a statute was constitutional, only to be overturned when its invalidity was demonstrated beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the statute was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Right to Jury Trial -- Petit Larceny

 CPL 340.40 (2), as amended by Laws of 1984 (ch 673), which provides that the trial of misdemeanor information in certain demographic areas be before a single judge where the maximum authorized term of imprisonment is six months or less, is constitutional since there is a strong presumption that a statute duly enacted by the Legislature is constitutional, only to be overturned when its invalidity can be demonstrated beyond a reasonable doubt, and there has been no such showing in the instant case; accordingly, defendant, who was charged with petit larceny as a first offender, is subject to a maximum exposure of six months' imprisonment and her contention that that crime is a serious offense mandating a jury trial must be rejected since the Legislature must be presumed to have gauged the locality's social and ethical judgments on the seriousness of the offense when it designated the penalty for its commission.

 

1623 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Curran


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Oneida County


128 Misc. 2d 306; 488 N.Y.S.2d 983; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2900



April 24, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parent sought a determination of the constitutionality of various provisions of the Runaway and Homeless Youth Act of 1978 (Act), N.Y. Exec. Law, art. 19-H, in a habeas corpus proceeding that sought an order directing respondent operator of a state-approved runaway youth program to transfer the children to the parent.


OVERVIEW: The parent's children ran away from home and were placed in the operator's runaway program without the parent being advised of the children's whereabouts pursuant to the Act. The parent obtained a writ of habeas corpus from the court and the children were returned. The court granted the parent's request to determine the constitutionality of the Act because mootness would likely be recurring. The parent contended that the children were being illegally detained because the Act impermissibly authorized an agency of the state to deprive him of the custody of his children without due process of law. The court held that the Act was constitutional and that the state had a duty as well as a right to protect the children's best interests where a conflict existed between the children and the parent. The court ruled that the state did not remove or detain the children but merely provided temporary refuge for the children when they left the parent's home without his consent. The court held that the children were the ones who sought refuge in the runaway program and that they were free to leave upon request unless a court order had been entered.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the Act was constitutional in allowing the children to be placed in the operator's runaway program without the parent being advised of where the children would be residing.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Runaway Children -- Temporary Shelter in State Program -- Mootness

1. Although a habeas corpus proceeding in which petitioner parent contended that the Runaway and Homeless Youth Act of 1978 (Executive Law art 19-H) unconstitutionally deprived him of the temporary custody of his two children has been mooted by the return of the children to petitioner, the court shall address the constitutional issue since, given that the Act authorizes a runaway child to remain in an approved program for no more than 30 days without parental consent, it is unlikely that the question of the Act's constitutionality would ever be reached prior to the proceeding becoming moot.

Parent and Child -- Runaway Children -- Temporary Shelter in State Program -- Constitutionality

2. The Runaway and Homeless Youth Act of 1978 (Executive Law art 19-H), which authorizes a runaway youth, defined as a person under the age of 18 who is absent from his legal residence without the consent of his parent, legal guardian or custodian, to remain in an approved runaway program on a voluntary basis for a period of 30 days, and which, except in "compelling" cases, mandates that the parent [***2] or guardian be notified that the child has been placed in the program, is not an unconstitutional deprivation of the parent's or guardian's due process rights since a child's running away from home must be considered an extraordinary circumstance which justifies and requires the State to intervene to protect the interests of the child; when a child has voluntarily left home, a necessity exists which requires the State, as parens patriae, to protect the child.

 

1624 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Perry


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


128 Misc. 2d 430; 488 N.Y.S.2d 977; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2940



April 22, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an issue of first impression, the court addressed whether a court conducting a Wade hearing could consider the constitutionality of a stop which preceded the challenged identification in the absence of a defense motion The case resulted after defendant, a female, was forcibly stopped and arrested after the police had been informed that two males had committed theft crimes.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for various theft crimes and the court held a Wade hearing. The court held that defendant's failure to timely raise the constitutionality of the police stop did not waive the determination of the illegal stop issue and that, since the stop violated the Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend IV, the subsequent identification and evidence secured by the stop were suppressed. The court found that the police made no inquiry of other persons, and neither of the co-defendants behaved in a suspicious manner nor attempted to flee when the police approached. The court held that the bare description of race, sex, blue jeans and sneakers did not justify the stop and frisk, especially where the defendant did not match the sex description of the burglars that the officer was seeking. The court found no waiver by defendant where it was only upon the officer's testimony at the Wade hearing that the officer's knowledge and understanding was revealed and indicated that the stop was objectionable. The court held that defendant did not deliberately bypass the objection and met the "good cause and interests of justice" test for nonwaiver of the motion.


OUTCOME: The court held that defendant's failure to timely raise the constitutionality of the police stop did not waive the determination of the illegal stop issue and that, since the stop was unconstitutional, the subsequent identification and evidence secured by the stop were suppressed.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Incredibility of Officer's Testimony

1. Upon an evaluation of a police officer's demeanor, his mode of testifying, the inconsistencies in his testimony and its inherent improbability, it must be concluded that his testimony concerning the circumstances surrounding his stop and forcible arrest of two men suspected of having just robbed a computer store was inherently incredible and patently tailored to nullify constitutional objections; the testimony of the officer, who remembered virtually nothing else of the incident (including even who his partner was), was important to explain his actions and connect the defendants to the crime, since a logical link was needed between reportedly westward fleeing robbers and the defendants, who were apprehended east of the crime scene.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Forcible Stop

2. The act by police officers of immediately placing defendants "against the wall" on a street is clearly a forcible interference with their liberty as well as a humiliation which undermined their human dignity; therefore, it constituted a forcible stop notwithstanding the fact that the police did not [***2] have their guns drawn. A forcible stop requires that the police have reasonable cause to believe that the persons stopped have committed, are committing, or are about to commit a crime, which is greater than the predicate needed to stop and inquire, where the police need only a justified suspicion that criminal activity is afoot.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Forcible Stop -- Reasonable Cause

3. Police officers lacked "reasonable cause" to believe that defendant had participated in a computer store burglary, and, thus, were not justified in forcibly stopping him on the street, where defendant and another were apprehended in the opposite direction from where a witness said the perpetrators had fled, where the description which the police officers had was as sparse, nonspecific and subject to mistake and abuse as is imaginable, lacking height, weight, physical disability, unusual clothing, facial characteristics or anything else capable of distinguishing between the men stopped and any other two black men within 20 blocks, and where the persons stopped engaged in no behavior, such as attempted flight, which might elevate suspicion or increase predicate knowledge;  [***3] indeed, one of the officers testified that defendant did not even appear to the officer to be male (he was a transvestite), and that he believed the computer one of the men was carrying was a television.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Waiver of Unconstitutional Police Conduct

4. The failure of defendant to make the appropriate motions pursuant to Dunaway and Mapp to object to the unconstitutional procedures employed by the police in effecting his arrest does not constitute a waiver of his objections; although defense counsel knew that the People intended to introduce physical evidence seized from the defendant, had access to the radio run heard by the arresting officer, and also knew that defendant was a transvestite, it was not until the arresting officer testified at the Wade hearing that the officer's knowledge regarding the gender of the men he sought, which would be critical to a determination of the officer's predicate/response vis-a-vis stopping defendant on the street, was revealed, and thus, it was at least arguable that the officer's belief that he was arresting a female -- like grounds first obtained through Rosario material -- could [***4] not have been discovered until the officer actually testified at the hearing. Accordingly, enough has been shown to meet the "good cause and interests of justice" test to deny any waiver of defendant's objections, and since the Dunaway motion was made immediately after the officer's testimony and a hearing was already underway, no delay resulted from consideration and determination of the additional constitutional claims.

 

1625 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


ACO Realty Corp. v. Srogi


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Onondaga County


129 Misc. 2d 450; 493 N.Y.S.2d 386; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2638



April 15, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The finding of this court, therefore, is that sufficient reason existed at the time demand was made upon respondent to refuse to admit to a rate equal to the equalization rate found by the SBEA and, therefore, petitioners' motion for reimbursement pursuant to Real Property Tax Law § 716 must be denied in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The matter was before the court upon a motion filed by petitioner landowners against respondent, a city commissioner of assessment, seeking reimbursement pursuant to N.Y Real Prop. Tax Law § 716.


OVERVIEW: There remained but one further determination to be made in the certiorari actions at bar before a final money judgment could be entered. Previous determinations had been made with respect to the value of the individual properties for the years in question, and the court, by a previously entered order, fixed the applicable equalization rates to be applied to said values for those years agreement could not be reached. Petitioners then made application to the court for an award of expenses, including reasonable attorney fees, for proving an equalization rate less than that contained in their demand served pursuant to § 716. Petitioner, having the right to time his or her demand, was required in fairness to be bound by the facts then existing. Therefore, while not expressly stated, implied within § 716 was the proposition that the reasonableness of the denial was required to be determined as of the time the denial was made. The court concluded that sufficient reason existed at the time the demand was made upon respondent to refuse to admit to a rate equal to the equalization rate found by the State Board of Equalization and Assessment. Thus, the court denied petitioner's motion.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion filed by petitioners.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Assessment -- Penalty against Municipality for Failure to Admit to Reasonable Equalization Rate

Petitioners, which, in tax certiorari proceedings against the City of Syracuse, for the years 1976 through 1982, demanded that the city admit to the equalization rates of the State Board of Equalization and Assessment (SBEA) for each year, are not entitled to an award for reasonable costs and expenses, including attorney fees, for proving an equalization rate less than that contained in their demand for the year 1977, the court having found an equalization rate above the State Board's rate for every year but 1977 for which a rate of .04% less than the rate demanded by petitioners was found, since the penalty against a municipality for its failure to admit to a rate that is found to be equal to or less than that demanded (Real Property Tax Law § 716)  [***2] is not automatic and should be imposed only if insufficient reasons existed for the denial at the time the denial was made and, in view of the fact that the SBEA rate was statutorily not admissible for the tax year in question (see, Real Property Tax Law § 720 [3]) and that the lower rate for 1977 resulted from respondent's own all sales method, sufficient reason existed at the time demand was made upon respondent to refuse to admit the SBEA rate; section 716 was not intended to be punitive in cases of reasonable disagreement but, rather, sanctions against a municipality should be imposed where it arbitrarily refuses to admit to a reasonable demand or, even absent a finding of bad faith or arbitrariness, where there is a meaningful difference between the amount demanded and that ultimately found by the court.

 

1626 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Impress Promotions, Inc. v. Saratoga Springs


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Saratoga County


127 Misc. 2d 1029; 488 N.Y.S.2d 126; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2778



April 12, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff street peddler moved for an order, by way of summary judgment, declaring an amendment of Saratoga Springs, N.Y., Code ch. 57, § 57-3 unconstitutional and permanently enjoining defendant city from enforcing it. The city cross-moved for summary judgment in its favor.


OVERVIEW: The seller had sold souvenirs at the back gate of a race track. The amendment of Saratoga Springs, N.Y., Code ch. 57, § 57-3 eliminated that site for the sale of souvenirs. The city alleged that the amendment of Saratoga Springs, N.Y., Code ch. 57, § 57-3 was intended to alleviate the congestion surrounding the race track on race days and was within the city's police powers. In striking down the amendment of Saratoga Springs, N.Y., Code ch. 57, § 57-3, the court found that it was unconstitutional. The city had a legitimate need to regulate traffic at the race track on racing days. However, nothing in the papers justified the city's overkill method that established a "dead zone" for street peddlers for 500 feet around the rather substantial land holding of the New York Racing Authority. Clearly, desired control could be achieved by more narrowly drawn regulations of peddlers, themselves, or the areas in which they were entitled to sell their goods.


OUTCOME: The court struck down the contested amended ordinance.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Prohibition of Street Vending

An amendment to the Code of the City of Saratoga Springs which prohibits the sale or offer of commodities on all streets, alleys or public ways within 500 feet of lands of the New York Racing Authority (NYRA) is unconstitutional; although control of traffic congestion and problems relating to free access of public streets are within the province of the police power, and great weight is to be given to the expertise of local authorities, there is no justification for the city's overkill method which, in essence, establishes a "dead zone" for street peddlers for 500 feet around the rather substantial land holding of NYRA; the amendment has the effect of abolishing, rather than regulating, street vending; desired control could and should be achieved by more narrowly drawn regulations of peddlers, themselves, or the areas in which they may sell their goods.

 

1627 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Trevett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


128 Misc. 2d 45; 488 N.Y.S.2d 584; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2874



April 11, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Based on the above reasons, the application for a retention order should be denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a director of a psychiatric center, filed an application for retention of a patient.


OVERVIEW: The patient wrote a communication to the director of the hospital regarding the retention. The medical director certified to the court that the patient had requested a hearing. The patient claimed that she had been retained for about nine months without a hearing. The court held that the retention of the patient for the nine months, without an order of retention, violated her constitutional rights and was contrary to time requirements of N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 9.33. The court stated that the legislature intended six months to be the maximum duration of an initial retention order, made subsequent to a hearing. Moreover, the court held that the effective date of an initial order of retention should relate to a determination of application for retention after a hearing, and not to the time the application was filed with the court.


OUTCOME: The application for retention order was denied.


HEADNOTES

Mental Health -- Order of Retention -- Duration

An application made by a psychiatric center for an order of retention concerning a mental patient who, over a six-month period, had alternately requested and withdrawn her request for a hearing shall be denied (see, Mental Hygiene Law § 9.33). Inasmuch as a court is authorized to immediately issue an order of continued retention for a period not to exceed six months from the date of the order in situations where there has been no request for a hearing, the maximum duration of an initial retention order made subsequent to a hearing should be six months; accordingly, retention of a patient for a period of approximately nine months, without an order of retention, is violative of her constitutional rights and contrary to the time requirements of the Mental Hygiene Law. Moreover, the effective date of an initial order of retention should relate to the determination of the application after the hearing, and not to the time the application was filed.

 

1628 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Consolidated Edison Co. v. Public Service Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


127 Misc. 2d 1085; 488 N.Y.S.2d 352; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2862



April 10, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner utility companies filed a motion pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 for declaratory judgment to annul a statement of policy issued by respondent New York State Public Service Commission (commission) that allowed certain consumer groups (CUBS) to use the utility companies' billing envelopes to communicate with ratepayers and to solicit membership.


OVERVIEW: The United States Supreme Court declared unconstitutional and in violation of the First Amendment, the restraint by the commission prohibiting the utility companies from inserting into their billing envelopes, the utility companies' opinions or viewpoints as to controversial issues of public policy. Thereafter, the commission allowed CUBS to use the utility companies' billing envelopes to communicate with ratepayers and to solicit membership. The utility companies filed a declaratory judgment action claiming that such order was in violation of the First Amendment by compelling the utility companies to use their property, the billing envelopes, to promulgate and disseminate views opposed to their own and that the right to speak guaranteed under the First Amendment also included the right to refrain from speaking. The court held the order of the commission unconstitutional and held that an ominous threat presented itself to the First Amendment in allowing a governmental agency the right to license others in the use of another's property especially where the use of such property pertained to ideology which could oppose the views of such owner.


OUTCOME: The commission's order through its policy statement requiring the utility companies to allow consumer groups to use the utility companies' billing envelopes infringed upon the utility companies' rights under the First and Fourteenth Amendments.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Public Utilities -- Use of Utility Billing Envelopes by Consumer Groups -- Freedom of Speech

The policy statement of the Public Service Commission which permits consumer activist groups to use the billing envelopes of utility companies to communicate with ratepayers on sometimes controversial subjects and to solicit membership, infringes upon the utilities' rights under the US Constitution 1st and 14th Amendments, since under the Commission's policy it is the Commission itself which would have the right to decide what groups shall have access to the envelopes, thereby presenting the "ominous threat" of allowing a governmental agency the right to license others in the use of another's property where the use of such property pertains to ideology which may oppose the views of the property owner.

 

1629 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Anzalone v. Watertown


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Jefferson County


131 Misc. 2d 557; 500 N.Y.S.2d 913; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3307



April 9, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner disabled police officers filed a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 petition to review a determination of respondent city affecting vacation pay and sick pay benefits of disabled police officers.


OVERVIEW: The city published a list showing accumulated sick leave and vacation time of its police officers. The list gave credit for disability time, but it was later revised to delete all vacation and sick pay accrued during disability periods. The officers filed a proceeding seeking to annul the city's determination. The court held that the matter could be reviewed in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding because there was no mandatory grievance procedure. The court also held that the city was correct in determining that its original computation of accumulated sick leave and vacation time was illegal because the legislature indicated no intention to include fringe benefits as an additional payment with respect to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 207-c.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Police -- Payment of Salary to Disabled Policemen -- Accrual of Sick Leave and Vacation during Disability Period

A municipal police officer's entitlement to receive full salary during the period of disability leave (General Municipal Law § 207-c) does not include the right to also accumulate sick leave and vacation time during the disability period since the statute established a policy to guarantee a "full annual wage not to be interrupted in any respect by an employment connected disability" and there is no indication of a legislative intent to include fringe benefits as an additional payment. Since there is no mandatory grievance procedure in the parties' collective bargaining agreement concerning the issue of accrual of sick leave and vacation time during disability periods, the city's refusal to give credit for disability time may be reviewed in an article 78 proceeding. In any event, any contractual provisions for sick pay allowances and vacation pay provisions during disability periods would be violative of the clear intent of section 207-c and would be unconstitutional gifts of public funds.

 

1630 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Spearman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Chemung County


128 Misc. 2d 112; 487 N.Y.S.2d 712; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3349



April 5, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to inspect the Grand Jury minutes and to dismiss the indictment against him on the grounds that the evidence before the Grand July was not legally sufficient to support the charges of sexual abuse in the first degree and endangering the welfare of a child and that the Grand Jury proceeding failed to conform to the requirements of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law art. 190.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that because the testimony of the victims was uncorroborated, it was insufficient to support the charges against him. He argued that a statutory amendment to N.Y. Penal Law § 130.16 that removed the requirement of corroboration when the victim's lack of consent arose solely by reason of age was an ex post facto law, a violation of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10. The court concluded that the change was not an ex post facto law because it was procedural only and defendant was not denied a substantial personal right within the meaning of the ex post facto clause. Defendant contended that the amendment altered the legal rules of evidence to receive less or different testimony than what was required when the offense was committed. The court doubted the vitality of that contention, but reviewed the issue further. Removing the corroboration requirement did not change the quantum or degree of proof essential to conviction. The change did not deprive defendant of any former right or advantage that would have enabled him to show his innocence, it only altered the mode of proof. The jury still had to determine what weight and credibility to give the victim's testimony.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment against him for sexual abuse in the first degree and endangering the welfare of a child.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Corroboration as to Sex Offenses -- Ex Post Facto Law

The amendment of Penal Law § 130.16 to remove the requirement of corroboration as to sex offenses when the victim's lack of consent arose solely by reason of age, did not constitute an ex post facto law since it did not punish as a crime an act which was innocent when done, enhance the punishment for a crime after its commission, or deprive defendant of any defense available to him according to the law at the time the act was committed; no element of the crime of sexual abuse in the first degree has been altered and each factual element of the offense must still be proven by competent and admissible evidence evincing guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. The statute in question is procedural and merely renders wholly competent to testify those who were previously considered only partially competent; as such, it has no "conduct-influencing" effect, does not deny defendant a substantial personal right, and does no violence to instinctive feelings of justice and fair play.

 

1631 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jack Resnick & Sons, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Yonkers


127 Misc. 2d 1031; 487 N.Y.S.2d 988; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2779



March 29, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the above-stated reasons, defendant's motion to dismiss the information is denied in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a corporation, filed a motion in a trial court (New York), seeking to dismiss an information that charged defendant with violating Yonkers, N.Y., Code § 103-1 (1961), by permitting noxious weeds, long grass, or other rank growths, or growths that were harmful, poisonous, or detrimental to health, on real property owned by defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought to have the information dismissed on the grounds that Yonkers, N.Y., Code §§ 103-1, 103-2 (1961) were unconstitutional and that the information was insufficient and defective on its face. A sanitation enforcement officer of the city's department of public works observed that a strip of land running the length of the defendant's property had become overgrown with high grasses and weeds. The officer returned to the office and researched the tax records of the city to determine the owner of the property. The officer then issued a "warning notice" to defendant, which allowed defendant five days to correct the violation. About eight days later, the officer determined that the grass and weeds had not been trimmed. He then issued the summons. Defendant argued that the ordinance failed to provide sufficient notice of the forbidden conduct in terms that were understandable to a person of ordinary intelligence. The court denied the motion to dismiss the indictment because the challenged ordinances were not unconstitutionally vague and in fact provided sufficient and proper notice of the type of conduct that was prohibited.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss the information was denied in all respects.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Noxious Weeds and Long Grass -- Constitutionality of Ordinance

A municipal ordinance which prohibits a landowner from permitting "noxious weeds, long grass or other rank growths, or growths which are harmful, poisonous or detrimental to health," to grow on his property is not unconstitutionally vague and provides sufficient proper notice of the type of conduct which is prohibited; when the ordinance is read in its entirety, it is abundantly clear that an owner of property is put on notice that he shall be penalized when he either consciously or through neglect allows grass, weeds or other vegetation to grow too long or to flourish to a point where they become harmful or detrimental to health; moreover, the ordinance in question provides a reasonable method and procedure of due process. While defendant questions the definition of "long grass", grass and weeds 2 to 2 1/2 feet high, which were on defendant's property, would be considered long to any person of ordinary intelligence, and vegetation of that length could also be considered unsightly,  [***2] provide an area for vermin to nest, thus possibly causing a health problem, and could attract litter, all problems of legitimate public concern.

 

1632 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Spring Realty Co. v. New York City Loft Bd.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


127 Misc. 2d 1090; 487 N.Y.S.2d 973; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2863



March 28, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff loft landlords and defendant city loft board (board) filed cross motions for summary judgment in the landlords' declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of the Loft Law, N.Y. Mult. Dwell. Law art. 7-C, and its accompanying regulations.


OVERVIEW: The loft law legitimated the conversion of commercial leases to residential purposes and provided for tenant status and landlord rights to collect rents. The loft law also created the loft board to provide regulations for the conversions and appropriate costs and fees that could be passed on to the tenants. The loft board promulgated interim rent guidelines when leases expired, which provided calculations of rate increases. The landlords sought a declaration that the loft law was unconstitutional on its face and as applied to the landlords. The landlords and the loft board both sought summary judgment. The court granted summary judgment for the loft board on the constitutionality of the statute. The court determined that the loft law was a valid exercise of police power, which did not offend the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution, N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(b)(2), or any other constitutional provision. The court, however, required the loft board to promulgate "hardship" regulations and schedule hearings for the landlords, holding that three years was not a reasonable period to have to wait for the institution of "hardship" regulations.


OUTCOME: The court denied the landlords' motion for summary judgment and granted the loft board's motion for summary judgment in the landlords' action challenged the constitutionality of the loft law. The court required the loft board to promulgate regulations and schedule hardship hearings for the landlords within 120 days.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and Tenant -- Loft Law -- Constitutionality -- Police Power

1. The Loft Law (Multiple Dwelling Law art 7-C) is a valid exercise of the State's police power and does not offend either US Constitution 14th Amendment or NY Constitution, article IX, § 2 (b) (2); the legislative findings which motivated the enactment of the statute indicate its legitimate concern with health, safety and reasonable rentals for both landlords and tenants, and a court should not substitute its judgment as to solutions and remedies where the legislation is reasonably related to the problem at hand, the test being whether the means adopted by the Legislature to carry out its avowed purpose was reasonable under the circumstances, and not whether it was the wisest course.

Landlord and Tenant -- Loft Law -- Constitutionality -- Permissible Delegation

2. The Loft Law (Multiple Dwelling Law art 7-C) is neither an unconstitutional "taking" of a landlord's property nor an impermissible delegation of authority to an administrative agency (the Loft Board), since it attempts to strike a balance between the financial interests of loft owners and tenants by regulating the rent levels prior [***2] to the owner's compliance with occupancy standards, and providing for "pass through" from owner to tenant of the reasonable and necessary costs incurred by the owner in achieving compliance; the statutory language provides a sufficient standard so as to validate the delegation of legislative discretionary power to an administrative agency.

Landlord and Tenant -- Loft Law -- Constitutionality -- Equal Protection

3. The Loft Law (Multiple Dwelling Law art 7-C) does not deny landlords equal protection under the State and Federal Constitutions since its legislative classification is reasonably related to several legitimate public purposes, is neither "wholly irrelevant" nor "patently arbitrary", and distinguishes commercial buildings with loft tenants from others because the loft occupancy has endangered public health and safety, and has created chaotic and unsettled legal problems. Moreover, assuming the statute impairs contractual obligations with respect to existing leases, the law is a valid exercise of the police power under a declared housing emergency, and is constitutional since it serves an important purpose for the general welfare.

Landlord and Tenant --  [***3] Loft Law -- Failure of Loft Board to Promulgate Regulations

4. Where the Loft Board has failed to act on any hardship applications or to promulgate regulations such as defining "necessary and reasonable" costs of legalization and the "buy out" provisions as required under the Loft Law (Multiple Dwelling Law art 7-C), plaintiff landlords are entitled to an order directing the Board to promulgate schedules mandated by the statute and commence holding hardship hearings, since the Board's three-year delay in complying with the mandatory provisions of the law is inordinate, unreasonable, and without legal justification.

 

1633 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Joseph P.M. on behalf of Karen S. v. Boyce R.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Warren County


127 Misc. 2d 931; 487 N.Y.S.2d 685; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3350



March 21, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, commissioner of social services and mother, sought a declaration that respondent putative father was the father of the mother's child, and they sought an order of support in a paternity proceeding. The parties were directed by the court to show cause why an order should not be made requiring the mother, child, and putative father to submit to one or more blood genetic marker tests. Petitioners had applied for such an order.


OVERVIEW: The putative father, a correction officer, testified in opposition to an order for blood genetic marker testing. The putative father stated that his duties, which required direct contact with a prison population, subjected him to a high risk of contracting acquired immune deficiency syndrome (AIDS), a fatal disease for which there was no known cure. The putative father testified that certain inmates at the prison suffered from AIDS. A medical expert testified that the putative father would not have been at risk of contracting AIDS by submitting to a blood test. Because the expert evidence before the court clearly and convincingly indicated that the taking of a blood sample from the putative father would not have jeopardized his health, such testing was ordered by the court pursuant to N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 532. The putative father's other objections to such testing were insufficient.


OUTCOME: The court issued blood genetic marker testing in a paternity proceeding in which the commissioner of social services and the mother sought a declaration that the putative father was the father of the mother's child.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children Born out of Wedlock -- Paternity Proceeding

Respondent putative father is directed to submit to blood genetic marker testing pursuant to Family Court Act § 532 in connection with a paternity proceeding; such testing will not put respondent at increased risk of contracting acquired immune deficiency syndrome (AIDS) although his work brings him into contact with persons suffering from AIDS or with persons in groups known to have a high incidence of AIDS, any evidence from the testing would not be testimonial or communicative in nature and there is no Fourth Amendment violation, and compelling respondent to submit to such a blood test does not violate his constitutional right of privacy or constitute an unprivileged and unjustifiable assault and battery upon his person since the administration of the test amounts to a minimal intrusion. Should respondent refuse to submit to the tests, petitioners will be permitted to introduce evidence of respondent's failure and the court will find that the results of those tests would have been most favorable to them.

 

1634 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Nelson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


127 Misc. 2d 820; 487 N.Y.S.2d 674; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2744



March 18, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendants' motion to dismiss the information pursuant to CPL 170.30 (1) (a) and 170.35 (1) (c) is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss a charge of jostling in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 165.25 claiming that the information filed against him and his codefendant was defective in that it lacked any allegation of intent. Defendant also sought dismissal pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 170.30 (1), 170.35 (1)(c) on the grounds that N.Y. Penal Law § 165.25 was void for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and his codefendant were charged with jostling in violation of § 165.25. A superseding information was filed alleging that a police officer saw both defendants commit the offense of jostling at a subway. Defendants were to be tried by a jury. At that time, defendant filed a motion to dismiss the charge pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 100.40(1), 170.35(1) claiming that the information was defective and that if intent to steal was not an essential element of N.Y. Penal Law § 165.25, the statute was void for vagueness because it violated the minimum standards for notice and definiteness imposed by the due process clauses. The court granted the motion and held that in an effort to save the jostling statute from constitutional challenges, some courts have read the additional element of intent to steal into the statute. The court held, however, that such a reading was an improper exercise of judicial power. Where the Legislature failed to insert such provisions in the law, the omission could not be remedied by construction, and the law was required to be considered defective to that extent. The lack of the element of intent in § 165.25 rendered it void for vagueness.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the information.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Jostling -- Void for Vagueness

The jostling statute (Penal Law § 165.25), which only requires that one intentionally and unnecessarily place his hand in the proximity of a person's handbag or pocket without explicitly requiring an intent to steal, is unconstitutionally vague and violative of the due process standards of notice and definiteness since a person of ordinary intelligence receives no guidance from the statute as to when the placement of his hand near another's handbag constitutes a crime; in addition, the statute's use of the word "unnecessary" encourages arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement by delegating to the police officer in the street the absolute subjective power to decide which hand movements should result in arrests; reading the additional element of intent to steal into the jostling statute in order to save the statute from constitutional challenges is an improper exercise of judicial power since it is the role of the Legislature to draft the necessary and proper statutory tools to assist in combating crime.

 

1635 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Nancy L. P. v. Daniel M. Z.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Schoharie County


127 Misc. 2d 674; 487 N.Y.S.2d 278; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2798



March 14, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent father brought a petition for downward modification of his child support obligation in accordance with the written separation agreement between the father and petitioner mother that he was not required to support his 18-year-old daughter.


OVERVIEW: The parties' separation agreement provided that support for the daughters was to terminate at age 18 and support for the son was to continue until age 21. The separation agreement was incorporated into the divorce judgment. The parties signed a modification agreement regarding the amount of support. By mutual agreement, the son went to live with the father, and the weekly support payments were reduced. When the oldest daughter turned 18, the father reduced his payments and sought modification. The family court denied the modification, found the father legally chargeable with the support of his 18-year-old daughter still in high school, and directed him to pay support for both daughters. The father contended that the court was not able to require him to support his 18-year-old daughter and brought another petition for modification. The family court denied the petition and held that the agreement of the parties contravened public policy, such that parents were chargeable with the support of the child until the age of 21, N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 413, unconstitutionally discriminated against the daughters, and did not realistically constitute an allocation of the support burden.


OUTCOME: The court denied the father's petition for modification of child support.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Husband and Wife -- Support of Children

Upon respondent father's petition for downward modification of child support in accordance with the parties' separation agreement, which provided that support for respondent's daughters was to terminate at age 18, the Family Court has jurisdiction to order respondent to support his 18-year-old daughter absent a showing of an unforeseen and unreasonable change of circumstances and a concomitant increased need; the agreement of the parties clearly contravenes the public policy of the State that "parents of a child under the age of twenty-one years are chargeable with the support of such child" (Family Ct Act § 413) and unconstitutionally discriminates against respondent's daughters by terminating their support at age 18, whereas support for respondent's son continued until age 21. The agreement did not realistically constitute an allocation of the support burden between the parties, and the fact that respondent's 18-year-old daughter failed a grade and is presently a high school senior constitutes a substantial change in circumstances which continues respondent's statutory duty to support her.

 

1636 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Flacke v. Onondaga Landfill Systems, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Onondaga County


127 Misc. 2d 984; 487 N.Y.S.2d 651; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2768



March 13, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Commissioner of Environmental Conservation of the State of New York (commissioner) filed a motion under N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law §§ 71-2703(1) and 71-2727(2) seeking to enjoin defendant landfill owner from interfering with the commissioner's administrative inspections of the landfill owner's landfill. The landfill owner challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 3-0301(2)(g), which authorized the inspections.


OVERVIEW: After refusing to allow the commissioner to make twice-daily inspections of a landfill, the landfill owner filed an action in a federal district court challenging the constitutionality of § 3-0301(2)(g). After the commissioner filed a motion to enjoin the landfill owner from interfering with the inspections, the federal district court dismissed the landfill owner's action under the abstention doctrine. The court denied the commissioner's motion for an injunction and held that (1) although the regulation of landfills was pervasive, the court was unable to determine, as a matter of law, that landfills were not entitled to some expectation of privacy; and (2) although frequent and unannounced inspections of landfills were warranted, § 3-0301(2)(g), and N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 6, § 360.1(j), authorizing routine warrantless inspections, were facially unconstitutional because they lacked proper limitations of time, place, and scope, and failed to minimally establish some guidelines as to regularity and frequency of inspection. Thus, the court fashioned a temporary remedy to accommodate reasonable inspections and urged the commissioner to establish proper inspection standards.


OUTCOME: The court denied the commissioner's motion seeking to enjoin the landfill owner from interfering with the commissioner's administrative inspections of the landfill owner's landfill. The court entered a temporary order allowing the commissioner to conduct routine inspections of the landfill owner's landfill, during normal business hours, at a frequency no greater than 3 times per week, with each inspection not to exceed 90 minutes in duration.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Environmental Conservation -- Solid Waste Management Facility -- Warrantless Administrative Inspection

Although a sanitary landfill is closely regulated and thus subject to routine, warrantless and unannounced administrative inspections, the State's inspection statute ( ECL 3-0301 [2] [g]) and regulations (6 NYCRR 360.1 [j]) which authorize the Department of Environmental Conservation to inspect a sanitary landfill and related facilities used by a landfill operator without restriction, are facially unconstitutional since the scope of inspection impermissibly exceeds the reasonable needs of a warrantless routine inspection scheme due to the lack of appropriate limitations of time, place and scope and the failure to minimally establish some guidelines as to regularity and frequency of inspection; in order to uphold the statute's constitutionality, ECL 3-0301 (2) (g) may be read as limited to areas actively utilized for the receipt, deposit, compaction, and cover of waste and areas previously utilized for those purposes and the scope of inspection may be limited to those activities of operation, closure and postclosure monitoring that directly affect the environment; however,  [***2] reasonable standards for regularity and frequency of inspections of landfills based upon neutral criteria must be established by the Department; accordingly, defendant is directed to submit to routine inspections of its landfill during normal business hours at a frequency not greater than three times per week with each such inspection not to exceed 90 minutes in duration.

 

1637 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Jefferds v. Ellis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Cattaraugus County


127 Misc. 2d 477; 486 N.Y.S.2d 649; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2620



February 26, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lessee filed a motion under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 71 for the return of a truck that had been repossessed without a default in payment and without prior notice or judicial action by defendant lessor. The lessor contested the motion on the grounds that the repossession was authorized by N.Y. U.C.C. §§ 9-503 and 9-504, which gave a secured party the right to retake and dispose of collateral without judicial process.


OVERVIEW: The parties had a lease agreement that called for monthly payments and a nominal last payment for the transfer of title. The lease was silent as to default or repossession. The court held that the lease was actually a security agreement pursuant to N.Y. U.C.C. § 1-201(37), so that §§ 9-503 and 9-504 appeared to apply. However, the court held that fundamental notions of procedural due process required that before the state could deprive a person of a significant property interest in aid of a creditor, that person must be given notice and an opportunity to be heard. The court held that state involvement, as opposed to direct state action, was all that was required under the state constitution. The court found that by allowing both repossession and collection of any deficiency, the state had changed the law of conditional sales and had transferred the traditional roles of judge, jury and sheriff without providing for any judicial supervision or other safeguards. The court held that N.Y. U.C.C. §§ 9-503 and 9-504 were unconstitutional as a violation of the due process clause of N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6, when applied to this lease.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion of the lessee for the return of the vehicle.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Secured Transactions -- Lease of Truck Tractor -- Repossession on Default without Judicial Process

An agreement for the lease of a truck tractor, which gives plaintiff lessee the option to purchase the vehicle at the end of the 15-month rental period for a nominal payment of $ 6, was one made for security ( UCC 1-201 [37]) and, as such, is governed by the repossession provisions of the Uniform Commercial Code in the absence of any specific provision concerning repossession; defendant lessor's repossession of the truck tractor from plaintiff's premises without notice and without judicial process pursuant to UCC 9-503 and 9-504 after plaintiff allegedly defaulted under the lease by not properly maintaining the vehicle, violated plaintiff's due process rights under the State Constitution since UCC 9-503 and 9-504, which grant the secured party the right to take possession of the collateral from the debtor's premises "on default", are unconstitutional to the extent that they permit a secured party to make a subjective determination of default under the provisions of a security agreement of the type here involved and then by delegation of sovereign authority give the secured party [***2] making such determination the right to repossess and sell the collateral and seek a deficiency judgment without providing the nonsecured party judicial process to stop such peremptory action before the damage is done; accordingly, defendant is directed to return the vehicle to plaintiff pursuant to CPLR article 71.

 

1638 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Nami v. Tingaris


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Onondaga County


127 Misc. 2d 312; 485 N.Y.S.2d 944; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2595



February 13, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant motor vehicle owner appealed from an order of the Syracuse City Court (New York), which granted plaintiff injured motor cycle owner's motion to videotape the plaintiff's physician witness' testimony and denied defendant's motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was driving a motorcycle and was involved in a collision with defendant. The trial court granted plaintiff's motion to videotape the physician witness' testimony and denied defendant's motion for summary judgment. Defendant appealed from both rulings. The court found that the ruling as to the videotaped testimony was not appealable under N.Y. Ct. App. Rules of Practice 1702 (a) (2). The court also affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion for summary judgment. Contrary to defendant's argument, plaintiff was not required to demonstrate "serious injury" prior to bringing suit for noneconomic loss under the state's no-fault statute, N.Y. Ins. Law § 671(4). The insurance laws clearly exempted motorcycles from the definition of motor vehicle and also excluded motorcyclists from the receipt of first party benefits. Because plaintiff was not a "covered person" within the meaning of N.Y. Ins. Law § 671(10), the state's no-fault statute did not apply to plaintiff.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the trial court's ruling as to the videotaped testimony was not appealable. It also affirmed the trial court's order denying defendant's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Appeal -- Appeal as of Right

1. A ruling of the Syracuse City Court, following a motion by defendant for a trial de novo after an arbitration panel awarded plaintiff an amount in excess of $ 5,000, which permitted plaintiff to videotape the testimony of a physician witness in order to lessen the expense associated with the witness' appearance in court is not, on defendant's appeal to County Court, appealable as of right since none of the provisions of UCCA 1702 are applicable; moreover, the City Court ruling is analogous to an evidentiary ruling made during the course of trial and, thus, is not now appealable.

Insurance -- No-Fault Automobile Insurance -- Action for Noneconomic Loss by Motorcyclist -- Serious Injury

2. The owner/operator of a motorcycle can bring an action for noneconomic loss against the owner/operator of a motor vehicle without having to show serious injury pursuant to Insurance Law § 671 (4); inasmuch as motorcycles are clearly exempted from the definition of motor vehicle, a motorcyclist is excluded from receipt of "first party benefits" (Insurance Law § 671 [2]) and, therefore, a motorcyclist is not a covered person within the meaning of [***2] the statute which precludes recovery for pain and suffering unless plaintiff can establish serious injury (Insurance Law § 673 [1]). Thus, an order which denied a motion by defendant motor vehicle operator for summary judgment dismissing plaintiff motorcyclist's action for noneconomic loss on the ground that plaintiff's proof failed to rise to the level of serious injury is affirmed.

 

1639 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re J.G.P.C. Enterprises, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


127 Misc. 2d 95; 485 N.Y.S.2d 486; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2557



February 4, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Application for order suppressing evidence is denied and the stay is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers filed an application for an order suppressing from use at a hearing all records, books, information, and any assessments made therefrom that were the result of an allegedly illegal search and seizure upon the taxpayers' premises, which was conducted by respondent New York State Tax Commission.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers sought an order suppressing from use at a hearing records, books, information, and any assessments made therefrom that resulted from an allegedly illegal search and seizure on the taxpayer's business premises, which was conducted by the Commission. The taxpayers contended that they were entitled to test the constitutionality of the seizure of their records and property by a prehearing proceeding. The court held that the proper procedure to be followed in a case such as the instant one, where a taxpayer claimed that the challenged tax assessment was predicated upon illegally seized evidence, was for the taxpayer to make a motion to suppress such evidence within the administrative proceeding, before the agency conducting the proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court denied the taxpayers' application for an order suppressing the evidence.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Sales and Use Taxes -- Prehearing Proceeding to Challenge Constitutionality of Seizure of Business Records

Petitioners may not challenge the constitutionality of the seizure of their business records and property by the State Tax Commission in an article 78 proceeding prior to the administrative hearing determining sales tax liability since judicial review of any assessed sales tax liability is not available until all matters and issues are finally determined (Tax Law §§ 1138, 1139) and the statutory and regulatory framework ( 20 NYCRR part 601) provides ample opportunity for a petitioning taxpayer to present and preserve claims of 4th Amendment violations on the part of the tax authorities either by way of objection at the formal hearing to the introduction of evidence alleged to be tainted or through a prehearing motion seeking suppression (cf. CPLR 3103 [c] [the provisions of the CPLR being applicable to motion practice]); accordingly, the proper procedure to be followed where a taxpayer claims that the challenged tax assessment is predicated upon illegally seized evidence [***2] is for the taxpayer to make a motion to suppress such evidence within the administrative proceeding before the agency conducting the hearing.
