Short Stop Industrial Catering Corp. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


127 Misc. 2d 363; 485 N.Y.S.2d 921; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2605



January 16, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Under these circumstances, plaintiff's motion for a preliminary injunction is denied in all respects and the stay of the forfeiture action is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff vendor sued defendant city seeking an order directing the city to issue it a food vendor's license and to return its property, as well as an order declaring the city's cap on the number of full-term food vending and vending vehicle permits unconstitutional. The vendor filed a motion for a preliminary injunction enjoining the city from seeking forfeiture of its property.


OVERVIEW: The vendor was selling food and coffee from a vehicle in a public area without a license. The vendor's vehicle and goods were seized. The city filed a forfeiture action pursuant to New York City, N.Y. Admin. Code § D22-17.0, prompting the vendor to file suit seeking a declaration that the regulatory cap on vending licenses was unconstitutional and seeking return of its property. The vendor's motion for a preliminary injunction was denied. The court held that § D22-17.0 was constitutional; the cap on food vending licenses had been rescinded and the cap on food vending vehicle licenses had a rational basis and was not oppressive. The forfeiture power afforded to the city in § D22-17.0 was the only effective means by which it could enforce the licensing requirements. The procedures provided in the regulation for forfeiture satisfied notice and due process requirements.


OUTCOME: The vendor's motion for a preliminary injunction enjoining the city from seeking forfeiture of its property was denied. The stay of the forfeiture action was vacated.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- Food Vendors

Administrative Code of the City of New York § D22-2.0 (b) (3), which placed a "cap" of 3,000 on the number of vending vehicle permits issued by the city, is a proper exercise of the city's police power to regulate the use of its streets or businesses in the public interest, in view of the city's serious street congestion problem; further, the portions of the statute providing for seizure of vehicles which do not have permits or which are being used by an unlicensed food vendor and seizure of any food sold or offered for sale from such vehicles, which vehicle and food are subject to forfeiture (Administrative Code §§ D22-17.0, D22-16.0), satisfy notice and due process requirements.

 

1641 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lopez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


126 Misc. 2d 1072; 484 N.Y.S.2d 974; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2530



January 14, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant contended that he was not competent to stand trial and that the court could not consider the testimony of two examining psychiatrists on the ground that they were not "qualified psychiatrists" within the meaning of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 730.10(5). Defendant interpreted § 730.10(5)'s requirement of board eligibility to impose an obligation on the examining psychiatrists to actively seek board certification.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law. § 730.20 authorized public hospital director to designate two qualified psychiatrists to examine defendants. The court concluded that the government satisfied its burden of proving that defendant's examining psychiatrists were "qualified psychiatrists" under § 730.10(5). Each had the requisite license to practice medicine and met the requirements of specialized training of the American Board of Psychiatry and Neurology. The court determined that the legislature's "delegation" of the power to determine who could examine defendants was reasonable and that the statute was sufficiently clear. The court found that defendant's history of mental illness did not diminish his competency to stand trial and that other facts supported the psychiatrists' determination that he was fit to proceed. Defendant cogently narrated the events surrounding his arrests, he rationally articulated defenses to the crimes charged, and he demonstrated familiarity with the criminal justice system and the roles of the judge and the lawyers. The court found that defendant was able to understand the proceedings against him and to assist in his defense and that he was fit to proceed.


OUTCOME: The court determined that defendant was competent to stand trial based on the testimony of two examining psychiatrists, which was supported by other facts in the record.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Fitness to Proceed to Trial -- Examination by "Qualified Psychiatrists"

Psychiatrists, who have the specialized training to be "eligible" for certification by either the American Board of Psychiatry and Neurology or the American Osteopathic Board of Neurology and Psychiatry but who have not passed the rigorous examinations required to become Board certified or actively sought such certification, are nonetheless "qualified psychiatrists" that may be appointed to determine whether a defendant is competent to stand trial since CPL 730.10 (5), in defining a "qualified psychiatrist" as a physician "who is eligible to be certified" by either Board, reflects the legislative intent to require adequate specialized training of the examining psychiatrists, not to require the physician to pass, apply for, or intend to take a particular private examination; CPL 730.10 (5) does not unlawfully delegate a legislative power to a private body since the purpose of the statute is not to license psychiatrists but to ensure that psychiatric examinations of criminal defendants are conducted by "qualified psychiatrists" based upon objective, verifiable criteria and adopting the Boards' stringent [***2] standards of experience as a minimum qualification for eligibility to examine defendants is reasonable; CPL 730.10 (5) is sufficiently clear and therefore not void for vagueness; accordingly, in view of the opinions of the two "qualified psychiatrists" and the court's own examination of defendant, defendant is found competent to stand trial.

 

1642 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. J.R. Cooperage Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


127 Misc. 2d 161; 485 N.Y.S.2d 438; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2568



January 11, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss the indictment is hereby denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged with unlawful dealing in hazardous wastes in the first degree, N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 71-2717, and one count of unlawful dealing in hazardous wastes in the second degree, N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 71-2715. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the indictment under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 210.30 because the evidence before the grand jury was not legally sufficient to establish the commission of the offenses charged.


OVERVIEW: Defendants asserted that the evidence before the grand jury was not legally sufficient to support the charges because the statutes unconstitutionally borrowed from the federal environmental regulations, the scientific evidence was unreliable, there was no evidence of attempted disposal, there was no evidence of the presence of waste, and there was no evidence of intent. The court denied the motion to dismiss because it determined that: (1) the charges were not based on those portions of the statutes that borrowed from the federal regulations and the only borrowed aspect, a testing procedure, had no effect on the crimes charged; (2) it was inappropriate to consider the reliability of the scientific evidence; (3) the placement of waste in steel drums in a receptacle for a sanitation company constituted a prima facie attempt to make a disposal. The court denied the request to dismiss the charges against the corporate officer because it determined that there was sufficient evidence that the individual knowingly and actively participated with the corporation.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss the indictment based on the assertion that the evidence before the grand jury was legally insufficient to establish the commission of the offenses.


HEADNOTES

Environmental Conservation -- Hazardous Waste -- Unlawful Dealing in Hazardous Wastes

The evidence before the Grand Jury was legally sufficient to establish the commission by defendants of unlawful dealing with hazardous wastes in the first and second degrees ( ECL 71-2717, 71-2715) where it was shown that defendants, operators of a drum recycling business, deposited drums containing a hazardous waste in a dumpster belonging to a commercial hauler of ordinary garbage not authorized to haul away hazardous waste; "hazardous waste" is defined in ECL 71-2702 (1) (a) as "Those wastes identified or listed in regulations promulgated pursuant to section 27-0903 of this chapter and all amendments thereto", and the indictment is constitutionally founded upon the definition of a hazardous waste contained in 6 NYCRR 366.3 (c) (1) (i), "characteristics of corrosivity"; although the regulation impermissibly incorporates by reference a testing protocol, the incorporation is de minimis in that it describes no proscribed conduct or substance and does not fail to give notice of the conduct proscribed by ECL 71-2717 and 71-2715. Any [***2] challenge to the validity of the testing methodology and the reliability of the results must be made at the time of trial.

 

1643 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Colonial Country Club, Inc. v. Ellenville


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Ulster County


126 Misc. 2d 814; 484 N.Y.S.2d 469; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2519



January 7, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant village sought a ruling on its motion for summary judgment in plaintiff property owner's tort suit and for the village's failure to enforce its statutes and ordinances.


OVERVIEW: The property owner's building caught fire and the village demolished it without notice to the property owner. The property owner filed a tort suit and for the village's failure to enforce its statutes and ordinances. The village filed a motion for summary judgment which the court granted in part and denied in part. The court granted summary judgment with regard to the cause of action for failing to enforce statutes and ordinance, holding that it had no merit. The court denied summary judgment as to the tort claims, holding that the decision to demolish was one involving judgment and was quasi-judicial. The court held, however, that the decision to demolish without notice and absent an opportunity to be heard was not a decision that was discretionary. The court held that due process was a fundamental liberty guaranteed by both the federal and New York State Constitutions, thus, the village could not demolish without notice.


OUTCOME: The court granted in part and denied in part the village's motion for summary judgment in the property owner's tort suit and for the village's failure to enforce its statutes and ordinances.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Immunity for Quasi-Judicial Acts -- Demolition of Fire-Damaged Building

Defendant village is not immune from liability for demolishing plaintiff's building because fire damage allegedly rendered the building dangerous to the public since, although the decision to demolish the building was a discretionary quasi-judicial act involving the exercise of reasoned judgment and a municipality ordinarily enjoys immunity with respect to such a discretionary act, defendant's decision to demolish the building without notice to plaintiff and absent an opportunity for plaintiff to be heard in violation of plaintiff's right to due process was not a discretionary act that entitles the village to immunity; even if the demolition was an administrative act, rather than a quasi-judicial act, there is insufficient information to determine as a matter of law if the determination was rational and defendant would therefore not be entitled to summary judgment dismissing the complaint in any event.

 

1644 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Perez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


126 Misc. 2d 1087; 484 N.Y.S.2d 771; 1985 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2532



January 7, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought dismissal of their indictments in the court, of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree (possessing an incendiary bomb), reckless endangerment in the first degree (operating a motor vehicle with flammable fluids), and of aiding others with respect to those counts.


OVERVIEW: Prior to their arrest, defendants were observed exiting a vehicle which was stopped on a ramp of a bridge. Defendants were under surveillance by detectives hired by the Metropolitan Taxi Board of Trade (board) to investigate acts of vandalism. Detectives learned that an incident was to occur at the site of the Midland Cab Co., and the ramp on which defendants' vehicle had stopped was adjacent to and overlooked the Midland Cab Co. In the trunk of defendants' car were found a lantern, a kerosene can, gasoline, and some other flammable liquid. Defendants were arrested and indicted for criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 265.02, and reckless endangerment in the first degree. Defendants sought dismissal of the indictment on grounds that the items they possessed in the trunk were not explosives or incendiary bombs within the meaning of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.02. The court dismissed the indictments and held that the term "incendiary" retained its everyday, commonsense meaning, and the statutory terms "incendiary", "bomb" and "explosive substance" were susceptible of reasonable application in accordance with the common understanding of men.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the indictments in favor of defendants.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Possession of Weapon -- Incendiary Bomb

A "hurricane-type lantern", a gasoline can and a kerosene can, seized from the trunk of defendants' vehicle by police after private detectives investigating acts of vandalism arising from a breakdown in labor negotiations with the taxi drivers union apprehended defendants on a ramp overlooking a cab company when the driver (a member of the taxi union) and the two other passengers exited the vehicle, opened the trunk and the driver walked to the railing of the ramp, are not "incendiary devices" so as to sustain the charge of unlawfully possessing an "incendiary bomb" (Penal Law § 265.02) in the absence of any testimony before the Grand Jury that any of the defendants ever removed or physically possessed any of the seized items from the trunk of the vehicle and in view of the failure of the District Attorney to properly instruct the Grand Jury with respect to circumstantial evidence and the definition of "incendiary bomb" or "device"; even if the seized items were "incendiary bombs", the charge of reckless endangerment as to all three defendants would still be dismissed due to the absence of any testimony that possession of the [***2] items in the trunk was an act of recklessness or that transporting such items is a violation of the law and the charge of criminal possession of a weapon against the two defendants who were mere passengers in the vehicle would likewise be dismissed.

 

1645 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gonzales


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


130 Misc. 2d 214; 500 N.Y.S.2d 906; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3789



December 27, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Prior to trial, defendant filed a motion to dismiss the case charging him with operating an automobile under the influence of alcohol on the basis that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192(2) was void for vagueness and failed to provide notice of the proscribed criminal conduct.


OVERVIEW: In pretrial proceedings, defendant filed a motion to dismiss the case against him for operating an automobile under the influence of alcohol for failure of fair notice. The court declined to grant the motion and ruled that the issue of whether § 1192(2) was void for vagueness was premature and speculative. The court determined that if, by the conclusion of the case, there was an insufficient showing that defendant knew or reasonably could have known that his blood count was in the forbidden level, counsel could raise the issue at that time. The court held that § 1192(2) was not facially void for vagueness because the controlling threshold contained in the statute was both explicit and unambiguous. The court noted that the only voluntary act required to be proven for conviction under § 1192(2) was the act of operating an automobile and that knowledge was not required. The court concluded that at trial, the prosecution may have been in a position to show that defendant should have known or reasonably should have known of the condition of his blood.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the case based on lack of notice.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motor Vehicles -- Driving while Intoxicated

 Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 (2), which prohibits motor vehicle operation while the operator "has .10 of one per centum or more by weight of alcohol in his blood", is not facially "void for vagueness" for failing to give someone of ordinary intelligence fair notice that his or her conduct is forbidden since the .10 of 1% threshold, the controlling norm contained in the statute, is both explicit and unambiguous whether or not the defendant knew if it had been exceeded. However, since the statute amounts to no-fault criminal law because knowledge by the operator of the condition of his or her blood is not required, defendant may challenge the constitutionality of the statute as applied to him if the prosecutor makes an insufficient showing that defendant knew or reasonably could have known that his blood was in the forbidden condition.

 

1646 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Ivory B. v. Ronald W.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


126 Misc. 2d 851; 483 N.Y.S.2d 936; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3712



December 27, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition therefore is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the alleged father, moved to dismiss the petitioner mother's paternity petition initiated almost 10 years after the birth of the child. The father sought to dismiss the petition on the ground that the statute of limitations pursuant to Family Ct. Act § 517[a] of five years had run.


OVERVIEW: The mother brought a paternity petition initiated almost 10 years after the birth of the child. The court granted the father's motion to dismiss the petition. The court held that it was the legislature's role to set statute of limitations and to establish whether the mother had a reasonable opportunity to proceed. The court held that it could not say as a matter of law that the five year statute of limitations bore no reasonable relation to the prevention of stale or fraudulent claims. The court held that the inherent arbitrariness of statutes of limitations made them a peculiarly appropriate subject for legislative rather than judicial control and the state was free to set periods of limitations without fear of violating some provision of the Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court granted the father's motion to dismiss the petition for paternity that was initiated by the mother after the statute of limitations had run.


HEADNOTES

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Paternity Proceeding -- Constitutionality of Five-Year Limitations Period

The five-year Statute of Limitations to commence a nonpublic assistance paternity proceeding following the birth of a child born out of wedlock ( Family Ct Act § 517 [a]) is not unconstitutional since it cannot be said as a matter of law that the State's five-year period for nonpublic assistance paternity proceedings, as compared with the 10-year limitations period for public assistance cases, bears no reasonable relation to the legitimate State interest in preventing prosecution of stale or fraudulent claims; the inherent arbitrariness of Statutes of Limitation makes them a peculiarly appropriate subject for legislative rather than judicial control and, therefore, a nisi prius court is not the proper forum for a determination of whether a limitations period is substantially related to a legitimate State interest.

 

1647 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Jones v. New York City Transit Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, New York County


126 Misc. 2d 585; 483 N.Y.S.2d 623; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3669



December 17, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a personal injury action, a motion was filed challenging a blind prospective juror for cause based on his blindness. The prospective juror opposed the motion, contending that the exclusion was improper under N.Y. Jud. Law § 510(3) (amended 1983).


OVERVIEW: The prospective juror opposed a motion challenging him for cause, contending that he should not have been excluded from serving as a juror because, under § 510(3), his blindness did not prevent him from performing in a reasonable manner the duties of a juror in the personal injury action. The court granted the motion challenging him for cause, concluding that the prospective juror was not competent to serve as a juror. The court held that a significant amount of the evidence consisted of physical evidence and that a blind individual's inability to visually perceive the physical evidence would have been a disadvantage during jury deliberations in following the court's instructions to consider all evidence presented at trial. The court also concluded that a juror's inability to perceive body movements and voice intonations would have been a disadvantage in formulating an opinion on veracity.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion for cause excluding the prospective juror based solely on his blindness.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Jury -- Disqualification of Juror -- Exclusion of Blind Person from Jury in Personal Injury Action

A blind person may properly be excluded from jury service through the use of a challenge for cause based solely on his blindness in a personal injury action involving substantial amounts of physical evidence since the juror qualification statute (Judiciary Law § 510 [3]), in providing that a person qualifies for jury service if such person does not "have a mental or physical condition or combination thereof, which causes the person to be incapable of performing in a reasonable manner the duties of a juror", is intended to eliminate the predetermination that individuals with physical disabilities are incapable of serving as jurors and does not preclude the court from determining whether the person's impairment would preclude him from serving as a reasonable juror in that particular case; since, in this case, a significant amount of the evidence consists of physical evidence, the blind individual would be at a disadvantage during jury deliberations in following the court's instructions to consider all the evidence presented at trial and, therefore, the blind individual's inability [***2] to visually perceive the physical evidence involved is a proper reason for excluding him from this particular jury.

 

1648 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Moore


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


126 Misc. 2d 482; 483 N.Y.S.2d 597; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3647



December 3, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's motion to suppress is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with possession of a loaded weapon and other weapons, with possession of three different controlled substances, and with possession of drug paraphernalia. He filed a motion to suppress evidence of the weapons and other contraband.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a passenger in a taxicab. Police officers assigned to a special taxicab crime fighting unit positioned themselves visibly near and behind the cab defendant was riding in. When he turned around three or four times to look at them, they stopped the taxicab. Defendant was found to be in possession of weapons and drug contraband. He moved to suppress that evidence on the ground that there was no probable cause for the initial stop. The court granted the motion. It considered the stop as a police/citizen encounter, under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 140.5(1), rather than a vehicle stop. In the court's opinion, the rationale for the stop was illogical because at the exact same moment of recognition, the officers gained and lost their stated reason for stopping defendant. There was no assertion that defendant had committed a crime, or was committing a crime. The court held that the police had seized defendant when they blocked him in the rear of the cab and that the seizure was not based on reasonable suspicion of criminal activity. Accordingly, the largely manufactured stop was constitutionally impermissible and the discovered evidence had to be suppressed.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to suppress.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Stop of Taxicab to Search Passenger

The practice of the New York City Police Department's Taxicab Robbery Squad of placing plain-clothes officers in unmarked cars in very visible positions in black or Hispanic areas and then stopping passing taxicabs to search passengers that the officers deem likely to be potential robbers is constitutionally impermissible where the officers have no objective reasons to stop the taxicabs; the law regarding automobile stops and the standing of a passenger in a vehicle for hire to move to suppress contraband seized from the stopped vehicle is inapplicable to such taxicab stops, which are akin to police-citizen street encounters requiring the police to have a reasonable suspicion that the person seized is about to commit a crime ( CPL 140.50), since a passenger in a cab has every right to continued and uninterrupted motion unless the police have a valid reason to interfere with that motion; accordingly, the fact that defendant turned around to look at the officers three or four times during the surveillance of the taxicab in which he was a passenger did [***2] not justify the stop of the taxicab and the weapons and other contraband seized must, therefore, be suppressed.

 

1649 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Buck v. Moran


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Queens County


126 Misc. 2d 836; 485 N.Y.S.2d 421; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3711



November 30, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, owner and tenants organization, filed motions to have a consent decree stayed or clarified to allow the tenants to remain in their buildings. The owner had entered a consent decree with the New York City Department of Housing Preservation and Development (DHPD) that required the owner to conform its buildings to the requirements of N.Y.C., N.Y., Housing Maintenance Code § D26-33.07 (Code) as the result of an enforcement action.


OVERVIEW: The DHPD treated the buildings as rooming units in a converted dwelling, whereas the owner and tenant organization argued that the buildings were dwelling units occupied by families. The tenant organization filed a motion to compel the department of aging (DA) and a private legal services organization to find housing for evicted tenants. On appeal, the court denied the motion because the DA only provided housing information, and it required specific statutory authorization to compel it to find housing. The court decided to stay enforcement of the consent decree and to allow the tenants to remain in their residences within the limits of the language § D26-33. In so holding, the court found that: (1) Section D26-33.07 was constitutional. (2) The city's definition of "family" in the Code was within its police power. (3) The ambiguity of the statutory language allowed the buildings to be viewed in two different ways. (4) Accepting the tenant's argument that the Code could be interpreted so that a "family" of one or more tenants occupying a room could rent adjoining rooms to boarders, where a family consisted of three unrelated persons, would lead to the least injustice.


OUTCOME: Enforcement of a consent decree between a building owner and DHPD was stayed, and the tenants were allowed to remain building residents. The motion to compel the DA and a private legal services organization to find housing for evicted tenants was denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and Tenant -- New York City Housing Maintenance Code -- Illegal Rooming Houses

In light of existing social and economic circumstances, the provision of the New York City Housing Maintenance Code which outlaws rooming units in "a tenement, converted dwelling or private dwelling" except in those buildings which were designated as such before 1954 (Administrative Code of City of New York § D26-33.07), should be interpreted so as to allow the tenants to remain in six buildings in Flushing, Queens, occupied as rooming houses by 95 single men, many of whom are ill, old and unemployed, since an interpretation of the Code that permits the buildings to be classified as multiple dwellings occupied by "families" living independently in dwelling units rather than in illegal rooming units in converted dwellings or that permits the creation of a "common household" through some structural changes in the buildings so that one or more of the tenants can be deemed a "family" that would "rent" one or more connected living rooms to boarders will lead to the least injustice; the original intent of the Code provision in 1954 to protect the health, safety and welfare of those living in [***2] rooming houses and single room occupancies by eliminating unsafe and unhealthy housing and providing them with better, safer accommodations is no longer completely valid today in view of the scarcity of adequate, affordable housing; accordingly, the Department of Housing Preservation and Development is stayed from taking any action against the owners of the subject buildings while they correct violations and formulate plans to conform the buildings.

 

1650 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Perry v. AAMCO Transmissions


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Rochester


126 Misc. 2d 454; 481 N.Y.S.2d 1007; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3641



November 29, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tool company brought a motion to vacate or strike defendant transmission company's demand for a trial de novo following an arbitrator's award.


OVERVIEW: The transmission company's attorney filed a demand for a trial de novo and alleged that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 22, § 28.12(a), which governed the time to make a demand for a trial de novo pursuant to a plan of compulsory arbitration of civil cases, violated the "due process" clause of U.S. Const. amend. V and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6 because it did not provide for "service" and "notice of entry" of an arbitrator's award before the 30 days in which to make a demand for a trial de novo began to run. On consideration of the issues, however, the court granted the tool company's motion to vacate the request for a trial de novo. Specifically, the court held that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 22, § 28.12(a) was constitutionally defective insofar as it did not provide that the 30 days within which to demand a trial de novo did not begin to run until the parties were served with a copy of the award. As a result, by analogy, the court applied the rules governing the time to take an appeal, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5513(a) and N.Y. C.P.L.R. 2103(b), (c), to calculate whether the demand was timely, but nevertheless determined that the demand was late by one day.


OUTCOME: The court granted the tool company's motion to vacate or strike the request for a trial de novo, which had been filed by the transmission company.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Arbitration -- Compulsory Arbitration -- Demand for Trial De Novo

The rule governing the demand for a trial de novo under the plan of compulsory arbitration of civil cases in effect in the Seventh Judicial District requiring a party to demand a trial de novo "within 30 days after the award is filed with the appropriate court clerk" (22 NYCRR 28.12 [a]), the arbitration commissioner being responsible for mailing the award to the parties at the same time he notifies the parties of its filing, is constitutionally defective insofar as it does not provide that the 30 days within which to demand a trial de novo do not begin to run until the parties are served with a copy of the award with notice of its filing since a party's time to file a demand for a trial de novo must be afforded due process in view of the preservation of the constitutional right to a jury trial through the availability of a trial de novo; until the rule is amended, the rules governing the time to take an appeal will be applied permitting an appeal to be taken within 30 days "after service upon the appellant of a copy of the judgment or order appealed from and written notice of its entry"  [***2] ( CPLR 5513, subd [a]) with an additional five days added on when a copy of the judgment or order and notice of its entry are served by mail ( CPLR 2103, subd [b], par 2; subd [c]); accordingly, excluding the day of mailings of the notice of the filing card along with the arbitrator's award on August 20, 1984 and adding five days to the prescribed period, defendant's demand for a trial de novo on September 24, 1984 is still untimely by one day and the court has no discretion to extend the demand period in the interest of justice.

 

1651 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Jancyn Mfg. Corp. v. County of Suffolk


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


126 Misc. 2d 463; 482 N.Y.S.2d 676; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3643



November 28, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff company filed an action against defendant county in a federal district court challenging the county's ordinance, County of Suffolk, N.Y., Local Law § 12-1980. The federal court abstained to allow a state court to determine whether state law preempted the ordinance. The company then filed an action against the county in the court. Both parties sought summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance barred the sale of chemicals used to clean sewers. N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law §§ 39-0101, -0105 (state law) limited its application to only the county and one other county. The state law prohibited the sale of sewer cleaners containing restricted chemicals. The court found that only where the state law was intended to occupy the entire field, marked by a pervasive scheme of state regulation, that the local legislation had to yield. The state law only prohibited the use of certain amounts of chemicals. There was no comprehensive and detailed regulatory scheme adopted by the state with respect to sewer system cleaners. The court noted that the New York State Department of Environmental Conservation, the agency responsible for interpreting the state law, deferred to the county's acceptance of the product analysis and the county's compliance requirements in the county ordinance. There was no evidence that the state intended to preempt the area at issue by way of Envtl. Conserv. Law §§ 15, 17, which stated that the state had exclusive jurisdiction to regulate and control water resources in the state.


OUTCOME: The court granted the county's motion for summary judgment in the company's action against the county claiming that the county's ordinance was preempted by state law.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Local Law Regulating Sewage System Cleaners and Additives

ECL article 39 does not preempt a local law which prohibits the sale of certain cesspool additives. There is no clear-cut showing that the Legislature intended to occupy the entire field of sewage system cleaners and additives; ECL 39-0105 prohibits only the use of certain amounts of restricted chemical material; nowhere does the legislation touch on any other areas of potential restriction as does the local law in question. Moreover, since there is no explicit preemption contained in the statute, and since the administrative agency charged with enforcing the statute has adopted a not unrational interpretation that ECL article 39 does not preempt the enactment or continued viability of the local law, the court will not rule otherwise. Neither ECL article 15 nor article 17, which vests the power to regulate and control water resources of the State exclusively with the State, contains a clear-cut intent to preempt the field of sewage system cleaners and additives.

 

1652 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Flinn


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Buffalo County


126 Misc. 2d 570; 482 N.Y.S.2d 418; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3666



November 23, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion seeking the dismissal of an information charging him with resisting an officer in the discharge of his duty, in violation of Buffalo, (New York), Ordinances ch. 9, § 24 (Ordinance) and resisting arrest, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 205.30.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating the Ordinance and with resisting arrest, based on the violation of the Ordinance. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss, arguing that the state preempted the area of the police power sought to be exercised in the Ordinance by enacting N.Y. Penal Law § 195.05. Defendant also argued that the Ordinance was unconstitutionally vague. The court found that that the state legislature did not preempt the field by the enactment of § 195.05. Further, because the purpose of the Ordinance was not in conflict with the purpose and language of §195.05, the Ordinance was not void as in conflict with state law. However, the court held that the language of the Ordinance was, on its face, indefinite, vague and without clear meaning. Thus, the Ordinance was unconstitutional and the court dismissed the charge based on the violation of the Ordinance. Further, inasmuch the Ordinance was unconstitutionally vague, it could not serve as the basis for the resisting arrest charge. Therefore, the violation of § 205.30 was also dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion by defendant to dismiss an information charging defendant with violating an Ordinance prohibiting resting an officer in the discharge of his duty and resisting arrest.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Resisting Officer in Discharge of Duty -- Void-for-Vagueness

A local ordinance of the City of Buffalo entitled "Resisting Officer in Discharge of Duty", which makes it illegal for a person to "refuse to obey a reasonable order or request of a police officer in the discharge of his regular duty" or to "resist or interfere with any officer or employee of the City in the discharge of his regular duty", is not invalid on preemption grounds since the gravamen of the State obstruction of governmental administration statute (Penal Law, § 195.05) is the prohibition of an intentional obstruction by means of intimidation, physical force or interference or by means of any independently unlawful act while the local ordinance restricts less serious behavior than that proscribed by the State statute; however, since there is no description nor definition in the ordinance as to what constitutes the regular duty of a police officer or employee of the city so as to enable persons of ordinary intelligence to understand the type of conduct proscribed, the ordinance is void as unconstitutionally vague and indefinite.

 

1653 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Under 21 v. City of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


126 Misc. 2d 629; 481 N.Y.S.2d 632; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3680; 37 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P35,377



November 15, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the plaintiff's requests for a preliminary injunction are denied. The temporary restraints are terminated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff organizations sought a preliminary injunction in the court against defendants, city and board members, to enjoin the enforcement of a resolution, which requested that certain private agencies seeking renewal of various social contracts avoid discrimination based on sexual orientation in hiring and firing persons connected with the contracts.


OVERVIEW: The New York City Board of Estimate enacted a resolution requesting that certain private agencies seeking renewal of various social service contracts avoid discrimination based on sexual orientation in their hiring or firing of persons connected with the contracts. Plaintiff organizations sought a preliminary injunction against defendants, city and board members, to enjoin enforcement of the board's resolution. The court denied plaintiffs' request for a preliminary injunction because plaintiffs had not demonstrated that the board's resolution was invalid. Neither res judicata nor collateral estoppel were applicable. The board's resolution bore a legitimate relationship to the best interests of defendant city and was within the realm of the board's authority. The court held that homosexuality was a protected category under both the state and federal constitutions, and that based on the concept of state action, plaintiffs were directly subject to the constitutional mandates ordinarily applicable only to government entities. The court further held that the hiring of employees by plaintiffs was integrally related to their public function and was not a collateral function.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiff organizations' request for a preliminary injunction against defendants, city and board members, to enjoin the enforcement of a resolution that certain private agencies avoid discrimination based on sexual orientation in connection with various social contracts because plaintiffs did not demonstrate that the resolution was invalid and homosexuality was a protected category under both the state and federal constitutions.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- New York City Board of Estimate -- Power to Adopt Resolution Preventing Discrimination Based on Sexual Orientation

1. It was a proper exercise of the power of the Board of Estimate of the City of New York to adopt a resolution designed to prevent discrimination in hiring based on sexual orientation and affectional preference, i.e., homosexuality, and to include provisions in extension contracts offered to plaintiffs which required them not to so discriminate. The Board of Estimate, pursuant to its power to approve contracts (NY City Charter, § 349, subd a), necessarily has the power to modify the terms of a contract or to insist that particular clauses be included in contracts; the subject resolution satisfies section 343 of the New York City Charter, which requires specific action on each contract approved by the Board, since it is specific and limited in application to one group of similar contracts. It is within the Board's power to determine that limiting the award of service contracts to agencies who will refrain from [***2] discriminatory practices serves the City's best financial interests by insuring that contract costs are not artificially and unnecessarily inflated as a result of a construction of the labor pool that is produced by discrimination. Moreover, there is support for the proposition that arbitrary treatment of persons based solely on their sexual preference may constitute an infringement of their rights under the United States Constitution and the New York State Constitution.

Civil Rights -- Discrimination Based on Sexual Preference -- Homosexuality

2. The right of individuals to be free of arbitrary and disparate treatment in their employment solely on the basis of sexual preference is one of constitutional magnitude both under the Fourteenth Amendment and under section 11 of article I of the New York State Constitution; homosexuality is a protected category under both the State and Federal Constitutions. Thus, the Board of Estimate of the City of New York did not engage in legislative action by adopting a resolution designed to prohibit discrimination in employment based on sexual orientation or affectional preference; the resolution merely stated the law as it presently [***3] is. Moreover, the Board, consistent with its duty to approve contracts, merely included within contracts offered to plaintiffs, by means of said resolution, the constitutional obligations by which the parties are necessarily bound.

Judgments -- Res Judicata -- Collateral Estoppel

3. Defendant City of New York is not barred under the doctrines of res judicata and collateral estoppel from litigating the issue of the validity of a resolution of the Board of Estimate concerning the prohibition of employment discrimination on the basis of "sexual orientation" or "affectional preference" adopted independently of any executive order and directed at specific contracts with plaintiffs, by a prior court decision holding that an executive order which also attempted to proscribe discrimination based on sexual preference was invalid and that a Board resolution, which ratified the invalid executive order, was equally invalid. Despite the broad language in the prior court's decision to the effect that the Board cannot legislate and cannot create social policy, it is clear that the earlier court did not and could not consider the powers of the Board of Estimate to adopt a resolution [***4] of specific import that is presently before the court; although both resolutions involve an attempt to proscribe discrimination based on sexual preference, the issues presented in the two cases differ substantially and compel the result that neither res judicata nor collateral estoppel are applicable.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- State Action -- Private Agency Funded by City of New York

4. Plaintiffs, private agencies who perform a public function and receive substantial funding from the City of New York, are performing a State action and, therefore, directly subject to the constitutional mandates ordinarily applicable only to government entities. Thus, plaintiffs, who provide social services to children pursuant to contracts with the City of New York, are subject to the constitutional requirement not to discriminate based on sexual orientation or affectional preference in the hiring of personnel in violation of equal protection provided by the Federal and State Constitutions; the hiring of employees by plaintiff agencies is integrally related to their public function and is not a collateral function which would possibly negate a finding of State [***5] action.

Municipal Corporations -- New York City Board of Estimate -- Power to Include Clause in City Contracts Forbidding Discrimination Based on Sexual Orientation

5. The Board of Estimate, as the agency of the City of New York charged with the duty to approve contracts, has an obligation under the State and Federal Constitutions to ensure that the City's funds are not used to support, encourage or in any other way implicate the City in the discriminatory conduct of others. Accordingly, consistent with its constitutionally imposed duty not to support private discrimination on the basis of sexual orientation or affectional preference, the City, by the Board of Estimate, had the right to include a clause forbidding such discrimination in hiring in the contracts of plaintiff agencies who receive substantial financial funding from the City and who have openly admitted that they intend to practice such discrimination.

 

1654 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Simpson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


126 Misc. 2d 162; 481 N.Y.S.2d 293; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3568



November 1, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Upon the foregoing reasoning, respondent's motion for a pretrial suppression hearing is denied without prejudice to such challenge at trial, consistent with the above, as respondent may be advised.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was respondent mother's motion for a pretrial suppression hearing that was filed pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 710, 60.45, which were the governing statutory provisions to test the issue of voluntariness in a criminal action and proceeding.


OVERVIEW: A civil child abuse proceeding was filed against the mother as the result of the death of an 11-month-old infant, and was filed for the protection of the two siblings. The mother made a motion for a pretrial suppression hearing in accordance with §§ 710, 60.45, which were the governing provisions to test the issue of voluntariness in a criminal action and proceeding. The court denied the mother's motion because the confession obtained during a custodial interrogation could not be challenged as involuntary upon the same grounds in a civil proceeding as in a criminal prosecution. The court held that the traditional standard of proof in a civil proceeding was prescribed as the preponderance standard for adjudication of abuse or neglect in a child protective proceeding. The court reasoned that a greater standard might unduly jeopardize the well-being of a child in a proceeding that was fraught with difficulty of proof.


OUTCOME: The court denied the mother's motion for a pretrial suppression hearing that was filed pursuant to a New York statute, which contained the provisions to test the issue of voluntariness in a criminal action and proceeding.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- Child Protection Proceeding -- Fifth Amendment Exclusionary Rule

1. In a child protection proceeding involving the siblings of respondent's deceased child, the Fifth Amendment exclusionary doctrine is not available to respondent to challenge admissibility of a "statement" made during the custodial-interrogation stage of a criminal action based upon the death of her child; the interests at stake in the conventional civil proceeding, even a child protection proceeding, do not fall within the aegis of the Fifth Amendment privilege.

Infants -- Child Protection Proceeding -- Common-Law Exclusionary Rule -- Procedure for Determining Voluntariness

2. In a child protection proceeding involving the siblings of respondent's deceased child, the common-law exclusionary rule is available to respondent to challenge admissibility of a "statement" made during the custodial-interrogation stage of a criminal action based upon the death of her child; application of such [***2] rule is required to ensure trustworthiness of the evidence and the integrity of the fact-finding process. Moreover, the appropriate procedure for determination of voluntariness in the nonjury trial is the traditional voir dire.

Infants -- Child Protection Proceeding -- Admissibility of Inculpatory Statement Made During Criminal Investigation -- Standard of Proof

3. In a child protection proceeding involving the siblings of respondent's deceased child, the standard of proof for admissibility of a "statement" made by respondent during the custodial-interrogation stage of a criminal action based upon the death of her child is the preponderance of the evidence standard; the preponderance standard is consonant with the clear legislative intent to safeguard the child and strikes the proper balance for the competing interests involved.

 

1655 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Shore Realty Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Nassau County


127 Misc. 2d 419; 486 N.Y.S.2d 124; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3759



October 31, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant realty corporation, charged with the violation of six sections of article III of the Nassau County Fire Prevention Ordinance (ordinance), which pertained to the storage of flammable chemicals, sought an order dismissing the information on the grounds, inter alia, that the statute defining the offense charged was unconstitutional and that the prosecution constituted selective enforcement in violation of the right to equal protection.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that because the ordinance incorporated by reference certain standards of a private association, it failed to notify an individual of the conduct proscribed, and constituted an improper delegation of legislative authority in violation of N.Y. Const. art. III, § 16. The court denied that portion of defendant's motion seeking a dismissal of the information on the ground of discriminatory enforcement, holding that the ordinance was constitutional; it provided adequate notice of the conduct proscribed and did unconstitutionally delegate legislative power. The ordinance survived a vagueness challenge because defendant, as both owner and operator of the facility where the violations were alleged to exist, had adequate notice that it was required to comply with the ordinance. The ordinance did not violate equal protection in providing for a greater maximum fine for a corporation than for an individual. Defendant proved its claim of discriminatory enforcement; a selective enforcement strategy could be directed at serious violations that had progressed beyond tolerable limits. The factual part of count six of the information was held to be insufficient on its face.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the information, but granted the government leave to amend count six to set forth the specific acts allegedly committed by defendant in violation of the ordinance.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Fire Code -- Discriminatory Enforcement

Article III of the Nassau County Fire Prevention Ordinance, by incorporating by reference certain standards of the National Fire Protection Association on file with the clerk of the County Board of Supervisors, does not constitute an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority (NY Const, art III, § 16) since the standards are on file with the legislative body for all to peruse and the ordinance thus provides adequate notice of the conduct proscribed; the ordinance is not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant for failing to specify whether the owner and/or operator of a facility is required to comply with its provisions since defendant is both the owner and operator of the facility in question and it thus had adequate notice of the conduct proscribed; the ordinance is not violative of defendant's right to equal protection for imposing a larger maximum fine for a corporation than for an individual violator since the distinction has a rational basis in view of the fact that a corporation cannot be imprisoned; the fact that the fire marshal did not enforce the fire code against the prior owners [***2] of the facility is insufficient to meet the burden of proof required to sustain a claim of discriminatory enforcement which requires proof that the prosecution is motivated by a constitutionally impermissible reason.

 

1656 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sposito


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Sixth District, Suffolk County


126 Misc. 2d 185; 481 N.Y.S.2d 613; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3573



October 30, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss the complaint is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state cited defendant property owner for a violation of Town of Brookhaven, N.Y., Code art. XLIII, § 85-421A, which prohibited the use of any part of a parking or loading area for "sales, dead storage, repair work, dismantling or servicing of any kind." The property owner filed a motion to dismiss and challenged the code provision on constitutional grounds.


OVERVIEW: The property owner utilized the front portion of her property for the storage of a camper and a truck, during the time periods when she was not on camping trips. The state cited the property owner for keeping her truck and camper as "dead storage" in violation of Town of Brookhaven, N.Y., Code art. XLIII, § 85-421A. The property owner sought dismissal, based upon her contention that the code provision was unconstitutionally void for vagueness. The court agreed with the position advanced by the property owner and granted the motion to dismiss. In reaching its decision, the court held that the term "dead storage" was not sufficient to give fair warning of what was prohibited by the code provision. Thus, the court concluded that the code provision was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to dismiss filed on behalf of the property owner.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Vagueness -- "Dead Storage" Parking of Vehicle

Defendant, who keeps a three-quarter ton truck and a slide-on camper in her front yard, is entitled to have a complaint charging her with violating a town ordinance prohibiting the use of any part of a parking area for "dead storage" dismissed since, in light of the void for vagueness doctrine, the term "dead storage" being defined nowhere in the ordinance, and in light of the strict construction standards that must be applied to zoning ordinances, fair warning of what is prohibited has not been given for the term "dead storage", which is open to varying interpretations.

 

1657 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Patricia R. v. Peter W.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


126 Misc. 2d 87; 480 N.Y.S.2d 1020; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3553



October 29, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother had established paternity in the proceeding. She filed a motion seeking counsel fees and various court costs. Respondent father opposed the request for counsel fees but conceded liability for court costs. He requested that any costs the court awarded be held in escrow pending the outcome of his appeal. He also requested a hearing on his ability to pay if the court awarded counsel fees.


OVERVIEW: The father had been ordered to pay child support. Part of the court's decision declared the statute of limitations in the N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act §517(a) unconstitutional. The father appealed both orders. Because of the significant questions in the appeal and the father's substantial contributions toward support of the child, the court granted the request to hold court costs in escrow. The mother's attorney was assigned from the indigent defendants legal panel pursuant to N.Y. County Law art. 18-B. Pursuant to N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 536 the court ruled that the father was liable for that portion of the mother's counsel fee which he was able to pay, even though the attorney was not privately retained. The mother's attorney requested the hourly rate he charged private clients, which was more than four times the rate paid to attorneys from the panel. The court concluded that his work justified the higher rate, but because he was assigned the attorney undertook to represent the mother at the rate paid to panel attorneys and must be held to that commitment.


OUTCOME: The court granted the mother's request for counsel fees and set the fee at the rate paid the indigent defendants legal panel. The court ordered the court costs that the father conceded paid into escrow pending his appeal. The court scheduled a hearing on the father's current ability to pay the counsel fee.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children Born out of Wedlock -- Paternity Proceeding -- Counsel Fees

Counsel fees may be awarded pursuant to section 536 of the Family Court Act, which provides that once an order of filiation is made the court may allow counsel fees to the attorney for the prevailing party if he or she is unable to pay such counsel fees, to an attorney assigned from the Indigent Defendants Legal Panel; section 536 must be construed in a manner which is consistent with the court's duty to see that public funds allotted for indigent litigants are appropriately utilized, and accordingly, respondent is liable for that portion of petitioner's counsel fee which he is able to pay, notwithstanding that the attorney was assigned rather than privately retained. The attorney is to be compensated at the rate paid to panel attorneys, and not at the rate he charges private clients.

 

1658 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Sanchez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Bronx County


126 Misc. 2d 199; 481 N.Y.S.2d 601; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3578



October 24, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner public assistance recipient brought an action to receive the proceeds of a claim arising from the death of her child. Movant Commissioner of the New York Department of Social Services sought to vacate a conditional settlement of a lien the Department held on the proceeds. He also sought to interpose a claim for the entire net proceeds of the recovery.


OVERVIEW: The recipient assigned her interest in her child's estate to the Commissioner. The recipient and the Commissioner stipulated that the recipient would receive $ 2,000 outright, with another $ 12,400 to be paid to her on the condition that she leave the United States for the Dominican Republic. In the event the recipient failed to leave the United States, the $ 12,400 would be paid to the Department. The stipulation also provided that upon receipt of the funds, the recipient would neither contest the termination of her public assistance case nor reapply for public assistance benefits in the United States for a period of two years from the date of such termination. Noting that the recipient was a citizen of the United States, the court held that the agreement violated her constitutional right to travel. The state could neither require her to remain in the Dominican Republic nor prevent her from returning to New York, even if her return resulted in her readmission to the public assistance rolls. Therefore, the stipulation could not be enforced. The court added that it would be permissible for the funds to be distributed to the recipient periodically in lieu of public assistance.


OUTCOME: The court granted the Commissioner's motion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Stipulations -- Setting Stipulation Aside -- Enforcement of Consideration Barred by Public Policy

A lienor is entitled to vacate a conditional settlement of its lien where the enforcement of the consideration it receives under the settlement is barred by public policy. Thus, where petitioner, the sole distributee of decedent's estate, and respondent Commissioner of the Department of Social Services entered into a stipulation which provided for the payment of a sum of money to petitioner upon the condition of her departing the United States, that in the event she failed to leave the United States, the money would be paid to the Department of Social Services, and that upon petitioner's receipt of said funds, she would neither contest the termination of her public assistance case nor reapply for public assistance benefits in this country for two years from the date of such termination, such stipulation is in clear derogation of petitioner's constitutional rights to travel and to due process of law and is, therefore, not enforceable; a contract made in violation of a statute is not enforceable, even though only one party's action would be in violation of the statute. Accordingly,  [***2] a motion by the lienor Commissioner to vacate the stipulation and to interpose a claim against the estate for the entire net proceeds of the recovery pursuant to an assignment in his favor executed by petitioner is granted.

 

1659 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


State Communities Aid Asso. v. Regan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


125 Misc. 2d 1083; 482 N.Y.S.2d 960; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3533



October 18, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendants' motion for summary judgment is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to N.Y. State Fin. Law § 7-A, plaintiff, a state communities aid association, sought a declaration that defendants, a State and its comptroller, caused, were causing, or had caused a wrongful expenditure or unconstitutional disbursement of state home energy assistance program (HEAP) funds. The association also sought relief under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The association renewed its summary judgment motion.


OVERVIEW: Originally, state households that received home relief (HR) were given grants for (a) food, clothing, and incidentals; (b) shelter; and (c) fuel for heating. Later, the State added a fourth component called "home energy" grants, which covered utility costs for lights, water, and cooking. The United States Congress then passed the Low Income Energy Act of 1981, authorizing HEAP grants to states to assist eligible households meet the cost of home energy. 42 U.S.C.S. § 8624(b) provided that the HEAP funds could be used to meet the cost of heating or cooling and not for any other purposes. The State adopted 1953 N.Y. Laws 53, substituting HEAP funds for the previously awarded "home energy" grants. The court awarded summary judgment to the association because HR households receiving HEAP funds both for heating and for utilities were receiving a greater share of HEAP funds than non-HR households, contrary to the heating cost-to-income ratio mandated by federal law. The court did not award attorney's fees pursuant to § 1983 because it did not appear that defendants intended to discriminate against any particular class.


OUTCOME: The court granted the association's renewed summary judgment motion and enjoined defendants from providing HR recipients with the additional HEAP grant.


HEADNOTES

Social Services -- Public Assistance -- Misallocation of Federal Funds

1. In a citizen-taxpayer action brought under article 7-A of the State Finance Law, plaintiff taxpayers are entitled to summary judgment and defendant State Comptroller is enjoined from providing home relief recipients with Home Energy Assistance Program (HEAP) funds as authorized by chapter 53 of the Laws of 1983; under section 8624 (subd [b]) of title 42 of the United States Code, States which receive Federal HEAP grants must use the funds only to assist eligible households with respect to home heating and cooling whereas chapter 53 authorizes HEAP funds to be used as a substitute for that component (home energy grant) of home relief covering utility costs for lights, hot water and cooking; consequently this misapplication of HEAP funds serves to diminish the amount available to other qualified classes and is in violation of the allocation requirements of the Federal law (US Code, tit 42, § 8624, subd [b]); furthermore, although the Federal scheme permits transfer of up to 10% of the grant, such funds may not be permissibly transferred to utility [***2] expenses.

Attorney and Client -- Compensation

2. Where a citizen-taxpayer group prevails in an action brought under article 7-A of the State Finance Law and demonstrates that a person, under color of statute, deprived the injured party of civil rights, the court may, in its discretion, allow attorney's fees to the prevailing party (US Code, tit 42, § 1988); however, where the defendants did not intend to discriminate against any particular class and they were not obdurate or obstinate in the implementation of the unlawful program, attorney's fees are unwarranted; furthermore, although section 123-g of the State Finance Law permits an award of attorney's fees in citizen-taxpayer actions from a fund established by section 123-h of that law, plaintiffs are not entitled to counsel fees from this fund in the absence of evidence that such fund exists and the amount of money therein.
