HNC Realty Co. v. Bay View Towers Apartments, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


125 Misc. 2d 1088; 481 N.Y.S.2d 261; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3534



October 16, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion of the Lienor's Committee is also denied, but without prejudice to its renewal or by a proceeding by separate new action to remove a cloud upon title, if so advised.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In foreclosure proceedings by plaintiff realty company against defendants, property owner and lienors, petitioner lienor's committee filed a motion to vacate a certain recorded deed to real property by the property owner to a certain corporation. Petitioner city filed a motion to vacate the stay of an administrative hearing on the issue of real property transfer taxes before the Commissioner of Finance (commissioner).


OVERVIEW: The matter concerned a certain deed of conveyance of real property executed to the corporation by the property owner on the day of final judgment in the foreclosure proceedings. The city filed a motion to vacate the stay of an administrative hearing concerning the real property transfer taxes arising from the deed. The court denied the city's motion and found that the city's delay of nearly two and one half years in making a determination on the transfer taxes was unreasonable. The city had all the information from the foreclosure proceedings, of which the city was a party but the corporation was not, to make it's determination. Furthermore, it should have been clear that the deed in question, made subject to the mortgage foreclosed upon, was not executed in lieu of foreclosure as the city alleged. Though the corporation had the burden of proving any prior liens, that burden was met because the city had actual or constructive knowledge of all the facts relevant to the corporation's position. The court also denied the motion by the lienor's committee to vacate the deed because the corporation, as grantee on the deed, was a necessary party.


OUTCOME: The court denied the city's motion to vacate the stay of an administrative hearing on transfer taxes on the property in question and denied the committee's motion to vacate a certain deed to real property from the apartment complex to the corporation.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- New York City Real Property Transfer Tax -- Notice of "Determination of Tax"

That portion of the real property transfer tax statute of the City of New York requiring the Director of the Department of Finance to give notice of the "determination of the tax" following a conveyance of real property to the person against whom the tax is assessed (Administrative Code of City of New York, § I46-7.0), to the extent that it does not provide for a specific time within which the Director is required to make the "determination", is unconstitutional for indefiniteness and absurdity since the substantive purpose of the real property transfer tax is of such importance as to require a very short and definite time standard within which the City must make its "determination" otherwise, a complete standstill in real estate transactions could occur for fear of adverse determinations being made an inordinate period of time after purchase or sale or even after the receipt of a mortgage; until the statute [***2] is amended, the time period within which the City is required to make a "determination" is construed to mean within a "reasonable" period of time; accordingly, the "determination" of the City that the subject deed-conveyance was a conveyance "in lieu of foreclosure", made more than two years after the real property transfer tax return was filed, is without merit factually and "as a matter of law".

 

1661 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Sondra S. v. Jay O.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


126 Misc. 2d 322; 482 N.Y.S.2d 660; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3611



October 15, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother instituted a proceeding to establish that respondent individual was the father of her child. The court issued a written opinion regarding its jurisdiction to consider the matter.


OVERVIEW: The mother filed a petition stating that she and the individual had planned to be married, that she became pregnant by him, and that he was killed in a car accident prior to the birth of the child. She sought an order of filiation to ensure that the child would receive any benefits due to him as a result of the individual's death. The trial court determined that it had jurisdiction over the matter, holding that the right to establish paternity existed after the death of the putative father. The court further determined that the requirement that the proceeding be adversarial could be properly met by the appointment of a representative to conduct the proceedings on behalf of the individual's estate. The court also found that it was necessary to appoint a guardian to represent the interests of the child.


OUTCOME: The court entered an order declaring that it had jurisdiction over the matter, named the individual's parents as his representatives, directed the mother to obtain a new summons and petition naming the parents as respondents, ordered the mother to effect personal service on the parents, and appointed a guardian for the child.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Declaratory Judgments -- When Remedy Appropriate

1. Since the Legislature has vested exclusive original jurisdiction over paternity proceedings in the Family Court and the function of a declaratory judgment is to determine justiciable controversies between parties which either are not ripe for adjudication by conventional forms of remedy or for other reasons are not amenable to the usual remedies, a paternity proceeding in the Family Court is the appropriate remedy for controversies concerning the determination of fatherhood.

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Paternity Proceeding -- Death of Putative Father before Commencement of Proceeding

2. A Family Court has jurisdiction to entertain a paternity proceeding against a putative father who died prior to the commencement of the proceeding since the Legislature, by its amendment of section 518 of the Family Court Act, made it clear that the right to bring a paternity proceeding exists after the death, mental illness or absence of the mother and to construe the legislative intent as being to exclude the survival of paternity actions after the death of the father would be inappropriate as the Legislature, which has expanded [***2] the obligations and benefits received by an order of filiation to include, among others, the right to inherit, the right to recover benefits under the Workers' Compensation Law, the Veterans' Benefits Act and the Social Security Act, and the right to recover serviceman's life insurance proceeds, would be providing rights to out-of-wedlock children that survive the death of the father and which attach by establishing paternity without providing a forum to obtain a remedy; furthermore, the requirement that the paternity proceeding be adversarial can be met by permitting an authorized person, such as the next of kin to the child, to conduct the defense of the proceeding and by appointing a Law Guardian to represent the interests of the child.

 

1662 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Fisher v. Velasquez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Kings County


126 Misc. 2d 24; 480 N.Y.S.2d 992; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3539



October 12, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] By reason of the foregoing, petitioners' motion for summary judgment pursuant to CPLR 3212 is denied and the petition and notice of petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlords filed a motion for summary judgment for an order of final judgment of possession pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. § 3212. Respondent tenant argued that the 30-day notice to terminate the tenancy was served in violation of an existing stay of a pending protest and that judgment of possession should not be granted to the landlord.


OVERVIEW: The tenant filed a timely protest after the landlords obtained a certificate of eviction. During the pendency of the tenant's protest proceeding, the landlords served upon the tenant a 30-day notice to terminate the tenancy. Thereafter, a notice of petition and petition in holdover was personally served on the tenant. The landlords filed a motion for summary judgment for an order of final judgment of possession pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3212. The tenant argued that the 30-day notice to terminate the tenancy was served in violation of the existing stay of the pending protest, and that judgment of possession should not be granted to the landlord. The court held that during the pendency of the protest determination, a statutory stay of the order of the certificate of eviction was in force. Thus, the court ruled that the 30-day notice to terminate, invalid at inception, could not be revived by a subsequent, favorable change of facts, i.e, the termination of the stay.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the holdover procedure for lack of subject matter jurisdiction because the proceeding was jurisdictionally defective by virtue of the invalid notice to terminate.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Certificate of Eviction -- Tenant's Protest

1. Pursuant to section 101 of the New York City Rent and Eviction Regulations a tenant's timely filing with the Rent Commission of a protest against his landlord's certificate of eviction stays the order granting the certificate until final determination of the protest by the Commissioner and this stay is effective notwithstanding landlord's lack of notice of the protest; furthermore, landlord's service of a 30-day notice to terminate the tenancy during the stay is without force or effect; in addition, since the notice to terminate is invalid, the court lacks subject matter jurisdiction over the instant holdover proceeding.

Landlord and Tenant -- Summary Proceedings -- Restoration to Calendar

2. A landlord's holdover proceeding which is marked off calendar due to tenant's filing of a protest against an order granting a certificate of eviction (New York City Rent and Eviction Regulations, § 101) is restored to the calendar when, after final [***2] determination of the protest, the tenant moves for summary judgment on the holdover proceeding.

Landlord and Tenant -- Summary Proceedings -- Tenant's Right to Possession

3. Assuming that this court had jurisdiction over this holdover proceeding, the court would find in favor of the tenant pursuant to chapter 234 of the Laws of 1984, which provides that a landlord is not entitled to owner-occupancy possession of a housing accommodation where the tenant has been a tenant in the building for 20 years or more, since the tenant meets the statutory requirements and since the tenant was still vested with a legal right to possession at the time the statute was enacted; although an order granting a certificate of eviction had been issued by the Rent Commission, the tenant's legal right to possession continues until all review proceedings are concluded.

 

1663 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Delford Industries, Inc. v. New York State Dep't of Environmental Conservation


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Orange County


126 Misc. 2d 355; 481 N.Y.S.2d 587; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3620



October 10, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is dismissed and the stay contained in the order to show cause is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation instituted a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding to obtain an order prohibiting and staying respondent, New York State Department of Environmental Conservation (department), from conducting an adjudicatory public hearing for alleged violations of 6 N.Y. C.R.R. 211.2, and to obtain an order declaring a portion of 6 N.Y. C.R.R. 211.2 unconstitutionally void for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: The corporation was charged with violating New York's air pollution regulations. The corporation argued that the department exceeded its authority in promulgating 6 N.Y. C.R.R. 211.2 pursuant to N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 19-0103. The department argued that the corporation had failed to exhaust its administrative remedies and 6 N.Y. C.R.R. 211.2 was not constitutionally vague. The court dismissed the corporation's petition and vacated the stay of the department hearing. The court found, inter alia, that (1) on the record it was impossible to determine what basis was used in formulating 6 N.Y. C.R.R. 211.2, such that the court could not conclude that the N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 19-0103 was violated in the adoption of that regulation; (2) the determination of whether the department had exceeded its authority in promulgating the regulation could best be made after both parties had an opportunity to present their evidence at the hearing; (3) no clear right to the relief of prohibition had been shown; and (4) the control of air pollution of necessity required language broad in nature, which could not be as precise as the corporation sought, but it was nonetheless adequate.


OUTCOME: The court (1) dismissed the corporation's petition to obtain an order prohibiting and staying the department from conducting as adjudicatory hearing for air pollution violations; and (2) vacated the stay of the department hearing contained in the order to show cause.


HEADNOTES

Environmental Conservation -- Air Pollution Control -- Scientific Basis of Regulation Pertaining to Prohibition of Air Pollution

1. The court cannot conclude that ECL 19-0103, which provides that rules and regulations established under ECL article 19 should be clearly premised upon scientific knowledge of causes as well as effects, was violated in the adoption of 6 NYCRR 211.2, which pertains to the prohibition of air pollution. On the record before the court, it is impossible to determine what basis was used in formulating the regulation; to grant such relief would require an assumption that respondents proceeded in excess of their jurisdiction; the court will not make such an assumption.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Regulation -- Vagueness -- Regulation Pertaining to Prohibition of Air Pollution

2. Petitioner is not entitled to a declaration that 6 NYCRR 211.2, which pertains to the prohibition of air pollution, is unconstitutional on grounds that it is vague, indefinite and uncertain. 6 NYCRR 211.2 was intended to protect the health and welfare of the public in [***2] a field which modern science has shown to be necessary; the control of air pollution of necessity requires language broad in nature which cannot be as precise as petitioner seeks.

 

1664 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Feimer v. Ward


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


127 Misc. 2d 853; 487 N.Y.S.2d 458; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3765



October 3, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the application is denied and the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applicant filed an application pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 for an order directing respondent police commissioner to appoint him as a city police officer or, alternatively, directing that his name be certified for the position of city police officer.


OVERVIEW: The applicant was notified that he was not qualified for a police officer position pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 434a-8.0(a) because he was more than 29 years of age. The applicant contended that § 434a-8.0(a) violated N.Y. Exec. Law § 296(1)(d), which made it unlawful to discriminate as to age unless it was a bona fide occupational qualification, and that the age limitation was an unconstitutional denial of equal protection. The court denied the applicant's application. Where there was neither a suspect classification nor infringement of a fundamental right, a classification would be sustained so long as there was a rational relationship between the classes established and a legitimate governmental interest. The age limitation furthered a legitimate state interest in assuring the physical preparedness of the police and did not violate equal protection. Age was a bona fide occupational qualification because it was related to ability to perform the rigorous tasks related to police work. Exceptions to maximum age limitations, whether based on veteran's status or discretion, did not affect the validity of the age limitation as a bona fide occupational qualification.


OUTCOME: The court denied the applicant's application for an order directing the police commissioner to appoint him as a city police officer or, alternatively, directing that his name be certified for the position of city police officer.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Police Officers -- Appointment and Promotion -- 29-Year Age Limitation for New York City Police Applicants

The age limitation restricting appointment for the position of police officer in New York City to only those under the age of 29 at the time of the filing of an application for civil service examination (Administrative Code of City of New York § 434a-8.0 [a]) does not constitute unlawful age discrimination since the age limitation is a bona fide occupational qualification ( Executive Law § 296 [1] [d]) that is rationally related to the ability to perform the job; the exceptions to maximum age limitations based upon veteran's status do not affect the validity of the age limitation as a bona fide occupational qualification.

 

1665 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Commissioner of Social Services v. Daniel S.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Kings County


126 Misc. 2d 32; 480 N.Y.S.2d 985; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3541



October 3, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Commissioner of Social Services of the City of New York filed an objection to a hearing examiner's report to the court that recommended that a support proceeding against respondent parent be dismissed because of the commissioner's failure to advance any knowledge as to the parent's income.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner brought an action against the parent to collect support paid by the department of social services to the parent's wife and three children. A hearing examiner recommended in his report to the court that the petition be dismissed because the commissioner's failed to show the parent's income. The commissioner filed an objection to the report, claiming that he was entitled to rely on the prima facie presumption contained in N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 437. The commissioner argued that the "prima facie" presumption allowed him to forego proof of the parent's income. The court dismissed the petition. The court held the parent's unsworn statements concerning his gross income, without an examination into his needs, were insufficient to meet the commissioner's burden of proof. Section 437 was a presumption of fact, which a trier of fact was authorized, but not required to draw from the evidence. It was not a conclusive presumption of law or a rebuttable presumption of law whose effect was to place the burden on the parent to come forward with evidence to rebut the presumption. The commissioner failed to prove whether the parent had enough income to support his wife and children.


OUTCOME: The court denied the commissioner's objection to the hearing examiner's report, confirmed the report, and dismissed the commissioner's petition to collect support from the parent that had been paid to his spouse and children.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Support -- Presumption of Sufficient Means

The presumption of sufficient means to support contained in section 437 of the Family Court Act does not relieve a petitioner in a support proceeding from demonstrating a respondent's ability to furnish such support for a spouse and/or children regardless of whether the gravamen of the support request is a "public charge" needs basis or a "means" basis; the sufficient means presumption is a presumption of fact which is not conclusive and may be rejected by the trier of the fact; moreover, in providing the presumption, section 437 does not distinguish between the bases for support. Accordingly, the hearing officer did not err as a matter of law in recommending dismissal of the petition in a support proceeding due to failure of proof as to respondent's income, where petitioner Commissioner of Social Services relied solely on the prima facie presumption of section 437 and presented no further proof of respondent's financial circumstances; petitioner's failure to demonstrate respondent's ability to pay support and mere reliance on the statutory presumption warrant dismissal of the petition.

 

1666 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Del Valle


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Queens County


126 Misc. 2d 78; 481 N.Y.S.2d 232; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3551



October 1, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant illegitimate children had been issued a citation to show cause why they should not be barred from any distributive share of the decedent's estate. After two of the children defaulted, plaintiff administratrix sought summary judgment dismissing the objections filed by the guardian ad litem on behalf of the minor child on the grounds that the child did not qualify as an out-of-wedlock child under N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 4-1.2.


OVERVIEW: In raising objections to the administratrix's accounting of the decedent's estate, the guardian contended that a child's legitimacy was determined by the law of his place of birth or domicile, and that under the law of Connecticut where the child was domiciled at the time of his decedent's death, the child would be considered legitimate. The court found that at the time of the decedent's death, the relevant statute in Connecticut was partially or wholly void by reason of that state's supreme court precedent. However, it also was the court's determination that the common-law classification of a child born out of wedlock as being a "filius nullius" was unconstitutional. Because no valid distinction could therefore be made in Connecticut between the legitimate and illegitimate children of a man at the time of the decedent's death, the court also declined to make such a distinction. The court thus concluded that because neither the amendment to § 4-1.2 nor Conn. Gen. Stat. § 45-274 was retroactive, the child was considered the legitimate child of the decedent. Accordingly, the administratrix was not entitled to summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court denied the administratrix's motion for summary judgment and directed the parties to complete what pretrial discovery was necessary regarding the other objections raised by the guardian ad litem to the account of the administratrix.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children Born out of Wedlock -- Inheritance Rights -- Children Domiciled in Another State

1. In an accounting proceeding wherein the inheritance rights of three out-of-wedlock children of a decedent are in issue, the law of the State where the children were domiciled at the time of decedent's death is controlling as to whether the children are entitled to inherit from decedent's estate.

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Inheritance Rights -- Applicable Law

2. Although the Connecticut statute in effect at the time of decedent's death would not allow an out-of-wedlock child to inherit from his father but would allow inheritance from the mother, a Supreme Court decision finding a similar statute unconstitutional as violative of the equal protection clause rendered the Connecticut statute either wholly or partially void; however the Connecticut statute will not be found to be wholly void where the result thereof would be the application of a common law which also would unconstitutionally deny inheritance rights to children born out of wedlock; consequently the Connecticut statute is found to have been void at the time of decedent's death only insofar as it denied inheritance [***2] rights to illegitimate children of a man and therefore the Connecticut-domiciled, illegitimate child of decedent is entitled to a distributive share of decedent's estate.

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Inheritance Rights -- Effect of Default to Citation

3. Puerto Rico-domiciled, illegitimate children of a decedent who are entitled under Puerto Rican law to inherit from decedent's estate may not be foreclosed from their distributive share by virtue of their default under a citation to show cause "why they should not be barred from any distributive share of the estate".

 

1667 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Commissioner of Labor v. Chudzik


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Cattaraugus County


126 Misc. 2d 968; 484 N.Y.S.2d 753; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3737



September 28, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The judgment creditor, New York Commissioner of Labor, filed an application against the judgment debtor and the garnishee to renew an application for an extension of a levy on the personal property of the judgment debtor.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner, as judgment creditor of the judgment debtor, sought to renew for the second time an application for an extension of a levy on the personal property of the judgment debtor. The court previously denied the commissioner's application for failure to provide adequate notice to the judgment debtor. The court denied the commissioner's application. The court held that the affidavit and papers submitted by the commissioner did not excuse the commissioner's failure to submit proof of service of notice of exempt property on the judgment debtor and that the commissioner was not prevented from bringing facts to the court's attention because of mistake, inadvertence, surprise, or excusable neglect. The court held that, even if notice of exempt property were given to the judgment debtor at the time of service of the original warrant, procedural due process required that additional notice be given for any motion or application for an extension of levy because the judgment debtor may have acquired property after the initial levy. The court reiterated that N.Y. Lab. Law § 573(2) was unconstitutional for failure to require service of notice of exempt property on a judgment debtor.


OUTCOME: The court denied the commissioner's application to renew an application for an extension of a levy on the personal property of the judgment debtor.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Unemployment Insurance -- Levy upon Personal Property for Nonpayment -- Due Process Requirement of Notice of Exempt Property

The motion by the Commissioner of Labor, through the office of the Attorney-General, to review a previously denied application for an extension of a tax compliance agent's levy upon personal property for nonpayment of unemployment insurance served upon a garnishee of assets of the judgment debtor pursuant to the Commissioner's statutory authority to issue and then enforce a warrant as a judgment (Labor Law § 573 [2]) must be denied (the Commissioner's initial ex parte application to renew the levy having been denied because of the lack of proof that adequate notice of exempt property had been given to the judgment debtor and also on constitutional grounds of inadequacy of statutory provisions relative to notice of exempt property when a tax compliance agent enforces the warrant [see, 13 Misc 2d 959]) since the Commissioner has failed to show any additional facts that would excuse the initial failure to submit proof of service of notice of exempt property on the judgment debtor; in addition, the application must be denied in any event since, even [***2] if notice of exempt property had been given to the judgment debtor or a garnishee at the time of the service of the warrant, due process requires that further notice of exempt property be given in connection with any application for an extension of levy; since the Attorney-General raised the issue of constitutionality upon intervening at the request of the Commissioner of Labor, there has been full compliance with Executive Law § 71 regarding notice to the Attorney-General on an issue of constitutionality of a statute; lastly, the court properly acted pursuant to its authority to protect a judgment debtor from any unlawful or unconstitutional "enforcement procedure" ( CPLR 5240).

 

1668 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Honeckman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


125 Misc. 2d 1000; 480 N.Y.S.2d 829; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3519



September 17, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion of defendant Seymour Vall is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff attorney general initiated a civil proceeding pursuant to the Martin Act against defendants, salesman and related individual, and obtained orders permanently enjoining defendants from participating in the purchase and sale of securities. The salesman filed a motion to dissolve the permanent injunction.


OVERVIEW: The salesman was convicted of the sale of securities without a license and securities fraud. The attorney general brought a civil action against the salesman and others and obtained an injunction pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 353, permanently barring the salesman from selling securities. The salesman filed a motion to dissolve the injunction and argued that the provisions of § 359-g(3) that prevented a court from modifying an injunction issued under § 353 were an unconstitutional limitation on the court's equity jurisdiction and that the certificate of relief from civil disabilities granted to him in his criminal conviction rendered the injunction ineffectual. The court found that § 359-g(3) was not unconstitutional because the legislature had the right to create new laws and to prescribe penalties for the violations of those laws. The court still retained the right to modify the injunction if the reason for its existence, the criminal conviction, disappeared. The court also found that the certificate of relief did not bar the attorney general from bringing a civil action it merely provided relief from automatic bans to employment imposed as the result of a conviction.


OUTCOME: The court denied the salesman's motion to dissolve the permanent injunction barring him from selling securities in an action by the attorney general against defendants for violation of the Martin Act.


HEADNOTES

Injunctions -- Permanent Injunction

A motion by a defendant convicted of failing to register as a salesman for a commodities brokerage firm (General Business Law, § 359-e) to modify or dissolve an order entered pursuant to article 23-A of the General Business Law (Martin Act) permanently enjoining him from engaging in any capacity in any business relating to the purchase and sale of securities in New York State is denied; the proscription of the jurisdiction of the court contained in section 359-g of the General Business Law, which provides that an application to dissolve or modify the injunction shall not be entertained if the applicant has been convicted of any crime involving securities, is valid and binding, and the statute does not constitute an attempt to limit the equitable power of the Supreme Court to modify its decrees since there has been no showing that defendant has been pardoned or that the conviction has been reversed by an appellate court. Further, the certificate of relief from civil disabilities issued to defendant pursuant to article 23 of the Correction Law does not render any automatic [***2] bar to the requested relief ineffectual, since the thrust of article 23 is the elimination of automatic bars to employment which are imposed solely as the result of a conviction without regard to whether the offense bears any relation to the character and fitness of the individual involved, and article 23 was not intended to supersede the provisions of the Martin Act with regard to permanent injunctions; defendant may seek employment in other fields, but his conviction for a securities violation permanently bars him from that line of endeavor.

Huggins v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


126 Misc. 2d 908; 484 N.Y.S.2d 748; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3725



September 11, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petitions are dismissed except for the "thirty-foot rule," as to which judgments shall be entered in favor of petitioners.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner vendors commenced a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding against respondents, city and others, alleging a failure to give proper notice of a hearing and an opportunity to comment on proposed amendments to a city ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The vendors were licensed to sell food on the streets of New York City. The vendors argued that a local law and its amendments were arbitrary and capricious in that they limited the vendors' access to various streets. The law prohibited food vending in designated areas during specified times. An amendment to the law imposed a 30-foot rule to further restrict access to streets. In ruling on the vendors' petition filed under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 alleging lack of proper notice of the hearing and an opportunity to comment on the proposed amendments, the court found that the city had concluded that the presence of food vendors could pose a health and safety hazard to an already congested street. Although reasonable people might disagree, there was ample support in the record for such a conclusion, and it was consistent with the municipality's mandate to promote and preserve the public welfare. However, as to the 30-foot rule, the city and others made no effort to justify the rule's application by the introduction of evidence concerning the traffic at the designated streets or in any other manner. Therefore, the decision adopting the 30-foot rule was not supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petitions filed by the vendors against the city and others challenging the enactment of an amendment to a local ordinance and held that the ordinance was a proper exercise of the city's police power to protect the public welfare, but a 30-foot rule was not supported by substantial evidence.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Street Vendors

A local law of the City of New York and the implementing regulation of the Department of Consumer Affairs which prohibit food vending in designated areas during specified times based upon a finding that the presence of licensed food vendors on a street already congested by pedestrian or vehicular traffic "would constitute a serious and immediate threat to the health, safety and well-being of the public" (Local Laws, 1983, No. 17 of City of New York), are not arbitrary and capricious since the conclusion of the City Council that the presence of food vendors may pose a health and safety hazard to an already congested street is consistent with the city's mandate to promote and preserve the public welfare and there is thus no need for the Commissioner of Consumer Affairs to demonstrate that the presence of food vendors in designated areas adds to existing congestion but only that the combination represents a threat to public health and safety; since the restrictions on the placement of vendors'  [***2] vehicles, pushcarts and stands are merely part of the city's continuing management of vending activity, the city was not required to prepare an environmental impact statement before promulgating the subject regulation (see, 6 NYCRR 617.13 [d] [15]); although the Commissioner's factual determinations with respect to the designated streets were properly based on substantial evidence, the rule proscribing vending within 30 feet of restricted streets is unsupported by substantial evidence and must be set aside.

Under 21 v. City of New York


Index No. 15046/84, Index No. 15056/84, Index No. 15185/84


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, NEW YORK COUNTY


1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3801



September 5, 1984, Decided

Affirmed in part and modified in part by Under 21 v. New York, 108 A.D.2d 250, 488 N.Y.S.2d 669, 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 48383 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, 1985)

 

1671 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rivera


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, New York County


125 Misc. 2d 516; 480 N.Y.S.2d 426; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3441



August 27, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from the trial court (New York), which convicted defendant of two counts of grand larceny and found that defendant was a second felony offender under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.21.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who spoke Spanish and who was hearing impaired, had two prior convictions in which he did not have the services of a qualified sign-language interpreter. He later was convicted on larceny charges and the trial court imposed second felony offender status under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.21 based upon the earlier convictions where defendant did not have an interpreter. He sought review. The court reversed the imposition of second felony offender status because defendant showed by substantial evidence that the earlier convictions were obtained in violation of his constitutional rights. A conviction obtained in violation of one's constitutional rights could not be used to enhance punishment for another offense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's imposition of second offender status and sentenced defendant as a first felony offender.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Fair Trial -- Conviction of Deaf Defendant Obtained in Absence of Qualified Sign-Language Interpreter

The prior felony conviction of a deaf or hearing-impaired defendant obtained in the absence of a qualified sign-language interpreter is unconstitutional for the purposes of adjudication as a second felony offender ( CPL 400.21, subd 7) since a hearing-impaired person could not have received a fair trial in the absence of a qualified sign-language interpreter (see Judiciary Law, § 390) and a conviction obtained in violation of the defendant's constitutional rights may not be considered as a predicate felony; accordingly, the failure of defendant, who suffers from a severe hearing impairment rendering him incapable of understanding normal speech, to appeal either of his two prior convictions because of inadequate interpretation cannot be deemed a waiver of the right to now raise the constitutional issue of the absence of an interpreter and he has met his burden of proving by substantial evidence that both of his prior convictions were obtained in violation of his constitutional rights and he may thus only be sentenced as a first felony offender.

 

1672 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Frances E. v. Peter E.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Nassau County


125 Misc. 2d 164; 479 N.Y.S.2d 319; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3385



August 9, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner paternal grandparents filed suit requesting visitation with the children of respondent parents. The matter was before the court on the parents' motion to dismiss the suit pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 72.


OVERVIEW: The paternal grandparents filed a petition for visitation with the parents' two children. The parents filed a motion to dismiss the petition on the grounds that N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 72 was inapplicable to an intact family and, in the alternative, that N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law was unconstitutional. The court denied the motion to dismiss and held that a hearing was necessary to determine whether visitation was in the best interests of the children. In reaching its decision, the court found that there was no evidence that the legislature intended to enact a derivative visitation statute excluding a petition for visitation with a grandchild residing with both married parents in an intact family. The court further found that N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 72 was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court denied the parent's motion to dismiss the paternal grandparents' petition for visitation.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Visitation Rights of Grandparents

 Section 72 of the Domestic Relations Law, which grants a grandparent the right to seek visitation with a grandchild "where circumstances show that conditions exist which equity would see fit to intervene", does not exclude an application for visitation with a grandchild who resides with both married parents in an "intact family"; the right of parents to raise their families as they see fit has no greater applicability to parents who are married and residing together in an "intact family", and the statute does not provide a grandparent with only a derivative right of visitation based upon the right of the deceased or noncustodial parent.

 

1673 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Clark v. Cuomo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


123 Misc. 2d 885; 478 N.Y.S.2d 802; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3100



August 1, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state chairman of a political party sought a preliminary injunction restraining defendants, governor and others, from implementing an executive order establishing a state program for voter registration.


OVERVIEW: The governor ordered state agencies, which have regular contact with the public, to conduct voter registration projects to assist citizens in registering to vote. The chairman argued that the program was unconstitutional and illegal and sought a preliminary injunction to enjoin implementation of the program. The court determined that the chairman was likely to prevail in the litigation. N.Y. Const. art. II. set forth the qualifications necessary to vote and invested the legislature with control of the means and methods of conducting voter registration and elections. The legislature had exclusive jurisdiction in the area of voter registration and was able to tailor its plans to meet local needs. In response to its duties, the legislature enacted a comprehensive plan to govern voting in the state. The governor failed to cite any law authorizing him to establish a voter registration program. The absence of such authority established a likelihood that the chairman would prevail in the action. The irreparable injury to the chairman and all citizens of the state was manifest if the program was implemented and it allowed the executive branch to exceed its authority.


OUTCOME: The court granted the preliminary injunction requested by the chairman.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Elections -- Registration -- Authority of Governor to Establish State Program for Voter Registration

Plaintiff, as chairman of the New York Republican State Committee, is entitled to a preliminary injunction restraining the Governor from implementing an executive order establishing a State program for voter registration whereby State agencies having regular contact with the public are directed to make mail registration forms available and to provide staff to assist the public in filling out the forms, since plaintiff has demonstrated the likelihood of showing that the Governor has no authority to establish a State program for voter registration inasmuch as the Legislature has exclusive jurisdiction under the State Constitution in the area of voter registration and participation in elections (NY Const, art II) as evidenced by the fact that the Legislature, during the 1983-1984 session, chose not to enact an almost identical plan directing each county board of elections to make registration applications available through distribution in Federal, State and local governmental buildings; irreparable injury if the plan is permitted to be implemented is manifest since all the citizens [***2] of the State suffer when one branch of government exceeds its authority at the expense of another and, in addition, defendants will suffer no harm if the status quo is maintained until the action is determined.

 

1674 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


31 West 21st Street Associates v. Evening of Unusual, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, New York County


125 Misc. 2d 661; 480 N.Y.S.2d 816; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3466



July 16, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord, owner of commercial premises used as a club for clientele interested in sadism and masochism, brought a summary proceeding under N.Y. Real Prop. Acts Laws § 711(5) against respondent tenant, seeking a judgment of eviction on the grounds that the tenant had maintained a house of assignation for lewd persons and had staged obscene performances that violated the criminal obscenity statutes.


OVERVIEW: The landlord of commercial premises used as a club for clientele interested in sadism and masochism brought a summary proceeding against the tenant, seeking a judgment of eviction because the lease prohibited the tenant from using the premises for any unlawful purpose. The landlord presented evidence that the tenant maintained a house of assignation for lewd persons in violation of N.Y. Real Prop. Acts Laws § 711(5) and staged obscene performances that violated the criminal obscenity statutes. The landlord also argued that the tenant permitted or promoted prostitution on the premises. The tenant asserted a constitutionally protected right of privacy as a defense, claiming that the club was a private accommodation. The trial court found that there was no evidence of prostitution, but the club was not a private accommodation and was not entitled to the constitutional protection sought. The trial court found that the landlord was entitled to a judgment of eviction because the evidence established that the tenant maintained a place of assignation for lewd persons and violated the criminal obscenity statutes. The trial court held that there was an independent basis for eviction.


OUTCOME: The court found that the landlord was entitled to a judgment of eviction based on the evidence that the tenant staged obscene performances and maintained a house of assignation for lewd persons. The court issued a warrant of eviction on a 20-day stay of execution, and awarded the landlord the costs and disbursements of the action.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and Tenant -- Summary Proceedings -- Use of Commercial Premises for Illegal Purposes

Petitioner, the owner of commercial premises, is entitled to a judgment of eviction against respondent tenant, which operated a "club" on the premises at which sadomasochistic and sexual conduct occurred, based upon the use of the premises as a "place of assignation for lewd persons" and for illegal business ( RPAPL 711, subd 5). Although prostitution, in its commonly understood meaning, has not been established to have occurred on the premises, in view of the facts that the activities taking place at the premises appeal predominantly to the prurient interest in sex (the conduct depicts sexual intercourse, sodomy, sadism and masochism in a lewd and patently offensive manner) and the club lacks any serious literary, artistic, political or scientific value, respondent has produced, presented or directed obscene performances within the meaning of section 235.05 of the Penal Law. Respondent is actually maintaining a commercial establishment open to the public and not a private membership club and there is thus no constitutional impediment based on respondent's right to privacy to granting [***2] a judgment of eviction in favor of petitioner.

 

1675 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Salmon


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Nassau County


125 Misc. 2d 221; 478 N.Y.S.2d 780; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3399



July 12, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Based upon all of the foregoing, the defendant's motion to dismiss the charges pending herein are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with violating N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 168-a, which prohibited ticket scalping, after he offered to sell the complainant a single ticket to a singer's performance. He filed a motion to dismiss the charges against him.


OVERVIEW: Defendant first argued that § 168-a was unconstitutionally broad and vague because the statute's heading, "Ticket speculators," had no interpretative value and thus left the statute's parameters inadequately defined. The court held that the heading was not only part of the statute, but clearly reflected the legislature's intent. Moreover, the meaning and purpose of the statute, to curb the practice of selling tickets at exorbitant prices, was clear from its legislative history. Next, the court rejected defendant's argument that law enforcement officials had enforced the statute in a discriminatory manner. Absent any consciously illegal discrimination, selectivity in enforcing penal statutes was not in itself unconstitutional. Here, the coliseum where defendant had attempted to sell the ticket was an area known for illegal ticket scalping, and the enforcement of the statute there was a proper and expected means of police deterrence. Finally, the complaint was not insufficient on its face because it alleged only one solicitation. The legislature did not intend to limit the meaning of "the business of selling" within the statute to habitual transactions.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute

 Section 168-a of the General Business Law, the purpose of which is to curb the practice of "hawking" tickets at exorbitant prices, is not unconstitutionally broad and vague; the restraint or prohibition of "hawking" has been historically regarded as a justifiable exercise of the police power, and defendant's allegation of selective enforcement of the statute at the location where he was arrested is not of an invidious discriminatory nature but is rather a proper and expected means of police deterrence. Further, an allegation of one solicitation constitutes the "business of selling" within the meaning of the statute; the purpose of the statute was clearly to include individual solicitations.

 

1676 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


New York State Inspection, etc., Dist. Council 82, etc. v. Cuomo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


124 Misc. 2d 876; 478 N.Y.S.2d 243; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3355



July 9, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, respondents' motion to dismiss the petition is denied and the respondents directed to serve their answer within 10 days after service upon them of a copy of this order with notice of entry.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, corrections employees, brought a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to enjoin respondent state officials from closing a correctional facility. The officials brought a motion to dismiss the petition pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7804(f), 3211(a)(2), (7), asserting nonjusticiability, failure to exhaust contractual remedies, failure of the petition to state a cause of action, and a lack of standing on the part of the employees.


OVERVIEW: The employees' petition alleged that the projected closing of the facility would inflict additional overcrowding on the remaining prisons and the concomitant creation of an unreasonably dangerous working environment in violation of N.Y. Lab. Law § 27-a. The court denied the officials' motion to dismiss. The court found that N.Y. Lab. Law § 27-a(6) and (7) permitted an article 78 action by public employees to enjoin conditions of public employment reasonably expected to cause death or injury. The court found that the matter's complexity did not make it nonjusticiable, and the court was competent to determine whether the officials had satisfied their statutory duty, and if not, to direct them to do so. The court found that the employees had standing under N.Y. Lab. Law § 27-a(7)(c), and that the requirement to comply with labor laws was not an "encroachment" into the officials' executive decision-making. The court noted that the petition challenged the actions as unconstitutional and beyond their statutory authority, thus exhaustion of contractual remedies was unnecessary. Assuming the employees' allegations to be true, the court found the petition stated a cause of action.


OUTCOME: The court denied the officials' motion to dismiss the petition by the employees seeking to enjoin the officials from closing a correctional facility.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding against Body or Officer -- When Remedy Available -- Challenge to Proposed Closing of Correctional Facility

In a proceeding brought on behalf of employees of the Department of Correctional Services to enjoin respondents from closing a State correctional facility, respondents' motion to dismiss is denied. Petitioners have standing to seek relief under section 27-a of the Labor Law, which requires employers to furnish a place of employment which is free from recognized hazards causing or likely to cause death or serious physical harm and providing reasonable and adequate protection to employees' lives, safety or health, and to comply with safety and health standards, and permits an action to be brought seeking injunctive relief to eliminate any conditions or practices in a place of public employment which could reasonably be expected to cause death or serious injury; claims do not present a nonjusticiable controversy merely because the activity contemplated on [***2] the State's part may be complex and rife with the exercise of discretion, and if a statutory directive is mandatory it is within the courts' competence to ascertain whether an agency has satisfied the duty imposed upon it; and petitioners need not exhaust their administrative remedies since they challenge respondents' action as being unconstitutional and beyond the Governor's grant of power. Petitioners state seven causes of action and the motion to dismiss upon the ground that the petition fails to state a cause of action, which is addressed to the petition as a whole, must be denied in its entirety since it cannot be said as a matter of law that petitioners fail to state a cause of action under section 27-a.

 

1677 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Harkness v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


125 Misc. 2d 216; 478 N.Y.S.2d 239; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3398



July 6, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two consolidated cases, defendant, a minor who admittedly committed several neighborhood burglaries, filed motions to set aside that portion of the verdicts against the minor for punitive damages. Plaintiff homeowners recovered compensatory and punitive damages and the question presented was whether punitive damages could be awarded against a minor.


OVERVIEW: The minor committed several burglaries in one neighborhood, where he worked as a paperboy, using the knowledge he gained to know when certain people would be gone so that he could burglarize their houses. Two victim homeowners brought civil actions against the minor and the jury awarded compensatory and punitive damages. The minor moved to set aside the award of punitive damages. The court held that minors could be treated as adults in civil actions, and punitive damages assessed against them, if there was evidence that the minor acted with malice or with deliberate knowledge of the rights of the plaintiffs and with the intent to interfere with them, or if the minor was guilty of other morally culpable conduct. The court held that the evidence presented showed a well thought out and deliberate plan, and that the jury considered defendant's age and his cooperation with police. The awards of punitive damages were upheld.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motions to set aside the portion of the jury verdict awarding punitive damages.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- Award of Punitive Damages against Infant -- Liability of 14-Year-Old Burglar to Victims

Defendant, who, as a 14-year-old paperboy, burglarized homes on his route at times when he knew the residents would be away for vacation but who was never prosecuted in return for his confession and his cooperation in prosecuting the "fence" to whom he sold the stolen property, was properly held liable to the burglarized homeowners by the jury for punitive as well as compensatory damages since defendant's infancy at the time of the burglaries does not make his conduct any the less reprehensible or morally culpable and subjecting him to recovery for punitive damages violates no public policy simply because he could not have been criminally responsible; the fact that 13, 14 and 15 year olds were made responsible as adults for certain criminal conduct is a clear legislative expression that under certain conditions infancy would no longer be a bar to adult responsibility.

 

1678 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ortiz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Bronx County


125 Misc. 2d 318; 479 N.Y.S.2d 613; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3412



July 5, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the defendant's motion to dismiss on constitutional grounds is denied in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was arrested and charged with illegal possession of a knife, in violation of § 436-5.2 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the charge on the ground that § 436-5.2 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that § 436-5.2 was unconstitutional on its face, as applied to him, vague, overbroad, and an unreasonable exercise of the police power by the municipality that enacted it. The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the illegal knife charge against him and held that § 436-5.2 was not void for vagueness as defendant was unable to show that the ordinance reached a substantial number of impermissible applications because it was reasonable in its scope, limited by an extensive list of commonsense exceptions, and based on a valid presumption that it was logically grounded in the realities of modern urban life. The court also held that: 1) the legislative presumption that persons carrying knives of the prohibited type posed a threat to public safety was rational and based on common experience; 2) the city council's enactment of § 436-5.2 was within its police power; 3) the city council was justified in dispensing with the scienter requirement due to its interest in public safety; and 4) the ordinance was not preempted by or inconsistent with N.Y. Penal Law art. 265.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the illegal knife charge against him.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Possession of Weapon -- Local "Knife Law" -- Illegal Possession of Knife with Four-Inch Blade

The New York City "knife law", which proscribes the possession or carrying of knives with four-inch or greater blade lengths "in public places, streets and parks of the city * * * without a lawful purpose" (Administrative Code of City of New York, § 436-5.2) promotes a legitimate governmental objective and is not unconstitutionally vague or an improper exercise of the city's police power since the local law is reasonable in its scope, limited by an extensive list of commonsense exceptions, and based on the valid presumption, logically grounded in the realities of modern urban life, that four-inch knives, when carried on the streets, are being carried for criminal purposes or for other reasons that are hazardous to the public safety; the legislative presumption of criminal intent based upon possession alone is not invalid since the rational connection between the prohibited act and the public safety justifies the lack of a scienter requirement; the "knife law" does [***2] not encourage arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement nor does it provide the police with unfettered discretion since the law provides adequate notice to the public and clear guidelines for adjudication; the "knife law" is not preempted by State law in the field of weapons regulation (Penal Law, art 265) since the "knife law" does not prohibit what is specifically allowed under State law or allow what the State law specifically forbids but merely supplements the Penal Law with additional reasonable requirements.

 

1679 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Saez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Kings County


125 Misc. 2d 125; 478 N.Y.S.2d 1002; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3379



July 3, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore, the People's motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State has applied pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.40 for an order setting aside as invalid defendant's sentence for his guilty plea of attempted sale of a controlled substance.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to the attempted sale of a controlled substance was sentenced to six months in jail and five years' probation. After the sentencing, the State discovered that defendant had been previously convicted of a predicate felony in another state. The State applied for an order to set aside defendant's sentence on the ground that it was invalid as a matter of law. The State argued that because defendant had not been sentenced under the second felony offender sentencing provisions of N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06, which was mandatory, the sentence imposed was invalid. The court found that it could only have set defendant's sentence aside if it were invalid as provided by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.40, that the State failed to meet its burden under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.21 by failing to discover defendant's prior felony, and that the State was not entitled to a second chance to sentence defendant as a second felony offender after defendant had been lawfully sentenced.


OUTCOME: The court denied the State's application.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence -- Second Felony Offender

The court may not set aside a sentence imposed upon defendant upon his plea of guilty on the ground that it was invalid as a matter of law ( CPL 440.40, subd 1) and then resentence him as a predicate felon where the prosecutor, due to a lack of knowledge of a previous felony conviction, failed to file a predicate felony information statement before the original sentencing ( CPL 400.21) since insufficient knowledge on the part of the prosecutor during plea bargaining does not render the sentence imposed on defendant invalid as a matter of law; the burden is on the People to establish the existence of a prior conviction and if the prosecutor does not meet the statutory burden and the sentence is not illegal, the nonsecond felony offender sentence must stand otherwise the policy of upholding the finality of criminal proceedings would be frustrated.

 

1680 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Torres


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


125 Misc. 2d 78; 478 N.Y.S.2d 771; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3373



June 18, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the charges filed against him pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192. Defendant based his motion to dismiss on the alleged unconstitutionality of the roadblock and further moved to suppress a field sobriety test and breathalyzer test results as well as related testimony.


OVERVIEW: After being stopped at a roadblock and subsequently arrested, defendant moved to dismiss the charge filed under § 1192 for operating a vehicle with blood alcohol content of .10 percent or more and operating a vehicle while under the influence of alcohol. Defendant alleged unconstitutionality of a roadblock and also moved to suppress breathalyzer test results as well as expected testimony relating to coordination tests administered to him as being obtained in violation of his rights under N.Y. Const. art I, § 12 and U.S. Const. amend IV. The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the charges because the initial stop of defendant was constitutional. However, the court granted defendant's motion to dismiss his filed sobriety test because defendant was not warned that that anything he did or said may have formulated the basis for his arrest. The court held that a police officer had to inform defendant that he could legitimately refuse to submit to the test, and that his refusal would not be admissible against him in a court of law. The court found that sufficient warnings were given to justify the denial of the suppression of defendant's breathalyzer test.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss charges filed against him for operating a vehicle with blood alcohol content of .10 percent or more and operating a vehicle while under the influence of alcohol, but granted defendant's motion to suppress the results of a field sobriety test. The results of a breathalyzer test performed at the precinct were not suppressed.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Validity of "DWI" Police Roadblocks

1. Police officers may properly stop one or more lanes of motor vehicle traffic temporarily at a driving while intoxicated (DWI) safety check roadblock conducted in a nonarbitrary, nondiscriminatory manner; the danger to be prevented and the lifesaving aspects to the drivers and other citizenry outweigh the inconvenience and render the intrusion reasonable in such a properly conducted DWI roadblock. Accordingly, the stop of defendant, who had been driving in a normal manner, at a roadblock set up for the purpose of apprehending intoxicated or impaired drivers in which all of the cars of one or two lanes of traffic were stopped and the drivers were briefly questioned was constitutional, and the motion by defendant to dismiss the information charging him with violating subdivisions 2 and 3 of section 1192 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law on the ground that the initial stop was in violation of his constitutional rights is denied.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Field Sobriety Test

2. The results of a field sobriety test must be suppressed where the test was administered to defendant without any warnings [***2] as to his rights when, in the course of the lawful, initial stop of defendant during a roadblock set up to apprehend intoxicated or impaired drivers, a police officer noticed that defendant's eyes were watery, his speech was slurred and he had the odor of alcohol on his breath; inasmuch as a defendant is in custody at that point and his taking of the test is the result of an "interrogation", the defendant must be warned that anything he says or does may formulate the basis for his arrest or be used against him in a court of law and the officers must also inform the driver/defendant that he may legitimately refuse to submit to the test and that his refusal will not be admissible against him in a court of law. Further, where consent is obtained, the People have a heavy burden to prove that it was a voluntary and knowing consent. However, the results of dexterity tests performed at the precinct after a breathalyzer test was administered to defendant need not be suppressed, since any defect arising out of errors during the field sobriety test is attenuated, sufficient time having passed and sufficient warnings having been given to justify the belief that defendant had been returned to [***3] the status of one who was acting voluntarily.

Crimes -- Evidence -- Preservation of Breathalyzer Ampoules

3. The failure of the police department to preserve breathalyzer ampoules for retesting by a defendant charged with driving while intoxicated or impaired goes to the weight of the test evidence offered rather than to its admissibility and provides no constitutional impediment to prosecution; the People are not required either to preserve the original ampoule or breath sample or to produce and preserve a second ampoule or breath sample for the defense. Accordingly, defendant's motion to suppress the results of his breathalyzer test in a prosecution on charges of violating subdivisions 2 and 3 of section 1192 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law on the ground that the evidence was not preserved for independent testing is denied.

 

1681 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Benson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


125 Misc. 2d 843; 480 N.Y.S.2d 811; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3491



June 18, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is denied in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for murder in the second degree, for felony murder, for two counts of robbery in the first degree, and for robbery in the second degree. Defendant filed as part of an omnibus motion a motion to dismiss the indictment on the grounds that N.Y. Penal Code § 125.25(3), the felony murder statute, was unconstitutional. The people opposed dismissal.


OVERVIEW: Regarding his felony murder indictment, defendant claimed that the affirmative defense that was established in N.Y. Penal Code §§ 125.25(3)(a)-(d) violated due process of law and equal protection because the statute did not contain as an element the intent to kill. Finding nothing unconstitutional in the felony murder statute, the court denied defendant's motion in all respects. The court held that the higher courts of the state clearly held that the elements of intent, or malice, or malice aforethought were not elements of the statute, thus it did not have the liberty to interpret the statute as requiring intent. The court also held that there were no equal protection violation by the specification of certain felonies as underlying felonies for felony murder because there was a rational basis for distinguishing between the enumerated felonies and the nonenumerated felonies.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's omnibus motion to dismiss the indictment for felony murder, robbery in the first degree, and robbery in the second degree.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Murder -- Felony Murder

Subdivision 3 of section 125.25 of the Penal Law, the felony murder statute, is constitutional; the affirmative defense contained in subdivision 3 does not violate a defendant's right to due process, and the lack of the element of intent to kill is not violative of a defendant's due process or equal protection rights, since nothing in the United States Constitution deprives Legislatures of the power to impose upon those who kill their victims in the course of inherently dangerous felonies the same sanctions they choose for those who kill their victims after meditation. Further, notwithstanding that the law "presumes" intent from the commission of the underlying felony, the statute is constitutional; since intent is not an element of the crime in reality there is no presumption of intent, and the presumption is not a true one but rather a rule of law. There is no equal protection violation because the statute specifies only certain felonies as underlying felonies for felony murder; there is a rational basis for distinguishing between the enumerated and the nonenumerated felonies.

People v. Burger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


125 Misc. 2d 709; 479 N.Y.S.2d 936; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3474



June 11, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore, defendant's motion to suppress is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was arrested when stolen property was found in his junkyard. Before trial, defendant filed a motion to suppress physical evidence. The motion was denied. Alleging an intervening decision required a contrary result, defendant requested a renewal and reargument of the previously denied suppression motion and asked for proper and equitable relief. Reargument was granted.


OVERVIEW: Defendant operated an automobile junkyard business. Upon a warrantless inspection of defendant's junkyard, police officers found defendant did not have a police book, which all auto junkyards were required to keep. Continuing their inspection of the yard, the officers radioed in the vehicle identification number of a vehicle in the yard and received information the vehicle was stolen. Subsequently, the officers arrested defendant and seized the stolen property. On reargument of his suppression motion, defendant alleged the officers were required to seek a search warrant when he failed to produce the police book. In response, the prosecution asserted both N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 415-a(5) and N.Y. City Charter § 436 permitted the inspection. The court denied defendant's motion to suppress because the officers' entrance onto defendant's yard and inspection of the items in the yard were authorized by § 436 as an administrative inspection. In addition, the court found defendant's arrest, and seizure of the stolen property, were proper.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to suppress physical evidence seized from his junkyard.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Administrative Inspection of Auto Junkyard

New York City police officers assigned to the division which handles inspections of automobile junkyards properly entered the premises of defendant's auto junkyard business without a warrant to inspect the "articles of merchandise" found therein as authorized by section 436 of the New York City Charter and thereafter lawfully arrested defendant when a check of a vehicle identification number of a vehicle in defendant's yard revealed that the vehicle was stolen since the officers did not undertake the inspection of the yard to gather evidence of a crime for which a search warrant would be required but, rather, they were clearly conducting an administrative inspection in view of their special training in this area and the lack of any reason to suspect criminal activity and a warrantless administrative inspection of such a pervasively regulated industry as the auto junkyard industry is constitutionally permissible; the lack of a "police book" which all auto junkyards are required to keep ( Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 415-a, subd 5, par [a])  [***2] did not require the officers to obtain a search warrant before continuing with their inspection since the "police book" is irrelevant for purposes of the inspection authorized by the City Charter nor does the fact that the officers have no set procedure for the inspection of auto junkyards diminish their power to make such inspections; New York does not require police officers to obtain an administrative warrant showing that an inspection is in pursuit of an administrative plan before conducting an administrative inspection of an auto junkyard.

 

1683 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Leto


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


124 Misc. 2d 549; 478 N.Y.S.2d 765; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3238



June 1, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The Mapp motion made by defendant is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state filed an indictment in the court (New York) against defendant accusing him of criminal possession of stolen property in the first degree, forgery of a vehicle identification number, and violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 415-a. Defendant argued that he was subject to an unlawful search and seizure and filed a motion to suppress various automobile parts. The state asserted that the seizure was incidental to a lawful arrest.


OVERVIEW: The court conducted a pretrial suppression hearing and held that in accordance with § 415-a the police, in order to make a routine administrative inspection, entered defendant's premises, which was engaged in business as a vehicle dismantler. The court found that the police entered without a warrant. The court held that when a person entered the business of vehicle dismantling, he entered a general area that had long been regulated. The court held that in conducting the specific business of dismantling motor vehicles, a person accepted the burdens, as well as the benefits of his trade, and consented to the restrictions placed upon him. The court held that the inspections authorized by § 415-a posed only a limited threat to a vehicle dismantler's justifiable expectation of privacy. The court held that the police were making a routine administrative inspection which did not run afoul of constitutional prohibitions against unreasonable searches and seizures. The court held that defendant had no objection to the inspection, showed his licenses, and cooperated with the police in their inspection. Thus, the police had probable cause to arrest defendant upon finding stolen property.


OUTCOME: The court found by clear and convincing evidence that defendant consented to the inspection, that the police had probable cause to arrest defendant, and that the police had the right to seize the stolen property contemporaneously with the arrest without a search warrant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Warrantless Administrative Searches

The routine administrative inspection by the police of defendant's premises, which housed a vehicle dismantling business, does not run afoul of the constitutional prohibitions against unreasonable searches and seizures since defendant, by entering into the business of vehicle dismantling, has entered into a general area -- motor vehicles -- which has long been regulated, and he has thereby effectively consented to the restrictions placed upon him by subdivision 5 of section 415-a of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, which authorizes the type of administrative search conducted herein and mandates the keeping of certain records; in conducting the business of dismantling motor vehicles, a person accepts the burdens, as well as the benefits of his trade, and the afore-mentioned statutory inspections pose only a limited threat to his justifiable expectations of privacy. Consequently, where the police seized items pursuant to a legitimate administrative search which was consented to by defendant, who was no neophyte, having been previously convicted of a [***2] felony, those items need not be suppressed because their seizure was unaccompanied by a warrant; rather, those items, which had been reported stolen, gave the police both probable cause to arrest defendant and the right to seize property contemporaneously with the arrest without a search warrant.

 

1684 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Beaumont Co. v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


125 Misc. 2d 87; 477 N.Y.S.2d 272; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3374



May 31, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporate borrowers filed an action against defendants, the State of New York, the City of New York and certain city and state departments (city), challenging the constitutionality of the retroactive application of N.Y. Tax Law § 253a and New York, N.Y., Administrative Code § W46-1.0 et seq. as amended by 1982 N.Y. Laws 57. Both sides moved for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The effect of the amendment of 1982 N.Y. Laws 57 on N.Y. Tax Law § 253a and New York, N.Y., Administrative Code § W46-1.0 et seq. was to increase the rate of taxation on the recording of certain mortgages recorded on or after February 1, 1982. The amendment was enacted on April 12, 1982. The corporate borrowers had paid the recording tax at the old rate and asserted that they were being deprived of due process of law by being required to pay the tax at the retroactive increased rate. The court granted the city's motion for summary judgment, holding that the corporate borrowers had no vested right in the rate of taxation. The court further found that the retroactivity of the tax at issue was not harsh or oppressive. The 10-week period of retroactivity was relatively short, the legislation was introduced prior to the date of its effectiveness, and the public purpose advanced for the tax was creditable.
