OUTCOME: The court granted the city's motion for summary judgment. The corporate borrowers' motions for summary judgment, class certification, and intervention were denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- Mortgage Recording Tax -- Retroactivity

The retroactive application of a statute which more than doubled the rate of the mortgage recording tax in New York City (see Tax Law, § 253-a; Administrative Code of the City of New York, § W46-1.0 et seq.) is not unconstitutional since there is generally no constitutional objection to retroactive application of a tax statute, and no taxpayer has a vested right in the rate of taxation; so long as the proposed retroactive application is not unreasonably long, serves a public purpose and does not unreasonably disappoint the taxpayer's justified reliance on prior law, such application will not violate due process. The retroactive application of the instant tax is not harsh or oppressive since the 10-week period of retroactivity is relatively short, the legislation was introduced prior to the date of its eventual effectiveness, and the public purpose advanced, to aid mass transit and to recoup revenue lost by the repeal of the city's gains tax on real property transfers, is creditable. In addition, there is nothing in the statute which nullifies provisions in any contractual agreements since the tax was in effect at the [***2] time the parties dealt with each other; a mere increase in rate did not impair contractual rights.

 

1685 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rotunno v. Rochester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


124 Misc. 2d 448; 477 N.Y.S.2d 941; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3221



May 24, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff police officers brought a complaint and sought declaratory judgment against defendant city that § 128 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law and § 100.1 of the New York State Liquor Authority Regulations, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 100.1, be declared unconstitutional and void. They also sought a permanent injunction to prohibit the city from enforcing the regulation and the statute.


OVERVIEW: The police officers brought their complaint and request for declaratory and injunctive relief after the police chief issued a directive prohibiting off-duty officers from working at any establishment licensed to sell alcohol for on-premises consumption. The court granted a preliminary injunction in favor of the police officers. The court held that nothing in § 128 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law prohibited the employment of off-duty police officers merely as guards or security personnel at local establishments licensed to sell alcohol. The court found that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 100.1 went beyond the legislative mandate of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law and appeared to be arbitrary and in excess of statutory authority. Thus, the action taken by the city was based on an invalid agency regulation and had to be enjoined. The court concluded that the likelihood of the success of the police officers on the merits of their case was high probability and the irreparable injury that could have been done to the public by enforcement of the regulation was clear.


OUTCOME: The court granted a preliminary injunction in favor of the police officers in their action for declaratory and injunctive relief.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Intoxicating Liquors -- Employment of Off-Duty Police Officers in Licensed Premises

 Section 128 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, which prohibits police officers from being either "directly or indirectly interested in the manufacture or sale of alcoholic beverages or to offer for sale, or recommend to any licensee any alcoholic beverages", does not prohibit the employment of off-duty police officers as guards or security personnel at the many establishments licensed to sell alcohol, since the statute's intent was to prevent police officers from owning bars or establishments that serve liquor, or working in those places as one who actually dispenses liquor; accordingly, a regulation of the State Liquor Authority (9 NYCRR 100.1), which created a blanket prohibition preventing the employment of off-duty officers in any capacity at any place licensed to sell alcoholic beverages other than beer, or licensed to sell beer for on-premises consumption, goes beyond the legislative mandate of the statute, and appears to be arbitrary and in excess of statutory authority. Therefore, a municipal police department's rule restricting the off-duty employment of its officers in the manner [***2] suggested by the regulation in question must be preliminarily enjoined since the likelihood of the officers' success on the merits is of high probability and the irreparable injury that may be done to the public by enforcement of the rule is clear inasmuch as the employment of the off-duty officers contributes to public safety.

 

1686 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Commissioner of Social Services v. Leford O.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Kings County


124 Misc. 2d 232; 477 N.Y.S.2d 934; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3184



May 16, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, in light of the aforesaid, the court grants respondent's cross petition to the extent of suspending the order of support for the period from October 2, 1977 through April 21, 1978 and denies petitioner's supplemental petition dated August 11, 1983 alleging that respondent is in violation of the support order and seeking to impose arrears.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Commissioner of Social Services (social services), as assignee of the wife, filed suit against respondent husband, claiming that the husband violated a support order.


OVERVIEW: After the husband was injured in a work-related accident, he received a 40 percent cut in salary for a time and received no pay for several months. During that time, the husband failed to make the court ordered support payments. Social services filed suit and sought to impose arrears. The husband filed a cross-petition, seeking a retroactive suspension of the order period for the time period that he was injured and claiming that he had good cause for failing to make the payments. The court ruled in favor of the husband. The court held that the husband presented a complete defense on the merits and a justifiable excuse under the surrounding circumstances for failure to come to court at that time to seek suspension of the support order. The husband, the court determined, was never advised and did not know that it was necessary for him to come to court to seek suspension of the support order based upon his inability to pay support in order to be excused from liability for such payments. Further, stated the court, the husband justifiably relied on the fact that the support payments were made by payroll deduction.


OUTCOME: The court ruled in favor of the husband and retroactively suspended the order of support for the period that the husband was disabled. The court also denied social services' petition that sought to impose arrears.


HEADNOTES

Husband and Wife -- Support -- Cancellation of Arrears

 Section 455 of the Family Court Act provides that where arrears in support payments have accrued a respondent may assert as a defense to commitment to prison his present inability to comply with the order, and that with respect to past inability to pay such arrears, the respondent may not raise a defense predicated on this issue unless he shows good cause for failure to make application for relief from the support order prior to the accrual of the arrears, and section 460 of the Family Court Act mandates entry of judgment of the amount of such accrued arrears unless the defaulting party shows good cause for failure to make application for relief prior to the accrual of such arrears; the statutes place both the willful party who has the ability to pay and the nonwillful [***2] party who lacks the ability to pay in the same category of defaulting party and due process requires the excusing of arrears where the respondent's failure to come to court at the onset of non-self-imposed inability to pay support emanates from the need to supply the necessities of life by finding gainful employment or genuine lack of knowledge of the requirement to seek relief from the court at that time. Accordingly, the Commissioner of Social Services may not impose arrears on a respondent who failed to pay support for a brief period some six years earlier due to loss of pay occasioned by an accident; despite the repeal of section 458 of the Family Court Act ("Cancellation of arrears") the court has inherent residual power to effect de facto cancellation of arrears in appropriate circumstances under the good cause mandate set forth in sections 455 and 460, and its continuing jurisdiction over its own support orders under section 451 of the Family Court Act.

 

1687 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Laguna


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


124 Misc. 2d 182; 475 N.Y.S.2d 783; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3172



May 9, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motions to dismiss the information are granted. All other branches of the defendant Angel Sanchez's motion are denied as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State charged defendants with criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 265.01. Defendants moved to dismiss the informations on the ground that they were defective.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree pursuant to § 265.01. Defendants moved to dismiss the information on the ground that it was defective. The informations alleged that defendants were wearing metal knuckles. Defendants were in possession of an item commonly referred to as "spikes." The question presented to the court was whether the items constituted "metal knuckles" for purposes of § 265.01(1). The court held that spikes were not metal knuckles within the meaning of the statute. The court was unable to find a definition of metal knuckles in the statute. Similarly, the legislative history was silent on the matter. Metal knuckles had never been defined by the legislature. The court, therefore, looked to the common dictionary definitions of metal knuckles. These definitions referred to "metal finger rings" or a "metal device." Accepting these definitions, the court concluded that leather straps with spikes protruding from them were not included in these definitions. Accordingly, the motions to dismiss the information were granted.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' motions to dismiss the informations, which charged them with possession of a weapon in the fourth degree.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Possession of Weapon -- "Metal Knuckles"

Leather straps with metal studs protruding from them which are worn about the wrist and cover the back of the hand are not "metal knuckles", the possession of which is a crime pursuant to section 265.01 of the Penal Law, since those devices do not come within the common dictionary definition of "metal knuckles", and if the Legislature had intended to include the devices in question, which were seized from defendants, in the list of specifically enumerated items whose possession is made illegal by the statute, it would have specifically mentioned them or defined the term "metal knuckles" to encompass them. Inasmuch as the information charging defendants with criminal possession of a weapon is defective, the court need not address the constitutional issue of vagueness which courts of original jurisdiction should pass upon only as a matter of last resort.

 

1688 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Schmidt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


124 Misc. 2d 102; 478 N.Y.S.2d 482; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3159



April 30, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with violating N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Laws § 1192(2) in relation to his arrest for drunk driving. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss and asserted that the § 1192(2) was unconstitutional because it defined criminal behavior solely in terms of a mathematical measure of blood alcohol content and that it failed to provide adequate notice of its prohibition to prospective violators.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with drunk driving under § 1192(2) and filed a motion to dismiss. The court denied defendant's motion and found that § 1192(2) was not void for preciseness. The court held the statute's absolute liability provision patently warned the drinking driver that his indulging must have stopped before his blood alcohol level exceeded the legal limit. The court ruled that the fact that § 1192(2) did not specify the precise number of drinks permissible or that a drinking driver might not have been able to discern whether his blood alcohol level was above the legal limit did not invalidate the provision on vagueness grounds. The court held that fair notice was provided by § 1192(2) and that a person who drank any alcohol was accorded an unambiguous warning that in order to avoid potential criminal behavior, he should have refrained from driving within a relatively short span of time. The court found no reason why a person of ordinary intelligence would have had any difficulty in understanding that if he had drunk anything containing alcohol and particularly any substantial amount thereof, he should not have attempted to take control of a motor vehicle.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss charges against him for drunken driving.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motor Vehicles -- Operating Vehicle while Under Influence -- Blood Alcohol Level

Subdivision 2 of section 1192 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, which sets a per se liability standard of .10% or more of alcohol in a driver's blood, reflects a legislative determination that when a defendant drives with that amount of alcohol in his blood, the question of guilt need not be defined by his subjective behavior and condition, and is not unconstitutionally vague for lack of notice of what is prohibited. Since the .10 absolute liability provision patently warns the drinking driver that his indulging must stop before his blood alcohol content (BAC) exceeds the legally acceptable limit, the fact that the statute does not specify the precise number of drinks permissible, or that a drinking driver might not be able to discern whether his BAC is .09, .10, or .11, does not invalidate the provision on vagueness grounds. The .10 BAC limit is a standard that an ordinary person can understand, and one need not be a mathematician or quantitative analyst in order to conform his behavior to the statutory limit.

 

1689 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Horney


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


124 Misc. 2d 22; 476 N.Y.S.2d 967; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3141



April 19, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the defendant's motion to set aside the verdict is hereby granted, and a new trial ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a New York City transit police officer, was convicted, after a jury trial, of assault in the second degree. Defendant filed a motion to set aside the verdict, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.30(1), (2) on the grounds that numerous incidents of jury misconduct and misconduct of others in relation to the jury deprived him of his constitutional and statutory right to a fair trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for assault in the second degree for using excessive force in effecting an arrest. The jury deliberated for four days before returning a guilty verdict. Defendant filed a motion to set aside the verdict. The court granted the motion and held that the verdict was unconstitutionally infirm. The court rejected claims that jurors were physically and mentally incompetent to deliberate. The appropriate time for such issues was when the verdict was announced and the jurors polled. The court found, however, that there were numerous pre-deliberation comments; jurors deliberated, while sequestered, without the presence of all 12 jurors; jurors viewed a headline of a newspaper concerning police brutality; and jurors received coercive communications from the bailiff that they would stay through the weekend if they could not reach a verdict by Friday evening. The verdict was returned at 5:00 p.m. on Friday. The court concluded that the record was replete with repeated instances of juror and other misconduct that affected a substantial right of defendant to have a fair trial. He clearly met his burden of proving by a preponderance of the evidence that he was prejudiced.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to set aside the verdict of guilty for assault in the second degree for using excessive force in effecting an arrest. The court ordered a new trial.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Verdict -- Impeachment of Verdict -- Exception

1. In New York, it has long been the general rule that jurors may not impeach their verdicts by affidavit or testimony, thereby fostering the public policies of finality of verdicts, open discussion among jurors in their deliberation, and protection from posttrial harassment; the rule against impeachment of verdicts encompasses the ways the individual juror was influenced or the effect on mental processes, and therefore, public policy reasons for not invading the jury deliberations must ordinarily override possible injustice to a defendant. However, an exception to the rule has been recognized in cases involving the introduction of extraneous material before the jury, and CPL 330.30 permits vacatur of a verdict where there has been improper conduct which may have affected a substantial right of the defendant; in each case the facts and circumstances must be examined to determine whether defendant was prejudiced, and defendant has the burden of proving by a preponderance of the evidence every fact essential to [***2] support a motion to vacate ( CPL 330.40, subd 2, par [g]).

Jury -- Disqualification of Juror -- Physical and Mental Incompetence

2. Defendant's posttrial allegation that two jurors were physically and mentally incompetent to deliberate as jurors must be rejected where those allegations were not supported by the record, and any inquiry would be beyond the permissible scope of the exception to the rule of nonimpeachment of jury verdicts; that a juror has a change of heart, or asserts that he was coerced into the verdict, or that he even believes in the defendant's innocence, shall not affect a verdict and must be ignored, since the appropriate time for the jurors to have raised these issues was when the verdict was announced in open court and the jurors polled. Moreover, defendant's contention that three jurors did not answer questions on voir dire truthfully, thereby depriving him of his right to make challenges, also must be rejected since there is no evidence in the record to support such a charge.

Crimes -- Setting Verdict Aside -- Juror and Other Misconduct

3. Defendant has met his burden of proving by a preponderance of the evidence that he has been [***3] prejudiced where, at a posttrial hearing, there was evidence that several jurors engaged in premature discussions of the case; that discussions of the case among four jurors took place outside the presence of the full panel after it had been charged; that some jurors viewed a newspaper headline dealing with the general subject of the case, and that there was an ex parte communication between a court officer and at least three jurors regarding the length of their sequestration. Although some of the irregularities by themselves would not warrant the relief sought, cumulatively, the damage to defendant's entitlement to a fair trial was overwhelming.

 

1690 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Stahl Associates Co. v. Darland


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, New York County


123 Misc. 2d 848; 474 N.Y.S.2d 948; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3092



April 17, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord filed a motion for summary judgment in a holdover action filed against defendant tenant. The tenant filed a motion to dismiss the landlord's complaint.


OVERVIEW: The landlord filed a holdover action against the tenant alleging that the tenant was precluded from assigning an apartment pursuant to the Omnibus Housing Act, 1983 N.Y. Laws ch. 403, which contained an amendment to N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 226-b that effectively abrogated the right to assign a lease without the landlord's consent. The landlord filed a motion for summary judgment and the tenant filed a motion to dismiss. The court denied both motions, but granted tenant a reverse summary judgment dismissing the landlord's petition. The court found the legislative purpose of the amendment to § 226-b was not to reach back to invalidate assignments completed prior to the effective date of the amendment, but rather to ensure reaching later assignments even though the lease being signed predated the amendment. The court held that if the new § 226-b were to be applied to an assignment completed prior to the effective date, it would have unconstitutionally impaired contract rights. The court concluded that because the old section of § 112-b governed, the tenant was entitled to proceed with the assignment of the lease because the landlord's refusal to approve the assignment was unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court denied the landlord's motion for summary judgment in a holdover action. The court denied the tenant's motion to dismiss, but granted the tenant a reverse summary judgment dismissing the landlord's petition.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and Tenant -- Lease -- Right to Sublease or Assign

In a holdover summary proceeding commenced upon grounds that respondent tenant assigned her lease on June 20, 1983 despite petitioner landlord's rejection of the proposed assignment, and such assignment was valid as a matter of law under former section 226-b of the Real Property Law, respondent is entitled to summary judgment dismissing the petition. The new section 226-b, which limits a tenant's remedy for a landlord's refusal to consent to an assignment to a release from the obligations of the lease no matter how unreasonable such refusal, does not reach back to invalidate assignments completed prior to June 30, 1983; the purpose of the new statute's retroactivity is to ensure reaching later assignments even though the lease being assigned predates the effective date of the new section 226-b. Moreover, application of the new statute to a valid assignment completed prior to June 30, 1983 would unconstitutionally impair contract rights.

 

1691 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Sussman v. Hendrickson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


123 Misc. 2d 949; 475 N.Y.S.2d 745; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3114



April 13, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant elderly homeowners sought a ruling on their motion to vacate a default judgment entered against them in plaintiff purchaser's suit to compel the determination of a claim to real property.


OVERVIEW: When the homeowners failed to pay a portion of a county tax due on their home, a tax sale was conducted and the property was sold to the purchaser. The purchaser then brought a suit against the homeowners to determine the ownership of the home. The homeowners failed to answer, and a default judgment was entered against them. The court granted the homeowners' motion to vacate the default judgment, holding that they may have been able to prove that the deed issued to the purchaser was invalid. The court held that because the county failed to notify the homeowners of their arrears as required by Nassau County, N.Y., Admin. Code § 5-14.0, and failed to serve a redemption notice on them or their adult son as required by Nassau County, N.Y., Admin. Code § 5-51.0, the tax sale proceeding was deficient. The court held that thus, the resulting tax deed given to the purchaser may not have been valid so as to pass title of the home to her. The court held that the homeowners should have been afforded an opportunity to plead their right to redeem.


OUTCOME: The court granted the homeowners' motion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- Tax Liens, Tax Sales and Tax Titles -- Notice of Default in Payment of Real Property Tax -- Notice to Redeem

In an action to compel the determination of a claim to real property, a motion by defendants to vacate a default judgment which decreed that plaintiff, who had purchased a tax lien on defendants' residence, located in Nassau County, was the owner in fee simple and barred the defendants from any right, title or interest in the premises is granted. A prerequisite for a purchaser to obtain good title in a tax sale is strict compliance with statutory notice requirements, and the Nassau County Administrative Code permits a defendant to make satisfaction "as if no deed had been issued" and compels the plaintiff to afford a defendant that right. Accordingly, defendants should be afforded an opportunity to plead their right to redeem; they lost their home because of an inadvertent failure to pay part of one year's tax, and were denied notice of that default required by the code to be shown on subsequent tax bills; moreover, the fact that their adult son, who resided in the subject premises while defendants wintered in Florida, was not served with a notice to redeem [***2] as required in the case of an occupant, may upon a trial be found to be an additional defect in the proceeding.

 

1692 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Ira G. Katz, Inc. v. Commissioner of Labor


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


123 Misc. 2d 739; 474 N.Y.S.2d 676; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3072; 26 Wage & Hour Cas. (BNA) 1699



April 9, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner manufacturer filed a petition to set aside a ruling by respondent, New York State Industrial Board of Appeals (board), which held that the federal Fair Labor Standards Act (Act), 29 U.S.C.S. § 201 et seq., did not preclude the New York State Department of Labor (DOL) from conducting on-premises inspections and interviews to ensure compliance with the state minimum wage and hour laws.


OVERVIEW: The manufacturer produced children's headwear and was engaged in interstate commerce. The DOL thrice attempted to inspect the manufacturer's records and to interview his employees, but the manufacturer refused permission to do so. The DOL notified the manufacturer that it had violated N.Y. Lab. Law §§ 26, 661 by failing to permit employee interviews concerning wages paid and hours worked and for failing to allow inspections of payroll records. The board ordered the manufacturer to comply with the DOL's demand for compliance. The manufacturer argued that the Act preempted the state's regulation of wages and hours and that the state had no right to inspect its records or to interview its employees. The court dismissed the manufacturer's petition to set aside the board's ruling. The court rejected the manufacturer's supremacy clause argument because there was no direct conflict between § 11 of the Act and N.Y. Lab. Law §§ 26, 661. The court held that the state's record-keeping and reporting requirements were not preempted by the federal requirements because the state's right to regulate gave it the implied power to do what was necessary to enforce the regulations.


OUTCOME: The court denied the manufacturer's application and dismissed its petition to set aside the board's ruling that the Act did not preclude the DOL from conducting on-premises inspections and interviews to ensure that the manufacturer complied with the state minimum wage and hour laws.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Labor -- Minimum Wage and Hours of Employment -- Federal Pre-emption of State Laws

A petition seeking to set aside a ruling by the New York State Industrial Board of Appeals (IBA) that the Federal Fair Labor Standards Act (FLSA) (US Code, tit 29, § 201 et seq.) does not preclude New York State Department of Labor officials from conducting on-premises inspections and interviews to ensure employer compliance with State minimum wage and hour laws is dismissed. The State's right to inspect an employer's wage and hour records is not pre-empted by the FLSA which clearly manifests a Congressional intent to preserve a role for State wage and hour laws, to encourage concurrent enforcement of State and Federal laws and thus to promote the elimination of substandard working conditions (US Code, tit 29, § 211, subd [b]; § 218).

 

1693 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. M.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Bronx County


124 Misc. 2d 888; 476 N.Y.S.2d 723; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3269



April 4, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] This court can come to no other conclusion than that the People have failed to show the legality of the police conduct in the first instance, and as a consequence, for all of the reasons hereinbefore stated, this application is granted in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed an application to dismiss the indictments on the grounds that the prosecution of the first defendant was barred pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 40.40. As to the other defendants, it was contended that the prosecution was bound by a promise related to a plea bargaining agreement.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were employed by a sanitation corporation. One defendant was indicted on two counts of forgery of a vehicle identification number (VIN), illegal possession of a vehicle identification plate, and two counts of stolen property in the first degree. Two other defendants were indicted on three counts of forgery of a VIN number, four counts of illegal possession of a vehicle identification number plate, six counts of criminal possession of stolen property in the first degree, and two counts of criminal possession of stolen property in the third degree. Upon the application to dismiss, the court ruled that the district attorney would be held to his agreement with respect to the first defendant to the extent that the plea and sentence previously imposed were reinstated and the indictment was dismissed. The court held that the state failed to show the legality of the police conduct. The court found that the other two defendants were in custody without having been given Miranda warnings. The court held that the so-called invitation could in no reasonable way have been interpreted, either express or implied, to justify an entry into the premises for the purpose of taking inventory.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' application to dismiss both of the indictments against them.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Former Jeopardy -- Nonconsensual Inventory Search

The People, having accepted defendant's guilty plea to an attempt to criminally possess stolen property in satisfaction of charges resulting from the discovery by police of stolen truck parts and an altered vehicle identification number plate on the premises of defendant's sanitation business, may not thereafter indict defendant for additional crimes based upon the discovery of additional stolen property on defendant's business premises since defendant's prosecution based upon new evidence found after the disposition of the complaint is barred by double jeopardy ( CPL 40.40) and, furthermore, defendant cannot be deprived of the benefit of his plea bargain even though the police investigation began prior to the plea and was ongoing at the time of the plea; the adjournments in contemplation of dismissal granted to the other defendants do not prevent a later prosecution [***2] on double jeopardy grounds; however, the initial search of the business premises conducted by police after they were invited inside for an explanation as to why a sanitation truck stopped outside the premises lacked a vehicle identification number plate was unlawful since the words "I'll show you" used to invite the police onto the premises cannot be interpreted as an express or implied consent to a police entry onto the premises for the purpose of making an inventory search.

 

1694 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Butler v. Griffin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


124 Misc. 2d 13; 475 N.Y.S.2d 726; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3139



April 3, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city employee sought an order and a judgment in his suit against defendant city under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, seeking to compel the city to make contributions to the New York State Employees' Retirement System to purchase creditable service for the employee's prior CETA service time, for counsel fees, and to certify the suit as a class action.


OVERVIEW: Before he became a permanent city employee, the employee worked at CETA service. After becoming a permanent employee, he filed an application pursuant to 1975 N.Y. Laws 769, § 3 to require the city to make contributions to the New York State Employees' Retirement System to purchase creditable service for the employee's prior CETA service time. When the city refused, the employee filed suit on his behalf and others similarly situated, requesting relief, counsel fees, and to certify the suit as a class action. The court ordered the city to make the contributions and denied counsel fees and class certification. The court held that an employer's contributions were mandatory under § 3 upon a proper application by an employee. The court held that the employee did not fully comply with the requirements of N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 9 regarding class actions.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the city to make contributions to the retirement system. The court denied the employee's application for class action status and counsel fees.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Actions -- Class Actions

1. Class action status shall be denied a petitioner who has not fully complied with the requirements of CPLR article 9; petitioner did not move for an order determining whether the action may be maintained, failed to submit sufficient evidence to establish to the court's satisfaction that the class of people he sought to represent was so numerous as to satisfy CPLR 901 (subd a, par 1), and failed to set forth the extent and nature of any litigation concerning the controversy already commenced by or against members of the class. Although class action status is denied, the rights of others so situated shall be governed by the principle of stare decisis.

Civil Service -- Retirement and Pension Benefits -- Former CETA Employees

2. The City of Buffalo is required pursuant to chapter 769 of the Laws of 1975 to make such contributions to the State Employees' Retirement System in an amount to be determined necessary to purchase creditable time for the prior CETA service time of a petitioner who subsequently was hired by the city, since the Legislature never intended to only grant retirement service credits to former CETA employees if their [***2] employer chose to make retroactive payments; an examination of the legislative history reveals that such payments were intended to be mandatory upon the employee making proper application.

 

1695 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Alweis v. Evans


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


123 Misc. 2d 627; 474 N.Y.S.2d 249; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3065



April 2, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] It is therefore the decision of this court that section 299 of the Judiciary Law is constitutional; the directive issued by Honorable William R. Roy, Administrative Judge of the Fifth Judicial District, dated May 12, 1981 is hereby declared null and void as are all directive orders issued by an Administrative Judge or Justice of this State, which directive or order seeks to compel a Trial Judge or Justice to order a transcript of any proceeding pursuant to a specific section of the Judiciary Law; and that plaintiffs' claim for monetary damages should be dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff court reporters filed suit against defendant judges seeking a declaratory judgment declaring that N.Y. Jud. Law § 299 was unconstitutional and enjoining the judges from requiring the court reporters to furnish free copies of transcripts.


OVERVIEW: The court reporters argued that N.Y. Jud. Law § 299, which required them to provide the judges with free transcripts, was in conflict with N.Y. Jud. Law § 302. N.Y. Jud. Law § 302 provided for payment to the court reporters for transcripts. The court reporters also requested monetary damages for the transcripts that they had furnished free of charge. The court entered judgment dismissing the court reporters' request for monetary relief, holding that N.Y. Jud. Law § 299 was not unconstitutional. The court further held that it was clearly the legislature's intent to let a trial court, in its sound discretion, determine if transcripts should be ordered under N.Y. Jud. Law § 299 or under N.Y. Jud. Law § 302. The court determined that a directive by one of the judges, which attempted to compel a trial judge to order transcripts under a specific section of the Judiciary Law, was null and void.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the court reporters' claim for monetary relief.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Transcripts of Judicial Proceedings

 Section 299 of the Judiciary Law, which requires that court reporters furnish Judges with free copies of transcripts upon request, does not conflict with section 302 of that law, which provides for payment to court reporters for transcripts provided to Judges, and is constitutional; it was the intent of the Legislature to allow the trial court, in its sound discretion, to determine if the transcripts should be ordered under section 299 free of charge, or under section 302 whereby the stenographer would be reimbursed. Directive orders seeking to compel a Trial Judge [***2] or Justice to order a transcript of any proceeding pursuant to a specific section of the Judiciary Law are declared null and void, and plaintiffs' claim for monetary damages is dismissed, since claims for money judgments against the State should properly be litigated in the Court of Claims.

 

1696 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Commissioner of Labor v. Chudzik


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Cattaraugus County


123 Misc. 2d 959; 474 N.Y.S.2d 920; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3116



March 30, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the reasons stated the motion to extend a levy on property is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commissioner of Labor (commissioner) filed an ex parte motion seeking an extension of a levy upon personal property of respondent judgment debtor on the debtor's property held by the garnishee. The commissioner contended that the debtor owed the commissioner, a judgment creditor, unpaid unemployment insurance.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner contended that authority for the motion was pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5232(a) and that the levy was served pursuant to Rule 5232(a) and N.Y. Labor Law § 573(1). N.Y. Labor Law § 573(1) provided that the commissioner could collect unpaid unemployment insurance by the institution of a civil action, and if a judgment was rendered in its favor it could be collected by the preparation of an execution with delivery of it to a sheriff for a levy on the debtor's property. N.Y. Labor Law § 573(2) authorized the commissioner to issue a warrant to a sheriff commanding the sheriff to levy upon and sell the real and personal property of an employer who had defaulted in the payment. However N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5232(c) imposed an obligation on the sheriff to serve a copy of the execution on the debtor and give notice to the debtor and any garnishee about possibly exempt property. The court found that the commissioner did not comply with the notice requirements of Rule 5232(c) and thus denied the commissioner's motion.


OUTCOME: The court denied the commissioner's ex parte motion that sought an extension of a levy upon personal property of the judgment debtor on the debtor's property held by the garnishee.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Levy Upon Personal Property for Nonpayment of Unemployment Insurance

The provisions of subdivision 2 of section 573 of the Labor Law relating to a levy upon personal property for nonpayment of unemployment insurance made on the issuance of a warrant by the Commissioner of Labor are unconstitutional due to the complete absence therefrom of notice requirements concerning exempt property. Accordingly, a motion by the Commissioner of Labor of the State of New York seeking an extension of a levy upon personal property for the purpose of collecting unpaid unemployment insurance is denied; to the extent that the supporting affirmation alleges compliance with statutory provisions that led to a "tax compliance agent's levy" it at best alleges compliance with the provisions of subdivision 2 of section 573; compliance with the provisions of an unconstitutional statute is a nullity. Moreover, although said affirmation also alleges that the initial levy was made pursuant to CPLR 5232 (subd [a]) and subdivision 1 of section 573 of the Labor Law, it is nonetheless [***2] deficient in failing to state compliance at any time with the determined constitutional requirements of notice concerning exempt property which is provided in CPLR 5232 (subd [c]).

 

1697 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Lugo v. Board of Elections


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


123 Misc. 2d 764; 474 N.Y.S.2d 910; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3075



March 23, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss is accordingly granted; the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was the motion to dismiss filed by defendant board of elections in the action of petitioner prospective candidate to force the board to validate his designating petition under N.Y. Elec. Law § 2-122, as amended by 1983 N.Y. Laws 1000. Although he wanted to run as a candidate for delegate to the Democratic National Convention from the 16th congressional district, his designating petition showed the 17th district.


OVERVIEW: In the instant election proceeding, the prospective candidate sought to validate his designating petition. He had hoped to run in a presidential primary election as a candidate for delegate from the 16th district to the Democratic National Convention. However, his statement of candidacy indicated that he was seeking election from the 17th district, his designating petition showed that he was from the 16th district, and the correction allegedly filed could not be found. The court dismissed the petition because the discrepancy in the district designation between the statement and the petition was a fatal defect, which in and of itself disqualified the prospective candidate. Judicial intervention would have prejudiced substantial rights of others and might have unconstitutionally upset the delicate balance of interests protected by the statutes and party rules. The prospective candidate could not be certified in either district because he had not filed the proper documentation in either. Further, the validation proceeding was not timely commenced under N.Y. Elec. Law § 16-102(2), and the prospective candidate offered no excuse for failing to timely institute the proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court granted the board's motion to dismiss the prospective candidate's petition to validate his candidacy for delegate to the Democratic National Convention.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- Presidential Primary -- Delegate -- Valid Statement of Candidacy

A petition seeking to validate petitioner's designating petition as a candidate for the party position of delegate to the Democratic National Convention is dismissed where petitioner filed a statement of candidacy representing that he was seeking election from the 17th Congressional District and subsequently circulated and filed a designating petition for the position of delegate from the 16th Congressional District. Pursuant to Democratic Party rules promulgated in accordance with the current presidential primary law (L 1983, ch 1000), the filing of a valid statement of candidacy listing the correct Congressional district is a condition precedent to a permissible candidacy, and judicial intervention would prejudice substantial rights of others and might unconstitutionally upset the delicate balance of interests protected by the interrelated statute and party rules. Accordingly, the discrepancy in district designation between the statement and petition is a fatal defect, and petitioner cannot be certified as a candidate [***2] in either district, since in neither district did he file both a proper statement of candidacy and a valid designating petition. Moreover, even if the discrepancy in district designation were not a fatal defect, the proceeding would be dismissed as not having been timely commenced; petitioner's four-day hesitation between receiving notice of the Board of Elections' adverse determination and obtaining an order to show cause evinces the absence of the diligence and alacrity which the law requires.

 

1698 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Commissioner of Social Services v. Jose T.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Kings County


123 Misc. 2d 893; 475 N.Y.S.2d 313; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3101



March 5, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Commissioner of Social Services of the City of New York filed a paternity proceeding against respondent father. The father admitted paternity and consented to an order directing support. The commissioner submitted orders of filiation and an order of support which contained a provision authorizing income deduction. The father alleged that the income deduction provision's notice requirement violated due process.


OVERVIEW: The court noted that an income deduction order was a prepared form declaring that its effectiveness upon (a) a finding by the commissioner's support collection unit that the father defaulted, and (b) service on the employer of a copy of the income deduction order. The court found that the proposed support order declared that there was to be no deduction until the support collection unit found an arrearage, the father was notified at least 15 days prior to service of the income deduction order, and he failed to pay the arrearages within this period. The court found that when the support collection unit was the payee of a support order, the unit itself was charged with finding good cause mandating an income deduction order. The court held that there was no provision for judicial review, and that the only due process right was the opportunity to pay within the 15-day notice period. The court held that blind application of the order resulted in a person being deprived of his compensation from employment. The court held the finding by the support collection unit was to be subject to judicial review on notice to the father with an opportunity to contest that finding.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that to satisfy due process considerations, support orders were to contain a clause providing that respondents were to be advised within 15 days of their right to contest a determination by the support collection unit's determination, and if a respondent successfully contested such a determination, the income deduction order would not then be served.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Support -- Income Deduction Order

The statute providing for the implementation of a contingent income deduction order upon the determination of the support collection unit of a Department of Social Services that "good cause" exists for implementing the order by reason of the respondent's default in making court-ordered support payments to the unit as assignee of the right to receive child support (Personal Property Law, § 49-b, subd 1, par [b]) is an unconstitutional denial of due process to the extent that no provision is made for judicial review of the administrative determination of "good cause" as would be the case if the payee under the support order is someone other than the support collection unit (Personal Property Law, § 49-b, subd 1, par [a]); therefore, in order to pass constitutional muster, paragraph (b) of the statute must be read together with paragraph (a) so as to require that the respondent be served with 15 days' notice of an opportunity to contest the unit's determination as well as to cure the arrears before the income deduction order is served on the employer; accordingly, a proposed contingent income deduction order signed at [***2] the time the support order is made is to be retained by the court in the support proceeding file to be released to the Department of Social Services upon ex parte filing of an affidavit or affirmation attesting that the support collection unit has determined that respondent is in the requisite amount of arrears, that the required 15 days' notice to respondent to either cure or contest has been served and that respondent has failed to either cure or contest the unit's determination within such period.

 

1699 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Cais v. Pichler


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Special Term, New York County


123 Misc. 2d 275; 473 N.Y.S.2d 719; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2991



March 2, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff judgment debtor filed a motion for summary judgment on the ground that there were no triable issues of fact or law with respect to the liability of defendant sheriff for an alleged unlawful seizure and sale at auction of the judgment debtor's vehicle. The sheriff filed a motion to dismiss the complaint for failure to state a cause of action pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a).


OVERVIEW: The sheriff seized the judgment debtor's vehicle pursuant to an execution filed by the judgment creditor. The sheriff's office contacted the Connecticut Department of Motor Vehicles requesting that it notify the vehicle's owner of the seizure. It also published a notice of the sale. The vehicle was sold at auction. In the judgment debtor's action for the alleged unlawful seizure, he filed a motion for summary judgment and the sheriff filed a motion to dismiss for failure to state a claim. The court granted summary judgment in favor of the sheriff and denied the judgment debtor's motion, holding that the sheriff satisfied his duty to notify the judgment debtor of the seizure and sale as required by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5232 since the sheriff was under no obligation to serve the judgment debtor with notice in Connecticut. With respect to the argument that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5232 was unconstitutional, the court noted that the legislature had corrected the constitutional deficiencies of the statute, but it was not done until after the auction. Therefore, the court ruled that in the absence of constitutional guidelines, the sheriff's execution and judicial sale were proper.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment in favor of the sheriff and dismissed the judgment debtor's complaint for the alleged unlawful seizure and sale at auction of the judgment debtor's vehicle. The court denied the judgment debtor's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Execution -- Notification of Seizure

Plaintiff's motion for summary judgment on the ground that there are no triable issues of fact or law with respect to defendant's liability for an alleged unlawful seizure and sale at auction of plaintiff's vehicle is denied, and summary judgment is granted to defendant, Sheriff of the City of New York; the attempt of the Sheriff's office to notify plaintiff of the March, 1982 seizure and impending sale to satisfy an underlying judgment satisfied the provisions of CPLR 5232, which direct that service be effected in the same manner as a summons, since service of process in a Civil Court action can generally only be made within the City of New York and defendant's communication with the Connecticut Department of Motor Vehicles, the vehicle having had Connecticut license plates, requesting that the owner be notified of the seizure and impending sale, amounted to more than sufficient service. Although the seizure took place in accordance with enforcement procedures which had been held unconstitutional shortly before the seizure, CPLR 5232 (subd [c]), an attempt to correct the constitutional deficiencies did not become effective until September,  [***2] 1982; if the action is deemed one for a wrongful taking pursuant to CPLR 5202, plaintiff has failed to state a cause of action since he did not allege a valid claim of exemption, and if the action is based on defendant's failure to provide plaintiff notice of possible exemptions before disposition of the property, the procedural guidelines established after CPLR 5232 (subds [a], [b]) were held unconstitutional had not yet been enacted at the time of the seizure, and in any event the property seized is of a type traditionally not subject to any exemptions. Further, the named defendant, the Sheriff of the City of New York, is not liable for the acts or omissions of his deputies (see Administrative Code of City of New York, § 1032-12.0, subd b), and the City of New York would be singularly liable for any wrongful acts of defendant's deputies.

 

1700 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pecora


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Justice Court of New York, Town of Wappinger, Dutchess County


123 Misc. 2d 259; 473 N.Y.S.2d 320; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2986



March 1, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was accused of speeding, in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1180(d), and refusing to submit to an alco-sensor test, pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1193-a. Before the court was defendant's motion to dismiss the accusation rendered under § 1193-a.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped and accused of speeding in violation of § 1180(d). At the time defendant was stopped, the police officer requested that she submit to an alco-sensor test pursuant to § 1193-a. Defendant was not asked to submit to field sobriety tests; nor was she asked to take a chemical test pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1194. Defendant refused to take the alco-sensor test and she received tickets for both violations. Defendant brought a motion to dismiss the §1193-a accusation, which the court granted. The court held that although § 1193-a did not violate the Fifth Amendment, it was unconstitutional as applied to defendant because there was no probable or reasonable cause present to justify the officer's request, in violation of the Fourth Amendment. Section 1193-a was unconstitutional when it allowed the test to be requested without probable cause. There was no evidence of any combination of slurred speech, alcohol on the breath, labored walking, watery eyes, etc. that would create probable or reasonable cause for the search. While probable cause existed to stop the vehicle for speeding, there were insufficient grounds to justify any further search.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the accusation for refusing to submit to an alco-sensor test.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Motor Vehicles -- Chemical Tests -- Driving while Intoxicated

 Section 1193-a of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, which provides that the operator of a motor vehicle involved in an accident or operated in violation of any provisions of the Vehicle and Traffic Law shall, at the request of a police officer, submit to a breath test, does not violate a defendant's right against self incrimination; such a breath test constitutes physical and not testimonial evidence and is not protected by the Fifth Amendment. However, insofar as section 1194 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law authorizes a search in the absence of probable or reasonable cause, it is unconstitutional as applied to a defendant stopped for speeding who is asked to submit to an alco-sensor test where no indicia of intoxication is articulated by the arresting officer.

 

1701 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Park West Village Associates v. Abrams


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


127 Misc. 2d 372; 491 N.Y.S.2d 886; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3757



February 24, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is accordingly granted, and the cross motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, grantee of a condominium apartment buildings, sought review of the determination of respondent attorney general that the grantee was required but failed to seek prior approval of its noneviction condominium conversion plan from the board of estimate and of his rejection of the plan. The grantee sought to annul the determination and to compel the attorney general to accept the plan. The attorney general filed a motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The grantor purchased land containing tenant-occupied apartment buildings from the city and agreed to relocate the existing tenants and to demolish the existing buildings. The agreement provided that the grantor and any subsequent grantee would devote the land for uses specified in the redevelopment plan and that there would be no changes in land use or density for a certain period without the consent of the board of estimate. The grantee acquired the land from the grantor and sponsored and filed a noneviction condominium conversion plan to convert the apartments to occupant-owned apartments. The attorney general rejected the plan on the basis that prior approval of the board was a prerequisite to the conversion. The court held prior board approval was not required for the conversion because (1) the city and grantor clearly intended to permit the occupancy of apartments by owners thereof as well as by tenants; (2) the limitation on changes requiring prior board approval was intended to apply only to changes in land use and in land density; and (3) the agreement between the city and the grantor controlled the rights and responsibilities of the grantee as a successor to the grantor.


OUTCOME: The court granted the grantee's petition to annul the attorney general's determination that the grantee's noneviction condominium conversion plan required the prior approval of the board of estimate and compelled the attorney general to accept the plan. The court denied the attorney general's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Condominiums and Cooperatives -- Conversion Plan -- Approval of Municipal Board

Prior approval by a municipal board of estimate of a noneviction condominium conversion plan is not a prerequisite to acceptance of the plan for filing by the Attorney-General though an agreement between the original owner of the land on which the apartments to be subject to conversion were built and the municipality provides that there be no changes in land use or increased density for a period of 40 years from the project completion date without prior board approval, where the court has determined by looking to relevant surrounding circumstances such as project financing that the intent of the contracting parties to the redevelopment agreement was to permit occupancy of the apartments by owners thereof as well as by tenants, and the substituted condominium form of ownership is not a change in land use since the use continues to be residential.

 

1702 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Guzman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


125 Misc. 2d 457; 478 N.Y.S.2d 455; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3437



February 17, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defense counsel, in a criminal trial, moved to challenge a prospective juror who was deaf, for cause, on the grounds that defendant would be denied a fair trial under the Sixth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. VI, if he were allowed to sit as a juror.


OVERVIEW: In a criminal trial, defense counsel challenged for cause a juror from the pool that was deaf. The defendant argued that he would be denied a fair trial under the Sixth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. VI, if the juror were allowed to sit because he could not be sure that the translation by the interpreter was accurate; that the deaf person would lose words if more than one person spoke at a time; that the deaf person could not hear subtle nuances of vocal inflections which are crucial in determining the credibility of any witness; that the sanctity of the jury deliberations would be violated by the presence of the interpreter; and that the presence of the interpreter would be too disruptive to both the trial and the deliberative process. The court found that a deaf juror is a member of a protected class and could not be excluded from the jury pool under state and federal law. To challenge him for cause would circumvent the intent of the legislature. Because the juror understood signed English, he met the statutory language requirement for jury service. The court rejected all of the defendant's arguments, and found no reason why the juror should be challenged for cause.


OUTCOME: Because neither the defense counsel nor any independent examination by the court disclosed any reason why the prospective juror who was deaf should be challenged for cause, the motion was therefore denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Jury -- Disqualification of Juror -- Exclusion of Deaf Juror in Criminal Trial -- Presence of "Sign Language Interpreter" in Jury Room

A deaf person who is otherwise qualified for jury service because of the 1983 amendment to the juror qualification statute, which eliminated the systematic exclusion of the handicapped by shifting emphasis from predetermination based upon the presence of a handicapping condition to an examination of the individual's capacity to perform "in a reasonable manner the duties of a juror" (Judiciary Law, § 510, subd 3) may not be challenged for cause in a criminal trial solely on the basis of his deafness since the deaf community is a cognizable group whose members cannot constitutionally be systematically excluded from jury service and there is no reason that a deaf person, with the aid of a qualified, court-appointed "sign language interpreter", cannot do as fine a job or better than hearing jurors; the presence of the interpreter in the jury room acting merely as a communications facilitator will not violate the sanctity of the jury deliberations since the rule excluding persons other than jurors from the jury room during deliberations pertains [***2] mainly to officers of the court and experience from other jurisdictions where the deaf have been seated on juries has shown that the presence of interpreters in the jury room has not led to any breach of confidentiality or caused any problems with respect to the panel not being able effectively to deliberate because of the deaf person.

 

1703 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Great Neck Electric, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


123 Misc. 2d 280; 473 N.Y.S.2d 686; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2992



February 15, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner bidder sought an order setting aside an award or a proposed award of contract by the city to respondent bidder for the installation of an emergency generator set and its related equipment at the public building. Intervenor bidder sought leave to intervene and to have any contract awarded to it.


OVERVIEW: In response to the city's solicitation of bids for a public contract, petitioner was informed that its low bid was rejected because of a failure to complete an employment report. The purpose of the report was to show the number of minorities and women in its work force. Petitioner argued that it was committed to a policy of nondiscrimination and that it would be a waste of funds not to give the contract to the lowest bidder. The city argued that could decline bids which did not comply with bid specifications.The court found that petitioner's bid was rejected because it failed to complete the information necessary for it to be awarded a bid. Petitioner's deliberate refusal to submit a completed employment report, although on notice that same was necessary to the submission of its bid, was a material variance of the bid specifications. Therefore, the city's rejection of petitioner's bid was proper. The court also found that intervenor had not submitted any document from the city indicating the reason for its rejection nor did it indicate that a formal request for such a reason had been made.


OUTCOME: The petition to set aside the award of a public contract was dismissed. The application to intervene was granted.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Bids and Bidders -- Employment Report as Prerequisite to Bid Acceptance

Respondent city's rejection of petitioner's low bid for the installation of an emergency generator set in a city building on the ground that said bid failed to contain a completed employment report as required by the city in furtherance of a policy of nondiscrimination in employment embodied in a Mayoral Executive Order is proper. Petitioner's deliberate refusal to submit the completed report, although on notice that same was necessary to the submission of its bid, was a material variance of the bid specifications; the requirement to fill out such report is not mandating a quota nor is it imposing on potential contractors the requirement to institute an affirmative action program. Moreover, inasmuch as intervenor, the second lowest bidder for said installation, has not submitted any document from the city indicating the reason for its rejection nor indicated that a formal request for such a reason has been made, its claim that it should be awarded the bid is denied without prejudice.

 

1704 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Attorney Gen. of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


126 Misc. 2d 278; 482 N.Y.S.2d 199; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3597



February 14, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent corporate controller brought a motion to dismiss petitioner New York Attorney General's proceeding against him and to quash a subpoena duces tecum served upon him in reference to his corporation's having fraudulently sold gold future contracts. The controller, although having operated his business in New York, quickly moved to Florida, from which he contested service of the subpoena.


OVERVIEW: A corporate controller's corporation was selling fraudulent gold future contracts. The New York Attorney General began his investigation of the matter and the controller quickly moved to Florida. Through the U.S. Post Office, the Attorney General effectuated service of a subpoena duces tecum upon the controller. The controller requested that the court quash the subpoena because service of a subpoena by regular mail violated his procedural due process rights per the U.S. Constitution. The court denied the motion to quash and motion to dismiss the Attorney General's proceeding against the controller. The court ordered the controller to appear before the Attorney General within three days of its order. The court held that there was no proceeding because it was an investigation and the subpoena's service was adequate under the circumstances. The court reasoned that a subpoena could be served in the same fashion as a summons, N.Y. C.P.L.R. 2303, and where the court was satisfied that traditional modes were or would be unavailing, it could fashion another, N.Y. C.P.L.R. 308(5). The court concluded that the statute was designed for the impracticable case.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to quash and motion to dismiss the New York Attorney General's proceeding against the controller because there was no proceeding, but an investigation. The court ordered the controller to appear before the Attorney General within three days of its order.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Attorney-General -- Investigatory Powers -- Subpoena

The target of an investigation by the Attorney-General into the fraudulent sale of gold future contracts may not avoid the investigation by removing himself from the State; his motion to dismiss is denied, since there is no proceeding to dismiss, and his motion to quash, as unconstitutional, a subpoena served on him by mail in Florida is denied since the Constitution, under the due process clause, does not prescribe a mode of service, but only requires that the method selected be reasonably calculated to give full and timely notice of the proceeding and an opportunity to be heard, and the instant motion demonstrated that the service by mail met constitutional standards. As to the contention that the subpoena is invalid because it was made under section 352-b of the General Business Law, which is designed to reach nonresidents doing or soliciting business in the State, it should be noted that a subpoena may be served in the same fashion as a summons and where the court is satisfied that traditional modes are or will be unavailing, it may fashion another, it being unnecessary that usual methods be attempted first since the [***2] statute was designed for the impracticable case.

 

1705 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Commissioner of Social Services on behalf of Aida S. v. Efrain V.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


122 Misc. 2d 667; 472 N.Y.S.2d 307; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2884



February 8, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner commissioner filed a motion to reargue respondent's motion to dismiss paternity proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Respondent's motion to dismiss paternity proceedings was based on the ground that they were barred by the statute of limitations found in the N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 517(b). The commissioner argued that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 517(b) was constitutionally defective because it unreasonably discriminated against nonmarital children seeking support from their fathers. The court found that N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 348 gave the commissioner a separate and independent right to sue as the mother's assignee. As her assignee he succeeded to her rights under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 517(a) and the statute of limitations, if any, set forth in § 517(a) governed. Therefore, when the commissioner sued as the mother's assignee he was also not subject to any statute of limitations. The constitutionality of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 517(b) was upheld. The court found that the commissioner was suing as the mother's assignee and as such he was not subject to any time limitation.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to reargue and denied the motion to dismiss the petition.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children Born out of Wedlock -- Paternity Proceeding -- Statute of Limitations

Subdivision (b) of section 517 of the Family Court Act, which provides that the Commissioner of Social Services shall have 10 years after the birth of a child within which to commence a paternity proceeding, is not unconstitutional, since it is within the purview of the Legislature to set reasonable limitations on actions and 10 years is not per se unreasonable; accordingly, when the commissioner sues on his own behalf, he is subject to the 10-year Statute of Limitations. However, section 348 of the Social Services Law gives the commissioner a separate and independent right to sue as the mother's assignee and as her assignee, he succeeds to her rights under subdivision (a) of section 517 of the Family Court Act, which gives a mother five years after the birth of the child to bring a paternity proceeding; inasmuch as subdivision (a) has been declared unconstitutional, the commissioner is not subject to any Statute of Limitations when he sues as the mother's assignee.

 

1706 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Suarez


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Bronx County


122 Misc. 2d 1040; 472 N.Y.S.2d 553; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2936



January 27, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion to withdraw his plea of guilty is granted, the said plea is vacated, and for the reasons set forth herein, indictment No. 974 of 1979 is dismissed and the defendant is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion in the Supreme Court of New York, Bronx County (New York) to withdraw a previously entered plea of guilty to criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree and for dismissal of the indictment. He alleged that his constitutional and statutory protections against double jeopardy were violated.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court found that defendant was charged in a prior indictment with robbery in the second degree, grand larceny in the third degree, criminal possession of stolen property in the third degree, and criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree, a misdemeanor arising from the same transaction as that in the case at bar. The court found that the indictment to which defendant pled guilty was a superseding indictment. The court further found that the trial court before which the prior indictment was heard dismissed all counts due to the People's failure to obtain witnesses. The court held that defendant's guilty plea to a charge that arose from the same transaction as the dismissed indictment must be withdrawn on the basis that double jeopardy barred defendant's prosecution for this offense. The court held that N.Y. C.P.L. R. 40.20 prevented a second prosecution arising from the same transaction as did the United States Constitution. The court vacated defendant's plea and discharged defendant.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to withdraw his plea of guilty and vacated his plea. The court dismissed the superseding indictment and discharged defendant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Former Jeopardy

1. A motion to withdraw a plea of guilty to an indictment charging defendant with criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree and for dismissal of the indictment is granted, where a previous indictment arising from the same transaction charging defendant with criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree and three other offenses resulted in dismissal of the weapons charge and a trial order of dismissal as to the remaining counts because the prosecution was unable to obtain its witnesses, and the superseding indictment elevating the degree of the purely possessory weapons charge was based solely and exclusively on the defendant's prior commission of a crime. The trial order of dismissal had the same effect as an acquittal for double jeopardy purposes, and CPL 40.20 guards against double prosecutions where the offense may be differentiated only esoterically in law; the only factual change is the prior conviction, which is such an [***2] esoteric difference.

Crimes -- Former Jeopardy -- Timeliness of Objection

2. Neither a plea of guilty nor a failure to raise a double jeopardy argument prior to the plea, constitutes a waiver of the constitutional right not to be prosecuted twice for the same offense.

 

1707 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Darryl M.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


123 Misc. 2d 723; 475 N.Y.S.2d 704; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3070



January 25, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motions are denied in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed motions to have N.Y. Penal Code § 245.00(a) declared unconstitutional, to have the information charging him with violating § 245.00(a) declared defective, and which requested a denial of plaintiff State of New York's motion to reargue a sexual abuse charge pursuant to N.Y. Penal Code § 130.55, in an action alleging defendant committed sexual abuse and public lewdness.


OVERVIEW: A police officer observed defendant rubbing his covered erect penis against the buttocks of three females, in a public place. Defendant was also observed repeatedly stroking his erect penis. Defendant was subsequently charged with sexual abuse and public lewdness in violation of N.Y. Penal Code § 245.00. Plaintiff State of New York dropped the charges of sexual abuse in violation of N.Y. Penal Code § 130.55, but filed a motion to reargue. Defendant sought to have the information against him declared defective and § 245.00 declared unconstitutional and to have plaintiff's motion denied. The trial court denied defendant's motions and granted plaintiff's motion to reargue. The court determined that defendant's conduct fell within the purview of the statute and that although he had no standing to challenge § 245.00, that provision and § 130.55 withstood constitutional challenges because they defined prohibited conduct in sufficient detail to give ordinarily intelligent people reasonable notice and to preclude arbitrary enforcement.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motions were denied, and plaintiff's motion to reargue was granted because defendant intentionally committed an act clearly proscribed by a statute, the statute did not violate defendant's constitutional rights as it was not overbroad or vague because it was sufficiently definite and clear to give unequivocal warning to citizens of the rule to be obeyed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Public Lewdness

1. Defendant's actions of rubbing his covered erect penis against the buttocks of three women at a subway station and repeatedly stroking his covered erect penis with his hand in public is encompassed by the public lewdness statute (Penal Law, § 245.00) since the statute, which, in addition to proscribing the intentional exposure of the private or intimate parts of the body in a lewd manner in a public place, also proscribes the commission of "any other lewd act * * * in a public place", is clearly not limited to acts of "exposure" alone; there is no requirement that defendant have acted "with intent that he be so observed" since any intentional lewd act committed in public can be proscribed regardless of whether the defendant intended that he be observed; even though defendant has no standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statute on grounds of vagueness since his conduct falls within the prohibition of the statute, the statute is not unconstitutionally vague and overbroad in any event since the phrase "any other lewd act" gives a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice of the type [***2] of conduct that is proscribed but the Legislature should consider drafting a more specific statute to assist in ensuring uniform law enforcement.

Crimes -- Sexual Abuse

2. A count of sexual abuse in the third degree (Penal Law, § 130.55), based upon defendant's acts of rubbing his covered erect penis against the buttocks of three women at a subway station, previously dismissed with consent of the People on an erroneous point of law concerning the requirement that the victim be present to testify in a sexual abuse case, may be restored to the information also charging defendant with public lewdness (Penal Law, § 245.00) without treating the prosecutor's motion as a "motion to reargue" subject to the requirements of CPLR 2221 and 2214; the fact that the People will not have testimony from any of the women defendant is charged with abusing is not a legal impediment to his conviction on the sexual abuse charge since police observations of defendant are sufficient to allow an inference of the lack of consent of the victims; the sexual abuse statute is not unconstitutionally vague as applied to this case since any unconsented contact between defendant's "intimate" part of his [***3] body and the victim's constitutes a violation of the statute.

 

1708 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Murer v. Butterfield


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County
