122 Misc. 2d 969; 472 N.Y.S.2d 539; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2927



January 17, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Superintendent of Highways of the Town of Huntington, applied for an injunction preventing respondents, the Supervisor and the Board of the Town of Huntington, from imposing a tax on town residents and directing that changes to the town budget be made.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, the town supervisor and board (town), budgeted a bond indebtedness into the highway budget of the Department of Highways of the town (department) with the effect of imposing taxes on some but not all of the villages of the town. Petitioner, the superintendent of the department, brought an action to enjoin the imposition of the tax and compel a change to the budget. Respondents made a motion to dismiss on the basis that petitioner lacked standing. The court held that petitioner had standing under N.Y. High. Law § 284 because he was a party to a written agreement with respect to the expenditure of funds. The court dismissed the action without prejudice so that the matter as a plenary action with a summons and complaint and so bondholders, village and town taxpayers could be joined as parties under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 1001(a).


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the action without prejudice on the basis that the action of petitioner, the town superintendent of department of highways, had to bring the matter as a plenary action and join bondholders and taxpayers as parties.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Bond Indebtedness for Highway Improvements -- Standing of Town Superintendent of Highways to Challenge Actions of Other Town Officials

The Superintendent of Highways of the Town of Huntington, although possessing no express statutory authority to challenge the actions of the Town Board and Town Supervisor removing the bond indebtedness for the improvement of certain sidewalks and highways in the town as a whole-town tax and instead budgeting the bond indebtedness into the town's highway budget to be paid only by those taxpayers who do not live within the four incorporated villages within the town, nevertheless has the implied authority to maintain an article 78 proceeding against the other town officers and boards since the Superintendent is a party to a written agreement with respect to the expenditure of funds as directed by statute (Highway Law, § 284) and, if the funds which are to be expended pursuant to the agreement have been illegally raised, the Superintendent must have standing to object to actions that could result in his personal liability or removal from office; however, the proceeding must be dismissed without prejudice to renewal in [***2] the form of a plenary action since a declaratory judgment action and not an article 78 proceeding is the proper vehicle to challenge the taxing scheme and the court cannot treat the article 78 proceeding as an action for declaratory judgment ( CPLR 103, subd [c]) due to the nonjoinder of necessary parties such as Suffolk County, the bondholders, and village and nonvillage taxpayers.

 

1709 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Harlem Teams for Self-Help, Inc. v. Department of Investigation


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


122 Misc. 2d 1066; 472 N.Y.S.2d 967; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2955



January 16, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, on the record as it stands, the motion to quash the subpoenas is granted, and the cross motion seeking enforcement is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, city investigatory agency and city officials, issued administrative subpoenas duces tecum to petitioner non-profit organizations for certain original records of the organizations and of petitioner executive director. The non-profit organizations and the executive director brought a motion to quash the subpoenas. The city investigatory agency and city officials brought a cross-motion to enforce the subpoenas.


OVERVIEW: The non-profit organizations and their executive director argued that the subpoenas were overbroad and lacked the requisite probable cause, and disputed the city investigatory agency's power and authority to conduct a personal criminal investigation of the executive director. The court agreed with the non-profit organizations and the executive director. Initially, the court found that administrative subpoenas were subject to the requirements of relevance and materiality, but in a purely investigatory administrative situation there was no necessity for any showing of probable cause such as the Fourth Amendment required for a criminal or civil warrant. The court held that an agency that asserted its subpoena power had to show the relevancy of the items sought and some basis for inquisitorial actions. The city investigatory agency's subpoenas failed to demonstrate the required degree of materiality and relevancy. Further, there was evidence that the materials sought were not for an administrative investigation, but in preparation or in aid of a criminal prosecution, which was not within the purview of the city investigatory agency's jurisdictional power.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to quash the subpoenas, and denied the cross-motion for enforcement of the subpoenas.


HEADNOTES

Witnesses -- Subpoena Duces Tecum -- Constitutional Requirements for Administrative or Office Subpoenas

The degree of preliminary showing for governmental interference with a person's property, whether by "warrant" or administrative or office subpoena, depends upon the purpose for which the material is sought to be used, i.e., from probable cause required for the "seizure" of evidence of a crime to a minimal showing of relevance and materiality for inspection in a preliminary or routine noncriminal investigation, so that a "warrant" will require a different preliminary showing depending on whether it is criminal or administrative as will a subpoena duces tecum depending on whether it has been issued by a Grand Jury or an administrative agency for investigatory or evidence-gathering purposes; judicial review to determine reasonableness is required whenever the government "searches", inspects, impounds or otherwise interferes with a person's property, the greater the intrusion, the higher degree of relevancy and materiality that must [***2] be shown to satisfy the test of reasonableness; accordingly, administrative subpoenas duces tecum issued by respondent New York City Department of Investigation seeking canceled checks relative to a loan transaction between two nonprofit, publicly funded social service organizations in connection with an investigation into alleged impropriety involving the salaries received by petitioner from both organizations must be quashed since it appears that the subpoenaed materials are intended for use as evidence in a criminal trial against petitioner and, aside from failing to show any probable cause, respondent has failed to demonstrate even that degree of materiality and relevancy required in a true administrative proceeding.

 

1710 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Harris Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Special Term, Clinton County


123 Misc. 2d 989; 476 N.Y.S.2d 235; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3120



January 12, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a motion with the court seeking an order dismissing an indictment against them for three counts of unlawful dealing in hazardous waste as prohibited by N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 71-2717 on the ground that § 71-2717 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: An indictment charged each defendant with the unlawful dealing in hazardous waste as prohibited by § 71-2717. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the indictment on the ground that § 71-2717 was unconstitutional because the rules and regulations promulgated by the Department of Environmental Conservation that were essential to its enforcement were unconstitutionally established. The court granted the motion to dismiss. The court held that regulations and rules promulgated by the department pursuant to Envtl. Conserv. Law § 27-903 violated N.Y. Const. art. IV, § 8 because they impermissibly "incorporated by reference" federal rules. The court noted that art. VI, § 8 forbid wholesale incorporation by reference and required the existence of a common and definite place where the exact content of rules and regulations could be found. The court determined that the department failed to comply with the prerequisites of the State Administrative Procedure Act because no prior notice or hearing on the actual text of the proposed regulations was ever effectuated. The court concluded that as a result, no notice of what conduct was prohibited under state law was promulgated.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the indictment against defendants for three counts of unlawful dealing in hazardous waste.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Hazardous Waste Regulations

An indictment charging defendants with unlawful dealing in hazardous waste as prohibited by ECL 71-2717 is dismissed on the ground that the statute is unconstitutional in that rules and regulations promulgated by the Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC) and essential to its enforcement were unconstitutionally established. The wholesale incorporation by reference of the Federal list of hazardous wastes in the rules and regulations promulgated violated section 8 of article IV of the New York Constitution; moreover, the subsequent submission of photocopies of the Federal regulations containing a list of hazardous wastes as an appendix to the Secretary of State did not comply with the prerequisites required by the State Administrative Procedure Act since no prior notice or hearing on the actual text of the proposed rules and regulations were ever effectuated. Accordingly, proper notice of what conduct was prohibited under New York State law and regulation was not promulgated by the DEC because of the incorporation by reference of the Federal rules and regulations in violation of section 8 of article [***2] IV.

 

1711 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Attco Metals Industries, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Suffolk County


122 Misc. 2d 689; 471 N.Y.S.2d 498; 1984 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2888



January 6, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After entering a guilty plea but prior to sentencing, defendant filed a motion for withdrawal of its guilty plea and reargument on the issue of the constitutionality of N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 27-0903.


OVERVIEW: The court granted reargument on the issue of the constitutionality of § 27-0903, which incorporated by reference federal hazardous waste regulations. The issue was whether the incorporation by reference of federal regulations was per se violative of N.Y. Const. art. IV, § 8. The court held that before any incorporation by reference would have been constitutionally acceptable the exact content of the material that was to have been incorporated by reference must have been filed with the Department of State. The federal list of hazardous wastes incorporated by reference into N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 27-0903 had not been filed with the Department of State. The court determined that the hazardous waste list in question should have been filed with the Department of State. Therefore, because the list of federal hazardous wastes had not been filed with the Department of State that incorporation by reference violated the plain language of N.Y. Const. art. IV, § 8.


OUTCOME: The court denied the argument proposed in using the statutory definition of hazardous waste to save and uphold the constitutionality of the statute and transferred the balance of defendant's application for further consideration.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Environmental Conservation -- Hazardous Wastes -- Incorporation by Reference of Federal List into State Law

Before any rule incorporated by reference can be constitutionally acceptable pursuant to section 8 of article IV of the New York Constitution, the entire text of the material sought to be incorporated by reference must first be filed with the Department of State. Accordingly, where the Federal list of hazardous wastes incorporated by reference into ECL 27-0903 had not been filed with the Department of State, such incorporation by reference violated the plain language of section 8 of article IV and is unconstitutional; the fact that said list of hazardous wastes was readily accessible does not obviate the need to have the list filed with the Department of State.

 

1712 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Guilbert


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Queens County


122 Misc. 2d 694; 472 N.Y.S.2d 90; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4148



December 20, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, charged with criminal possession of stolen property in the third degree (N.Y. Penal Law §165.40), fraudulent accosting (N.Y. Penal Law § 165.30), and loitering (N.Y. Penal Law §240.35), sought to dismiss the charges of loitering on the grounds that N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(7) violated the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment and the Fourth and Fifth Amendments.


OVERVIEW: Defendants argued that § 240.35 was unconstitutional because it violated the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, as well as the Fourth and Fifth Amendment. The statute provided that a person was guilty of violating § 240.35 if he loitered or remained in any transportation facility, or was found sleeping therein, and was unable to give a satisfactory explanation of his presence. The court ruled that in order for defendants to overcome the presumptive constitutionality of the statute, they had the burden of showing that the statute was invalid beyond a reasonable doubt. The court found that defendants failed to discharge their burden. The court concluded that there was a reasonable relationship between the welfare of the public at large and § 240.35. The court observed that § 240.35 was enacted to insure the safety of the patrons utilizing transportation facilities, which often served as the setting of the most violent crimes. The court held, therefore, that defendants failed to prove the statute's unconstitutionality beyond a reasonable doubt. The court denied defendants' motions to dismiss the loitering charges.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion to dismiss the loitering charges against them.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Loitering in Transportation Facility

Subdivision 7 of section 240.35 of the Penal Law which prohibits loitering in transportation facilities is constitutional. Statutes which prohibit loitering at a specific location are sustainable if the prohibited behavior is properly restricted to specific facilities where the likelihood of illegal activity is notorious; there is a reasonable relationship between the welfare of the public at large and the concern and efforts of the Legislature to insure the safety of the many patrons who utilize transportation facilities which too often serve as the setting of society's most violent crimes. Accordingly, motions by two defendants to dismiss charges of loitering on the ground that subdivision 7 of section 240.35 is unconstitutional are denied.

 

1713 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


1025 Fifth Ave., Inc. v. Marymount School of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


123 Misc. 2d 756; 475 N.Y.S.2d 182; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4176



December 20, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner cooperatives filed a proceeding, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, against respondent school and respondent commission seeking to vacate an order and decision of the commission which permitted the school to construct a rooftop gymnasium.


OVERVIEW: The cooperatives owned apartment houses adjacent to the school. The buildings were all within a historic district. The commission had aesthetic jurisdiction over the buildings within the historic district. Neighboring property owners, the residents of the cooperatives in particular, charged the construction of the rooftop gymnasium, even as modified, would have obstructed views from their apartments and lessened property values, as well as detracted from the fashionable ambiance of the neighborhood. Despite this opposition, the commission entered its order and decision issuing the school a certificate of appropriateness. In their petition, the cooperatives argued the failure to include statutory provisions for the commission to rule upon applications to make alterations to a non-landmark building in a historic district made the issuance of the notice to proceed an ultra vires act. The court held dismissal of the cooperative's petition was appropriate because the issuance of the notice to proceed was within the commission's jurisdiction, and the issuance of the notice was supported by facts in the record and was neither arbitrary, capricious, nor contrary to law.


OUTCOME: The court denied the cooperatives' application and dismissed their petition, which sought to vacate the order and decision of the commission regarding the school's gymnasium.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Preservation of Landmarks -- Alteration of Nonlandmark Building in Historic District

A proceeding to vacate an order and decision of the New York City Landmarks Preservation Commission (Commission) which permitted respondent, a nonprofit school, to construct a rooftop gymnasium on its nonlandmark buildings which were situated within an historic district is dismissed despite the fact that the Landmark Law (Administrative Code of New York, art 8-A) authorizes permits only for demolition of nonlandmark buildings and alteration of landmark buildings within an historic district. The omission of specific words authorizing alterations to nonlandmark buildings from section 207-8.0 (subd a, par [1]) of the Administrative Code was a simple oversight, and it was the legislative intent to give the Commission power to afford lesser relief to the owner of a nonlandmark building in such a district than the permission to demolish. Moreover, inasmuch as the Commission determined that respondent's charitable purpose of providing a well-rounded quality education was being undermined by the lack of a gymnasium and that the education of the students was being hindered [***2] by such lack, its decision to issue the notice to proceed with alterations to respondent had a rational basis and was neither arbitrary, capricious nor contrary to law; such determination meets the standard applicable to nonprofit institutions which is analogous to the insufficient return standard applicable to profit-making entities.

 

1714 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. De Jesus


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


122 Misc. 2d 190; 471 N.Y.S.2d 195; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4094



December 15, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action before the Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County, defendant asked the trial court to prevent the prosecution from proving the allegations of a special information in which the state alleged that defendant operated a vehicle while under the influence as a felony in violation of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 200.60.


OVERVIEW: Defendant faced felony charges based on a prior misdemeanor conviction and the enhancement provision under N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192(5). He attempted to challenge the special information by claiming that his original misdemeanor conviction was based on the prior trial court's wrongful acceptance of defendant's faulty plea, which appeared to be improperly allowed by the initial trial court. Nevertheless, the current trial court denied defendant's motion after recognizing that defendant's only possible way to attack the prior misdemeanor judgment against him was through a petition for writ of coram nobis, which necessarily had to be brought in the initial trial court, where defendant's misdemeanor conviction occurred.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion and allowed the felony proceedings to continue.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Operating Vehicle while under Influence -- Felony Charge -- Collateral Attack on Constitutionality of Predicate Misdemeanor Conviction

Defendant may not collaterally challenge the constitutionality of his prior misdemeanor conviction for driving while under the influence of alcohol or drugs, entered upon his guilty plea, at the time of the filing of a special information charging him with that same offense as a felony by reason of the prior misdemeanor conviction (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1192, subd 5; CPL 200.60) since the CPL contains no procedure similar to the predicate felony sentencing procedures by which such a collateral attack on the constitutionality of an earlier misdemeanor conviction may be made before the sentencing court to prevent the use of the earlier conviction in either enhancing the grade of the offense or the sentence; defendant may adequately assert his collateral constitutional attack on the predicate conviction by way of writ of error coram nobis on a posttrial motion to vacate the judgment of conviction ( CPL 440.10).

 

1715 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Gless v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


121 Misc. 2d 1030; 470 N.Y.S.2d 527; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4029



December 6, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff businesses brought an action for declaratory judgment that New York City, N.Y., Local Law 23 (1983), which prohibited the sale of drug paraphernalia, was unconstitutional because it was preempted by state law, it was void for vagueness, it infringed upon protected speech, and it violated their equal protection rights. Defendant city filed a motion for summary judgment, and the businesses filed a cross motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The city passed Local Law 23, and the businesses brought the action to declare it unconstitutional. The parties both filed motions for summary judgment. The court granted summary judgment to the city. The court found that the state had not declared an intent to occupy the entire area of drug paraphernalia regulation and therefore had not pre-empted local legislation, even when provisions from several titles were considered jointly. The court found the state's approach was fragmented and piecemeal. The criminal penalties of Local Law 23 were not inconsistent with the civil penalties of state law because inconsistency required more than mere difference. The court found the businesses only had standing to attack the multi-use items of Local Law 23, because they did not sell single-use items. The court found that Local Law 23 had a sufficient core meaning that defined under its terms what constituted drug paraphernalia to withstand the facial challenge and deter arbitrary enforcement. The businesses' remaining challenges were without merit.


OUTCOME: The court granted the city's motion for summary judgment in the businesses' constitutional challenge to the drug paraphernalia ordinance.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Sale of Drug Paraphernalia -- Permissible Local Legislation

An ordinance of the City of New York which makes criminal the sale of drug paraphernalia (Administrative Code of City of New York, § E22-1.0 et seq.) is not invalid on the ground that local drug paraphernalia legislation is pre-empted by the State drug paraphernalia law which merely imposes civil penalties for the sale of drug paraphernalia (General Business Law, art 39) since it cannot be inferred from the enactment of a State-wide statute with civil penalties in an attempt to curtail the operation of head shops that the Legislature intended to preclude criminal penalties at the local level nor does the State law constitute a "comprehensive and detailed regulatory scheme" in the area of drug paraphernalia so as to pre-empt local legislation; the existence of criminal rather than civil penalties does not render the ordinance inconsistent with State law since mere differences between State and local laws do not render them inconsistent; the ordinance is not unconstitutionally vague since it gives fair warning of the conduct proscribed and is sufficiently clear on its face for law enforcement purposes [***2] to deter arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement, plaintiffs having standing to challenge the facial validity of the ordinance as to multiuse items prior to enforcement since they do not sell single-use items that have no possible function other than the preparation or ingestion of drugs, nor does the ordinance unconstitutionally infringe upon protected speech, violate due process and equal protection, or constitute a bill of attainder.

 

1716 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Velasquez v. Perales


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


122 Misc. 2d 185; 470 N.Y.S.2d 83; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4093



November 30, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff public assistance recipient, individually and on behalf of others, filed an action against defendants, the commissioners of the state and city departments of social services, which challenged the commissioners' policy of denying all cash public assistance to employed applicants when the applicants' gross income exceeded 150 percent of the statutory standard of need as calculated by the commissioners.


OVERVIEW: The recipient filed a motion for an order pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 902, 6301, and 3211 to make the matter into a class action, to enjoin the commissioners from applying N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 131-a to deny public assistance to members of the recipient's class, and to grant summary judgment to the class. The commissioners filed a cross-motion for summary judgment and opposed class action treatment. The court held that § 131-a was unconstitutional to the extent that it denied public assistance to individuals solely on the basis of their gross income exceeding 150 percent of the established standard of need. The court concluded that it was irrational and ironic that if the recipient had not gone to work she would have received a full monthly welfare grant but because she had gone to work she could not even receive the necessary money to bring her available income up to the level of a welfare grant.


OUTCOME: The court granted the recipient's motion for summary judgment in all respects, granted the motion for a class action, and denied the commissioners' motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Social Services -- Public Assistance

Subdivision 10 of section 131-a of the Social Services Law and 18 NYCRR 352.18 are unconstitutional to the extent that they deny public assistance to individuals solely on the basis of their gross income exceeding 150% of the established standard of need; a person is eligible for assistance in an amount representing the difference between net or available earned income and the statutorily established standard of need. Gross income does not bear any usual or necessary correlation to available income.

 

1717 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Chambers v. Chambers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


122 Misc. 2d 671; 471 N.Y.S.2d 958; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4147



November 25, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Despite the lack of a removal of barriers statement by defendant, I find and decide that plaintiff is entitled to the judgment of divorce which she seeks.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife brought an uncontested cause of action for divorce against defendant husband pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 170(6) based upon the parties having lived apart for more than one year pursuant to a written separation agreement and substantial compliance with that agreement by the wife. The issue before the court was the application of the Get Statute, N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 253(4).


OVERVIEW: The Get Statute required that where the marriage was performed by a clergyman, the plaintiff had to make a statement of removal of barriers to the defendant's remarriage. The wife voluntarily submitted a removal of barriers statement, but the problem was that the husband was also required to submit such a statement by the Get Statute. The court granted the wife the divorce she requested despite the lack of removal of the barriers statement by the husband. The court held that the Get Statute, as it applied to the parties, was void as a violation of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1. The court found that there was no way for the wife to force the husband to make a removal of barriers statement and that the Get Statute's requirement that no final judgment was to be entered unless the husband made such a statement constituted a cutoff of judicial remedies which accrued under a contract at the time the contract was made.


OUTCOME: The court granted the wife a divorce from the husband despite the lack of removal of a barriers statement from the husband.


HEADNOTES

Husband and Wife -- Divorce -- Removal of Barriers to Remarriage

In an uncontested action for a divorce based upon a separation agreement made in New York and entered into subsequent to the Divorce Reform Law in 1966, but before the effective date of section 253 of the Domestic Relations Law (August 8, 1983), the requirement of subdivision 4 of section 253 that no final judgment shall be entered unless the defendant makes a statement that he or she has taken all steps solely within his or her power to remove all barriers to the plaintiff's remarriage violates article I (§ 10, cl 1) of the United States Constitution, which provides that no State shall pass any law impairing the obligation of contracts, and is void; under the law in existence at the time of the making of the separation agreement, each party had the right, after the passage of one year and upon substantial compliance, to obtain the judicial remedy of a divorce, and section 253 represents an impermissible modification of the agreement.

 

1718 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rhodes


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Jefferson County


121 Misc. 2d 701; 468 N.Y.S.2d 837; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3986



November 18, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment is therefore denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was indicted for violating the traffic law, sought dismissal of the indictment against him.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating the vehicle and traffic law. Defendant sought to dismiss the indictment against him on the ground that the statutes that he had been charged with violating were unconstitutional under both the federal and state constitutions. Defendant contended that the statutes were inapplicable to him because he suffered from the disease of alcoholism and that persons who suffered from alcoholism were not criminal and should not be treated as such. The court stated that the statutes did not legislate against persons who suffer from the disease of alcoholism or discriminate against persons suffering from the disease of alcoholism. The court concluded defendant failed to demonstrate that he had been discriminated against or that he had been deprived of due process either under the New York or United States Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment against him.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Driving while Intoxicated

A motion to dismiss an indictment charging the defendant with violating subdivisions 2 and 3 of section 1192 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law on the ground that the defendant suffers from alcoholism is denied; the statutes are malum prohibitum in nature and apply universally to all persons who have either .10 of 1% of alcohol in the blood or who drive while in an intoxicated condition, regardless of whether the person is an alcoholic. There is a relationship between the conduct prohibited by the statutes and the legitimate interests of the members of the community who should remain free from being injured or killed by an intoxicated motorist, whether or not the intoxicated motorist is an alcoholic; the defendant has not demonstrated that he has been discriminated against or that he has been deprived of due process.

 

1719 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Allen v. North Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


121 Misc. 2d 795; 469 N.Y.S.2d 528; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4004



November 18, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, judgment is granted in favor of plaintiffs declaring that subdivision B of section 70-89 of the code of the Town of North Hempstead is unconstitutional and void.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff senior citizens sought a declaration that a portion of a zoning ordinance, Town of North Hempstead, N.Y., Code of Ordinance art. X, § 70-89, was unconstitutional and invalid on the grounds that it mandated both an age and residency requirement.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance provided for a Golden Age Residence District permitting only multiple residence dwellings and accessory buildings designed for use by "senior citizens." Section 70-89 provided that "senior citizens" meant people of 62 years of age or more, and that senior citizens applying for residence in a Golden Age Residence District should have legally resided in the town for at least one year prior to the date of their application. The senior citizens challenged the constitutionality of § 70-89 on equal protection grounds. The court held that the residency requirement of § 70-89 was unconstitutional. The durational residency requirement was a "suspect" classification because it infringed upon the fundamental right of travel, and as such was subject to the strict scrutiny test under equal protection analysis. The burden was therefore upon the town to prove that the classification was justified by a compelling state interest. The durational residency requirement did not further the legitimate governmental objective of providing for the housing needs of the elderly. The residency restriction was arbitrary and discriminatory and served no legitimate or valid governmental purpose.


OUTCOME: The court granted judgment in favor of the senior citizens, declaring that the subdivision of the code of the town was unconstitutional and void.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Zoning Ordinance Conditioning Use on Age and Durational Residence

A provision of a town zoning ordinance which restricts use in one zoning district to individuals who are 62 years of age or more and who have resided in the town for at least one year prior to their application for residency in said district constitutes a denial of the equal protection guarantees of the United States and New York Constitutions; the durational residency requirement does not further the legitimate governmental concern of providing for the housing needs of the elderly, and such restriction is arbitrary and discriminatory; the need for housing is no greater among those senior citizens who have resided in the town for one year than it is among those who have resided in the town for a shorter period or are now nonresidents; moreover, the goal of preventing an influx of outsiders is constitutionally impermissible.

 

1720 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bright Horizon House, Inc. v. Zoning Bd. of Appeals


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


121 Misc. 2d 703; 469 N.Y.S.2d 851; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3987



November 16, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a proposed Christian Science care facility (facility), challenged a finding by respondent, the Zoning Board of Appeals of the Town of Henrietta, Monroe County (board) (New York), that the use proposed by the facility was not a permitted use under Henrietta, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance art. V, § 127-6A(6), and challenged the board's denial of the facility's application for a use variance.


OVERVIEW: The facility sought to operate a care facility within an area that was zoned for, among other things, churches and public hospitals, pursuant to local zoning ordinance § 127-6A(6). The board determined that the proposed use was not permitted and also denied the facility's application for a use variance. On appeal the court ruled that the board's determinations were not arbitrary or capricious or otherwise erroneous. The court ruled that the facility, which would have provided a place rest, relaxation, and contemplation and study of Christian Science tenets, all for the purpose of healing the physical maladies of fee-paying tenants, was not a church or a public hospital within the meaning of the zoning law. Moreover, the court ruled, the fact that religious tenets would be used for the purpose of healing did not render the facility a religious entity entitled to First Amendment protections for religious institutions. The use variance was properly denied, the court ruled, because the town officials' promise that a permit would issue did not constitute a right to such a permit and there was no evidence that the property would not yield a reasonable return if used for a permitted use.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the board's determinations. The board properly concluded that the facility's proposed use of its premises was not a hospital or public church within the meaning of the applicable zoning law and the alleged religion-oriented activities of the facility was insufficient to invoke First Amendment protections. The use variance was properly denied because there was no evidence that that property would not yield a reasonable return.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Public Hospital or Church Use

1. A proposed facility which is residential in nature, providing room and board to fee-paying individuals, does not contain a common area or sanctuary set aside for worship, is staffed by accredited Christian Science nurses and practitioners, and which is intended as a place for rest, relaxation and contemplation where the residents, all Christian Scientists, would study the tenets of their religion for the purpose of healing does not qualify as a church or a public hospital or conventional health care facility, so as to constitute a permitted use under a town zoning ordinance; the facility's purpose is not public worship, an activity typically associated with a church, and it does not take on a religious use simply because its residents pray and contemplate as part of the process of healing; moreover, the facility would not perform conventional medical treatment or surgery and, thus, is not a public hospital with all the manifold purposes and services such an institution provides. Accordingly, the finding of the town zoning board of appeals that petitioner's proposed use was not permitted under the zoning ordinance [***2] was not improper.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Religious Use Immunity

2. Petitioner's proposed Christian Science accredited care facility intended for the curing of physical ailments through religious worship, study, contemplation and prayer does not qualify as a religious use so as to invoke First Amendment protection and immunity from a town zoning ordinance which prohibits the use intended by petitioner; the fact that religious study and prayer may be employed to achieve the purpose of healing does not thereby convert that intended result into a religious one, even though the means used conform to the beliefs and discipline of the church. Moreover, petitioner's CPLR article 78 proceeding is defective since the essence of petitioner's attack is the constitutionality of the ordinance itself rather than its application, and the court cannot convert the proceeding to the proper action for declaratory judgment due to petitioner's failure to make the town a party.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Variance for Unnecessary Hardship

3. Petitioner's application for a variance to permit the use of land for a Christian Science accredited care facility asserting [***3] that strict application of a town zoning ordinance which prohibits such use would result in hardship because the land was acquired for the purpose of constructing such a facility and that in reliance upon the representation of town officials that the facility would be permitted, petitioner invested substantial sums to acquire the property and obtain architectural and site plans, was properly denied. A landowner is not entitled to a nonconforming use simply because issuing authorities promised to issue a permit for such use; moreover, there has been no showing that the land cannot yield a reasonable return if used for any purpose permitted in the zone; it is not uniqueness of the plight of the owner, but uniqueness of the land causing the plight, which is the criterion for granting a variance due to unnecessary hardship.

 

1721 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Dana Marie E.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


123 Misc. 2d 112; 473 N.Y.S.2d 1008; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4167



November 16, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner foster care agency brought a motion for the permanent termination of parental rights of the natural parents of two children. Before the court was the agency's motion to challenge venue of the trial in New York County.


OVERVIEW: The agency sought to permanently terminate the parental rights of the natural parents of two children. The action was commenced in Queens County. A new administrative directive of the Family Court required that trials of all contested termination of parental rights cases be referred to a new city-wide trial part in New York County. The agency sought to keep the trial in Queens County. The court denied the agency's challenge and determined that venue in New York County was proper. The agency's contention that Queens County was the only county where the matter could have originated was without merit. Pursuant to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b(3)(c), the matter could have properly been originated in the Family Court of New York County by the Commissioner of Social Services (Commissioner) because the Commissioner was the true legal custodian of the children and the Commissioner's office was in New York County. The agency's reliance on § 174 of the Family Court Act to keep the case in Queens County was misplaced. Section 174 was inapplicable because the new administrative directive did not create a transfer and because § 174 conflicted with N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b(1)(b).


OUTCOME: The court denied the agency's motion to challenge venue of the trial for termination of parental rights in New York County.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights -- Transmittal for Trial to City-Wide Trial Part

A proceeding for permanent termination of parental rights brought by an authorized foster care agency, commenced by the filing of the petition with the Queens County Family Court, may properly be transmitted for trial to the city-wide trial part in New York County pursuant to an administrative directive of the Family Court requiring trials of all contested termination of parental rights cases to be so referred; section 384-b (subd 3, par [c]) of the Social Services Law mandates only that a termination of parental rights proceeding be commenced in the county where the authorized agency has its place of business or where the child or his parent resides, and the requirement directing where the proceeding shall be "originated" does not include any mandate that subsequent proceedings be conducted in that county; the venue provisions of section 174 of the Family Court Act are not controlling insofar as they conflict with the specific venue provisions of section 384-b, since such a conflict is prohibited by section 384-b (subd 3, par [f]). A referral for trial to New York County [***2] does not constitute a "transfer" under section 174, but rather, only a transmittal for a limited purpose, after which the proceeding is returned to the county of origination for further disposition; article VI (§ 19, subd h) of the New York Constitution does not prohibit an intra-court transfer such as a trial referral, and any inconvenience to any party caused by the transmittal for trial is outweighed by the public policy of expediting the resolution of the status of the child who is the subject of the petition. Finally, there is no discrimination against Queens County attorneys caused by the directive.

 

1722 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Salamino


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Queens County


121 Misc. 2d 1046; 469 N.Y.S.2d 524; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4030



November 14, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, defendant's motion to suppress is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to suppress evidence obtained as a result of a search of his premises and to find New York City, N.Y., Charter ch. 18, § 436 unconstitutional, in a prosecution for criminal possession of stolen property in the first degree and illegal possession of a vehicle identification number (VIN) plate in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §§ 165.50, 170.70.


OVERVIEW: Police officers found a vehicle from which the VIN plate had been removed during a warrantless search of premises leased by defendant to an auto repair shop. The State contended that the search was made with defendant's consent and that a warrantless search of the premises was authorized by New York City, N.Y., Charter ch. 18, § 436. Defendant argued that the search was warrantless and nonconsensual and thus violative of his constitutional rights and that § 436 was unconstitutional as drawn and in the manner applied. The court granted the motion. The court concluded that the State had not carried the "heavy" burden of proving the voluntariness of defendant's consent. The court was of the opinion that auto repair shops did not fall within the categories enumerated in § 436. The court stated that because the authority upon which the State relied for the warrantless search had been held inapplicable to the case, the State had failed to carry its burden.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence found in a warrantless search in the prosecution of defendant for criminal possession of stolen property and illegal possession of a VIN plate.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Administrative Inspection of Auto Repair Shop

A vehicle identification number plate seized from defendant's auto repair shop by New York City police officers during a warrantless administrative inspection must be suppressed as the product of an unlawful nonprobable cause search since the People have failed in their "heavy" burden of proving that defendant voluntarily consented to the search nor was the warrantless search of defendant's premises a valid administrative inspection under section 436 of the New York City Charter, which authorizes the police to examine the "books, business premises, and any articles or merchandise" on the premises of "junkshop keepers" and other named businesses, since auto repair shops do not fall within the categories enumerated in the statute and are instead regulated by the State Department of Motor Vehicles pursuant to the Motor Vehicle Repair Shop Registration Act (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 398 et seq.) so that any local legislation granting police officers the right to inspect the premises and records of auto repair shops would be pre-empted [***2] by the State law.

 

1723 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Megalopolis Property Assoc. v. Buvron


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Queens County


121 Misc. 2d 662; 468 N.Y.S.2d 819; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3981



November 9, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Thus, this court finds that the lease provision prohibiting the respondent from keeping his dog shall be deemed waived and the petition is hereby dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord brought a holdover summary proceeding against respondent tenant, residing in a privately owned multiple dwelling, for violating a substantial obligation of his tenancy by harboring a dog.


OVERVIEW: The lease contained unequivocal covenants prohibiting the harboring of pets. The lease contained a "no waiver" clause if the landlord accepted rent with knowledge of breach of a covenant. The tenant kept a small dog open and notoriously for six years with the landlord's knowledge. New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § D26-10.10(b)(1983) stated that if a tenant open and notoriously for three months or more harbored a pet with the premises owner's knowledge and the premises owner did not seek to enforce the prohibition, the lease provision was waived. But the law only applied to "existing and future leases and renewals." The court dismissed the petition and held that because "existing" leases meant leases entered into prior to the regulation's passage, the law applied retroactively. Application of the law to current leases did not unconstitutionally impair the obligation of contracts and deprive the landlord of property without due process of law. Where public health or safety was jeopardized, the legislature's police powers allowed it to apply laws retroactively.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the landlord's petition in a holdover summary proceeding against the tenant. The court ruled that the lease provision was waived.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Harboring Animals in Leased Premises

A holdover summary proceeding brought against a tenant for violating a substantial obligation of his tenancy by harboring a dog is dismissed, despite the fact that the lease contained a "no waiver" clause should the landlord accept rent with knowledge of a breach of any covenant, where the dog has been harbored openly and notoriously with the knowledge of the owner or its agent for six years; section D26-10.10 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, which was signed into law in October, 1983 and provides that where a tenant openly and notoriously harbors a household pet for three months and an owner or his agent with knowledge of this fact fails within the three-month period to commence a summary proceeding or action to enforce a lease provision prohibiting the keeping of such household pets, such lease provision shall be deemed waived, also provides that it shall take effect immediately and shall apply to existing and future leases and renewals.  [***2] The law applies retroactively to protect respondent tenant, since "existing" leases are leases entered into prior to the passage of the law; contentions that the statute impairs and abridges the obligations and rights of contracts and deprives the landlord of the property without due process of law have no merit, and where the public's health, safety and welfare have been affected by emergencies, Legislatures, under their police powers and home rule laws, have properly applied certain laws retroactively.

 

1724 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rose Towers Realty v. Aviv


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Queens County


121 Misc. 2d 1016; 471 N.Y.S.2d 445; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4028



November 9, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord instituted holdover proceedings, seeking possession of respondent tenants' apartments for breaching a provision of the lease prohibiting the harboring of pets.


OVERVIEW: A local law was enacted which prohibited a landlord from attempting to evict a tenant for violating a no pet clause in the lease when the landlord had knowledge that the tenant harbored a pet for three months but took no action to enforce the no pet clause. Relying on the law, the tenants claimed that the landlord waived its right to commence the proceedings against them because they had their dogs more than three months and the landlord took no prior action. The court rejected the landlord's argument that the local law was unconstitutional because its retroactive application violated the sanctions and restrictions of the Urstadt Law, 1971 N.Y. Laws 372, as well as due process and the impairment of obligation of contracts. First, it was clear that the local law was intended to apply retroactively. Second, the retroactive application of the local law did not violate the Urstadt Law either in spirit or in letter. Indeed, the only enactments invalidated by the Urstadt restrictions involved those restricting rents. Third, the constitutional provision against the impairment of contracts was not absolute, if the disputed legislation was for the general good of the public.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that there had been an effective waiver of the landlord's no pet covenants and dismissed the petitions with prejudice.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and Tenant -- Harboring Animals in Leased Premises -- "Waiver of No Pet Covenant Law" -- Retroactive Application to Existing Leases

The "waiver of no pet covenant law" (Administrative Code of City of New York, § D26-10.10), enacted by the New York City Council, effective October 26, 1983, which provides that a no pet covenant in all "existing and future leases and renewals" is waived where a tenant "openly and notoriously for a period of three months or more following taking possession of a unit, harbors or has harbored a household pet * * * and [the] owner fails within this * * * period to commence a summary proceeding or action to enforce [the] lease provision", is a constitutional and valid exercise of the city's emergency and police powers; the retroactive application of the "no pet law" does not violate the sanctions and restriction of the Urstadt Law (L 1971, ch 372), which prohibits the city from enacting "more stringent or restrictive provisions of regulation and control than those presently in effect", since the Urstadt provisions are intended to prevent more stringent economic and rent-control restrictions from being imposed on owners and the "no pet [***2] law" imposes no new economic restrictions on landlords but merely codifies current case law which has been applying the equitable theory of laches in situations where owners have acquiesced to the breach of a no pet clause for extended periods of time; there is no unconstitutional impairment of private contracts since the "no pet law" is a legitimate exercise of the police power, enacted in response to a continuing housing emergency and flagrant abuses by landlords enforcing no pet clauses for ulterior motives, and a no pet clause is not such a "substantial property right" as would outweigh the need to stop the documented abuses upon tenants.

 

1725 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Michael B. v. Sendi Diann W.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


121 Misc. 2d 475; 467 N.Y.S.2d 1009; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3943



October 24, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner putative father filed an action against respondent mother seeking to establish that he was the father of the subject infant and filed a motion to strike uncorroborated testimony of the mother.


OVERVIEW: The putative father filed an action against the mother seeking to establish that he was the father of the subject child. The mother denied his paternity and claimed that she had liaisons with other men during the crucial time period. The putative father filed a motion to strike her uncorroborated testimony pursuant to N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 531. On review, the court held that under newly amended general neutral terms, the exclusionary provision of § 531 established a requirement of corroboration for all testimony which might have tended to prove sexual liaison and/or access to the mother during the crucial time prior to conception by any person other than the accused putative father. The court determined that the mother's testimony of access by others during the crucial time in issue was stricken.


OUTCOME: The court granted the putative father's motion to strike the mother's testimony.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children Born out of Wedlock -- Paternity Proceeding -- Corroboration of Testimony

In a paternity proceeding in which a male petitioner seeks to establish that he is the father of the subject infant and the mother of the child denies his allegation of paternity, the testimony of the mother which might tend to prove sexual liaison and/or access to herself by any person other than the putative father during the crucial period of time prior to conception is admissible only where it is independently corroborated pursuant to section 531 of the Family Court Act since the amendment of section 531, undertaken by the Legislature as part of its redefinition of the rights conferred under article 5 of the Family Court Act into gender-neutral terms (L 1976, ch 665), changed the term "respondent" to "alleged father" when speaking as to whom is competent to testify at a paternity hearing while leaving undisturbed the word "respondent" in the text of the corroboration requirement contained therein, constituting a legislative recognition that, with gender neutrality now a fact, the broad category of respondents is no longer limited to putative fathers and the defense of access by others is [***2] available to both sides.

 

1726 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Landmark Colony at Oyster Bay v. Bd. of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


121 Misc. 2d 23; 467 N.Y.S.2d 170; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3864



September 28, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motions upon which the court reserved decision at trial are disposed of as herein indicated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff builder brought a declaratory judgment action against defendant county seeking a determination that a county ordinance that imposed fines for selling real estate before required documents were approved and filed with local officials was unconstitutional, illegal, and void.


OVERVIEW: Nassau County, N.Y., Ordinance 229-80(2) provided for a $ 500 fine for each unit of real property sold or advertised for sale prior to approval and recording of the subdivision plat. N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 334-a(13) provided for a $ 100 fine for the premature sale of lots. After being assessed a $ 15,000 fine by the county, the builder sought a declaratory judgment that the county law was unconstitutional. The court held that the county ordinance was void and unenforceable because the state law specifically and entirely addressed the matter of a builder selling or conveying plots or units before or without filing the plat. Furthermore, the county fine of $ 500 exceeded its authority when the state only imposed a $ 100 fine. The county had no authority to issue a fine except by a civil action, which was not a requirement of the ordinance. However, the ordinance was not beyond a reasonable doubt unconstitutionally vague as applied to the builder. The county was also estopped from enforcing the ordinance because it was responsible for many of the delays the builder experienced in getting the required approvals.


OUTCOME: The court held that the county ordinance imposing fines for the sale of real estate before proper planning documents were approved and filed was void and unenforceable.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Planning -- Penalty for Not Filing Subdivision Map

An ordinance of Nassau County imposing upon a subdivision developer a penalty of $ 500 for each unit "transferred, sold, agreed to be sold, negotiated, offered or advertised for sale" prior to the final approval of the subdivision plat by the county planning commission is invalid since the subject matter of the ordinance has been pre-empted by State legislation applicable to Nassau County which imposes no more than a $ 100 ($ 500, eff Jan. 1, 1984) penalty per plot or unit on a developer selling or conveying plots or units before or without due filing of the requisite map (Real Property Law, § 334-a, subd 13) and the county also lacked authority to collect the $ 15,000 fine imposed on plaintiff by administrative action rather then by a civil action; in addition, the county, by its conduct in delaying the processing of plaintiff's application, is estopped from imposing an unconscionably harsh penalty in summary fashion as a condition precedent to approval of plaintiff's map; plaintiff's declaratory [***2] judgment action to recover the penalty which it paid under protest is not time barred since an article 78 proceeding, for which a specific limitation period is statutorily provided, would not have been an appropriate one to test the county ordinance; plaintiff is not entitled to attorney's fees since such fees are not generally considered proper items of expense recoverable as general or special damages in the absence of statutory or contractual authorization.

 

1727 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brabham


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


121 Misc. 2d 897; 470 N.Y.S.2d 275; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4024



September 21, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree. Several years prior, defendant pleaded guilty to three prior felonies, one of which was used to form a predicate for his adjudication as a persistent violent felony offender. Defendant challenged his previous conviction as a predicate on grounds that his counsel was ineffective at the time he pleaded to the prior charge.


OVERVIEW: After his third conviction, defendant's first and second convictions were used to enhance his sentence for that crime, after defendant admitted to being a second felony offender and waived a hearing. By his admission, defendant waived any claim that the prior convictions were unconstitutionally obtained, unless he could show good cause for his failure to assert the claim. The State contended that pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.21(8), defendant was estopped by judgment from challenging the prior conviction. However, because both prior convictions were listed in enhancing his previous sentence and only one was required, it was unclear whether a challenge to the current predicate was also waived. On that matter, the court ruled that because the earlier court was free to rely on both convictions and neither was challenged, that was sufficient to conclude that both prior convictions were relied upon. The court concluded that if the issue of estoppel was one of fact, then the State met their burden of showing waiver. The court further concluded that defendant had not shown any good cause for not challenging the earlier conviction now used as a predicate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Sentence -- Second Felony Offender

1. A defendant whose 1970 conviction for manslaughter in the first degree was unconstitutionally obtained in that he was not advised before pleading guilty of his right to remain silent and to confront and cross-examine witnesses (see Boykin v Alabama, 395 U.S. 238) was not ineffectively represented by counsel in 1975, when, after pleading guilty to robbery in the third degree, he was sentenced as a second felony offender based upon the 1970 conviction; under the law as it stood in 1975, the failure of the court which accepted the 1970 plea to inform defendant of his "Boykin rights" did not vitiate the plea as a predicate for second felony offender status because it was sufficient for the court to determine that the plea was voluntarily and knowingly taken without specific mention of the Boykin rights.

Crimes -- Sentence -- Persistent Felony Offender

2. A 1975 adjudication of defendant as a second felony offender is held to be based upon both the 1970 and 1972 predicate felonies cited by the People where sentencing minutes are unavailable and it is not possible to reconstruct the sentencing proceeding; section [***2] 70.06 of the Penal Law, as it read in 1975, defined second felony offender as a person convicted of a felony after having previously been subjected to one or more predicate felony convictions, and since defendant did not challenge, in 1975, the People's predicate felony statement citing both prior convictions, it must be presumed that the sentencing Judge relied upon both prior convictions in adjudicating defendant a second felony offender. Either of the two prior convictions was sufficient to sustain the second felony offender adjudication, and the People have thus met their burden of proving that the defendant is estopped (see CPL 400.21, subd 8) to challenge the constitutionality of the 1970 conviction and he may be sentenced following a 1983 plea of guilty to criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree, as a persistent violent felony offender. The fact that there may have been good cause for failure to challenge the constitutionality of the 1970 conviction in the 1975 proceeding does not permit the defendant to challenge the constitutionality of the same conviction as a predicate for persistent violent felony adjudication; the provision of CPL 400.21 (subd 7, par [b])  [***3] that failure to challenge the previous conviction constitutes a waiver of any allegation of unconstitutionality unless good cause is shown for such failure is independent of CPL 400.21 (subd 8), which provides that a finding that a defendant is a second felony offender shall be binding upon the defendant in any future proceeding in which the issue arises.

Crimes -- Sentence -- Persistent Felony Offender

3. A defendant may be sentenced as a persistent violent felony offender for an offense committed after adoption of the violent felony offender statutes, where his predicate violent felony convictions were not defined as such by the law applicable when he committed or was sentenced for them; there is no objection to reliance on those convictions based on the ex post facto clause of the United States Constitution, nor does section 70.04 of the Penal Law preclude their use as predicate violent felony convictions.

 

1728 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Lazy Acres Park, Inc. v. Cape Vincent


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Jefferson County


122 Misc. 2d 215; 470 N.Y.S.2d 70; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4099



August 31, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Since the petitioner has failed to sustain its burden of proof and since the original assessments do not exceed the value of the property as determined by the referee, the report of the referee should be confirmed as modified herein and judgment in favor of respondents should be entered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent tax assessors filed a motion for an order pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. § 4403 confirming the report of a referee in a tax assessment proceeding. Petitioner taxpayer objected to the tax assessment on his property for a four-year period.


OVERVIEW: The subject property was operated as a seasonal mobile home park and was improved by 75 trailer sites with underground water, sewer and electrical connections. The taxpayer contended that the report of the referee had to be rejected for two reasons. He argued that because the assessors' appraiser failed to testify to the market value of the first year and the fourth year, his appraisal had to be accepted for those tow years. Next he argued that the referee incorrectly utilized N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 102(12)(g) and, in so doing, contravened N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 2. Upon review, the court held that the assessors' failure to present appraisals for the two years was not sufficient to justify accepting the taxpayer's appraisal. In addition, the taxpayer failed to make out a prima facie case by showing that § 102(12)(g) was unconstitutional as applied in his case. Finally, the report had to be modified for the last two years because it was inconsistent with the proof since there was testimony that there were fewer trailers on the premises for such years.


OUTCOME: The court modified the referee's report, confirmed it as modified, and entered a judgment in favor of the assessors.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Assessment -- Inclusion of Value of Mobile Home in Assessment of Land on which it is Located

The report of the referee in a tax assessment proceeding fixing the value of petitioner's property, operated as a seasonal mobile home park from May 1 to October 1 and improved by 75 trailer sites with underground water, sewer and electrical connections, for the years 1979 to 1982 on the basis of the market value of the trailers located on the premises on May 1, 1980 and May 1, 1981 must be confirmed since the fact that respondents' appraiser failed to testify as to market value for 1979 and 1982 does not require acceptance of petitioner's far lower appraisal for those two years inasmuch as petitioner still has the burden of rebutting the presumption of validity which attaches to all assessments by proving by substantial evidence that the assessments are excessive and petitioner has [***2] failed to show that the statute including the market value of the trailers in the assessment of premises (Real Property Tax Law, § 102, subd 12, par [g]) is unconstitutional as applied to its assessment.

 

1729 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dempsey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Richmond County


120 Misc. 2d 1035; 466 N.Y.S.2d 923; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3845



August 30, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendants' motions are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated action, defendants filed motions to dismiss summons charging them with transporting flammable liquids within city limits during restricted hours in violation of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code §§ C19-57.0(b), C19-152.0(b), C19-53.0(c)(5), and New York City, N.Y., Charter § 489.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with transporting flammable liquids within city limits during restricted hours. In a consolidated action, defendant contested their convictions. On appeal, defendants contended that the statutes defining the offenses were unconstitutional based on the Commerce Clause of U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. The court found that, while the curfew restrictions undoubtedly caused some additional expense and delay for those attempting to transport flammable liquids during the restricted hours, clearly those carriers making final delivery of hazardous cargo to points within the city posed a greater safety threat than those merely passing through. The court determined that any burden imposed on interstate commerce was minimal and denied defendants' motions.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motions to dismiss the summons against them.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Transportation of Flammable Liquids

The sections of the Administrative Code of the City of New York (§ C19-57.0, subd b; § C19-152.0, subd b; § C19-53.0, subd c, par 5) and the New York City Charter (§ 489) which prohibit transportation of flammable liquid shipments passing through New York City without a pickup or final delivery point within city limits during morning and afternoon rush hour periods do not violate the commerce clause of the United States Constitution (art I, § 8, cl 3); although carriers delivering hazardous cargo to points within the city are exempt from the curfew regulations, such carriers must use vehicles constructed and equipped in accordance with stringent fire department standards, whereas those transporting flammable liquids through the city need not comply with any of such detailed local safety regulations. Although the curfew regulations might place some burden on interstate commerce, the regulatory scheme is reasonable and designed to promote public safety, which is a legitimate local concern; there is no attempt to discriminate against nonlocal carriers in order to protect the economic interests [***2] of local carriers, and the inconveniences which result from the curfew are not unconstitutionally disproportionate when balanced against the interest in avoiding a potential catastrophe in a densely populated urban environment.

 

1730 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. De Stefano


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Suffolk County


121 Misc. 2d 113; 467 N.Y.S.2d 506; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3881



August 25, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant husband was charged with one count of rape in the first degree for having unwanted sexual intercourse with his separated wife. Defendant moved to dismiss on constitutional grounds.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that he was legally with the victim and there was no formal separation agreement. The only restraint was a protective order by the court, which defendant claimed did not give him notice that the sexual intercourse that he had with the victim at knifepoint was an offense. He contended that for not requiring the notice, the statute under which he was charged violated the due process and equal protection clauses of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court determined that defendant was not entitled to the spousal exemption to rape because he failed to provide any justification for excluding an estranged wife from gender-based statute that rendered her the protection. It held that defendant had actual notice of the protective order and that he was properly charged.


OUTCOME: Defendant was convicted of rape for having an unconsented sexual intercourse with his estranged wife.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Rape -- Immunity of Husband

To the extent that subdivision 4 of section 130.00 of the Penal Law grants a husband immunity from prosecution for the rape of his lawful wife, it is violative of the equal protection clauses of the State and Federal Constitutions; a wife has a right to the protection the law provides for nonspouses, and has been denied equal protection of law by the existence of immunity; no governmental interest can be served by its continued existence and it bears no reasonable relation to the class it should protect; accordingly, defendant husband who, in violation of an order of protection which mandated that defendant remain away from his estranged wife's premises and person and refrain from any acts or threats of physical violence, engaged in sexual intercourse with his wife without her consent, may be prosecuted for rape; said order was sufficient to apprise defendant that he could be charged with rape if he thereafter engaged in unconsented to sexual intercourse with his wife; moreover, a husband possesses no right to engage in such unconsented to sexual intercourse with his wife regardless of the existence of an order of protection.

 

1731 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Coleman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Nassau County


120 Misc. 2d 691; 466 N.Y.S.2d 620; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3784



August 19, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, this court grants the motion of the defendant and dismisses this action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was arrested and charged with a violation of N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 3397(4). Defendant filed a motion under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 170.40 to dismiss the action.


OVERVIEW: Defendant saw a psychiatrist for her pre-existing psychotic condition. He prescribed the controlled substance valium as part of the medication for her treatment. On the same day, defendant saw another doctor for back pains. He also prescribed valium as part of the medication for her back pain. Defendant did not mention to either physician the medication prescribed by the other. After examining § 3397(4), the court found that it clearly contained ambiguities in putting the onus on the patient to know what the practitioner was prescribing, that it had been declared a controlled substance, and to inform that practitioner that another controlled substance was supplied by some other doctor. However, the court also held that it should not decide the constitutionality of the statute because its decision could rest on alternative grounds. In determining that dismissal was proper the court considered, in part, (1) the seriousness and circumstances of the offense; (2) the extent of harm caused by the offense; (3) the evidence of guilty; (4) the history, character, and condition of defendant; and (5) the impact of a dismissal.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion and dismissed the action.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Controlled Substances -- Dismissal in Interest of Justice

A criminal action based upon defendant's alleged violation of subdivision 4 of section 3397 of the Public Health Law, which pertains to a patient's receipt of controlled substances from two doctors while not informing one about a prescription written by the other, should be dismissed in the interest of justice where defendant received two prescriptions on the same day for the same controlled substance from two doctors, each of whom was treating defendant for a different ailment, since the People have failed to make out a prima facie case under the statute, which requires a patient to see one doctor for an ailment, receive a controlled substance, and then see another doctor for the same ailment and receive a controlled substance; therefore, since the circumstances surrounding defendant's obtaining a controlled substance outweigh the seriousness of the crime charged and the imposition of a sentence for defendant's "offense" would be a travesty, the criminal action shall be dismissed.

 

1732 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Valero


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Trial Term, Suffolk County


120 Misc. 2d 539; 466 N.Y.S.2d 600; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3756



August 17, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the request to dismiss the indictment upon the grounds that section 135.25 (subd 2, pars [a], [b]) of the Penal Law denies defendant due process of law and is violative of the Federal and State Constitutions is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss an indictment charging him with the commission of two counts of kidnapping in the first degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §§ 135.25 (2)(a) and (b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant, aided by a codefendant, encountered a victim outside of a store and falsely offered her money to watch their child. Once defendant deceptively lured the victim into his car, he drove her to an apartment where the victim was confined for over 12 hours. Defendant physically violated and abused the victim sexually and intended to accomplish and advance the commission of promoting prostitution in the third degree. Defendant was convicted of kidnapping in the first degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 135.25(2)(a) and (b). Defendant claimed that the indictment should be dismissed. The court found that the evidence presented to the grand jury was legally sufficient to establish that defendant abducted or restrained the victim. Further, the court found that the victim's testimony did not need to be corroborated because she was not an accomplice within the meaning of N.Y. Penal Law § 230.35.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Kidnapping -- Restraining Victim

1.
