Evidence presented to a Grand Jury was legally sufficient, if accepted as true, to establish every element of kidnapping in the first degree (Penal Law, § 135.25, subd 2, pars [a], [b]) where the victim was allegedly tricked into getting into defendant's car, and transported and confined under threats for a one-week period by defendants who physically violated and sexually abused her and intended to commit a felony, i.e., promoting prostitution in the third degree, since her initial confinement was accomplished by means of a cleverly concocted deception which overcame her ability to consent, and her freedom to move was intentionally restricted by threats and violence.

Crimes -- Kidnapping -- Jurisdiction

2. Geographical jurisdiction of a county to prosecute for criminal activity is a question of fact which need not be established beyond a reasonable doubt, and CPL 20.40 (subd [***2] 1, par [a]) confers jurisdiction upon a county when conduct occurred within such county sufficient to establish an element of the crime charged; accordingly, where the evidence adduced before the Grand Jury permits a fair and reasonable inference that a kidnapping victim was restrained and abducted in Suffolk County and then transported by defendant's vehicle to Queens County and held for one week, jurisdiction properly lies in Suffolk County, and therefore, the private vehicle trip statute ( CPL 20.40, subd 4, par [g]), which confers jurisdiction where strict adherence to the common law would create insurmountable problems to prosecuting crimes whose sites could not be clearly identified, is inapplicable.

Crimes -- Corroboration of Accomplice Testimony -- Kidnapping

3. The testimony of an alleged kidnap victim need not be corroborated as a matter of law since she was not an "accomplice" within the meaning of section 230.35 of the Penal Law, despite defendants' arguments that their intentions to use her as a prostitute made her an accomplice, where there was not one scintilla of evidence which would warrant the assumption that she was a prostitute or that defendants [***3] were her pimps; a victim of a kidnapping was never intended by the Legislature, implicitly or otherwise, to be deemed an accomplice within the meaning of the law.

Crimes -- Kidnapping -- Period of Restraint

4. The more severe penalties for kidnapping in the first degree where the victim was abducted and held for more than 12 hours is not violative of due process since there is a rational basis for increasing the penalty when the statute requires not only that the victim be held for more than 12 hours, but that the prolonged abduction was for a specific and intentional criminal purpose.

 

1733 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rivera v. Laporte


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


120 Misc. 2d 733; 466 N.Y.S.2d 606; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3789



August 17, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion for a default judgment against Colon is accordingly denied without prejudice to renewal on proper notice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff bus dispatcher filed a motion for a default judgment in the action against defendants, city and correction officers, for assault and battery, false imprisonment, and malicious prosecution arising from a traffic dispute.


OVERVIEW: The bus dispatcher sought a default judgment following service of the complaint. The city and one correction officer made an appearance, but one correction officer failed to appear. The bus dispatcher then sought a default judgment against the party that failed to appear. The bus dispatcher claimed that the time for that party to answer had expired, so a default judgment was warranted under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3215(a). The court denied the motion. No notice of the default motion was served on the correction officer who failed to appear or on the city. The court held that a motion for a default judgment must be given to a codefendant who has appeared and could be adversely affected by the judgment, even if no default judgment was being sought against that codefendant. The city was entitled to the constitutional protection of the Due Process Clause. Without all revenant facts before it, the court could not state with certainty that the city would not ultimately be required to indemnify the correction officer who failed to appear.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion for a default judgment as to one correction officer.


HEADNOTES

Judgments -- Default Judgment -- Notice to Defaulting Party

1. In a tort action, a motion by plaintiff for a default judgment against one codefendant on the grounds that his time to appear has expired and he has not answered or moved with respect to the complaint need not be prefaced by notice to said codefendant; notice need not be given to a party in default for less than one year, who has not appeared, even if he is the party against whom entry of the default judgment is sought; said codefendant was apparently validly served with process and chose to default and, thus, neither CPLR 3215 nor due process standards require any further warning to him.

Judgments -- Default Judgment -- Notice to Codefendant Who Has Appeared

2. In a tort action against two individual codefendants and the City of New York in its capacity as the employer of said codefendants and as a tort-feasor itself, the city is entitled to notice of a motion for default judgment against one of the individual codefendants; CPLR 3215 (subd [f], par 1) requires that notice of a [***2] motion for default judgment be given to all defendants who have appeared, whether or not subsequent action or inaction has placed them in default, and whether or not a default judgment is being sought against them; moreover, the possibility exists that the city, as the codefendant's employer, may be held liable to indemnify him, and a judgment against him could have consequences which would effectively deprive it of a full and fair opportunity to defend on the merits; accordingly, fundamental fairness and due process require that the city be served with notice of the motion; where a municipality is a party to litigation not involving the State, due process operates to guarantee the municipality the same protection as any other similarly situated litigant.

 

1734 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Farrar


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


120 Misc. 2d 464; 466 N.Y.S.2d 221; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3741



August 15, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss on constitutional and statutory grounds is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the state's action charging him with violating New York's fraudulent accosting statute, N.Y. Penal Code § 165.30. Defendant asserted that the statute was void for vagueness in that the terms "trick" and "swindle" were so vague and indefinite that the statute was constitutionally defective.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with fraudulent accosting in that with intent to defraud, by means of trick or swindle, he offered to sell to passersby "14 karat" gold chains, when in fact, the chains were not 14 karat gold. Defendant sought to dismiss the charges, claiming that what he was doing did not constitute a confidence game, and that the statute was void for vagueness. The court denied the motion, finding that while the mere representation by defendant that the chains were gold did not make the scheme a confidence game, defendant's actions fell within the purview of the statute because it specifically prohibited people from accosting others in public places with intent to defraud by trick, swindle, or confidence game. The court also found that although the words trick and swindle had broad meanings, they were sufficiently definite and gave fair notice of what was prohibited under the statute. The court reasoned that a statute would not be struck down as vague when the language had a generally understood meaning, and found that an ordinary person could understand the words trick and swindle within the context of the statute and that defendant's alleged conduct violated the statute.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the state's charges of violations of New York's fraudulent accosting statute.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Fraudulent Accosting -- "Trick" or "Swindle"

Defendant's conduct in allegedly offering to sell to passersby 14 karat gold chains which were not in fact gold falls within the ambit of the fraudulent accosting statute ( Penal Law, § 165.30) which proscribes accosting a "person in a public place with intent to defraud him of money or other property by means of a trick, swindle or confidence game" since, although defendant's actions do not constitute a confidence game, the statute is not solely aimed at confidence games inasmuch as the words "trick" and "swindle" are separate from the term "confidence game"; the words "trick" and "swindle" are not so vague and indefinite as to violate constitutional standards since, although the words have broad meanings and are not defined in the Penal Law, they must be interpreted in tandem with the rest of the fraudulent accosting statute and in this context they are sufficiently definite and give fair notice of what is prohibited.

 

1735 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Consolidated Edison Co. v. State Bd. of Equalization & Assessment


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Albany County


120 Misc. 2d 617; 466 N.Y.S.2d 575; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3771



August 15, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent State Board of Equalization and Assessment (SBEA) sought order for partial summary judgment to dismiss portions of the petitions that sought to establish that franchise assessment valuations were erroneous. Petitioner energy utility filed a cross motion seeking a determination and order declaring that the Real Property Tax Law § 744(1) violated the New York State and Federal Constitutions and was void.


OVERVIEW: The legislature amended Real Property Tax Law § 744(1). The amendment retroactively precluded a special franchise holder from resorting to the Real Property Tax Law § 720(3), and restricted proof to the equalization rate used by the SBEA in determining the assessment at issue. The court denied the cross motion and granted the motion. Due process did not require the right to demonstrate the inequality of the assessment because the equalization rate was considered a legislative act, and therefore, no hearing, was constitutionally required. The evidence was insufficient to demonstrate that the energy utility was denied equal protection of the law. The energy utility failed to show that application of the amended statute would deprive it of a vested right, or would be so harsh and oppressive as to violate due process. The parties agreed that if the constitutionality of the amendment were upheld, then the SBEA's motion for partial summary judgment would be granted.


OUTCOME: In the action concerning the assessment of franchise valuations, the court denied the energy utility's cross motion and granted SBEA's motion for partial summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- Assessement -- Special Franchise Assessments -- Proof of Inequality

1. The 1982 statutory amendment, which restricts proof on the issue of inequality of a special franchise assessment to the State equalization rate used by the State Board of Equalization and Assessment (SBEA) in determining the final assessment under review (Real Property Tax Law, § 744, subd 1), thereby altering prior judicial declarations which treated special franchise holders, such as petitioner public utility, no differently than conventional real property owners and allowed such special franchise holders to demonstrate inequality by interclass comparisons (Real Property Tax Law, § 720, subd 3), is not unconstitutional as violative of equal protection since, although the amendment is procedural rather than substantive, differences between conventional real property and special franchises justify differential treatment; the amendment does not unconstitutionally delegate to the SBEA the function of equalizing special franchise assessments without adequate safeguards or standards since the SBEA is required to apply the latest State equalization rate or special equalization rate (Real Property [***2] Tax Law, § 606, subd 1) and there are ample checks upon the SBEA's exercise of its limited role in ascertaining equalization rates (Real Property Tax Law, § 1202); the amendment is not violative of due process by denying special franchise holders the right to a de novo review of the equalization rate since the equalization rate is a legislative rule-making act for which no hearing is constitutionally required and such a hearing would serve no useful purpose in any event since the methodology of the SBEA in arriving at the rate is universally accepted by the courts.

Taxation -- Assessment -- Special Franchise Assessments -- Proof of Inequality

2. The retroactive application of a 1982 statutory amendment restricting proof of inequality of a special franchise assessment to the State equalization rate used by the State Board of Equalization and Assessment in determining the final assessment under review (Real Property Tax Law, § 744, subd 1) is not violative of due process since the application of the amended statute does not deprive a special franchise holder, such as petitioner public utility, of a vested right inasmuch as reliance on prior judicial declarations giving [***3] special franchise holders the same rights as conventional real property owners to contest their assessments on inequality grounds does not constitute a vested property right such as would preclude a change or repeal by the Legislature; retrospective application of the statute is not violative of equal protection by differentiating between conventional real property owners and special franchise holders since the differences between the two classes warrant the treatment of special franchises as a separate class.

 

1736 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Victor P.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


120 Misc. 2d 770; 466 N.Y.S.2d 572; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3793



August 12, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, this branch of the defendant's motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the information that charged him with sexual abuse in the third degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 130.55 for grabbing a female's buttocks on a subway.


OVERVIEW: Two police officers observed defendant grabbing and tackling a female's buttocks, causing her to jump away and to express alarm. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the information against him and argued that §§ 130.00 and 130.55 were unconstitutionally vague as applied to him; the information was facially deficient under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 100.40, and people would be unable to sustain their burden of proof on the issue of nonconsent because the victim would not testify. The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the information. The court found that N.Y. Penal Law §§ 130.00 and 130.55 were not unconstitutionally vague. A reasonable man would have known that the female breasts and buttocks were not touched or fondled without the female's consent; it was clear that the buttocks were an intimate part within the meaning of the sexual assault statute. The information stated the elements of sexual abuse, which were: sexual contact and the lack of consent, and § 130.05(2)(c) did not require that the victim testify as to the nonconsent. The officer could testify that the female jumped away and expressed alarm.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the information, which charged him with sexual abuse in the third degree.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Vagueness of Sexual Abuse Statute -- Fair Notice to Reasonable Man Test

1. Section 130.55 of the Penal Law, which defines sexual abuse in the third degree as subjecting another person to sexual contact without the latter's consent, thereby incorporating the definition of sexual contact found in subdivision 3 of section 130.00 of the Penal Law, in particular, the words "any touching of the sexual or other intimate parts", is not unconstitutionally vague when applied to a defendant who allegedly grabbed an unidentified woman's buttocks without her consent in public. Though no explicit standard as to what constitutes a "sexual or intimate" part is contained anywhere in article 130 of the Penal Law, the test as to whether these ordinary terms are sufficient and clear is whether a reasonable man subject to the statute would be informed of the nature of the offense prohibited and that which is required of him. A reasonable man in this society would consider defendant's act to be proscribed behavior under this sexual abuse statute. In addition, courts in this jurisdiction have consistently [***2] held the buttocks to be an intimate part within the meaning of subdivision 3 of section 130.00 of the Penal Law.

Crimes -- Sexual Abuse -- Sufficiency of Accusatory Instrument

2. In order for an information to be facially sufficient pursuant to CPL 100.40, it must state the offense with which the defendant is charged and the particular facts establishing each and every element of that offense. The essential elements of sexual abuse in the third degree are sexual contact and lack of consent; where it is alleged by a police officer rather than the victim that defendant grabbed and tackled the victim's buttocks in public, these allegations are sufficient to establish the element of sexual contact, and intent may be inferred from the factual portion of the information. Nonconsent need not be sworn to by the victim since section 130.05 (subd 2, par [c]) of the Penal Law provides that lack of consent may result from "any circumstances, in addition to forcible compulsion or incapacity to consent, in which the victim does not expressly or impliedly acquiesce in the actor's conduct." Though the absence of the victim at trial may be a factor for the trier of fact to consider [***3] in determining whether the People have met their burden of proof, the victim's testimony is not essential for the limited purpose of establishing facial sufficiency of the information. The policeman's observations, though hearsay, are admissible under the exceptions of nonassertive conduct, evidence of the victim's state of mind or as a spontaneous declaration.

 

1737 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Patricia R. v. Peter W.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


120 Misc. 2d 986; 466 N.Y.S.2d 994; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3837



August 10, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Respondent's motion to dismiss is denied, and the matter is set down for hearing on the merits in Part VI of this court on September 28, 1983.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding to adjudicate the paternity of a 16-year-old boy, respondent putative father filed a motion to dismiss the proceeding as barred by the statute of limitations set forth in § 517(a) of the Family Court Act. Petitioner alleged that respondent had acknowledged paternity by furnishing support for the child, thereby establishing an exception to the statute of limitations.


OVERVIEW: Except for the final payment to petitioner of $ 200, the purpose of which was unspecified, respondent made no payments that were substantial in amount. The only payments that respondent made on a fairly regular basis were for the child's allowance of $ 5 to $ 10 a month. The court denied respondent's motion to dismiss an action filed by petitioner to adjudicate the paternity of a 16-year-old boy. In the absence of evidence that respondent had made any regular, consistent, or substantial contributions toward the basic items of support for the child, the court could not conclude that the payment of allowance constituted a clear and unequivocal acknowledgment of paternity. Thus, petitioner had not carried the evidentiary burden to establish an exception to the statute of limitations set forth in § 517(a) of the Family Court Act. However, the court found that the statute of limitations was unconstitutional, despite an amendment that lengthened the period from two years to five years. The statute had no substantial relationship to the legitimate state interest in preventing the litigation of stale or fraudulent claims.


OUTCOME: The court denied respondent's motion to dismiss an action filed by petitioner to adjudicate the paternity of a 16-year-old boy.


HEADNOTES

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Paternity Proceeding -- Support as Exception to Statute of Limitations

1. In a proceeding to determine the paternity of a 16 year old, petitioner has not established by clear and convincing evidence that respondent has acknowledged paternity by furnishing support for the child so as to constitute an exception to the Statute of Limitations, where it was shown that the only payments respondent made on a regular basis were for the child's allowance of $ 5 to $ 10 a month; in the absence of evidence that respondent has made any regular, consistent or substantial contributions toward the basic items of support, the payment of an allowance does not constitute a clear and unequivocal acknowledgment of paternity.

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Paternity Proceeding -- Statute of Limitations Unconstitutional

2. Subdivision (a) of section 517 of the Family Court Act, as amended by chapter 305 of the Laws of 1983, which lengthens to five years after the birth of the child the period within which [***2] a proceeding may be brought to establish paternity, is unconstitutional, since it bears no substantial relationship to the legitimate State interest in preventing the litigation of stale or fraudulent claims; the distinction between the 10-year Statute of Limitations for social services officials as against five years for the mother or child not on public assistance is irrational. Furthermore, although the Statute of Limitations is tolled during the minority of the mother, it is not tolled during the child's minority, as are most actions, and the many safeguards in the New York statutory scheme for the rights of respondents in paternity proceedings mitigate possible injustice to a respondent caused by a delay in the institution of a proceeding.

 

1738 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Martine S. v. Anthony D.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Kings County


120 Misc. 2d 567; 466 N.Y.S.2d 194; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3762



August 5, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent potential father sought review of an order directing that he submit to a composite blood grouping test to determine paternity in an action filed by petitioner mother. Respondent asserted that test would contravene his religious beliefs in violation of his First Amendment right to the free exercise of his religion.


OVERVIEW: In an affidavit in support of his position, respondent stated that submitting to the blood test was against his religious conviction because it was an integral part of his religion to follow his conscience as his convictions lead. But respondent did not suggest any scriptural or doctrinal basis for his refusal. The court held that because defendant's refusal was based on his conscience and personal convictions, the order did not violate his right to the free exercise of his religion. The court held that the two part test in determining whether a person's right to the free exercise of his religion was infringed, depended upon a finding that the belief was sincere, and it was rooted in theological conviction. In applying the above test to the instant case, the court held that assuming respondent had met the first prong, the second prong was not met because his choice was philosophical and personal, rather than religious. The court reasoned that respondent failed to establish any nexus between his refusal to submit to a blood test and a cardinal principal of his religion.


OUTCOME: The court ordered respondent to submit to the blood grouping test.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Religion -- Refusal to Submit to HLA Blood Test

1. An order directing a putative father to submit to a human leucocyte antigen composite blood-grouping test does not violate his constitutional right to the free exercise of religion since the putative father's opposition to the test was based on his conscience and personal convictions, amounting to a subjective evaluation and rejection of a validly enacted statutory scheme, where the "father" merely stated that submitting to the test was against his religious convictions because it was an integral part of his religion to follow his conscience, and where he neither suggested any scriptural or doctrinal basis for his refusal, nor established any nexus between his refusal and a "fundamental" mandate of his religion.

Parent and Child -- Paternity Proceeding -- HLA Blood Test

2. A putative father's argument that to compel him to submit to a human leucocyte antigen composite blood-grouping test would violate his right to be secure against unreasonable searches is meritless since the concern for the welfare of the child, an underlying objective of a paternity proceeding, represents a matter [***2] of legitimate State interest, and under the test set forth in Schmerber v California (384 U.S. 757), that blood tests be conducted under circumstances involving legitimate State interests and be properly administered, justifies a compulsory blood test.

Parent and Child -- Paternity Proceeding -- HLA Blood Test

3. In a paternity proceeding in which the mother claims that her then husband is not the child's father, suspending the administration on the putative father of a human leucocyte antigen (HLA) composite blood-grouping test until after the mother presents evidence rebutting the presumption of legitimacy would offend common sense, public policy and reason since the efficacy of the HLA test as an evidentiary tool in resolving paternity disputes was recognized by the Legislature when it amended sections 418 and 532 of the Family Court Act to allow such test results into evidence, and chose not to impose the type of limitations proposed by the putative father. It would be senseless to commence a trial for the purpose of establishing nonaccess by the husband of the mother, and then continue the trial to receive test results which might have excluded the putative father [***3] in the first instance; moreover, the test is highly accurate on the issue of paternity and, therefore, should be used to avoid unnecessary litigation.

 

1739 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Melevoi v. Yang


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Queens County


120 Misc. 2d 982; 467 N.Y.S.2d 20; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3836



August 2, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a summary landlord-tenant proceeding, respondent tenants requested the court to dismiss petitioner landlord's action pursuant to 22 N.Y. C.R.R. 2900.21 because the landlord was expelled from the Rent Stabilization Association. The landlord requested the court to transfer the action and alleged due process violations.


OVERVIEW: The New York City Conciliation and Appeals Board terminated the landlord's membership in the Rent Stabilization Association. The landlord's challenge to the termination was pending in a related proceeding in a different court along with an action initiated by the tenants. The court dismissed the landlord's eviction action without prejudice pursuant to 22 N.Y. C.R.R. 2900.21 because the landlord was not a member in good standing in the Rent Stabilization Association and the landlord's apartments were not subject to rent control. The court advised the landlord that he could bring a common-law action for ejectment in a different court. The court also concluded that it could not transfer the action to another court because N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 19(f) only authorized transfer when the court lacked jurisdiction and the landlord's inability to comply with the pleading requirements regarding membership in the Rent Stabilization Association was not a jurisdictional defect. The court also concluded that it could not address the landlord's due process claims because those issues would have been raised in the other pending actions.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the landlord's summary proceedings against the tenants without prejudice.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and Tenant -- Summary Proceedings -- Allegations Required

A motion by respondent tenant to dismiss landlord's summary proceeding for non-payment of rent on the ground that landlord was expelled from the Rent Stabilization Association and has not registered the subject apartment with the New York City department of Rent and Housing Maintenance must be granted since in every petition brought to recover possession of property in a summary proceeding the landlord must allege either membership in good standing in the association or that the apartment is subject to rent control and the rent charged does not exceed the maximum prescribed by the department (22 NYCRR 2900.21) and petitioner's allegation of membership without proof thereof is insufficient to comply with the statutory requirement; landlord's failure to allege or establish compliance with the Civil Court rules is not a jurisdictional defect and therefore precludes transfer to Supreme Court where landlord's proceeding for reinstatement into the association is pending.

 

1740 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rentz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Tompkins County


120 Misc. 2d 165; 465 N.Y.S.2d 982; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3686



July 26, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The court having determined that the trial court's failure to disqualify or further question the juror Moriah did not violate defendant's statutory or constitutional rights to a fair trial by impartial jurors, in accord with due process of law, and the court further noting, as a matter of policy, that the Court of Appeals has recently held that efforts to undermine a jury verdict by systematically questioning the individual jurors long after they have been dismissed in hopes of discovering some form of misconduct should not be encouraged (see People v Friedgood, 58 NY2d 467), defendant's motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.10 (1)(f)(h) to vacate a judgment of conviction for murder in the second degree on the ground that the trial court's failure to disqualify a juror who informed the court midtrial that he was acquainted with two witnesses violated defendant's statutory right to a trial by an impartial jury under U.S. Const. amends. IV, VI and N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 270.35, 270.20(1)(c).


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed a motion to vacate a judgment of conviction for second degree murder on the ground that his statutory and constitutional right to a fair trial were violated when the trial judge failed to disqualify, or to further question, a juror who told the trial judge midtrial of his acquaintance with two witnesses. Defendant contended that under § 270.35, the trial judge should have held a hearing to determine whether the juror was capable of rendering an impartial verdict. The court denied the motion, concluding that the trial judge's failure to disqualify the juror did not violate defendant's statutory or constitutional rights to a fair trial by impartial jurors. The court held that a claim of unconstitutional impartiality required a higher standard of review and that defendant failed to sustain his burden of proving actual prejudice, overt psychological pressure, or a presumption of patent partiality. Defendant also failed to satisfy the grossly unqualified standard required under § 270.35 as there was no proof that the juror was involved in an ongoing relationship with the witnesses that involved business, personal friendship or socializing.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to vacate his judgment of conviction for second degree murder.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Misconduct of Juror

Defendant's statutory and constitutional rights to a fair trial by an impartial jury were not violated by the trial court's failure to disqualify, or to further question, a juror who informed the court during trial that he had served as a probation officer and drug counselor to a prosecution witness several years earlier in a drug abuse halfway house and that he had served on professional panels with another prosecution witness, since the juror's professional relationships with said witnesses did not make him "grossly unqualified" to [***2] serve in the case ( CPL 270.35) and the juror's statements did not suggest the possibility or raise a presumption of actual bias toward the witnesses. Further, defendant did not have a constitutional right to a hearing on the issue or to inquiry by counsel, since the relationships adverted to in the juror's communications with the Trial Judge were not such as to trigger a constitutional duty on the part of the Judge to investigate further. Accordingly, defendant's motion pursuant to CPL 440.10 to vacate a judgment convicting him of murder in the second degree, made more than seven years after his trial, is denied.

 

1741 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Ryan v. Manhattan & Bronx Surface Transit Operating Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


120 Misc. 2d 524; 466 N.Y.S.2d 879; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3753



July 15, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Since the court finds the statement of July 4, 1975 is not tainted by any illegality, and that even if it was, it may still be considered at the reinstatement hearing, the motion by the petitioner for an order pursuant to CPLR 3103 (subd [c]) suppressing this statement and directing the respondents to return all copies thereof to the petitioner, is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee filed a motion, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3103(c), for an order suppressing a recorded statement given by the employee in a criminal proceeding and directing respondent employer to return all copies of the statement to the employee.


OVERVIEW: The employee was arrested for possessing coins that were stolen from the employer. The employee made a recorded statement in the presence of his attorney to the district attorney. The employer terminated the employee. After a criminal indictment was dismissed, the employee sought reinstatement to his position with the employer. The trial court denied the employee's motion. The court held that: (1) the employee's prior motion was made pursuant to the sealing provisions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 160.50; (2) the requirement of identity of issue was not met, and the employee was not collaterally estopped from bringing the action; (3) the recorded statement was not illegally obtained because it was voluntary and was made in the presence of the employee's attorney; (4) there was no evidence that the statement was previously suppressed or that it violated the employee's Fifth or Sixth Amendment rights; (5) redress for an illegally obtained statement under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3103(c) was moot; and (6) the employer was entitled to consider the recorded statement at the employee's reinstatement hearing.


OUTCOME: The court denied the employee's motion for an order suppressing the recorded statement and directing the employer to return all copies to the employee.


HEADNOTES

Judgments -- Collateral Estoppel

1. For the doctrine of issue preclusion to apply, it is required that the issue as to which preclusion is sought be identical with the issue decided in a prior proceeding; accordingly, the denial of a motion by petitioner seeking the return of a stenographic statement, made by petitioner in the course of a criminal investigation, under the sealing provisions of CPL 160.50 does not preclude a subsequent motion by petitioner for suppression of said statement; denial of the former motion on the ground that the statement is not subject to a sealing order does not constitute a decision on the merits of the latter claim which alleges that evidence suppressed in a criminal proceeding must be similarly suppressed in a subsequent civil proceeding.

Evidence -- Admissibility  [***2] of Illegally Obtained Evidence

2. An incriminating statement voluntarily made by petitioner in the presence of counsel under a promise of immunity by the District Attorney cannot be imbued with any illegal taint notwithstanding the dismissal of a criminal indictment against petitioner; said dismissal was based upon the promise of immunity, not upon suppression of said statement and, therefore, the statement is not subject to suppression in a subsequent civil proceeding pursuant to the rule that illegally seized evidence may not be used affirmatively by the government in a subsequent civil action against the person from whom such evidence was obtained; moreover, even if said statement had been illegally obtained, petitioner would not be entitled to its suppression in the subsequent civil proceeding in which he seeks reinstatement to his position with the Manhattan and Bronx Surface Transit Operating Authority from which he was dismissed following his giving of the statement implicating him in the theft of fares; petitioner may not shield himself in the reinstatement hearing from being cast in the role of a thief by the artifice of removing his stenographic statement from consideration [***3] on the question of his reinstatement; the constitutional shield preventing illegally seized evidence from being used against a defendant at trial cannot be perverted into a license to use perjury by way of a defense, free from the risk of confrontation with prior inconsistent utterances.

 

1742 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jobson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Trial Term, Nassau County


119 Misc. 2d 985; 464 N.Y.S.2d 988; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3632



July 11, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Leave is granted to the District Attorney to submit the case to another Grand Jury.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree. He objected that the indictment was fatally defective, particularly calling the court's attention to the district attorney's charge regarding the presumption of possession.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sitting on the front passenger seat of an automobile, which belonged to his friends. When the police arrived on the scene, having been summoned by a resident, they found him alone in the car, apparently sleeping. Officers searched a console between the seats and found the weapon on which the indictment was based. The People relied on N.Y. Penal Law § 265.15(3), which provided that the presence of a firearm in a car was presumptive evidence of possession by all persons occupying the vehicle. The grand jury was given instructions, which consisted of a recitation of the § 265.15(3) and N.Y. Penal Law 265.02(4) relating to criminal possession. The grand jury was not given instructions that the presumption was not a conclusive presumption. The court held that the failure to so instruct the grand jury was reversible error. Without this explanation the instruction concerning the presumption was misleading. The People's case was marginal, and the grand jury could have been misled into concluding that they were to disregard defendant's testimony because it was conclusively presumed that he possessed the weapon.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the indictment against defendant as insufficient in law. No other matter was decided. The district attorney was granted leave to submit the case to another grand jury.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Mandatory Presumption

1. A mandatory presumption as to an element of a crime is unconstitutional because it conflicts with the overriding presumption of innocence as to every element of a crime and invades the jury's fact-finding jurisdiction.

Grand Jury -- Instructions

2. Where the People present no evidence as to the substantive provisions of the crime of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree (Penal Law, § 265.02, subd [4]) but rather, rely solely on the statutory presumption of possession arising from the presence of the weapon in an automobile (Penal Law, § 265.15, subd 3) and the District Attorney gives instructions on the law that consist of a recitation of the provisions of those statutes, and does not explain to the Grand Jury that the presumption is rebuttable and not conclusive, which they need not accept and can disregard, the indictment must be dismissed as insufficient in law; even if the District Attorney does not inform the Grand Jury that the presumption is conclusive and binding upon them, the instructions [***2] are so misleading that the indictment cannot be permitted to stand even though supported by legally sufficient evidence.

 

1743 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Roseann R. v. William R.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


119 Misc. 2d 874; 465 N.Y.S.2d 453; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3607



July 1, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner New York Commissioner of Social Services commenced a support proceeding against respondent father, which was referred to a hearing examiner for the court. The hearing examiner recommended that the father pay $ 50 weekly for the support of the wife and his child effective as the date of the hearing and the first return date of process. The Commissioner filed a motion to disaffirm the report.


OVERVIEW: The hearing examiner denied the Commissioner's motion to make the order retroactive to the date the petition was filed as required by N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 449. The Commissioner contended that the hearing examiner improperly concluded that § 449 was unconstitutional and that the more appropriate amount of weekly support was $ 70, not $ 50. The court held that the amount of support ordered was proper, and that the Commissioner failed to preserve the issue. Section 449, which mandated that a support order be made retroactive to the date of the filing of the petition, was constitutional, being based upon recognition of the fact that support was a continuing obligation. However, the court counseled that § 449 should not be utilized as a "strait jacket" to impose undue, unjust, and unfair hardship on the father but must be read in conjunction with N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 458, which granted the court discretion to excuse support arrears for "good cause" shown. The court observed that consideration of the father's ability to pay and "good cause" for nonpayment prior to the imposition of retroactive payments avoided an unconstitutional application of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 449.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the hearing examiner's report insofar as the amount of weekly support was concerned and set the matter down for a hearing on the issues pertaining to retroactive support.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Husband and Wife -- Support -- Effective Date of Support Order

While section 449 of the Family Court Act, which mandates that a support order be made retroactive to the date of the filing of the petition, is constitutional, being based upon recognition of the fact that support is a continuing obligation, the statute should not be utilized as a "strait jacket" to impose undue, unjust and unfair hardship on a litigant but must be read in conjunction with section 458 of the Family Court Act, which grants the court discretion to excuse support arrears for "good cause" shown, so as to require consideration of ability to pay and "good cause" for nonpayment before imposition of any retroactive payment in order to avoid a violation of due process; the key to proper application of section 449 is the necessity for considering whether the respondent exhibited culpable conduct in the period for which retroactive support is claimed entailing scrutiny of the respondent's ability to meet his or her support obligation during such period since the court may not impose retroactive support for an amount certain which, at the effective date of the commencement of the period covered by such retroactivity,  [***2] it could not properly impose prospectively; the "good cause" test, while permitting support to be retroactive to a date other than the filing date of the petition or prospective only, also permits the holding of retroactive support "in abeyance" where directing payment forthwith in periodic sums, even in miniscule amounts, would work an undue hardship.

 

1744 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Williams


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Bronx County


120 Misc. 2d 68; 465 N.Y.S.2d 648; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3664



June 30, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of New York charged defendant with two class A misdemeanors. The district attorney reduced the charges to attempts, which were class B misdemeanors. Defendant was granted leave to challenge the procedure of the district attorney, who allegedly made the reductions on the brink of trial in order to deprive defendant of her right to a jury trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was originally charged with assault and criminal possession of a weapon. On the brink of trial, the district attorney reduced both charges to attempts under Cal. Penal Law §§ 110.00, 120.00 and Cal. Penal Law §§ 110.00, 265.01, respectively. The legal aid society challenged the district attorney's practice of reducing certain A misdemeanor cases, punishable by a maximum term of one year, to B misdemeanors, punishable by a maximum term of three months. The reduction was to deprive defendants of their right to a jury trial. The court decided that the district attorney could reduce substantive offenses to attempts where the allegations in the original information made out a completed act. Therefore, defendant's motion, based on the argument that the charges were hypothetical, was denied. Defendant faced only a three month incarceration, the charges were petty, and she was not entitled to a trial by jury. Defendant's motion attacking the district attorney's reduction procedure was denied in all respects. However, defendant's Clayton motion warranted a hearing to determine whether compelling factors existed warranting dismissal because of defendant's sarcoidosis condition.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion that attacked the district attorney's reduction procedure. However, in reviewing defendant's Clayton motion, the court found that a hearing was required to determine whether compelling factors were in existence sufficiently to warrant the dismissal of the matter.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Right to Jury Trial -- Reduction of Charges on Eve of Trial -- Attempt

The practice of the Bronx County District Attorney of reducing certain A misdemeanors punishable by a maximum term of one year to B misdemeanors punishable by a maximum term of three months on the eve of trial thereby depriving defendants of the right to trial by jury is properly within the prosecutor's almost unfettered discretion in determining how and when to prosecute and, therefore, the reduction of the felony assault and criminal weapon possession charges against defendant to class B misdemeanor attempt charges (Penal Law, § 110.00) on the brink of trial without notice to defendant is not violative of any of defendant's statutory or constitutional rights; the attempt charges, although inconsistent with the theory of completed crimes charged in the original information, are not unconstitutionally vague nor are they hypothetical or illusory crimes concocted by the prosecution solely to deprive defendant of the right to a jury trial without due process of law since conviction for an attempt is not precluded by the fact that the crime was completed and the District Attorney may, thus, reduce [***2] substantive offenses to attempts where the allegations in the original information make out a completed act; the right to a jury trial is governed solely by the statutory six-month imprisonment rule ( CPL 340.40) and cannot be based upon a subjective determination of the inherent seriousness of each crime regardless of the penal sanctions involved.

 

1745 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Esther W. v. Melvin H.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


119 Misc. 2d 690; 464 N.Y.S.2d 667; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3579



June 17, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother filed a paternity petition against respondent alleged father seeking an order of filiation declaring the alleged father to be the child's natural father and an order of support.


OVERVIEW: The mother filed a paternity petition against the alleged father seeking an order of filiation declaring the alleged father to be the child's natural father and an order of support. The alleged father denied paternity and interposed that the mother's claim was barred by the two-year statute of limitations. Fam. Ct. Act. § 517(a). The mother contended that the limitation period was tolled because the alleged father acknowledged paternity by supporting the child. On consideration, the court set the case for trial on the issue of paternity. The court found that the statute of limitations was not tolled by payment of support, because the evidence did not establish a pattern of support by alleged father which would clearly and unequivocally constitute an acknowledgment of paternity. However, the court held that § 517(a) which allowed only two years after the birth of a child for filing of a paternity petition, was unconstitutional, in that the limitation violated the child's right to equal protection under the Fourteenth Amendment. The two-year limitation on paternity cases was not substantially related to the state interest of avoiding stale or fraudulent claims.


OUTCOME: The court set the case for trial on the issue of paternity.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Limitation of Actions -- Paternity Proceeding -- Constitutionality of Two-Year Limitation Period

Subdivision (a) of section 517 of the Family Court Act which provides a two-year Statute of Limitations within which to commence a paternity proceeding is unconstitutional as a violation of the child's right to equal protection; there is clearly not a substantial relationship between the two-year limitation period and the State interest in avoiding stale or fraudulent claims; the proof problems, which the limitation period aims to avoid, are not so significant as to preclude tolling of other statutory periods during a plaintiff's infancy, and the expanded judicial use of blood test results serves the State interest of limiting welfare rolls and lessens the chance of fraudulent claims; accordingly, the court refused to dismiss a paternity petition filed in June, 1982, wherein it was alleged that respondent is the father of petitioner's daughter, born in June, 1974.

 

1746 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Pauk v. Board of Trustees


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


119 Misc. 2d 663; 464 N.Y.S.2d 953; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3574



June 16, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant university moved to dismiss plaintiff professor's action that was brought after the university denied the professor tenure, pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 6212. In bringing the action after receiving an unfavorable verdict in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding, the professor claimed the university had breached the employment contract, had violated the professor's constitutional rights, and had failed to justify its denial of tenure.


OVERVIEW: After the professor was denied tenure, pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 6212, the professor brought an unsuccessful N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding against the university. Thereafter, the professor brought another action against the university, claiming the university had breached the employment contract, had violated the professor's constitutional rights, and had failed to justify its tenure denial. In partially granting the university's motion to dismiss, the court held a university was not required to give a non-tenured teacher its reasons for denying reappointment. Moreover, the court held the professor's constitutional claim should have been raised in the art. 78 proceeding; thus, it was barred by principles of res judicata. However, because the professor's contract claim was not assertable in an art. 78 proceeding, the court held the contract claim was not barred by res judicata. Furthermore, because the essence of the contract claim was a violation of the professor's constitutional rights, the court held the six-year residual limitations period of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 213(1) was applicable, rather than the shorter periods for statutory actions and art. 78 proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the professor's claims that the university had violated the professor's constitutional rights and had failed to justify its denial of tenure but refused to dismiss the professor's claim that the university had breached the employment contract.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Colleges and Universities -- Teachers -- Denial of Tenure

1. Plaintiff teacher's claim attacking the constitutionality of defendant City University's policies of neither releasing the numerical results of balloting by an academic tenure committee nor giving reasons for its decisions in his action seeking renewal of his employment contract and a grant of tenure fails to state a cause of action; no reasons need be given a nontenured teacher who has been denied reappointment; it is not unconstitutional to withhold the reasons for a denial of tenure.

Judgments -- Res Judicata -- Effect of Prior Mandamus Proceeding Upon Subsequent Damages Action

2. Plaintiff teacher is not barred by res judicata from commencing an action seeking damages for breach of contract stemming from his dismissal and denial of tenure by defendant university on the ground that he had previously commenced a CPLR article 78 proceeding seeking reinstatement and an order declaring his right to a permanent position at the college from which he had been dismissed which was adjudicated against plaintiff; plaintiff could not have asserted his current contract claim in the article 78 proceeding because [***2] he would have been entitled neither to the relief requested nor to prosecute the action in the form in which it was brought; inasmuch as one proceeding cannot encompass both a petition for mandamus relief and a damages action sounding in contract, it is not unfair to permit plaintiff to bring two separate suits; however, plaintiff's cause of action asserting that defendant's conduct is violative of his rights under article V of the New York Constitution and section 6212 of the Education Law is barred by res judicata; said constitutional claim was directly raised in the prior article 78 proceeding and, therefore, is barred.

Limitation of Actions -- What Statute Governs -- Action Based on Constitutional Guarantee

3. The essence or gravamen of a cause of action determines the applicable Statute of Limitations and, therefore, where plaintiff's cause of action claimed that by failing to renew plaintiff's teaching appointment on the basis of merit and fitness as guaranteed by the New York Constitution, defendant university breached plaintiff's employment contract, the six-year residual limitation period of CPLR 213 (subd 1) is applicable; the essence of said cause of action [***3] is neither contractual nor statutory but constitutional in that it claims that defendant breached plaintiff's employment contract only in that it violated pre-existing obligations independent of the contract provided by the New York Constitution; accordingly, since the Constitution is not a statute for Statute of Limitations purposes, CPLR 213 (subd 1) provides the applicable limitation period.

 

1747 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rochester v. Chiarella


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


121 Misc. 2d 257; 467 N.Y.S.2d 948; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3903



June 16, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city filed a reverse class action suit against defendant taxpayers seeking a judgment declaring whether it could formulate a method of payment by which it could refund the unconstitutional taxes that had been paid by defendants.


OVERVIEW: The city levied unconstitutional taxes against its taxpayers, and some defendants paid the taxes without protest while others paid them under protest. The city filed a reverse class action suit against defendants seeking a judgment declaring whether it could formulate a method of payment by which it could refund the unconstitutional taxes that had been paid by all defendants. After it was determined that only those defendants who had protested the taxes had a legal right to a refund, the appellate division remanded the suit to the court for a determination regarding the nonprotesting defendants. The court entered a judgment declaring that the city could refund the unconstitutional taxes that had been paid by the nonprotesting defendants. The court held that pursuant to N.Y. Gen. City Law § 20(5), the city could authorize the refunds as equitable claims against the city. The court further held that the nonprotesting defendants' failure to protest the tax did not preclude their equitable rights to refunds because the failure to protest the tax did not alter the merits of defendants' claims for the refunds.


OUTCOME: The court entered a judgment declaring that the city could formulate a method of payment by which it could refund the unconstitutional taxes that had been paid by defendants.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Payment of Equitable Claims -- Refund Claims of Nonprotesting Taxpayers

Claims of nonprotesting taxpayers of the City of Rochester for refunds of unconstitutional taxes collected during four years that the city exceeded its constitutional taxing limitation, although not legally enforceable due to the failure to pay the taxes "under protest" (see 58 NY2d 316), are nonetheless "moral obligations", "equitable claims" or "claims founded in equity and justice" that may properly be paid by the city without violating the constitutional proscription against the making of gifts (NY Const, art VIII, § 1) pursuant to its power to waive procedural or technical defects and honor otherwise barred equitable claims (General City Law, § 20, subd 5) since protest is merely a "procedural requirement" that does not affect the underlying substantive merit of the nonprotestors' claims for refunds of unconstitutional taxes; since the nonprotestors' claims are moral or equitable obligations, the extent of which such claims against the city should be recognized is primarily for the city itself to determine.

 

1748 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Castro


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


119 Misc. 2d 787; 464 N.Y.S.2d 650; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3597



June 10, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a motion for the return of forfeited cash bail that he had deposited on behalf of a criminal defendant.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, who paid cash bail on behalf of a criminal defendant, moved for remission or, alternatively, to vacate forfeiture. Remission was barred by one-year statute of limitations in N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 540.30(2); however, the motion to vacate was timely because it claimed that the forfeiture was void for illegality. Petitioner had standing as one entitled to receive proceeds of exonerated bail. However, depositors of cash bail had fewer rights than sureties of bail bonds. Statutory discrimination was permitted, because no fundamental right was involved, and a rational basis test applied. In cash bail, the law looked to money already in possession of the state to insure defendant's appearance. The depositor was not entitled to notice because there was no "taking." Furthermore, the depositor was assumed to have consented to grounds for cash forfeiture. The depositor was not an obligor, so there was no surety relationship. Thus, while the court acknowledged a need for change, it denied the petition, because such action was not supported by statute.


OUTCOME: The petition for return of forfeited cash bail was denied, because the court would not judicially expand the statutory rights and duties of the depositor of a cash bond.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Bail -- Remission of Forfeiture

1. Although an application for bail remission addressed to the discretion of the court made more than one year after forfeiture of bail is untimely ( CPL 540.30, subd 2), the one-year time limitation is inapplicable to an application to vacate the forfeiture of bail when the forfeiture of bail is claimed to be unconstitutional or illegal and therefore void; an application for bail remission must set forth the extent of the applicant's resources and bare allegations of severe hardship and affidavits by attorneys representing applicants are insufficient to grant remission.

Bail -- Remission of Forfeiture -- Standing of Depositor of Cash Bail

2. A depositor of cash bail, as opposed to a surety, has standing to make an application for bail remission since the statute (Judiciary Law, § 798) does not restrict the category of persons who may make a remission application; a depositor of cash bail, as a person entitled to receive the proceeds of any exonerated bail (see General Municipal Law, § 99-m) whom the court is "bound to protect", has standing to seek vacatur of a cash bail forfeiture since the cash bail is deemed to be owned by the [***2] defendant rather than the actual depositor only for purposes of the deposit until the criminal proceeding has been terminated after which time the actual depositor is deemed to be the owner with a superior right to the return of cash bail as against the defendant.

Bail -- Forfeiture -- Cash Bail

3. The New York statutory scheme giving depositors of cash bail fewer rights than sureties of bail bonds (see CPL 210.10, 530.80, subd 2; 540.10) is not violative of equal protection since the differences in the underlying theories behind bail bonds and cash bail, the law looking to the surety to guarantee the defendant's appearance in bail bonds while looking to the money already in the hands of the State to insure the defendant's appearance in cash bail, supply a rational basis for the distinctions in the rights, duties and obligations between bail bondsmen and depositors of cash bail; the failure in the statutory scheme to require that either prior notice of forfeiture or notice of "taking" after forfeiture be given to the depositor of cash bail is not violative of due process since the depositor has a duty to know what occurs in open court and therefore has constructive knowledge [***3] of the actual forfeiture and there is also no due process requirement for notice of the "taking" of the cash bail after forfeiture since the money is already "in the custody and control of the court" and there is thus no "taking" of the depositor's property; the depositor of cash bail is "assumed" to know the exact circumstances in which the deposit might be irretrievably lost at the time the cash bail is posted and the depositor cannot be granted surety status by judicial fiat contrary to the clear legislative mandate nor does the fact that the cash receipt given to a depositor of cash bail states "surety" give such depositor a legal right not granted by statute and case law; the statutory scheme with regard to cash bail should be changed to at least grant depositors of cash bail the opportunity to surrender defendants at any time.

 

1749 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


State v. General Motors Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


120 Misc. 2d 371; 466 N.Y.S.2d 124; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3721



June 8, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion of defendant General Motors Corporation is granted only to the extent of dismissing the fourth cause of action for failure to honor implied warranty and is otherwise denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant car manufacturer sought dismissal of the State's complaint for failure to state a cause of action, under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a)(7). The complaint, filed pursuant to N.Y. Exec. Law § 63(12), alleged that defendant's failure to disclose a transmission defect constituted a fraudulent practice to prospective customers and that the limited warranty and disclaimer of all other warranties were unconscionable contract provisions.


OVERVIEW: The State filed an action against defendant car manufacturer, based upon use of defective transmissions which were known to defendant and the use of limited warranties to limit liability. The State sought reimbursement to vehicle owners and an injunction against future use of that part. The action was filed under N.Y. Exec. Law § 63(12) and alleged causes of action for failure to disclose a known defect, unconscionable limitation of warranties, deceptive warranty, failure to honor implied warranty, and unconscionable business practices. Defendant sought dismissal for failure to state a cause of action, under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a)(7). The court held that all claims were actionable except for breach of an implied warranty and only dismissed as to that cause of action. The court held that the essence of the suit arose from defendant's alleged knowledge of the defect and the attempt to limit its liability and that it was not a typical consumer complaint. Accordingly, the court concluded that the claims were encompassed within the meaning of § 63(12). However, the court noted that no privity existed between the consumers and defendant, and accordingly, there was no implied warranty.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant car manufacturer's motion to dismiss the State's complaint as to the cause of action for breach of an implied warranty, holding that there was no privity, as required, between defendant and the consumers. However, all other claims, which were based on defendant's conduct and the express warranty were valid, where it was alleged that defendant knew of the defective transmissions and acted fraudulently thereunder.


HEADNOTES

Consumer Protection -- Fraudulent Business Practice -- Installation of Defective Transmissions in New Cars

An action brought by the Attorney-General under subdivision 12 of section 63 of the Executive Law alleging that defendant car manufacturer engaged in fraudulent and unconscionable business practices by installing defective transmissions in its automobiles which it knew would fail shortly after the expiration of its new-car 12,000-mile limited warranty period states a cause of action under the statute since the statute, although usually applied to activities such as pyramid sales, bait and switch sales and misleading advertising, also encompasses a consumer product complaint where the essence of the suit is not the quality of the product per se, but the knowledge of the defect and the attempt to limit liability by means of concealment and limited warranties for the defective product; the complaint also states a cause of action for an unconscionable contractual provision [***2] and a deceptive warranty practice since knowledge of the nature of the defect would have adversely affected a consumer's decision to purchase one of defendant's cars; the fact that statute has not previously been applied in the context of a breach of warranty in a consumer product transaction does not render the statute as applied unconstitutionally vague since the statute is sufficiently clear in describing the type of commercial activity which is to be enjoined; the action for breach of implied warranty for economic loss only must be dismissed since such an action could not have been brought against defendant by individual consumers due to the lack of privity between defendant and the consumers; the fact that there are several other suits pending involving substantially the same issues does not warrant a stay of this action at this time.

 

1750 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Flynn


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Bronx County


119 Misc. 2d 561; 463 N.Y.S.2d 719; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3556



May 31, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner niece brought an application against respondent, executrix and stepdaughter, seeking a determination that two Totten trust bank accounts established by decedent had been revoked pursuant to the terms of the decedent's will.


OVERVIEW: During his lifetime, decedent created two Totten trust bank accounts, the proceeds of which constituted substantially all of his assets. These accounts were established prior to the execution of the will and designated his stepdaughter as beneficiary upon his death. The niece argued that the Totten trust bank accounts were revoked by the provisions of the will. The niece conceded that the accounts at issue were not revoked in accordance with the requirements of N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 7-5.2 but argued that either the statute was inapplicable to the accounts, or, if applicable, it was unconstitutional. The court concluded that: (1) merely because a Totten trust bank account was created prior to the effective date of part 5 of N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts § 7, the statute in no way impaired any vested right where the decedent had died subsequent to the effective date of the statute and left a will that was also executed subsequent to that date; and (2) the accounts were both payable to the stepdaughter without the stepdaughter's automatic right to the accounts upon the death of decedent being in any respect impaired by decedent's subsequent execution of his will.


OUTCOME: The court denied the niece's application in estate proceedings.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Trusts -- Totten Trust -- Applicability of EPTL

1. A Totten trust bank account created by decedent after the effective date of part 5 of EPTL article 7 is not exempt from application of the article as a result of the language of EPTL 7-5.7 which provides that the statute is applicable to "all funds in trust accounts * * * which are in existence on its effective date"; such reference is not a limitation of the statute to only such accounts, but a simple explicit rebuttal by the Legislature of the presumption that all legislation is simply prospective, and to construe the statute otherwise would frustrate its clear purpose.

Trusts -- Totten Trust -- Constitutionality of Application of EPTL

2. The application of EPTL 7-5.2 to a Totten trust bank account created before the statute's effective date and allegedly revoked by the terms of decedent creator's will does not constitute, by ex post facto legislation, an unconstitutional impairment of a contractual relationship between the decedent and the depository where he created the account; the application of the statute to a trust account created by decedent who died subsequent to its effective date and left a will that [***2] was also executed subsequent to that date did not impair any vested right since the decedent creator's right to revoke the designation of beneficiary at will at the depository did not confer upon decedent a vested right to revoke the account by testamentary instrument without regard to the statutory requirements for such an act, where such requirements were a part of the law at the time the instrument was executed, even if the requirements were created subsequent to the establishment of the account; there is no vested right in existing legislation or rules of law that precludes a change or repeal by the Legislature so long as it does not impair an already vested property right.

 

1751 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Kessel v. Purcell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


119 Misc. 2d 449; 463 N.Y.S.2d 384; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3534



May 27, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner individuals brought an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 against respondents, a county executive, a county board of supervisors, and the New York State Legislature, seeking a judicial determination annulling 1983 N.Y. Laws ch. 18, which authorized the County of Nassau, New York, to increase its local sales tax. The county and the State moved to dismiss the petition.


OVERVIEW: The individuals contended that 1983 N.Y. Laws ch. 18 was a nullity because the board of supervisors failed to adopt a formal home rule message requesting increased taxing authority from the legislature. The court granted the State's motion to dismiss the petition against the legislature because individuals failed to serve the petition upon the legislature as required by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 2214(d) and 7804(c). The court denied the county's motion to dismiss. The individuals erroneously brought their action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, and the action should have been brought as a declaratory judgment action. The court directed the proceed to be converted to a declaratory judgment action and issued a declaration of the parties' rights. 1983 N.Y. Laws ch. 18 validly authorized the county to increase its sales tax. No home rule message was required prior to the enactment of the law 1983 N.Y. Laws ch. 18, because the tax increase did not affect the property, affairs and government of a municipality within the meaning of N.Y. Const. art. XI, § 2(b)(2).


OUTCOME: The court granted the State's motion to dismiss. The court denied the county's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Proceeding against Body or Officer -- Action against State Legislature

1. The petition must be dismissed as against the State Legislature in a proceeding pursuant to CPLR article 78 for a judicial determination annulling chapter 18 of the Laws of 1983, which authorized Nassau County to increase its local sales tax from 3% to 4%, where the pleadings were not served upon the Legislature; in addition, the Legislature enjoys absolute immunity from liability for actions which are, as here, in the sphere of legitimate legislative activity. However, since the court acquired jurisdiction over the other parties, it retained jurisdiction and directed that the proceeding be converted into an action for declaratory judgment, the appropriate remedy for a determination concerning the validity of a statute.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute

2. Chapter 18 of the Laws of 1983, which authorized Nassau County to increase its sales tax from 3% to 4%, and the subsequent increase of the sales tax by local law in Nassau County, were duly enacted and proper in all respects; this delegation of the taxing power is a matter of valid State concern requiring no formal home rule message [***2] (Municipal Home Rule Law, § 10, subd 1, par a, cls [8], [9]; NY Const, art III, § 1; art XVI, § 1).

 

1752 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Alyakoub


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


119 Misc. 2d 470; 463 N.Y.S.2d 380; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3539



May 25, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to criminal possession of marihuana in the first degree, N.Y. Penal Law § 221.30. At issue before the court was whether an incongruity in the law that required a more severe punishment for defendant than would be imposed on a similarly situated person in possession of the same drug in concentrated form rendered N.Y. Penal Law § 70.00(4) unconstitutional as violative of the 14th Amendment.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to defendant's plea bargain, the prosecutor was to recommend five years' probation and six months' jail time. But before sentencing, it was found that N.Y. Penal Law § 70.00(4) did not apply to marihuana cases and that the minimum statutory prison term was one year. The trial court sentenced defendant pursuant to the prosecutor's recommendation, holding that it would violate the 14th Amendment to give the longer sentence. Applying the rational basis test, the court concluded that there was no legitimate State purpose in affording different and discriminatory treatment to persons sentenced for offenses under N.Y. Penal Law art. 221 as opposed to those sentenced for offenses under art. 220 for the commission of more serious crimes of the same general nature. The court held that unless N.Y. Penal Law § 70.00(4) was read to include class C felonies under art. 221, such as defendant's, that § 70.00(4) would be unconstitutional. The obvious legislative intent was to include those offenses, and they were excluded only because of a palpable legislative oversight.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the felony committed by defendant was intended to be included in the sentencing law and, accordingly, sentenced defendant to six months' imprisonment to run concurrently and as a condition of a sentence of five years' probation.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Sentence -- Controlled Substances

Subdivision 4 of section 70.00 of the Penal Law, which allows a court to impose a definite term of imprisonment of one year or less when a defendant is convicted of a class C felony specified in article 220 of the Penal Law relating to controlled substances, must be read to include persons convicted under article 221 of the Penal Law, the so-called Marihuana Reform Act, inasmuch as the statute would otherwise be unconstitutional as violative of the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment; there is no legitimate State purpose in affording different and discriminatory treatment to persons sentenced for offenses under article 221 by imposing the one-year minimum sentence generally required (Penal Law, § 70.00, subd 3), as opposed to those sentenced for offenses under article 220 for the commission of more serious crimes of the same general nature, and said result was not intended by the Legislature. Accordingly, defendant, who was convicted of criminal possession of marihuana in the first degree under section 221.30 of the Penal Law upon his plea of guilty, is sentenced to a six-month term of imprisonment, to run concurrently [***2] with five years' probation (Penal Law, § 60.01, subd 2, par [d]).

 

1753 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Commissioner of Labor v. Eagle Comtronics, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Onondaga County


120 Misc. 2d 58; 465 N.Y.S.2d 411; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3662



May 25, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the foregoing reasons, this judgment is vacated and the parties shall be referred to the appropriate administrative tribunal for a determination of this matter on its merits.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After entry of a judgment based upon plaintiff New York Commissioner of Labor's finding of labor law violations involving home-based workers, defendant employer filed a motion seeking to vacate the judgment on grounds of excusable neglect, lack of jurisdiction, and unconstitutionality of the method of proving the penalty.


OVERVIEW: The employer's petition challenging the assessment of the penalty was untimely because of a process server's error. The agency declined to consider the employer's petition and rendered a decision against the employer, which was filed with the county clerk and docketed as a judgment. The court concluded that, under the circumstances, the employer was entitled to its day in court. Moreover, the court held that the procedure set forth in N.Y. Comp.
