Codes R. & Regs. tit. 12, § 65.30 violated the employer's constitutional due process rights by requiring the employer to disprove the existence of the alleged violation. The agency should have had the burden of proving its allegations by the preponderance of the evidence standard.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment and referred the matter to the agency for a determination on the merits.


HEADNOTES

Judgments -- Vacatur

A manufacturer, against which the State Division of Labor Standards issued an ex parte order determining that it had violated the Labor Law and assessing a $ 2,500 civil penalty which was subsequently docketed as a judgment, is entitled to have that judgment vacated where the agency's action in rejecting the manufacturer's attempted administrative appeal was arbitrary and capricious, thereby depriving the manufacturer of its day in court, and where minimal due process standards, compliance with which was required owing to the significant constitutionally protected property and liberty interests involved, had not been met since the agency's rules of procedure shifted the burden onto the manufacturer to disprove the allegations brought against it.

 

1754 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Jamal B.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, Queens County


119 Misc. 2d 808; 465 N.Y.S.2d 115; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3599



May 4, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner child care agency brought an action pursuant to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b(4)(b) to terminate the parental rights of respondent mother and against respondent father pursuant to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 384-b(4)(d) on the grounds of abandonment and permanent neglect and to transfer custody and guardianship of the child.


OVERVIEW: The child care agency sought to transfer custody and guardianship of the child to itself and the Commissioner of Social Services with the right of either agency to place the child for legal adoption without further notice to or consent of the mother and father on the grounds that he was permanently neglected. The court rejected the request for transfer of custody, finding that the child was not a permanently neglected child and dismissed the child care agency's petition concluding that it was not established that the child was rightfully in the custody of the Commissioner of Social Services. The court found that the child's maternal grandmother had a strong interest in him having had primary responsibility for his care prior to placement and having exhibited a continuing and abiding concern for his health and welfare. The court found that she had been in close contact with the child care agency and had shown interest in his educational, medical and emotional needs. The court concluded that the grandmother's actions placed her in loco parentis. The court found that the mother had acquiesced in the grandmother's exercise of authority in placing the child.


OUTCOME: The court rejected the child care agency's request to terminate the mother and father's parental rights of the child on the grounds of permanent neglect.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights -- Necessity of Foster Care for Provision of Essential Services

1. A proceeding to terminate parental rights of the parents of a retarded and autistic child who has been institutionalized in foster care for over six years on the grounds of abandonment and permanent neglect is dismissed; the child's maternal grandmother, who stands in loco parentis, had primary responsibility for his care prior to placement and has exhibited a continuing concern for his health and welfare, and the petitioner child care agency has failed to substantiate its claim that the child is no longer in need of foster care in a residential treatment center and is therefore an appropriate candidate for adoption requiring the severance of parental ties, since the child cannot function in an ordinary or "normal" family home and his family is unable to provide him with the extraordinary care he needs; accordingly, where continuing foster care is needed in order to provide essential treatment and services, termination of parental rights and adoption are not a proper recourse in accordance with the statutory scheme and purpose, particularly since the transfer [***2] of custody as a condition precedent to the placement of a child in a residential treatment facility has been held unconstitutional.

Parent and Child -- Termination of Parental Rights -- Notice to Grandmother as Person in Loco Parentis

2. The maternal grandmother of a retarded and autistic child who has been institutionalized in foster care for over six years is entitled to notice and an opportunity to be heard in a proceeding to terminate parental rights on the grounds of abandonment and permanent neglect since she stands in loco parentis; there has been no reported contact between the child and his alleged father since 1971, and although the child's mother cared for the child during his early childhood, she has continuously failed to visit the child in the institutions where he has been placed, and has failed to remain in contact and keep the petitioner child care agency apprised of her address; the maternal grandmother had primary responsibility for the child's care prior to placement, has exhibited a continuing concern for his health and welfare, has been in close contact with the petitioner and has taken the child to her home for weekend visits on a regular basis.

 [***3] Parent and Child -- Person in Loco Parentis

3. In loco parentis refers to a person who has put himself completely in the situation of a lawful parent by assuming all the obligations incident to the parental relationship and who actually discharges these obligations, and whether the status of in loco parentis exists is a question of fact to be determined from a full review of the facts and circumstances; among the factors to be considered are whether the party intends to assume all the obligations of parenthood, the level of involvement in the child's home, school, recreation and social activities, whether or not the party is receiving compensation for his or her services such as in a foster care arrangement, whether the party means to take the place of the lawful parent not only in providing support but also with respect to educating, instructing, and caring for the general welfare of the child, and whether the party has a true interest in the well-being and general welfare of the child.

Weinstein v. Gitters


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial and Special Term, Suffolk County


119 Misc. 2d 122; 462 N.Y.S.2d 553; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3470



April 29, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore the petitioner's restraining notice served upon the respondent bank is dismissed without prejudice for failure to serve the judgment debtor in the manner prescribed by statute ( CPLR 5222, subds [d], [e]) and the petition of G. E. Weinstein in this turnover proceeding is dismissed without prejudice for the above-stated reason and for failure to serve a necessary party in the matter prescribed by statute ( CPLR 1001, 308, subd 4).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In turnover proceedings, petitioner judgment creditor filed a motion pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5222 to enforce a judgment by requesting an order compelling respondent bank to pay over certain sums of money held in savings accounts in the name of respondents, judgment debtor and his wife.


OVERVIEW: The judgment creditor secured a judgment against the judgment debtor and his wife and served a restraining notice upon the bank to pay over certain sums of money held in a savings account in the names of the judgment debtor and his wife. In turnover proceedings against the judgment debtor and the bank, the judgment creditor filed a motion pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5222 to compel the bank to pay over the sums of money. The court dismissed the restraining order for failure to serve the judgment debtor in the manner prescribed by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5222(d), (e). The court also dismissed the petition in the turnover proceeding for the same reason and for failure to serve a necessary party in the manner prescribed by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 1001, 308(4). Only one copy of the notice and verified petition had been attached to the door of the judgment debtor, and only one copy had been mailed to the usual place of abode; however, there were two parties, the judgment debtor and his wife, so no service was effected upon one of the parties.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the judgment creditor's restraining notice served upon the bank and the judgment debtor's petition in the turnover proceeding.


HEADNOTES

Execution -- Restraining Notice -- Notice to Judgment Debtor before Service

A restraining notice served by petitioner judgment creditor upon respondent bank regarding money the bank holds in two separate joint savings accounts of respondent judgment debtor and his wife must be dismissed due to the failure of petitioner to comply with new statutory notice procedures which dictate the requisite notice that must be given to a judgment debtor before service of a restraining notice ( CPLR 5222, subd [d]) and the content of such notice which must include a warning that the judgment debtor may consult an attorney ( CPLR 5222, subd [e]); in addition, the turnover proceeding must be dismissed for failure to properly effectuate service on [***2] both parties to the joint accounts by "nail and mail" service ( CPLR 308, subd 4) inasmuch as only one copy was affixed to the door and only one copy was mailed to the usual place of abode.

Council for Owner Occupied Housing, Inc. v. Koch


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


119 Misc. 2d 241; 462 N.Y.S.2d 762; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3495



April 25, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion of plaintiffs for judgment declaring section YYYY51-1.0 et seq. of the Administrative Code of the City of New York unconstitutional is denied and the cross motions of the defendants for summary judgment dismissing the action is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sponsors brought an action against defendant mayor challenging the constitutionality of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § YYYY51-1.0 et seq. (1983) (code). The mayor filed a cross-motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The code regulated the conversion of rental units in the city to co-operative or condominium status. The code required that the sponsor of a conversion establish a reserve fund for repairs or improvements and post housing violations. Under N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 352-e (statute), the attorney general promulgated rules for the submission of conversion plans. Those rules did not require reserve funds or the posting of violations. The court held that the code was neither inconsistent with nor preempted by state law. Only if the state evidenced a desire to exclusively occupy an entire filed was the city powerless to act. But the statute was essentially a disclosure law of facts needed to guide investors. The fact that state law did not require posting and reserve funds did not render the code inconsistent with state law. Inconsistency required that the municipal law contradicted or was incompatible with state law. The code supplemented the state regulatory process and met the city's special housing problems. Thus, the court denied the corporation's motion and granted the mayor' cross-motion.


OUTCOME: The court denied the corporation's motion to have the code section declared unconstitutional, and granted the mayor's cross-motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Condominiums and Co-operatives -- Conversion Plan -- Reserve Fund for Capital Repairs

Local Law No. 70 of 1982 of the City of New York (Administrative Code of City of New York, § YYYY51-1.0 et seq.), which requires the sponsor of a conversion of rental units to co-operative or condominium status to establish a reserve fund equal to 3% of the total purchase price of the conversion to be used exclusively for capital repairs, replacements or improvements and to also post a listing of all violations of record filed against the building, is not unconstitutional on the ground that the State has pre-empted the field of co-operative and condominium conversions since the State regulatory statutes are in essence disclosure laws mandating disclosure of the minimum facts necessary to guide potential investors in making a decision (General Business Law, §§ 352-e, 352-eeee) and do not evidence an intent to occupy the [***2] entire multifaceted field of conversion to the exclusion of local laws; nor is the local law invalid for being inconsistent with existing State laws and regulations (see 13 NYCRR subch B, parts 16-21) which do not require a sponsor to establish a reserve fund or to post building violations since the local law merely supplements the State regulatory process and represents a reasonable exercise of the legislative power to meet the special housing problems existing in the city.

 

1757 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Everett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Bronx County


118 Misc. 2d 900; 462 N.Y.S.2d 151; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3416



April 22, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion for protection order vacating and setting aside District Attorney's demand for a bill of particulars with respect to alibi defense, and alternative relief, is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion for a protective order vacating and setting aside the district attorney's demand for a bill of particulars with respective to defendant's alibi defense.


OVERVIEW: The district attorney demanded that defendant file a bill of particulars with respect to his alibi defense. The court denied defendant's motion for a protective order. The court did not agree with defendant's contention that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 250.20(2) was unconstitutional. Section 250.20(2) provided that within a reasonable time after receipt of defendant's witness list but not later than 10 days before trial, the people must serve upon defendant and file a copy thereof with the court, a list of witnesses the people proposed to offer in rebuttal to discredit the defendant's alibi at the trial. A witness who would testify that defendant was at the scene of the crime was not such an alibi rebuttal witness. The court held that § 250.20(2) embodied the reciprocal discovery that was required to ensure fundamental fairness. Although § 250.20(2) did not provide defendant with as many discovery rights as Fla. R. Crim. Pro. 1.200, the legislature's desire to prevent witnesses from being harassed was a legitimate governmental interest that justified its exclusion of those witnesses who would testify that defendant was present at the scene of the crime.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion for a protective order vacating and setting aside the district attorney's demand for a bill of particulars with respective to defendant's alibi defense.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Alibi Witness -- Reciprocal Discovery Except for Witnesses to Crime

 CPL 250.20 (subd 2), which provides that the People, following receipt of the names and addresses of the defendant's alibi witnesses, must likewise supply the defendant with "a list of witnesses the people propose to offer in rebuttal to discredit the defendant's alibi at the trial" except that a "witness who will testify that the defendant was at the scene of the crime is not such an alibi rebuttal witness", is not unconstitutional because of a "lack of meaningful reciprocal discovery" since the limitation on the disclosure of the names and addresses of witnesses to a crime at the pretrial stage serves a legitimate governmental interest of avoiding the possibility of harassment of witnesses, and, moreover, the restricted reciprocal discovery as it applies to alibi is entitled to a strong presumption of constitutionality; however, the People, for tactical advantage, may not withhold a witness whose testimony would be properly [***2] presentable on its direct case and use such witness in rebuttal to the alibi defense.

People v. Dinan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Long Beach, Nassau County


118 Misc. 2d 857; 461 N.Y.S.2d 724; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3412



April 19, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L. 170.30(1)(a) to dismiss the information charging him with aggravated harassment, N.Y. Penal Law § 240.30, alleging that the statute was unconstitutional and that it violated the freedom of speech and due process clauses of both the Federal and State Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with aggravated harassment, N.Y. Penal Law § 240.30, after he attempted to enter the victims' home, cursing and making threats. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the information under N.Y. C.P.L. 170.30(1)(a), alleging that the statute was unconstitutional and that it violated the freedom of speech and due process clauses of both the Federal and State Constitutions. The court denied the motion. Suppression of speech under the statute was incidental and not the primary purpose of the law. In any event, such fighting words were not entitled to absolute protection. The state had the right to provide for the public good and promulgate laws to that end. The statute was a valid exercise of police power where there was a reasonable relationship between the public welfare and the conduct proscribed. Unconstitutionality of the statute was not shown beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the information on constitutional and sufficiency grounds.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Harassment

1. Subdivision 3 of section 240.30 of the Penal Law, which provides that a person is guilty of aggravated harassment in the second degree when, with intent to harass, annoy, threaten or alarm another person, he subjects another person to physical contact or attempts or threatens to do the same because of the race, religion or national origin of such person, is not unconstitutional; the purpose of the statute is to condemn a particular type of harassment and it may incidentally inhibit freedom of speech to the degree that any accompanying racial epithet could be used as evidence to establish an element of the crime; further, a statute defining a crime is a valid exercise of police power so long as there is a reasonable relationship between the public welfare and the conduct proscribed.

Crimes -- Information -- Harassment

2. An information charging defendant with aggravated harassment in the second degree (Penal Law, § 240.30, subd 3) in that, with intent to harass, annoy, threaten or alarm another, defendant, while attempting to enter the complainants' home, threatened that he was "going to get you * * * Jews" is not defective; the meaning of [***2] the quoted words is a question of fact for the jury in light of the entire incident.

People v. McCarthy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Village Court of New York, Muttontown, Suffolk County


119 Misc. 2d 263; 462 N.Y.S.2d 965; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3498



April 15, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state charged defendant landowner with violations of Muttontown, N.Y., Building Zoning Ordinance, arising from work done on his stable. Defendant asserted that he did not receive a copy of the charges against him; thus, he did not have proper notice, and that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with zoning violations arising from work done on his property without a building permit. At the conclusion of the trial, defendant filed a motion to dismiss the case on the ground that he did not receive proper notice and that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague. The court denied both motions, holding that notice had been sent to defendant by certified mail signed for by a workman at the property. The court determined that the workman was defendant's agent for purposes of receiving notice. The court found that defendant failed to disprove that he had received notice. Finally, the court held that the Muttontown, N.Y., Building Zoning Ordinance was sufficiently specific to overcome the constitutional challenge.


OUTCOME: The court denied both motions. The parties would be notified by the clerk of a date for sentencing.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Proper Service of Notice of Violation -- Implied Agency

A letter mailed to defendant by the village via certified mail, return receipt requested, received and signed for by a workman at defendant's residence, which notified defendant that he was being charged with a violation of the village zoning ordinance for having work done on a stable on his premises without a required building permit, constitutes proper service of a notice of a zoning violation so as to afford defendant adequate notice and knowledge of the charges against him since the receipt of the letter by the workman is attributed to defendant under the theory of implied agency inasmuch as the workman's acceptance of a letter addressed to defendant creates the natural inference, unrebutted by defendant, that he was authorized to do so and that he gave the letter to defendant; the notice requirement of the zoning ordinance is not unconstitutionally vague since it plainly and clearly states what the prohibited conduct is.

 

1760 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bunt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Justice Court of New York, Town of Rhinebeck, Dutchess County


118 Misc. 2d 904; 462 N.Y.S.2d 142; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3417



April 14, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an original proceeding before the Justice County of New York, Town of Rhinebeck, Dutchess County Court, defendant filed a motion that the statute under which he was charged, N.Y. Agric. & Mkts Law § 353, relating to the prevention of cruelty to animals, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the information charging him with beating a dog in violation of N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 353. Defendant argued that (1) the statute was irreconcilable with the due process requirement since the acts specified in the introductory lines was not qualified while subsequently they were qualified by the adverb "unjustifiably;" (2) that the words "wilfully set on foot, instigates, engages in, or in any way furthers any act of cruelty to any animal" absent the adverb qualifier unjustifiably "prohibited virtually any and all human conduct" towards animals; and (3) that it was not clear which animals were included. The court held that the statute was constitutional, although not well drafted. The court found that defendant was amply informed of the prohibited activity and that the statute contained provisions to allow the question of justification to be raised and considered. The court noted that the statute set forth a legitimate legislative activity in prohibiting the unjustified, needless, and wanton inhumanity towards animals and was not so broad that it failed to meet the standard of due process, yet it allowed sufficient room for application to varied situations.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion and held that the statute was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Animals -- Cruelty to Animals -- Constitutionality of Statute

 Section 353 of the Agriculture and Markets Law, which provides that a person who overdrives, overloads, tortures or cruelly beats or unjustifiably injures, maims, mutilates or kills any animal, or causes, procures or permits any animal to be overdriven, overloaded, tortured, cruelly beaten, or unjustifiably injured, maimed, mutilated or killed or who willfully sets on foot, instigates, engages in, or in any way furthers any act of cruelty to any animal or any act tending to produce such cruelty, is guilty of a misdemeanor, is constitutional, although not well drafted; a defendant is amply informed of the prohibited activity, the statute contains provisions to allow the question of justification to be raised and considered, and sets forth a legitimate legislative activity in prohibiting the unjustified, needless and wanton inhumanity towards animals; the statute is not so broad that it fails to meet the standard of due process, yet it allows sufficient room for application to varied situations.

 

1761 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Briggins v. McGuire


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


118 Misc. 2d 964; 462 N.Y.S.2d 357; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3429



April 13, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, police department and officers (department), filed a motion to reargue an earlier motion to dismiss petitioner officer's action for reinstatement, which had been denied by the court.


OVERVIEW: The officer was convicted of forgery, and was terminated by the department. The officer's conviction was subsequently reversed. The officer sought reinstatement. The department refused to reinstate the officer or to grant him a hearing. The officer filed a petition for reinstatement. The department filed a motion to dismiss. The court denied the motion. After a case relied upon by the court was reversed on appeal, the department sought reargument. The court granted the motion for reargument, but reaffirmed its prior judgment as modified. The court determined that the officer had a property interest in his job, but that the property interest was extinguished when the officer was convicted of a crime. N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 30(1)(e). The court held that in order to harmonize § 30(1)(e) with N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 470.20, which stated that the courts should take any necessary corrective action to rectify any injustice suffered by a person whose conviction was reversed, the officer's property interest in his job was revived after the conviction was reversed. The court held that the officer was not entitled to an automatic reinstatement, but was entitled to a hearing.


OUTCOME: The court granted the department's motion for reargument, but reaffirmed its prior judgment denying the department's motion to dismiss the officer's action for reinstatement, except that the court dismissed that portion of the action which sought automatic reinstatement.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Public Officers -- Removal from Office upon Felony Conviction -- Posttermination Hearing upon Reversal of Conviction

Petitioners, New York City police officers, who were automatically dismissed from their permanent civil service positions by operation of law upon their felony convictions (Public Officers Law, § 30, subd 1, par e), retain a property interest in their former positions upon the reversal of their convictions entitling them to due process protection and a posttermination hearing, but not to back pay for the period between conviction and reversal or automatic reinstatement by court order prior to a determination after hearing, since the operation of the statute vacating the "office" of a "public officer" upon a felony conviction is subject to the condition that a "conviction" exists and, upon reversal of the convictions, petitioners should, so far as reasonably possible, be treated as though they had never been accused at all and given the same opportunity to be heard prior to termination as any other civil service employee who is charged with misconduct (see Civil Service Law, § 75); even if petitioners are not entitled to a full disciplinary hearing they are [***2] at least entitled to a name-clearing hearing.

 

1762 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gilmore


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Mount Vernon


120 Misc. 2d 741; 468 N.Y.S.2d 965; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3790



April 13, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For these reasons the motion to suppress the film cassettes and the recorder which were seized is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the charges against him for obscenity in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 235.05 and promoting prostitution in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 245.02. Defendant also sought to suppress videotapes seized from his bar without a warrant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant operated a bar. A police officer entered the bar and observed naked dancers and a sexually explicit movie. Based upon the officer's observations and without obtaining a warrant, police officers entered the bar and seized videotapes containing sexually explicit material. Defendant was charged with promoting prostitution in violation of § 245.02 and obscenity in violation of § 235.05. Defendant filed a motion challenging the constitutionality of the statutes and seeking to suppress the videotapes from his trial. Upon review, the court upheld the statutes and granted defendant's motion to suppress the videotapes. The court held that defendant did not have standing to challenge the definition of obscenity in § 235.05 based upon its inclusion of sadism and masochism because he was not charged with promoting sadism or masochism. The court ruled that § 245.02 did not violate the equal protection clause by referring only to nudity by females. The court held that communicative materials could not be seized solely based upon a police officer's conclusion that the materials were obscene, so the warrantless seizure of the videotapes was unlawful.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the statutes prohibiting obscenity and the promotion of prostitution. The court granted defendant's motion to suppress the videotapes from his trial for obscenity and promoting prostitution.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Obscenity -- Permitting Prostitution and Promoting Exposure of Female

1. Defendant, charged with obscenity for showing movies depicting obscene sexual acts in his bar (Penal Law, § 235.05, subd 1), permitting prostitution (Penal Law, § 230.40) and promoting the exposure of a female (Penal Law, § 245.02) for permitting females to walk naked about his bar, may not challenge the obscenity statute on the ground that the inclusion of sadism and masochism renders the statute overbroad since that part of the statute has no application to the charges against him; the use of the phrases "contemporary community standards" and "patently offensive manner" in the definition of obscenity (Penal Law, § 235.00), consistent with the standards and guidelines established by the United States Supreme Court in obscenity cases, does not render the obscenity statutes unconstitutional for vagueness since the statutes are more than adequate to advise as to the proscribed conduct when measured by common understanding and practices; the statute proscribing promoting the exposure [***2] of a female is not unconstitutionally overbroad since the exposure of the female body in furtherance of artistic pursuits and endeavors is not threatened by the statute nor is the statute violative of equal protection for dealing solely with the exposure of females; the charge of aiding and abetting prostitution is not invalid merely because there is no allegation that defendant failed to make reasonable efforts to halt or abate the prostitution since that neglect is fairly implied from the claim that he facilitated prostitution on his premises.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Obscene Films

2. Communicative materials such as writings and films, being protected by the First Amendment, may not ordinarily be seized without a warrant and warrants to seize such material may not be issued if the determination of obscenity is made solely by a police officer without close judicial supervision and scrutiny unless there are "exigent circumstances" in which police action must be "now or never" to preserve the evidence of the crimes; accordingly, films depicting allegedly obscene sexual acts which were seized from defendant's bar without a warrant by police officers who had [***3] previously watched the films on the bar's television screen must be suppressed since there were no "exigent circumstances" which justified the warrantless search and seizure inasmuch as the police officers had ample opportunity to describe the films to a Judge and to thereby obtain a warrant for the seizure nor were the films so unidentifiable that they were incapable of being specifically described in a warrant.

 

1763 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Hercules Chemical Co. v. VCI, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


118 Misc. 2d 814; 462 N.Y.S.2d 129; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3405



April 11, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant property owner made a motion pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6514(b), or alternatively, N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6515, to cancel the notice of pendency plaintiff company filed against a building owned by defendant. Plaintiff opposed the motion to cancel and cross-moved for leave to conduct discovery regarding the circumstances surrounding defendant's acquisition of the subject building.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff company sought to impose a constructive trust upon the property of defendant property owner, but provided no allegations as to how the corporate funds were diverted to enable defendant to purchase the subject property. Defendant came forward with substantial evidence indicating that no such diversion of funds took place and made a motion pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6514(b) or alternatively N.Y. C.P.L.R. 6515, to cancel the notice of pendency. In its cross-motion, plaintiff acknowledged the court's authority to consider the merits of its claim, and requested leave to conduct further discovery to adequately respond to the allegations raised by defendant. The court held that where a substantial question was raised by a defendant intent on selling as to whether the property which was the subject of the lis pendens was also, truly, the subject of the plaintiff's action, a court was constitutionally required to determine that claim. The court granted the cross-motion for leave to conduct discovery and stated that upon completion of the discovery, defendant could renew her motion to cancel the notice of pendency.


OUTCOME: The court held that it had a constitutional obligation to provide defendant property owner with an opportunity for a review of the notice of pendency on the merits. The court granted plaintiff company's motion for leave to conduct discovery, and stated that upon completion of the discovery, defendant could renew her motion to cancel the notice of pendency, which would then be considered on the merits.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Lis Pendens -- Cancellation

CPLR 6501 et seq., the notice of pendency statute, must be read to grant the court the authority to consider the underlying merits of an action where an aggrieved property owner moves to cancel a lis pendens; while the filing of a notice of pendency may not amount to the taking of a "significant property interest", the interference is sufficient to trigger the constitutional requirements of due process. Accordingly, plaintiff is granted leave to conduct discovery and, upon completion of discovery, defendant may renew her motion to cancel a notice of pendency, which shall be considered on the merits, where plaintiff seeks to impose a constructive trust upon defendant's real property, but provides no allegations regarding how certain funds belonging to a corporation that is allegedly indebted to plaintiff were diverted to enable defendant to purchase the property, and defendant has come forward with substantial evidence indicating that no such diversion of funds took place.

 

1764 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


National Enerdrill Corp. v. Crown Drilling, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


119 Misc. 2d 162; 462 N.Y.S.2d 533; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3479



April 6, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation filed a motion for summary judgment in lieu of complaint against defendant nonresident corporation, seeking to collect on six promissory notes executed by the nonresident corporation.


OVERVIEW: The corporation in its affidavit in support of its motion for summary judgment in lieu of complaint did not state that the nonresident corporation was doing business in New York, or that the cause of action in some manner factually related to New York. The nonresident corporation did not file papers in opposition to the corporation's motion. The court denied the corporation's motion with leave to renew. The court held that the corporation failed to establish the requirements for the court's exercise of long-arm jurisdiction over the nonresident corporation pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 301 or N.Y. C.P.L.R. 302 by failing to adequately establish that the nonresident corporation was doing business in New York or that the corporation's cause of action was factually related to New York. The court held that the corporation's allegation that upon information and belief that the nonresident corporation maintained an office in New York County, New York, was insufficient to establish the court's personal jurisdiction over the nonresident corporation.


OUTCOME: The court denied the corporation's motion with leave to renew.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Courts -- Jurisdiction -- Long-Arm Jurisdiction

1. Plaintiff's motion for summary judgment in lieu of complaint against defendant, an Ohio corporation personally served in that State, based on a series of six promissory notes is denied with leave to renew since the allegation, upon information and belief, that defendant is authorized to conduct business in this State and has its principal office in New York County is insufficient to show that defendant is doing business in New York for purposes of obtaining long-arm jurisdiction and the court may not grant judgment absent at least a minimal showing of jurisdiction.

Interest -- Statutory 9% Rate on Claims and Judgments

2. The proper rate of interest applicable to a motion for summary judgment in lieu of complaint based on a series of six promissory notes is the statutory rate of 9% as provided in the 1981 amendment to CPLR 5004 since, although the title of the 1981 bill (L 1981, ch 258) stated that its purpose was to amend the CPLR "in relation to the rate of interest on judgments", the title of a bill does not become part of the enactment and CPLR 5004 clearly covers claims and verdicts as well as judgments.

 

1765 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


M. H. v. C. M.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


118 Misc. 2d 810; 461 N.Y.S.2d 696; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3404



March 31, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Respondent's application to vacate is denied, and the registered child support order is confirmed. (Domestic Relations Law, § 37-a, subd 6, par [b].)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent child support provider filed an application to vacate petitioner child's attempt to register a foreign child support order.


OVERVIEW: The child support provider's separation agreement was not incorporated in the divorce degree, which was obtained in a foreign state. The agreement contained provisions regarding child support. The foreign court forwarded an order of child support to be filed by the court. The child support provider filed an action to vacate the registration of the foreign child support order. The court found the fact that the foreign court forwarded papers that incorporated a modification of child support was evidence that the incorporation of the separation agreement as modified was permitted the divorce decree. Accordingly, the court confirmed the registration of the child support order by the foreign state.


OUTCOME: The court denied the application to vacate.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Support -- Uniform Support of Dependents Law

Respondent father's application to vacate the registration of a foreign child support order is denied and the order is confirmed (Domestic Relations Law, § 37-a), where a Maryland court filed and forwarded to a New York Family Court a divorce decree which incorporated but did not merge the parties' separation agreement containing a specific provision for child support; whether Maryland law permits the parties to modify the support obligation in the separation agreement without court approval is not relevant in the instant case, as the filing and forwarding of the papers containing the separation agreement indicates that the particular modification bears the imprimatur of the Maryland court. Further, the fact that arrears may eventually be sought also does not preclude registration and, in any event, registration would not entitle petitioner to arrears, which may be sought in a supplementary proceeding following registration. Finally, section 37-a of the [***2] Domestic Relations Law is not unconstitutional on the ground that it permits enforcement without due notice or an opportunity to be heard, since the statute affords a respondent mail notice of registration; moreover, a respondent against whom a judgment was entered in another State was presumably subject to the jurisdiction of the foreign court and given a full and fair opportunity to defend the underlying action.

 

1766 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re In Rem Tax Foreclosure Action


Serial No. 9099, Section 51, Block 11610, Lot 249


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


118 Misc. 2d 1081; 462 N.Y.S.2d 113; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3444



March 25, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city initiated a tax foreclosure proceeding against respondent property owner. The city subsequently filed a motion for summary judgment. The property owner filed a cross-motion for leave to verify its answer and to consolidate this proceeding with another foreclosure action.


OVERVIEW: With respect to the branch of the property owner's cross-motion requesting consolidation, the court observed that the pleadings in the second proceeding had not been provided to the court. Consequently, the court denied that branch of the motion because it was impossible for the court to determine whether the issues were identical. The court further ruled that the city was entitled to summary judgment. In so ruling, the court observed that the property owner did not plead that he was not diligent in the payment of his taxes or that the property was exempt. As such, the city made out a prima facie case. The court rejected the property owner's claim that he was entitled to a set-off noting that New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § D17-9.0(d) (current version at New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § D17-9.0(c)) did not provide for counter-claims. Moreover, judgment for the city on the tax lien did not amount to an unconstitutional taking because New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § D17-9.0(e) (current version at New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § D17-9.0(d)) provided for a six-month delay in foreclosure to permit the property owner to sell the property or satisfy the lien.


OUTCOME: The court granted the branch of the property owner's cross-motion for leave to verify the answer. The court denied the remainder of the property owner's cross-motion. The court also granted the city's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- Tax Liens -- Burden of Sale where Property Value Exceeds Lien

A property owner may not assert his entitlement to a setoff in an in rem tax foreclosure proceeding brought by the City of New York since subdivision c of section D17-9.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York specifically prohibits counterclaims in such proceedings; furthermore subdivision d of section D17-9.0 of the Administrative Code, which provides that a property owner in an in rem tax foreclosure proceeding who alleges in a verified answer that the property to be taken has a value substantially in excess of the tax lien is entitled to a six-month extension of time to sell the property, is constitutional notwithstanding the statute's placing the burden of sale on the property owner; while the Constitution prohibits the taking of property valued substantially in excess of the tax lien thereon without affording the owner an opportunity to recover the excess value, there is no constitutional requirement that the foreclosing governmental entity sell the property and recoup the surplus on behalf of the owner.

 

1767 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Adoption of G. F. C.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Kings County


118 Misc. 2d 705; 461 N.Y.S.2d 949; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3386



March 21, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, natural mother and her husband, instituted proceedings to adopt the child of the natural mother. Respondent natural father, who was incarcerated in a state correctional institution, challenged the proceedings on the grounds that N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111(2)(d), was unconstitutional as applied to him because under the statute, his consent to the adoption was not necessary.


OVERVIEW: The natural mother and her husband began proceedings to adopt the natural mother's child. The natural father was serving a sentence for a felony conviction in a state correctional institution. As a result, under the N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 111(2)(d), the natural father's consent to the proposed adoption was unnecessary. The natural father challenged the proceedings and argued that, as to him, the statute denied his rights to due process and equal protection of the law, and denied his child's associational rights. The court rejected the natural father's constitutional arguments. The court then held that, upon the evidence, including that there had been a prolonged interruption of the natural father's custody and visitation that was not entirely attributable to blameworthy conduct by the natural mother, that the child was integrated into a new family unit, and that it was doubtful that the child had any recollection of the natural father, the best interests of the child required that an order of adoption be entered.


OUTCOME: The court rejected the natural father's challenge to the adoption proceedings, and held that an order of adoption should be entered in favor of the child's natural mother and her husband.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Adoption -- Consent of Imprisoned Father

The court granted an order of adoption in the best interests of the child without requiring the consent of respondent natural father where he was imprisoned upon a conviction for armed robbery; section 111 (subd 2, par [d]) of the Domestic Relations Law is not unconstitutional as applied to respondent, in that it obviates the requirement of his consent to the child's adoption due to the deprivation of his civil rights (Civil Rights Law, § 79); respondent was not denied his right to procedural due process by his lack of veto power over the adoption since he enjoyed the assistance of appointed counsel at the adoption hearing and, in addition to a full and fair opportunity to voice his objections to the adoption, he had the opportunity by means of his own testimony and otherwise to establish the history of his own relationship with his natural son; respondent's right to equal protection and substantive due process were likewise not abridged since, if the law were not to permit the parental rights of a felon to be terminated so as to allow an adoption in the child's best interests, that fact would itself embody a choice favoring the parent [***2] over the child and the Constitution does not forbid selection of the alternative which promotes the public welfare; there has been a prolonged interruption of the respondent's custody and visitation which, to a great extent, is the consequence of his own moral turpitude; moreover, although petitioner mother contributed to said interruption by removing the child from this jurisdiction immediately following a prior grant of visitation to respondent, the mother's conduct has not been sufficiently egregious to warrant a denial of the adoption petition on public policy grounds.

 

1768 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Herloski


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Onondaga County


118 Misc. 2d 358; 460 N.Y.S.2d 470; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3320



March 17, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People requested that a hearing be held pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 400.16 to determine whether defendant was a persistent violent felony offender. Defendant, through his attorney, answered the motion contending that the persistent felony offender statute was unconstitutional as violative of the ex post facto clause of the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted, after a nonjury trial, of attempted murder in the second-degree, criminal possession of a weapon in the second-degree, grand larceny in the third degree, and criminal mischief in the fourth degree. Accordingly, the People requested that the court find defendant to be a violent felony offender. A two-prong test had been established to assist in the determination. The two-prong test asked whether defendant was convicted of a N.Y. Penal Law § 70.02(1) violent felony offense; and had defendant previously been subjected to two or more predicate violent felony convictions within the meaning of N.Y. Penal Law § 70.04(1). Since both attempted murder in the second degree and criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree were violent felony offenses as defined in N.Y. Penal Law § 70.02(1), the first prong of the two-prong test had been satisfied. However, the court found that defendant was not a violent felony offender at the time of his hearing because defendant's manslaughter convictions did not meet the second prong of the test; the sentence for the latter two convictions was imposed contemporaneously with the first.


OUTCOME: The court found that defendant did not qualify for treatment as a persistent violent felony offender. Based upon that determination, it was unnecessary to address defendant's contention that a determination of persistent felony offender was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Persistent Violent Felony Offender

Defendant cannot be sentenced as a persistent violent felony offender (Penal Law, § 70.08) on the basis of three prior manslaughter convictions for which he received concurrent sentences upon his guilty pleas to three counts of a single indictment since a prior conviction is deemed a predicate violent felony conviction only if sentence "upon such prior conviction" was imposed "before commission of the present felony" (Penal Law, § 70.04, subd 1, par [b], cl [ii]) and defendant's convictions do not, therefore, constitute "two or more predicate violent felony convictions" necessary for persistent violent felony offender treatment inasmuch as the sentence for the latter two convictions was imposed contemporaneously with the first.

 

1769 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


A & C Sandblasting & Spraypainting, Inc. v. Albany


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Albany County


119 Misc. 2d 168; 463 N.Y.S.2d 983; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3481



March 16, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] By reason thereof, the plaintiff has failed to prove its cause of action and the motion to dismiss by the defendant is hereby granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city filed a motion to dismiss a complaint filed by plaintiff corporation for a declaratory judgment asking the court to declare an ordinance to be unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The city adopted an ordinance prohibiting the restoration of the brick or stone exterior of any building by sandblasting, wire brushing, or other abrasive means. The corporation requested a declaratory judgment that the ordinance was unconstitutional. It contended that the ordinance denied it from carrying on its brick restoration business and that the ordinance had no rational basis in its application. The city moved to dismiss the action. The court granted the motion. The evidence demonstrated that the city had a reasonable apprehension that abrasive methods to remove paint from brick surfaces damaged the bisque or outer covering of the brick and thereby were harmful or destructive to rehabilitation and renovations to brick buildings. Although the corporation's method caused less damage than other abrasive methods, it fell within the category of abrasives and did in fact remove the bisque. The corporation failed to overcome the presumption that the ordinance was constitutional. Further, that the ordinance did not prohibit other methods was not an equal protection violation. The evidence was that the abrasive method was entirely different from other methods.


OUTCOME: The court granted the city's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Sandblasting of Brick Buildings

Plaintiff, a sandblasting company which utilizes the allegedly "least damaging" "pressure grit washing" method of removing paint from existing brick buildings, has failed to demonstrate the unconstitutionality of an ordinance of the City of Albany that prohibits sandblasting and other abrasive methods of restoring the brick exterior of any building since there is a reasonable apprehension that abrasive methods of removing paint from brick surfaces are harmful to the rehabilitation and renovation of brick buildings and plaintiff's system, at best, still "causes some damage"; so long as the classification of abrasive cleaners has a reasonable or rational basis to the evil reasonably apprehended, there is no violation of equal protection merely because the ordinance does not include other paint removal methods.

 

1770 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


405 Co. v. State


Claim Nos. 66960, 67135, 67340


Court of Claims of New York


118 Misc. 2d 305; 460 N.Y.S.2d 455; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3310



March 14, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant taxpayers filed an action against the State of New York for a refund of taxes paid pursuant to a state tax law that had been repealed as of its effective date.


OVERVIEW: The state enacted a tax law on certain capital gains and the taxpayers paid the taxes. Thereafter, the state repealed the law and made the repeal retroactive to the effective date of the law thereby wiping it out as if it had never existed. Two of the taxpayers filed written requests for return of the taxes paid and one filed an action, prior to the laws repeal, to have the statute declared unconstitutional. Thereafter, all of the taxpayers filed actions for repayment of the tax. The State admitted the taxpayers were entitled to a refund but asserted that no mechanism was in place to refund the money and that the court lacked jurisdiction because the law was never declared unconstitutional. The court granted the refunds and held that it had jurisdiction to grant a money judgment because the actions for money had and received, though based upon equitable principals, were deemed actions in law. The failure of the State to put in place an administrative provision for the refund of the taxes did not prevent the court from granting the refunds, which were admittedly due.


OUTCOME: The court granted judgment in favor of the taxpayers against the State in their actions for refunds of taxes paid under a repealed statute.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] State -- Court of Claims -- Recovery of Moneys Paid under Repealed Tax Statute

The Court of Claims has jurisdiction over a claim to recover moneys paid to the State in satisfaction of taxes levied pursuant to a 1981 tax statute that was repealed in 1982 retroactive to the date of its enactment, the statute (Tax Law, former art 31-A) having assessed a tax upon capital gains resulting from the sale of commercial or industrial property in New York City, even though the statute was never declared unconstitutional or illegal and there is no statutory procedure providing for a refund since the repeal of the statute ab initio terminated the authority of the State to collect and retain the money paid pursuant to it so that the claim is primarily one for money damages against the State, a matter over which the Court of Claims has jurisdiction; the tax payments cannot be considered voluntary since they were paid under protest under the duress of a statute which provided that failure to pay the tax would place a lien on the property that would prevent closing of title; the failure of the State Comptroller to maintain a reserve fund for reimbursement of persons paying the tax does [***2] not give rise to a Federal civil rights claim (US Code, tit 42, § 1983) and, in any event, neither the State nor a department of the State is considered a "person" amenable to actions under the Federal statute.

 

1771 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Taylor v. Eli Haddad Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


118 Misc. 2d 253; 460 N.Y.S.2d 886; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3302



March 14, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The direction in my order to show cause dated October 26, 1982, that plaintiff pay use and occupancy is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tenant brought an action against defendant landlord to declare that the landlord waived any breach of the lease and that the Loft Law, N.Y. Mult. Dwell. Law § 285 was applicable. The landlord filed motions to dismiss the harassment and fraud causes, to strike the attorney's fees demand, and to direct the tenant to pay use and occupancy. The tenant obtained an order to stay eviction proceedings and sought an injunction.


OVERVIEW: The parties' lease required the tenant to use the premises as a photo studio. The tenant converted the space for joint living-working use. The landlord served a notice to cure. The court stayed the landlord from taking any steps to evict the tenant and tolled the running of the time under the notice to cure, provided that the tenant posted a bond and paid current use and occupancy and any arrears. The court found that (1) the tenant's motion for a preliminary injunction was viable, (2) the landlord's motion to dismiss the tenant's original complaint was moot, (3) his motion attacking the tenant's amended complaint was procedurally proper, (4) the landlord's constitutional attack on the Loft Law was both theoretical and premature, (5) injunctive relief for the landlord was not proper, (6) the landlord was estopped from relying on the lease's no-waiver clause, and (7) the tenant's harassment and fraud claims failed. The landlord allegedly instigated and participated in the tenant's breach of the lease's use and occupancy covenant by telling the tenant at the commencement of the lease that he could reside on the premises and by taking rent knowing that the tenant lived there.


OUTCOME: The court granted the tenant's motion for a preliminary injunction with conditions, granted in part and denied in part the landlord's motion to dismiss, and denied the landlord's motion challenging the constitutionality of the Loft Law.


HEADNOTES

Injunctions -- Preliminary Injunction -- Amended Pleading

1. Plaintiff tenant's service of an amended complaint does not affect the viability of his motion for a preliminary injunction brought under the original complaint requiring defendant landlord to stay efforts to enforce a notice to cure or to evict plaintiff because of his residential use of commercial loft premises since the pleading need not be annexed to the motion for a preliminary injunction when it is not sought on the basis of a cause of action for a permanent injunction and, consequently, the amendment of the pleading during the pendency of the motion, in a manner of no consequence to the application for a preliminary injunction can have no effect on its consideration; service of the amended complaint does not circumvent the motion addressed to the sufficiency of the original complaint and the court should address the amended pleading especially where defendant has made a second CPLR 3211 (subd [a], par 7) motion attacking the amended complaint; the single-motion rule of CPLR 3211 (subd [e])  [***2] is not offended when the second motion raised the ground of CPLR 3211 (subd [a], par 7) since a motion on this ground may be made at any time.

Landlord and Tenant -- Loft Law -- Injunctive Relief

2. Plaintiff tenant, who was served with a notice to cure his residential use of the demised loft premises in violation of the lease which provided that the premises were to be used only for a photo studio, has adequately pleaded the applicability of the new Loft Law (Multiple Dwelling Law, art 7-C) since the Loft Law, designed to protect residents of certain "interim multiple dwellings" that had previously been used for commercial purposes from eviction, is not limited to only artists and the question of whether three families lived in the building and whether residential use could be permitted by administrative certification cannot be resolved on the face of the pleading; defendant landlord's constitutional attack on the Loft Law based upon the claim that the law "denies the landlord the right to collect rent unless interim multiple dwelling status is conceded" is both theoretical and premature since the landlord's interest can be protected by conditioning injunctive relief [***3] in favor of tenants upon their payment of rent; plaintiff is entitled to injunctive relief in order to preserve his right to cure should he be in error regarding his joint living-working uses of the premises since plaintiff can cure without vacating the premises by simply discontinuing his residential use.

Landlord and Tenant -- Loft Law -- No-Waiver Clause -- Retaliatory Eviction

3. A no-waiver clause of a lease of loft premises which provides that the landlord's receipt of rent knowing of a breach does not constitute a waiver of that breach, the lease having limited the use of the premises for a photo studio, does not mandate dismissal of plaintiff tenant's waiver claim based upon defendant landlord's acceptance of rent with knowledge of plaintiff's residential use of the premises since defendant's alleged instigation and participation in the offending condition from the inception of the lease is so pervasive and active that it may be estopped from relying on the no-waiver clause; plaintiff, who was only served with a notice to cure his residential occupancy, does not state a cause of action for retaliatory eviction or harassment (Real Property Law, §§ 223-b, 235-d) based [***4] upon his efforts to secure protection under the new Loft Law (Multiple Dwelling Law, art 7-C) since the prohibited conduct does not embrace the landlord's attempt to enforce its legal rights under the literal wording of the lease; plaintiff's allegations that defendant falsely represented that his residential use would be permitted does not state a cause of action for fraud and since plaintiff is not alleging the breach of any covenant under the lease by the landlord, the existence of a clause in the lease in favor of the landlord for attorney's fees does not trigger plaintiff's right to recover attorney's fees (Real Property Law, § 234).

 

1772 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Hirschel v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


117 Misc. 2d 190; 460 N.Y.S.2d 438; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3147



March 2, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the State, a city, and certain senior citizen tenants, filed a motion to dismiss plaintiff landlords' complaint. The landlords filed a motion for summary judgment for the relief demanded in the complaint. The landlords had filed an action against defendants for a declaration that New York City, N.Y., Local Law 51 (1972), as amended by New York City, N.Y., Local Law 19 (1977), and 1980 N.Y. Laws 598 were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The landlords commenced a declaratory judgment action against defendants, contending that New York City, N.Y., Local Law 51 (1972), as amended by New York City, N.Y., Local Law 19 (1977), and 1980 N.Y. Laws 598 were invalid on the grounds that they were violative of the Urstadt Amendment, 1971 N.Y. Laws 372, they exceeded the authority granted the city by N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 467-b (1972), and that they violated the Fourteenth Amendment and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 7. The court granted the landlords' motion for summary judgment and denied defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint. The court held that New York City, N.Y., Local Law 51 (1972), as amended by New York City, N.Y., Local Law 19 (1977), and 1980 N.Y. Laws 598 were unconstitutional because they did not fully reimburse the landlords for the rent exemptions required therein. The sole imposition upon the city's landlords of the burden of affording the city's elderly poor with subsidized housing deprived the landlords of property without due compensation.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that two of the city's statutes were unconstitutional. The court also granted the landlords' motion for summary judgment and denied defendants' motion to dismiss the landlords' complaint.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Exemption for Needy Elderly

Local Law No. 51 of 1972 of the City of New York as amended (Local Laws, 1977, No. 19 of City of New York and L 1980, ch 598) which extended indefinitely a rent-control ordinance providing for a one-year and five-month rent increase exemption for elderly tenants of restricted income (Local Laws, 1970, No. 31 of City of New York) is unconstitutional in that it deprives landlords who own the buildings in which the needy elderly reside of their property without due process since these landlords are compelled to themselves partially absorb the costs of the rent increase exemptions on the basis that they are reimbursed by way of real estate tax abatements for the rent-regulated property. The City of New York should fully subsidize housing for its senior citizens by spreading the cost among the public taxpayers versus singling out individual landlords particularly in the event that the dollar amount of rent increase exemptions exceeds the total real estate tax offsets allowed in one year, thus causing landlords to incur losses that may not be recouped in a carry over tax abatement for the following year. Because [***2] of the impact on the elderly, judgment is stayed for a period of 90 days to permit the enactment of appropriate corrective legislation.

 

1773 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Department of Housing Preservation & Dev. v. Debona


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Queens County


118 Misc. 2d 990; 462 N.Y.S.2d 102; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3432



February 28, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Department of Housing Preservation and Development of New York commenced a proceeding for the imposition of civil penalties against respondent landlord for failure to provide hot water as required under the housing code. The landlord moved to dismiss on the grounds that the city administrative code resulted in an unconstitutional imposition of penalties without due process of law.


OVERVIEW: An inspector for the department determined that there was an inadequate supply of hot water at the landlord's building and placed a notice of violation sticker on a card in the hallway. The landlord claimed that he never received this notice and that he first became aware of the violation when he received an order to show cause. The administrative code, New York City, N.Y. Admin. Code. § D26-51.01(k) (1981), created a legal presumption that a condition constituting a violation continued from the day when the notice was posted until the landlord proved it had been corrected. The landlord argued that merely posting the sticker in the public hallway was not adequate notice for due process purposes to subject him to penalties based on the presumption of a continuing violation from the date of that notice. The court agreed. The court noted that, in addition to the posting of notice, there had previously been a requirement that notice be mailed to the person in charge of the premises or the owner or agent of any violation but that such requirement had been eliminated. Absent such a requirement, the court held that § D26-51.01(k) did not meet the test of procedural due process.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the department's petition for the imposition of civil penalties with prejudice.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Notice of Administrative Penalties

Subdivision (k) of section D26-51.01 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, which provides for the administrative imposition of civil penalties for a landlord's failure to comply with established heat and hot water minimum temperature standards, said fines commencing upon the posting of a notice of violation in a conspicuous place on the premises, is violative of the due process requirements of the Fourteenth Amendment since the statute, in failing to require a second notice by mail, does not provide for reasonable and adequate notice of the violation and penalty.

 

1774 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bailey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Bronx County


118 Misc. 2d 860; 462 N.Y.S.2d 94; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3413



February 22, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For all the foregoing reasons the People's application for a bail revocation hearing is granted, the hearing to be conducted forthwith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state filed an application with the court for a hearing pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 530.60(2) for the revocation of bail and remand of defendant.


OVERVIEW: In a multicount indictment against defendant for attempted murder in the second degree, the people made an application for a hearing for the revocation of bail and remand of defendant. Defendant challenged this application as time barred. The court held that the people's application for a bail revocation hearing was granted. The court found that there was no need to remand defendant for a hearing because he remained in custody after bail had been set as a result of his inability to make bail. The court held that defendant's remaining in custody for a period in excess of 72 hours from the time of the people's request for this hearing and the date of the hearing did not result in a denial of due process or an infringement upon any constitutional right.


OUTCOME: The court granted the people's application for a bail revocation hearing regarding a charge against defendant for attempted murder in the second degree.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Bail -- Revocation

 CPL 530.60 (subd 2) provides for bail revocation only in those instances where a defendant is charged with a felony and there is reasonable cause to believe that, while at liberty as a result of bail or an order of recognizance, he has committed a subsequent class A or violent felony offense; further, the defendant may be remanded to the custody of the Sheriff for a period not to exceed 72 hours pending a bail revocation hearing. However, defendant's remaining in custody for a period in excess of 72 hours between the time of the People's request for a bail revocation hearing and the date of the hearing did not result in a denial of due process or an infringement upon constitutional rights, where defendant was already in custody as a result of his inability to make the bail set for a violent felony offense he allegedly committed while at liberty on an order of recognizance issued pursuant to CPL 180.80 on a prior felony charge; under these circumstances, there was no need to remand defendant for a hearing because [***2] he had remained in custody after bail was set.

 

1775 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Santana


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Bronx County


117 Misc. 2d 1016; 459 N.Y.S.2d 705; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3244



February 10, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of robbery in the second degree and assault in the second degree and faced sentence as a persistent violent felony offender pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 70.08. Defendant objected to being so classified arguing the statute was unconstitutional as applied to him because it was in effect an ex post facto law.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced in June 1977 and received 2 to 6 years on each indictment, said terms to run concurrently. On September 1, 1978, N.Y. Penal Law § 70.02 was enacted defining violent felony offenses which required minimum sentences. Although criminal possession of a weapon, third degree, was not at that time included, further amendment of the statute in 1980 did include that crime. The court stated that both § 70.02 as amended and N.Y. Penal Law § 70.08 were enacted after defendant was sentenced. The issue was whether subjecting him to a persistent violent offender sentence for the conviction that occurred in November of 1982 was in fact ex post facto because it unconstitutionally elevated the degree of his punishment. The court held that it was not. There was no difference in legislative intent between the persistent violent offender statute, § 70.08, and N.Y. Penal Law § 70.02, which defined violent felony offenses requiring minimum sentences of imprisonment. Both enhanced punishment to recidivists - a compelling State interest. The manner in which defendant's prior sentences were imposed was totally irrelevant for the purpose of determining violent felony offender status.


OUTCOME: The court adjudicated defendant as a persistent violent felony offender and he was sentenced to a term of 8 years to life imprisonment, the minimum for a class D felony under the statute.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Persistent Violent Felony Offender

Criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree was not defined as a violent felony offense under section 70.02 of the Penal Law at the time of defendant's conviction of three counts of said crime; however, these convictions may properly serve as prior violent felony convictions under the amended statute, subjecting defendant to enhanced sentencing as a persistent violent felony offender pursuant to section 70.08 of the Penal Law upon his subsequent conviction of robbery in the second degree and assault in the second degree. The statute is not an ex post facto law as applied to defendant, since section 70.02, as amended, and section 70.08 of the Penal Law were in force and effect prior to defendant's commission of the act for which he now faces sentencing and section 70.08 increases punishment for the present violation only; further, the fact that the sentences imposed upon defendant's prior, multiple convictions ran concurrently, does not alter his status as an individual with two or more previous violent felony convictions.

 

1776 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Methodist Hospital of Brooklyn v. State Ins. Fund


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


117 Misc. 2d 178; 459 N.Y.S.2d 521; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3146



February 3, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The court denies plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment and grants defendants' cross motion for summary judgment declaring that the provisions of chapters 55 and 404 of the Laws of 1982 considered herein are constitutionally valid.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the State Insurance Fund policyholders, and defendants, the State Insurance Fund and its administrators, made cross-motions for summary judgment regarding the constitutional validity of 1982 N.Y. Laws 55 and 404. The State Insurance Fund (SIF) concurred with the policyholders' arguments.


OVERVIEW: The legislation provided for transfer of monies from the SIF, which provided workers' compensation insurance, to the New York State General Fund. Upon consideration, the court granted the administrators' motion for summary judgment. The court held that ch. 55 and ch. 404 did not impair any contractual obligation to the policyholders because prospective losses of dividends under future policies were not within the impairment clause. The court also held that the policyholder had no protected property interest in the SIF because it was a state agency whose monies belonged to the state. The court additionally noted that property was not being taken but only transferred. The court found that an improper debt was not created because the SIF was a state agency so, therefore, no debt was created by the state in favor of a separate entity. The court held that the legislation did not create an improper tax because the policyholders were not forced to make a contribution. The court noted that, even if the legislation created a tax, it would be permissible as a special tax on one class of property.


OUTCOME: The court granted the administrators' motion for summary judgment regarding the constitutional validity of legislation regarding the transfer of funds from the SIF to the New York State General Fund.


HEADNOTES

Workers' Compensation -- Appropriations to State Insurance Fund

1. Chapters 55 (§ 92, subd 2) and 404 of the Laws of 1982, amending the Workers' Compensation Law by mandating the transfer of $ 190 million from the State Insurance Fund to the State's general fund, and adding section 87-f, providing for the appropriation of moneys to the State Insurance Fund, do not constitute an unconstitutional impairment of plaintiff policyholders' contractual obligations with the State Insurance Fund in violation of section 10 of article I of the United States Constitution. Plaintiffs have failed to show that they possess contractual rights as to insurance premium rates and the disposition of reserves from the Workers' Compensation Law and policies; nor have they proven a "substantial impairment" or even an "incidental" effect upon their alleged contract rights.

Workers' Compensation -- Appropriations to State Insurance Fund

2. Chapters 55 (§ [***2] 92, subd 2) and 404 of the Laws of 1982, amending the Workers' Compensation Law by mandating the transfer of $ 190 million from the State Insurance Fund to the State's general fund, and adding section 87-f, providing for the appropriation of moneys to the State Insurance Fund, do not effect a taking of property without due process of law, since plaintiff policyholders have failed to show a property interest in State Insurance Fund assets and contractual rights to certain insurance rates and dividends; the State Insurance Fund is a State agency whose moneys are State moneys rather than fiduciary funds; nor is the property herein being taken, but, rather, it is being internally transferred within the State financial structure and a substitute fund is being legislatively created in lieu thereof.

Workers' Compensation -- Appropriations to State Insurance Fund

3. Chapters 55 (§ 92, subd 2) and 404 of the Laws of 1982, amending the Workers' Compensation Law by mandating the transfer of $ 190 million from the State Insurance Fund to the State's general fund, and adding section 87-f, providing for the appropriation of moneys to the State Insurance Fund, do not constitute a legislative [***3] intrusion upon the discretionary powers of officers of the executive branch; section 18 of article I of the New York Constitution expressly provides for a broad scope of legislative power over the field of workers' compensation.

Workers' Compensation -- Appropriations to State Insurance Fund

4.
