Chapters 55 (§ 92, subd 2) and 404 of the Laws of 1982, amending the Workers' Compensation Law by mandating the transfer of $ 190 million from the State Insurance Fund to the State's general fund, and adding section 87-f, providing for the appropriation of moneys to the State Insurance Fund, do not violate article VII (§ 8, subd 1) of the New York Constitution which prohibits a loan or gift of the State's credit; the section is inapplicable since the State Insurance Fund is a State agency rather than a corporate entity.

Workers' Compensation -- Appropriations to State Insurance Fund

5. Chapters 55 (§ 92, subd 2) and 404 of the Laws of 1982, amending the Workers' Compensation Law by mandating the transfer of $ 190 million from the State Insurance Fund to the State's general fund, and adding section 87-f, providing for the appropriation of moneys to the State Insurance [***4] Fund, do not create a debt from the State to the State Insurance Fund in violation of sections 9 and 11 of article VII of the New York Constitution, referring to short- and long-term debts; the State Insurance Fund is a State agency, and no debt was created in favor of a separate entity.

Workers' Compensation -- Appropriations to State Insurance Fund

6. Chapters 55 (§ 92, subd 2) and 404 of the Laws of 1982, amending the Workers' Compensation Law by mandating the transfer of $ 190 million from the State Insurance Fund to the State's general fund, and adding section 87-f, providing for the appropriation of moneys to the State Insurance Fund, do not create an appropriation scheme prohibited by section 7 of article VII of the New York Constitution; the present statutory scheme does not involve impoundment but instead provides for an expenditure within the two-year limitation measured from the time of the appropriation.

Workers' Compensation -- Appropriations to State Insurance Fund

7. Chapters 55 (§ 92, subd 2) and 404 of the Laws of 1982, amending the Workers' Compensation Law by mandating the transfer of $ 190 million from the State Insurance Fund to the State's [***5] general fund, and adding section 87-f, providing for the appropriation of moneys to the State Insurance Fund, do not violate section 22 of article III of the New York Constitution which requires a tax and its object to be distinctly stated; the transfer of funds and consequent loss of interest income are not a tax since no forced contribution of plaintiffs is involved and since insurance coverage is available elsewhere.

 

1777 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bradshaw v. Silversmith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


122 Misc. 2d 544; 472 N.Y.S.2d 237; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4138



January 21, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, dog pound employee and police department, sought a motion to dismiss plaintiff accused's complaint filed after the accused was taken into custody for allegedly violating law licensing requirements.


OVERVIEW: The accused was arrested for an incident in which she was not involved, but she was not subsequently released as an outstanding warrant against her was discovered. The accused pled guilty to the later charge, but filed a cause of action against defendants for malicious prosecution, negligence, and a violation of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The court found that the accused's guilty plea barred a cause of action for malicious prosecution. The court denied summary judgment on the claims for negligence and a violation of § 1983 because proper issues of material fact were demonstrated by the accused. The court found that the negligence alleged was not the mistaken issuance of a summons, but rather the failure to make known to the accused that the summons with appearance was required. The court held that the fact that there were grounds for the summons issuance and that the accused pled guilty did not bar her from contending that the warrant which resulted in her detention would not have been issued if defendants acted properly, including informing her that a dog license was required. The court also found that a § 1983 claim was proper where a traditional remedy was unavailable.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion for summary judgment finding that a viable cause of action for negligence and deprivation of civil rights had been set forth by the accused. The court determined that the claim for malicious prosecution could not be sustained.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Malicious Prosecution -- What Constitutes

1. In an action in which it was alleged that plaintiff appeared at the ASPCA to change the name and registration of a dog she had acquired from a friend; that she was given papers which she did not read and was not made aware that among the papers was a summons for the violation of harboring an unlicensed dog; that several months later she was mistakenly arrested as a burglary suspect and was kept in jail because the police discovered that there was an outstanding warrant on her based on the failure to appear to answer the ASPCA summons; and that the next day she admitted that she did not have the license for the dog, pleaded guilty and was released, causes of action sounding in malicious prosecution are dismissed. The summons for the violation of harboring an unlicensed dog and the warrant issued upon plaintiff's nonappearance were colorably valid, and the criminal proceeding was not terminated favorably to plaintiff because she entered a guilty plea.

Negligence -- What Constitutes

2. In an action in which it was alleged that plaintiff appeared at the ASPCA to change the name and registration of a dog she had acquired [***2] from a friend; that she was given papers which she did not read and was not made aware that among the papers was a summons for the violation of harboring an unlicensed dog; that several months later she was mistakenly arrested as a burglary suspect and was kept in jail because the police discovered that there was an outstanding warrant on her based on the failure to appear to answer the ASPCA summons; and that the next day she admitted that she did not have the license for the dog, pleaded guilty and was released, plaintiff's claim of negligent institution of a prosecution states no cause of action. However, a claim of negligence could be sustained based upon the allegation that plaintiff was misled because the ASPCA employee never told her that she was being given a summons, and a question arises as to whether the clerk acted reasonably in a context in which plaintiff may have been led to believe she was receiving a license application form.

Civil Rights -- Federal Civil Rights Claim

3. In an action in which it was alleged that plaintiff appeared at the ASPCA to change the name and registration of a dog she had acquired from a friend; that she was given papers which she [***3] did not read and was not made aware that among the papers was a summons for the violation of harboring an unlicensed dog; that several months thereafter she was mistakenly arrested as a burglary suspect and was kept in jail because the police discovered that there was an outstanding warrant on her based on the failure to appear to answer the ASPCA summons; and that the next day she admitted that she did not have the license for the dog, pleaded guilty and was released, a cause of action that plaintiff was unconstitutionally deprived of her liberty under color of law (US Code, tit 42, § 1983) has been validly stated.

 

1778 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Leston


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


117 Misc. 2d 712; 459 N.Y.S.2d 364; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3207



January 19, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the sentence imposed after he pled guilty to the crime of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that a 1976 manslaughter conviction, upon which the State relied as the basis for sentencing him as a predicate violent felony offender, was not constitutionally obtained and could not serve as the basis for sentencing him as a second felony offender. The court found that defendant had been subjected to a predicate violent felony conviction because he had failed to come forward with any showing of unconstitutionality. Furthermore, the court held that the State had met its burden through the presumption of regularity by bringing forth testimony of the judge that conducted the hearing on the manslaughter charge. The court found that the judge had conducted a practice in accepting guilty pleas only after he advised defendant of his constitutional rights. The court also found that defendant had failed to call any witnesses and ruled that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.30 set forth the procedure for moving to vacate a judgment or set aside a sentence. That statute provided that on such a motion, the grounds for which included constitutional challenges, defendant had the burden of proving by a preponderance of the evidence every fact essential to support the motion.


OUTCOME: The court found that use of the prior conviction was properly obtained and applied for a determination of defendant's sentence after his conviction for criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Second Violent Felony Offender -- Predicate Offense Prior to Effective Date of Violent Felony Legislation

1. Sentencing of a defendant who pleaded guilty to the crime of criminal possession of a weapon, third degree, as a second violent felony offender based upon a conviction obtained prior to the effective date of the violent felony legislation does not violate defendant's due process rights or the ex post facto prohibition since reliance upon the prior conviction serves only to enhance defendant's sentence for his present crime and not to retroactively increase the penalty for the predicate offense.

Crimes -- Second Violent Felony Offender -- Constitutionality of Previous Conviction -- Burden of Proof

2. The ultimate burden of proof as to the constitutionality of a conviction relied on as the basis for sentencing a defendant as a predicate violent felony offender is upon the People since the fair import of CPL 400.15 (subd 7, par [a]) is that the defendant is first required to specify the nature of his constitutional challenge and offer proof in support thereof and, at that point, the People's burden to disprove the challenge becomes operative. Accordingly,  [***2] the court found that the defendant had been properly subjected to a predicate violent felony conviction for the purpose of sentencing him as a second felony offender where defendant failed to come forward with any proof as to the unconstitutionality of the predicate conviction thereby never making operative the People's burden to disprove defendant's challenge to the conviction's constitutionality; moreover, even if defendant was to have no burden of coming forward, his challenge must fail since, whether the People's burden of proof is held to be one of "clear and convincing evidence", or a "preponderance of the credible evidence", that burden is met by the presumption of regularity in court proceedings as buttressed by the testimony of the Justice who presided at the taking of defendant's plea that, although he had no specific recollection of the proceeding, it was his unvarying practice in accepting guilty pleas to advise a defendant of those constitutional rights which he waives by a guilty plea.

 

1779 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Allison


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, New York County


117 Misc. 2d 463; 458 N.Y.S.2d 496; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3170



January 17, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law §§ 220.25(1) and 265.15(3), to dismiss an indictment accusing him of possessing a controlled substance and weapon found in an automobile that he was driving for hire, while operating on a suspended license.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for criminal possession of a controlled substance and a weapon, after police retrieved the items from the vehicle that defendant was driving for hire. At the time, defendant's license was allegedly suspended. Defendant claimed that the statute afforded him an exemption from the presumption that the presence of a controlled substance and a firearm in a vehicle was evidence of its possession by all of its occupants. The prosecutor argued that while defendant's license was suspended at the time of the incident, he was not duly licensed and therefore was not exempt from the statute. The court held that the statutory presumption could not be sustained if there was no rational connection between the fact proved and the ultimate fact presumed. The court found that defendant was a duly licensed operator within the purview of the exceptions. Thus, the presumptions were not applicable and the indictment, based on erroneous legal instructions, had to be dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the indictment as it was based on an erroneous instruction that the grand jury could presume that the controlled substance and firearm belonged to defendant.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Gun in Car -- Drugs in Car -- Presumption of Possession by Motor Vehicle Occupants -- Licensed Driver for Hire Exception

The presumption of knowing possession of a controlled substance (Penal Law, § 220.25, subd 1) and of a firearm (Penal Law, § 265.15, subd 3) by all who occupy a vehicle at the time such contraband is found is inapplicable to a duly licensed driver for hire since there is no reasonable basis to presume that such a driver is aware of the presence of contraband and culpably involved in its possession in a vehicle not necessarily owned by him, not accessible exclusively to him, and used by passengers not ordinarily known to him. Accordingly, said presumption is inapplicable to such a driver whose license has been temporarily suspended since such a driver can no more reasonably be said to have been aware of and culpably involved in the possession of such contraband than his counterpart with a currently valid license and, therefore, it was error for the District Attorney to instruct the Grand Jury that it could apply said presumptions in indicting such a driver for possession of an unlicensed firearm and a quantity of a controlled substance allegedly [***2] recovered by police from the rear of the vehicle he was operating.

 

1780 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Tommy & Tina, Inc. v. Dep't of Consumer Affairs


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


117 Misc. 2d 415; 459 N.Y.S.2d 220; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3165



January 13, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner individuals sought review of respondent Department of Consumer Affairs of the City of New York's (department) revocation of the individuals' common show licenses for the operation of video games. The individuals challenged the constitutionality of the department's regulation governing the city's licensing of common shows, contending that the regulation was unconstitutionally vague and its application had been arbitrary and capricious.


OVERVIEW: The court denied the petition, holding that the regulation was not void for vagueness. The court held that the test for determining whether a regulation was void for vagueness was whether persons of common intelligence must have necessarily guessed at its meaning and differed as to its application. The court held that the regulation clearly set forth that no common show license should have been issued for an establishment within 200 feet of an elementary or secondary public or private school. The court concluded that the fact that a precise method of measurement was not contained in the statute did not render the regulation unconstitutionally vague. Further, the court held that the application of the regulation was not arbitrary or capricious. The court held that there was a rational basis for the regulation because it was not unreasonable or irrational for the department to restrict the licensing of the games near schools.


OUTCOME: The court denied the individuals' petition against the department and found that the regulation was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- Common Show License -- Proximity to School

A regulation of the Department of Consumer Affairs of the City of New York stating that no license for common show games, which include video games (Administrative Code of City of New York, § B32-40.0), shall be issued for an establishment located within 200 feet of a private or public elementary or secondary school, is constitutional: (1) the regulation does not infringe upon conduct protected by the First Amendment of the United States Constitution; (2) there is a rational basis for the regulation, and (3) the regulation is not unconstitutional for vagueness by reason of the fact that no method of measurement is contained in the regulation. Furthermore, the Commissioner of the Consumer Affairs Department acted within his authority in directing that said distance be measured from building line or property line to building line, whichever is the shortest distance between the licensed premises and a school, rather than from door to door. Accordingly, since it is undisputed that petitioners' premises are within 200 feet of a school, as measured by respondent's inspectors, the revocation of their common [***2] show licenses was not arbitrary or capricious and will not be disturbed; petitioners' CPLR article 78 proceeding was converted into a declaratory judgment action insofar as it challenged the constitutionality of the regulation.

 

1781 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Aco Realty Corp. v. Srogi


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Onondaga County


117 Misc. 2d 409; 458 N.Y.S.2d 822; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3164



January 5, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers brought the action for assessment review against respondent Commissioner of Assessment of the City of Syracuse, New York. The taxpayers filed a motion for summary judgment on issues involving State equalization rates applied to the City of Syracuse for nine years, the constitutionality of limiting interest on tax refunds, and their entitlement to additional allowances under N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 722(2).


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers brought the challenge to their property assessments and filed a motion for summary judgment. The court first found that proof of the State equalization rate was sufficient to establish a prima facie case of the validity of the assessment ratio, pursuant to 1981 N.Y. Laws ch. 1057, § 7, but that evidence of the State rate was not conclusive of the validity of the ratio. The City was not precluded from questioning the validity of the State rate. The court also ruled that the City was not estopped from challenging the State rate, except with regard to years in which it was deemed valid through litigation. Because the State rate was deemed valid for four of the years in question, the court granted the taxpayers' motion for summary judgment for tax assessments issued during those years. The court then concluded that the taxpayers were entitled to be made whole for any overassessment. This included interest on the overpayments, at a reasonable rate that was not limited to 3 percent under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 3-a. The taxpayers were also entitled to additional allowances after offering sufficient proof, as required under N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 722(2).


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion for summary judgment with regard to the State equalization rate applicable to the City for four years. The court found in favor of the taxpayers on the interest rate issue and directed them to offer further proof in order to establish its rights to an additional allowance.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Real Property Tax Assessment -- State Equalization Rate

1. In a real property tax assessment review proceeding, petitioners' contention that the State equalization rates for the years 1972 through 1980 are the proper rates to be applied to the City of Syracuse on the ground that existing court decisions have established that the proof of the State rate is conclusive is without merit since existing authority provides that the State rate alone is sufficient evidence to establish a prima facie case but does not preclude the taxing authority from questioning the validity of the State rate and, thus, it is not conclusive ( Guth Realty v Gingold, 34 NY2d 440); however, the taxing authority is estopped from contesting the State rate for those years in which that rate has been fully litigated or for which the taxing authority has admitted its validity.

Taxation -- Assessment -- Court's Power to Compensate for Overassessment

2. There is no indication that in enacting section 3-a of the General Municipal Law the Legislature intended to pre-empt the field of compensation for overassessment of tax and, in addition, the court has general equitable [***2] power, which the Legislature may not usurp, to provide just compensation for an overassessment of tax regardless of the taxing authority's motive in levying such overassessment. Accordingly, in a real property tax assessment review proceeding, the court is competent, pursuant to said equitable power, to provide just compensation, as equity requires, in the form of a fair and reasonable interest rate for any amounts improperly collected, and the readily determinable interest rate for the tax anticipation notes with which municipalities often fund their everyday operations provides a reasonable basis for establishing the interest rate for the tax refunds.

Taxation -- Assessment -- Additional Allowance for Unlawful Overassessment

3. A municipality's failure to comply with the statutory mandate to assess real property at its fair market value and its repeated imposition of unlawful assessments against a petitioner's property represent precisely the abuse which the Legislature sought to correct when it enacted subdivision 2 of section 722 of the Real Property Tax Law which provides an additional allowance for a taxpayer whose property has been wrongfully overassessed and, therefore,  [***3] in a real property tax assessment review proceeding, petitioner may establish its right to such penalty allowance by offering proof that it has been so treated by respondent municipality.

 

1782 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Donohue v. La Pierre


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


117 Misc. 2d 148; 457 N.Y.S.2d 737; 1983 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3144



January 3, 1983 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action commenced by plaintiff to recover for personal injuries she sustained when her vehicle collided with an automobile owned and operated by defendant driver, the driver filed a motion for an order pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a)(8) dismissing the action for lack of personal jurisdiction, and plaintiff filed a cross motion for an order dismissing the driver's third and fifth affirmative defenses set forth in the driver's answer.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff allegedly sustained injuries when her automobile collided with the driver's automobile. Plaintiff filed a personal injury complaint against the driver. The process server attempted service upon defendant at an address in a city, where he delivered a copy of the summons with notice to a person of suitable age and discretion. Soon after, the process server mailed a copy of the summons with notice to the address given by plaintiff. The driver filed a motion to dismiss plaintiff's complaint because the trial court did not obtain personal jurisdiction over him because the address that plaintiff gave to the process server was correct, but the city was incorrect. The court granted the driver's motion for an order dismissing the complaint and denied plaintiff's cross motion for an order dismissing the third and fifth affirmative defenses set forth in the driver's answer. The court took judicial notice of matters that related to the mail. The court held that by mailing the summons with notice to a nonexistent post-office address plaintiff failed to comply strictly with N.Y. C.P.L.R. 208(2). Therefore, the court held that it lacked personal jurisdiction over the driver.


OUTCOME: The court granted the driver's motion for an order dismissing plaintiff's personal injury complaint against him on the ground of lack of personal jurisdiction. The court denied plaintiff's cross motion for an order dismissing the third and fifth affirmative defenses set forth in the driver's answer.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Process -- Service by Mail -- Mailing to "Last Known Residence"

The phrase "last known residence" used in CPLR 308 (subd 2), which requires that a copy of a summons left with a person of suitable age and discretion be mailed to the named defendant at his "last known residence", is synonymous with the term "last known address" so that the failure to use the correct post-office address in mailing process to the defendant results in the court not obtaining personal jurisdiction of the party upon whom service has been attempted; strict compliance with both the delivery and mailing requirements is necessary in order to obtain personal jurisdiction under CPLR 308 (subd 2) and defendant's mailing of a copy of the summons to 1950 Central Avenue, Colonie, New York, when defendant's post-office address is 1950 Central Avenue, Albany, New York, there being no such mailing address as Colonie, New York, does not satisfy the mailing requirements of the statute and the action must therefore be dismissed for lack of personal jurisdiction of defendant.

 

1783 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Zimmerman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Suffolk County


117 Misc. 2d 121; 458 N.Y.S.2d 468; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4032



December 29, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was charged with criminal possession of a weapon, stolen property, and bribery, sought to suppress physical evidence of a weapon seized by the police from the trunk of a car, as well as custodial statements attributed to defendant, on the grounds that they fell within the exclusionary rule.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with criminal possession of a weapon, stolen property, and bribery. He sought to suppress evidence of a weapon obtained from the trunk of a car, as well as a statement he made, claiming they fell within the exclusionary rule. The court declined to suppress evidence of the weapon, finding its seizure came under exceptions to the exclusionary rule. The court also denied the motion to suppress a bribe offer by defendant. The court found that the police acted in good faith in relying on defendant's act of consent in opening the trunk and permitting them to look inside. The good-faith error by the police, which was judgmental, did not require application of the exclusionary rule. Moreover, in such cases, there was a societal interest in the prosecution of a person charged with possessing and transporting automatic weapons. Also, there was a diminished right of privacy in a parked vehicle. The bribe offer was not suppressed because it was an independent act, and was not a spontaneous reaction to an unlawful search after arrest.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to suppress evidence of a weapon seized from a trunk and a bribe offer was denied on the grounds that such evidence was not protected under the exclusionary rule. The police acted in good faith in making the search where they believed defendant had consented, and the independent act of making a bribe was not the result of an illegal search or seizure.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Exclusionary Rule -- Good-Faith "Judgmental Error"

1. The police, having received an anonymous tip that automatic weapons could be found in the trunk of a specifically described car and having then observed defendant enter the car at 2:30 a.m. and drive it to a public parking lot adjacent to a bar, had the right to approach defendant and request his driver's license and registration and when defendant could not produce a driver's license or registration for the car, the police properly made further inquiry and then lawfully arrested defendant upon learning from a computer check of two outstanding warrants against him for nonsupport and driving with a suspended license; although defendant was lawfully arrested, the subsequent search of the locked trunk of the vehicle cannot be justified on the ground of consent inasmuch as the People failed in their burden proving beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant "voluntarily consented" to the search of the trunk nor can the search be justified as a routine inventory search; however, while defendant's consent technically lacked the quality to constitute a waiver of the right of privacy, the exclusionary rule should not be invoked [***2] to suppress the contents of the trunk where the police acting in good faith made a mere "judgmental error" in relying upon defendant's apparent consent to search the trunk, the deterrent effect of the exclusionary rule being outweighed in this case by the societal interest in prosecuting a person charged with possessing and transporting automatic weapons involving a search of a vehicle rather than a home.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- "Bribe Offer"

2. Defendant's "bribe offer" to a police officer to overlook a handgun in the passenger compartment of the vehicle he was driving, made after defendant had been arrested and invoked his right to counsel, furnished a valid independent basis for interrogation and investigation and the police, armed with such "probable cause", lawfully seized the weapon.

 

1784 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. McNeil


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


117 Misc. 2d 96; 457 N.Y.S.2d 409; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4026



December 22, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted upon his plea of guilty to burglary in the second-degree and challenged the filing of a persistent violent felony offender statement, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law §§ 400.15, 400.16(2), on the ground that his two prior violent felony convictions were unconstitutionally obtained. He urged that he had not known of the constitutional rights that he was waiving by negotiating pleas and sentences in the prior cases.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted on a guilty plea to burglary in the second-degree. The People filed a persistent violent felony offender statement under §§ 400.15, 400.16(2), and defendant objected on the grounds that his two prior violent felony convictions had been obtained unconstitutionally. Specifically, he alleged that he had not realized the constitutional rights that he was waiving by negotiating the pleas and bargaining sentences in the prior proceedings. The trial court concluded that the two prior violent felony convictions could not be used in a determination that defendant was a persistent violent felony offender because the prior convictions were constitutionally infirm. Both times that defendant pleaded guilty, he was not advised of each of his constitutional rights. His lack of knowledge rendered those convictions unconstitutional. Defendant testified that he had not been aware of the constitutional rights that he was waiving; he only knew that he was being offered shorter sentences and that had been the basis for his acceptance of the pleas. The People failed to introduce any evidence to controvert the contention that he had not knowingly waived his constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The trial court granted the motion of defendant to preclude the use of two prior violent felony convictions in sentencing enhancement.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Persistent Violent Felony Offender

1. Defendant's sentence upon his third violent felony conviction is subject to enhancement since the offense for which he currently stands convicted was committed at a time when violent and second felony offender laws had become effective ( CPL 400.15, 400.16); such a sentence does not increase the punishment for acts committed prior to the effective date of said laws in violation of the constitutional prohibition of ex post facto laws.

Crimes -- Second Felony Offender -- Predicate Conviction

2. Defendant's failure to challenge the constitutionality of his original violent felony conviction when he was arraigned as a second felony offender would ordinarily be deemed a waiver of his right to challenge that conviction; however, where defendant asserts that he was told by his attorney that he could only challenge whether he was the person named in the predicate felony statement and this is supported by the fact that the court did not inform him that he had a right to controvert the constitutionality of the prior conviction, defendant did not waive his right to challenge the constitutionality of the prior convictions and he [***2] can raise the issue upon his third violent felony conviction when a persistent violent felony offender statement is filed against him.

Crimes -- Persistent Violent Felony Offender -- Predicate Conviction

3. When a defendant pleads guilty to a crime, no special litany or specific allocution of the constitutional rights he is waiving is required; however, the waiver of those rights must be knowing and voluntary. Accordingly, defendant's prior convictions cannot be used in a determination that defendant is a persistent violent felony offender ( CPL 400.16) where he asserts that he was not made aware of the constitutional rights he waived by negotiating pleas on two prior violent felony convictions, stating that he knew only that the sentences offered were less than the sentences he might receive if he were found guilty at trial, and the People have failed to controvert this contention.

 

1785 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mid-Manhattan Independent Merchants Asso. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


117 Misc. 2d 388; 458 N.Y.S.2d 151; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4062



December 20, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, plaintiff's motion is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff merchants association filed a motion for a preliminary injunction to restrain defendant city from implementing and enforcing New York City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 74 (1982), which required a narrowly defined class of retailers in midtown Manhattan to apply for a special license to do business.


OVERVIEW: The city enacted New York City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 74 (1982) as a means of protecting tourists, who frequented the midtown Manhattan area, from being overcharged for camera and video equipment and Persian rugs. The association filed an action contending that New York City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 74 (1982) was unconstitutional and moved for an injunction to restrain its enforcement. In denying the motion, the court held that the association was not likely to succeed in the action. In so deciding, the court held that: (1) the city's use of size and geographic location in New York City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 74 (1982) did not violate equal protection; (2) although the licensing requirements of New York City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 74 (1982) only pertained to smaller merchants within a three square block area, the city was entitled to implement its program step by step with the ultimate goal of enacting regulations to eliminate the overcharging problem in all areas; and (3) the association was unlikely to have been able to sustain its burden of proving that the city's economic regulation was not reasonably calculated to serve a proper governmental purpose.


OUTCOME: The court denied the association's motion.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Economic Regulation -- Regulation of Small Retail Businesses Catering to Tourists

A New York City local law requiring a narrowly defined class of retailers in midtown Manhattan which sell audio or video equipment, calculators, cameras or "Oriental" rugs and which have less than 10,000 square feet of display space to apply for a special license to do business and thus be subject to more stringent regulation is not unconstitutional since, in the area of economic regulation, only a "minimal level of rationality" need be proffered to satisfy the strong presumption of constitutionality and the city has made far more than a minimal showing of the need to protect the city's important tourist industry from instances of overcharging, deception and outright fraud that have been demonstrated by the city; plaintiff's contention that the local law is in potential violation of the antitrust laws lacks merit in view of the absence of even an allegation of conspiracy.

 

1786 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Autieri


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Mount Vernon


120 Misc. 2d 725; 466 N.Y.S.2d 549; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4119



December 10, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged separately with the offense of harassment of a police officer in violation of Mount Vernon, N.Y., Code § 147-2. Each of them challenged the constitutionality of § 147-2.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with directing obscene language against a police officer, in a public place, in the presence of another person or other persons with intent to annoy, harass, or alarm the police officer, violations of § 147-2. Section 147-2 mirrored the language of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.25(2), the State statute defining the offense of harassment, except that the ordinance was confined to police officers while the State statute made no distinction. The ordinance was enacted to fill a perceived gap in the law created by judicial interpretations of the State statute, which required measurement of the offensiveness of the language against the sensibilities of the ordinary, reasonable police officer, rather than the ordinary reasonable man. Defendants argued that the city council had no authority to enact the ordinance. The court found no intention either legislatively or judicially to confine the protection of § 240.25(2) to private citizens, and thus concluded that the State had preempted the entire area of offenses covered by that statute. The court further held that the ordinance could not be sustained by a claimed local problem where the problem was not peculiar to the city.


OUTCOME: The court declared the ordinance unconstitutional and dismissed the charges against defendants.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Harassment -- Obscene Language Addressed to Police Officer

An antiharassment ordinance of the City of Mount Vernon, which proscribes directing obscene language or gestures against a police officer, in a public place, in the presence of another person or persons with intent to annoy, harass or alarm the officer, the purpose of the ordinance being to overcome the effect of judicial interpretations of the State harassment statute (Penal Law, § 240.25, subd 2) confining the offense of harassment by obscene language or gestures addressed to policy officers to "fighting words" as perceived by a reasonable police officer, is unconstitutional for being inconsistent with the Penal Law harassment provisions which pre-empt the entire field of harassment offenses; the State harassment statute, as interpreted, does not exclude obscene language or gestures addressed to police officers but merely, in order to protect the statute's constitutionality, distinguishes between words addressed to a reasonable man and words directed to a police officer in view of the higher degree of restraint expected of a police officer trained to cope with obscene outbursts and their responses to words [***2] which would incite the average citizen; judicial interpretations of the harassment statute do not create a special local problem such as will sustain local legislation in the face of pre-emption by the State.

 

1787 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Haag v. Hogue


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


116 Misc. 2d 935; 456 N.Y.S.2d 978; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3982



December 7, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs nonunion employees brought an action against defendants, a school district and an education association, challenging the constitutionality of N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 208(3)(b), which authorized the association to deduct association dues from the employees' salaries. The employees, the association, and intervenor State of New York each moved for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The employees brought an action challenging the constitutionality of N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 208(3)(b), arguing that the statute violated their rights of freedom of expression and association by allowing the association to use their money for purposes other than collective bargaining and related union activities. The employees, the association, and the State all moved for summary judgment. Upon consideration, the court denied the motions. The court found that the employees were not entitled to summary judgment, noting that the statute was constitutional on its face, and there was no evidence that the employees' dues were being impermissibly used to further the association's ideological activities or that resort to the association's refund mechanism would have been inefficacious or inequitable. The court found that neither the State nor the association were entitled to summary judgment, noting that the employees' complaint fairly raised questions of fact requiring a trial.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motions for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil Service -- Employee Organizations -- Agency Shop Fee Deduction -- Constitutionality

1. With respect to plaintiff nonunion employees' contention that by allowing the deduction from wages of agency shop fees equal to defendant union's dues, section 208 (subd 3, par [b]) of the Civil Service Law permits agency shop fee deductions exceeding plaintiffs' pro rata share of collective bargaining expenses to be used by the union, at least temporarily, for impermissible purposes, it is clear that there is no constitutional infirmity in the authorization by section 208 (subd 3, par [b]) of an agency shop fee equal to the union's dues ( Railway Employees' Dept. v Hanson, 351 U.S. 225). The present record is devoid of hard evidence showing how defendant union actually used the agency shop fees of plaintiff nonunion employees. The holding in Hanson (supra) requires the presumption that fees will be used only for permissible purposes and, accordingly, the exaction of an agency shop fee equal to union dues is not unconstitutional per se.

Civil Service -- Employee Organizations -- Agency Shop Fee Deduction Refund Procedure -- Constitutionality

2. Plaintiff [***2] nonunion employees' contention that the agency shop fee deduction refund procedure authorized by section 208 (subd 3, par [b]) of the Civil Service Law inhibits their First Amendment rights and is equivalent to a prior restraint upon speech, and that agency shop fees should be deposited in escrow until the union proves to a court, or to the New York Public Employment Relations Board, how much of the fee represents plaintiffs' pro rata share of the collective bargaining expenses which are properly deductible, misperceives the nature of plaintiffs' rights. A dissenting nonunion employee is aggrieved not by the union's receipt of his agency shop fee, but only by its use for impermissible purposes, and until a prima facie showing of unauthorized use is made there is no reason to deny the union control over the full agency shop fee. The escrow requirement which plaintiffs seek to engraft upon the statute would amount to a forbidden injunction.

Civil Service -- Employee Organizations -- Agency Shop Fee Deduction Refund Procedure -- Constitutionality

3. It cannot be determined, on the present record, whether the defendant union's internal agency shop fee deduction rebate [***3] procedure impermissibly shifts the burden of proof to plaintiff nonunion employees, since the record lacks evidence that plaintiffs' agency shop fees are improperly used and is also devoid of proof as to the efficacy of the union's rebate procedures. Section 208 (subd 3, par [b]) of the Civil Service Law neither expressly limits dissenting nonunion employees to an internal union remedy nor purports to shift the burden of justification from the union to plaintiffs; moreover, the burden of justification is a condition precedent to the use, not the receipt, of plaintiffs' agency shop fees. That the statutory directive is capable of being administered in an unlawful manner is not sufficient to render it unconstitutional.

Civil Service -- Employee Organizations -- Agency Shop Fee Deduction Refund Procedure -- Constitutionality

4. Plaintiff nonunion employees' contention that section 208 (subd 3, par [b]) of the Civil Service Law is unconstitutional because it requires the union to refund only that portion of plaintiffs' agency shop fees which represent their pro rata share of "expenditures by the organization in aid of activities or causes of a political or ideological [***4] nature only incidentally related to terms and conditions of employment", which phrase omits numerous causes to which nonunion employees may not constitutionally be compelled to contribute, is rejected as premature.

Civil Service -- Employee Organizations -- Agency Shop Fee Deduction Refund Procedure -- Constitutionality

5. Plaintiff nonunion employees' contention that section 208 (subd 3, par [b]) of the Civil Service Law unconstitutionally permits the defendant union to require plaintiffs to register their objections annually with the union through a cumbersome and costly union-operated procedure, is rejected. The statute requires neither repeated objections nor that the agency shop fee deduction refund procedure it mandates be administered by the union; moreover, even if the statute were construed to permit the union to administer its refund procedure, this alone would not be a constitutional infirmity.

 

1788 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


American Trucking Assos. v. New York State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


120 Misc. 2d 191; 467 N.Y.S.2d 744; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4115



December 1, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tax payer filed suit seeking a declaratory judgment against defendant New York State Tax Commission (tax commission).


OVERVIEW: Taxpayers brought an action for declaratory judgment declaring N.Y. Tax Law § 184 unconstitutional. Both partied filed for summary judgment. The taxpayers contended the tax was in violation of all four prongs of the test of taxing statutes. The court did not find this argument persuasive. The taxpayers argued the taxing formula taxed gross receipts having no nexus with the State of New York. The court stated the tax statute taxed a 100% of gross receipts based upon New York State revenue miles and did not in any way tax gross receipts based upon revenue miles in other states or counties. The court determined that §184 did not tax plaintiffs on gross receipts not having a nexus with the State of New York. The taxpayers contended the taxing statute violated the commerce clause and the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment because the tax was not fairly related to services rendered by the state. The court concluded the tax was fairly related to services rendered by the state. The court granted tax commission's motion for summary judgment because the taxpayers failed to meet their burden of proof showing that the tax law was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted the tax commission's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- Franchise Tax on Business Corporations -- Mileage Apportionment as Measure of Taxation

1. Section 184 of the Tax Law, which levies a franchise tax upon trucking carriers, is not unconstitutional on the ground that it taxes gross receipts having no nexus with New York; section 184 apportions gross receipts into those having a substantial nexus with New York based upon actual revenue miles within the State and those gross receipts derived wholly from external sources; accordingly, the statute taxes 100% of gross receipts based upon New York State revenue miles and does not in any way tax gross receipts based upon revenue miles in other States or countries; moreover, section 184 provides that if distortions in taxes occur based upon different receipts for different types of goods carried, the Tax Commission shall prescribe methods of allocation or apportionment which fairly and equitably reflect gross earnings from all sources within the State.

Constitutional Law -- Commerce Clause -- Equal Protection Clause -- Franchise Tax on Trucking Carriers

2. Section 184 of the Tax Law, which levies a franchise tax upon trucking carriers, does not violate the commerce [***2] clause and the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment on the ground that the tax is not fairly related to the services rendered due to the fact that many of the taxpayer plaintiffs are newer present in the Metropolitan Transit Authority area for which some of the funds generated by the tax are earmarked; said tax is measured as a percentage of the value of services rendered in the State of New York in the form of three quarters of 1% of gross receipts for such services which complies with the test of constitutionality that the measure of the tax be related to the extent of contact with the State; moreover, there is no requirement that the State use the proceeds of the tax for any specific purpose nor any proscription to the levy of more than one tax based upon highway use.

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Franchise Tax on Motor Carriers

3. Section 184 of the Tax Law, which levies a franchise tax upon trucking carriers but not upon gross receipts derived from out-of-State sources, does not discriminate against interstate commerce; moreover, section 184 does not impose a more burdensome tax on motor carriers than upon others with no rational [***3] justification in violation of the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment; it is permissible for a State to levy more than one tax based upon highway usage; the increased tax burden upon plaintiffs is justified since New York confers substantial benefits upon plaintiffs and the services it renders to plaintiffs exceed general services rendered to other businesses.

 

1789 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Deignan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Oswego, Oswego County


116 Misc. 2d 955; 457 N.Y.S.2d 378; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3985



November 29, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The first defendant appealed his conviction for harassment and the other defendants appealed their convictions for harassment and resisting arrest.


OVERVIEW: The issues on review were what constituted a public place for purposes of the offense of harassment and whether abusive language directed to police officers constituted harassment. The court affirmed the convictions. The court found that the crime of harassment did not require proof that a search or seizure was authorized. The court determined that even if a pat down search was unauthorized, dismissal of the harassment charges as a matter of law was not required. The court also found that the convictions of resisting arrest could not stand if the harassment arrests were unauthorized, but, because the area at which the defendants made their remarks was a public place in the sense that the public had access to the area, the arrests were authorized. The court concluded that verbally abusive conduct by individuals against police officers was prosecutable as disorderly conduct or harassment.


OUTCOME: The court found that all of the defendants were guilty as charged and affirmed their convictions.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Harassment -- "Public Place" -- Abusive and Obscene Language to Police Officers

The backyard of two apartment buildings and a deserted loading dock in the area in back of a closed supermarket are "public places" for purposes of the harassment statute, for which a "public place" is defined as a "place to which the public * * * has access and includes, but is not limited to * * * hallways, lobbies and other portions of apartment houses * * * not constituting rooms or apartments designed for actual residence" (Penal Law, § 240.00, subd 1), since the backyard of an apartment building constitutes an "other portion * * * of an apartment house" within the meaning of the statute and the loading dock is an area to which the public has access; in view of the heavy presumption of constitutionality which attaches to all statutes, the definition of "public place" is not unconstitutional insofar as the backyard of an owner-occupied one- or two-family dwelling would not be deemed a "public place"; accordingly, defendants were properly convicted of harassment for using abusive and obscene language to police officers in the above-described "public places" (Penal Law, § 240.25, subd [***2] 2), since verbally abusive conduct by individuals against police officers is prosecutable as harassment inasmuch as a police officer is "another person" and there is thus no justification for creating a judicially imposed exception or defense to the statute when the complainant is a police officer.

 

1790 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Talbert


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Albany County


116 Misc. 2d 771; 456 N.Y.S.2d 347; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3955



November 24, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to suppress cocaine and marihuana seized from his person by the police incident to his arrest for possession of a "billy" in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.01(1). Defendant argued that the item at issue was not a "billy" under § 265.01(1) and that § 265.01(1) was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a passenger in an automobile stopped by a trooper for speeding. While checking the driver's registration and operator's license, the trooper saw a stick 24 to 30 inches in length, taped on both ends, located on the floor of the car. He identified the instrument as a "billy," the possession of which was prohibited by § 265.01(1), and arrested the vehicle's occupants for possession of the billy. The drugs found on defendant were incident to that arrest. On appeal, defendant argued that the search was illegal because the arrest was illegal in that the stick was not a "billy" under § 265.01(1). The court held that the instrument was a "billy." A broomhandle had been sawed-off and taped at both ends. It looked identical to the billy clubs carried by police. When defendant was asked about the weapon, he said it was a "billy." The determinative factor controlling the classification of the stick as a "billy" was the purpose for which it was designed, which was as a weapon. The transformation of the broomhandle to look like and serve the purpose of a "billy" made it a "billy" under § 265.01(1). The term "billy" was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to suppress.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Possession of Weapon -- Homemade "Billy"

Defendant, a passenger in a vehicle stopped by a State trooper for speeding, was properly arrested for criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree pursuant to subdivision (1) of section 265.01 of the Penal Law when the trooper spied a "stick" 24 to 30 inches in length, taped at both ends, on the floor of the car between the front seat and door while he was seeking to check the driver's license and registration; the "stick", a broomhandle adapted to serve solely as a weapon, using a design which was the same in all material respects as a manufactured policeman's club, is a "billy" within the meaning of the statute. Accordingly, since the trooper had probable cause for stopping the vehicle and probable cause for believing that the instrument he observed was a prohibited "billy", he had probable cause for the arrest of defendant, and defendant's motion to suppress cocaine and marihuana seized from his person as an incident to said lawful arrest is denied.

 

1791 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Florentino


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


116 Misc. 2d 692; 456 N.Y.S.2d 638; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3941



November 16, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss the charges brought against him under the Martin Act, N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 352-c, on the basis that the statute did not apply to insider trading and that it was unconstitutional for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating the Martin Act, N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 352-c, for insider trading. Defendant moved to dismiss on the grounds that the Act did not apply to insider trading and that it was unconstitutional. In particular, defendant contended that the Act was limited to those transactions where privity existed between the buyer and seller and that its application beyond that scope was unconstitutional for vagueness. As to clarity, the court held that there was nothing unclear or ambiguous in the language of the Act. The terms employed by the Act had an accepted meaning long recognized in law and life and could not be held to be so vague and indefinite as to afford defendant insufficient notice of what was prohibited. In light of the Act's legislative history, the court found that there was no sufficient basis upon which to limit the Act's application to transactions where privity exists.


OUTCOME: Finding that the Martin Act was not unconstitutional for vagueness and that its application was not limited to those transactions where privity existed, the court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the charges against him for insider trading.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Insider Trading in Securities -- Prosecution under Martin Act

The antifraud provisions of the Martin Act (General Business Law, art 23A) apply to insider trading; moreover, criminal prosecution to punish frauds perpetrated on the general public under section 352-c of the General Business Law is proper. Accordingly, defendant, an attorney and member of a law firm representing issuers of securities in takeovers and mergers, can be prosecuted by the Attorney-General for acting on secret, advance information of such transactions by buying "target" company securities on the open market through a broker and, after the transactions were announced, liquidating his position at elevated prices to realize huge profits; section 352-c of the General Business Law is not unconstitutional for vagueness as applied to defendant, who is charged with intentional fraud in violation of a fiduciary obligation owed to his law firm and its clients, nor does such an application of section 352-c burden interstate commerce or offend Federal legislation under the Williams Act (US Code, tit 15, §§ 78m, 78n).

 

1792 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Zimmerman v. State


Claim No. 65900


Court of Claims of New York


116 Misc. 2d 521; 455 N.Y.S.2d 974; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3909



November 5, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant brought an action to recover on a claim bill, N.Y. S.B. S-6883, 1981 N.Y. Laws ch. 608, that awarded claimant the right to seek damages because of the wrongful prosecution and conviction of claimant for first-degree murder. Claimant sought a partial summary judgment as to liability, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3212.


OVERVIEW: An appellate court had determined that a key witness had testified falsely and that the prosecutor had known of the false testimony, failing to correct the falsehood. The claim bill waived the State's sovereign immunity and conferred jurisdiction on the court to determine damages to claimant. The State conceded liability but stated that the bill was required to be constitutional at its inception. However, the State also claimed that it was the attorney general's duty to defend the action, and thus he chose not to take any position. Therefore, the court found that the State's argument was a nullity and that there was no material issue of fact before the court on the issue of liability. Nowhere in the oral argument or in the answer did the State allege that the statute might be unconstitutional. In fact, the attorney general had recommended that the statute be enacted into law. The court lacked jurisdiction to determine the constitutionality in the first instance. Further, the court had no authority to render a declaratory judgment. The court could not sua sponte declare unconstitutional a claim bill that was duly passed by the legislature and signed by the governor.


OUTCOME: The court granted a partial summary judgment on the question of liability and set the matter for trial on the issue of damages.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] State -- Court of Claims -- Authority to Render Summary and Declaratory Judgments

In an action against the State based upon a statute which specifically conferred jurisdiction upon the Court of Claims to determine claimant's claim against the State for damages resulting from an erroneous conviction of murder, the court granted claimant's motion for partial summary judgment on the issue of liability since such remedy is available in the Court of Claims and defendant, in its answer and opposing papers, has conceded liability and admitted that the facts as set forth in the claim and statute are accurate; moreover, the court was constrained to deny the Attorney-General's application for a declaration that the statute upon which the claim is based did not pass constitutional muster since the Court of Claims has no authority to render a declaratory judgment, and, in addition, the Attorney-General failed to raise the issue of constitutionality in the answer or opposing papers and the court may not sua sponte declare a duly enacted statute unconstitutional.

 

1793 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Schurm v. Union Nat'l Bank


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Greene County


116 Misc. 2d 255; 455 N.Y.S.2d 532; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3865



November 3, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The court will grant judgment declaring that petitioner has no interest in the real property which is subject to the Sheriff's sale, will dismiss the petition, and vacate the stay heretofore granted, all without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner husband placed his residence in his wife's name and borrowed money from the bank with his wife's personal guaranty. The husband defaulted on the loan and filed for personal and corporate bankruptcy. The wife sought a divorce and the husband was awarded temporary occupancy of the residence. Respondent bank sought to sell the property to satisfy its judgment against the wife. The husband sought to determine his rights in this property.


OVERVIEW: The husband argued that the sheriff's sale should be permanently enjoined because he had a vested equitable interest in the residence, the sale would contravene the divorce order prohibiting his wife from transferring her interest in the property, and it would work an unconscionable result upon him and his children. The court rejected these arguments, declared that the husband had no interest in the property, dismissed the petition, and vacated the stay of the sheriff's sale. The court held that the sheriff's sale would not violate the divorce order because it only precluded the wife's voluntary conveyance of the property and it did not restrain her judgment creditors. The court also held that the husband had no vested interest in the property because the wife acquired her interest free and clear of his control when he placed it in her name. The court also held that the sale would not harm the husband and his children. The court held that the bank was entitled to recoup the money the husband borrowed but failed to repay and that there was no showing that the husband or the wife would be unable to meet their child support obligations from income or other assets.


OUTCOME: The court entered a judgment declaring that the husband had no interest in the real property that was subject to the sheriff's sale, dismissed the husband's petition, and vacated the stay of the sheriff's sale.


HEADNOTES

Execution -- Restraint on Voluntary Conveyance -- Sheriff's Sale

1. An order restraining respondent wife from making a voluntary conveyance of her interest in certain real property presently occupied by petitioner husband pursuant to court order pending the outcome of a divorce action between the parties does not preclude disposal of the property at a Sheriff's sale since the order restrained respondent from making a voluntary conveyance and did not restrain her judgment creditors.

Husband and Wife -- Equitable Distribution -- Conveyance of Property by Husband to Wife

2. Petitioner husband who purchased certain real property and placed the title in the name of the respondent wife does not have a vested equitable interest in the property by virtue of the Equitable Distribution Law ( Domestic Relations Law, § 236, part B) so as to enjoin a Sheriff's sale of the property to satisfy claims of the wife's judgment creditors since property acquired [***2] by a wife as the result of a gift from her husband is hers free and clear of his control ( Domestic Relations Law, § 50) and the Equitable Distribution Law has done nothing to alter that rule of property.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process -- Sheriff's Sale of Real Property

3. Petitioner's contention that the statute authorizing the Sheriff's sale of real property ( CPLR 5230), purchased by him and conveyed to his wife, is unconstitutional as violative of due process by not requiring notice to judgment debtors prior to the application of their property in satisfaction of a judgment is without merit since petitioner is not a judgment debtor, has no interest in the property so as to give rise to due process protection and did in fact receive notice; moreover, CPLR 5236 does require personal service of a copy of the notice of sale upon the judgment debtor and all holders of record interest in the property and the Legislature has acted to correct any constitutional deficiencies in the statutory scheme (L 1982, ch 882).

Execution -- Sheriff's Sale of Real Property

4. Where petitioner husband purchased a marital residence, placed title in his wife's name and thereafter [***3] borrowed large sums from respondent bank for business purposes, prevailing upon his wife to personally guarantee the loans and defaulted in repayment, a Sheriff's sale of the property to satisfy the debt will not work an unconscionable result upon petitioner and his children who presently occupy the residence since the respondent bank, through means established by the Legislature, is merely seeking to recoup part of the money petitioner borrowed, spent and failed to repay and there has been no showing that petitioner and his wife will be unable to meet their joint obligation to support the children if the property is sold.

 

1794 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Collins v. Brennan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Tompkins County


116 Misc. 2d 985; 456 N.Y.S.2d 931; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3991



November 3, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, nonresident summer visitors, filed an action against respondent village and sought to nullify the results of a referendum election held in the village.


OVERVIEW: The village's board of trustees authorized a sanitary sewer construction project to be paid through the issuance of serial bonds. The resolution pledged the village's full faith and credit for the bonds but provided that a tax would be levied if necessary on taxable real property in the village. The village then held a special election in which the referendum passed. The nonresident summer visitors were not permitted to vote in the election although they held property rights in the form of 99-year leases. The nonresident summer visitors filed suit because they claimed that unqualified persons were permitted to vote contrary to N.Y. Village Law § 9-902 and the court agreed. The court treated the action as one for summary judgment and determined that the election had to fail because the voter qualification was improper as was the disparity in notice. The court found the bond resolution was not legally submitted to the voters at the election and that an unauthorized class of voters was registered to, and did, vote during the election.


OUTCOME: The court found in favor of the nonresident summer visitors and determined that the village's referendum election had to be nullified.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Elections -- Special Village Election -- Qualification for Voting on Proposition

Although a proceeding challenging a special village election which approved a bond resolution for the construction of a sanitary sewer system is not entitled to summary treatment under section 15-138 of the Election Law since special elections limited to referendum issues are excluded from coverage of the Election Law, the summary proceeding is nonetheless converted to one for declaratory judgment ( CPLR 103, subd [c]; 3001) in order to declare the rights and legal relations of the parties in a controversy that is both ripe and justiciable; accordingly, the special village election cannot stand since nonresident property owners, including corporations and associations, were allowed to vote contrary to the statutorily mandated qualifications for voters (Village Law, § 9-902, subd 2; Election Law, § 5-102) and the village erred in publishing an informal "General Information" summary which stated that nonresident property owners could vote on the referendum.

 

1795 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Benthall


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


116 Misc. 2d 252; 455 N.Y.S.2d 527; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3864



October 27, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.10(b)(h), defendant filed a motion to vacate the court's judgment of conviction for possession of a weapon and possession of a controlled substance, asserting that the court erred in applying N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 140.15(4) regarding warrantless arrests.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested without a warrant and the court held that the arrest was lawful pursuant to § 140.15(4). Although a decision had been issued by the Supreme Court of the United States holding that § 140.15(4) was unconstitutional, the court interpreted the decision as applying only to searches occurring after the opinion was issued. Defendant's search and arrest occurred prior to the issuance of the decision. As such, the court applied § 140.15(4) as though it were constitutional. Defendant appealed the judgment and filed a motion to vacate the judgment. On hearing the motion to vacate, the court noted that it erred in applying § 140.15(4) because a subsequent decision was issued which ordered that the decision finding the statute unconstitutional was to have been applied retroactively. However, the court could not grant defendant's motion because N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.10(2)(b) required the court deny the motion to vacate in that the judgment was pending on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to vacate the judgment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- Arrest -- Warrantless Arrest -- Motion to Set Aside Judgment -- Effect of Appeal

Defendant's motion to vacate a judgment based upon the court's erroneous conclusion that the decision in Payton v New York (445 U.S. 573) which declared CPL 140.15 unconstitutional was not retroactive must be denied since, although the court recognized that its initial holding was erroneous, the defendant had an appeal of the judgment pending in the Appellate Division which, pursuant to CPL 440.10 (subd 2, par [b]), precludes the court from granting a motion to vacate; moreover, the court cannot exercise its inherent power to vacate the judgment in the interest of justice since the People, on the appeal, have raised issues other than the retroactivity of the Payton decision and, therefore, such action would not bar a renewed appeal by the People and would only have the practical effect of reversing the posture of the appellants resulting in an unnecessary duplication of the appellate process.

 

1796 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Tafnet Realty Corp. v. New York City Dep't of Bldgs.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


116 Misc. 2d 609; 455 N.Y.S.2d 341; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3927



October 26, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner building owner filed suit to challenge the revocation of a building alteration permit. The city counterclaimed for injunctive relief to abate the harassment of tenants.


OVERVIEW: The building owner obtained an alteration permit to convert a hotel on Manhattan's upper west side into a class A multiple dwelling, but the city revoked the permit two weeks later without giving the building owner notice or an opportunity to be heard based on political pressure from the mayor's office. The building owner filed suit to challenge the revocation and the city counterclaimed for injunctive relief to abate the harassment of tenants. The court granted relief to both parties. The court found that the city's revocation of the permit was arbitrary and contrary to law and an abuse of discretion because the stated reasons were vague and conclusory, because the building owner's supposed failure to file rent control documentation was no failure at all, since none was necessary in light of the entire premises being subject to rent stabilization, not rent control, because there was no evidence that the projected cost was underestimated, and because the summary disposition without notice or a hearing violated due process. The court also granted injunctive relief to the city on its tenant harassment claim.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the city's revocation of the building alteration permit and granted injunctive relief to the city on its claim of tenant harassment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal Corporations -- Summary Revocation of Building Alteration Permit -- Owner's Due Process Rights

1. The buildings department's summary revocation of an alteration permit permitting the conversion of petitioner's single-room occupancy residence to a class A multiple dwelling, without giving petitioner notice or an opportunity to be heard, is vacated. Petitioner's due process rights were violated because the revocation was ordered without affording petitioner notice or an opportunity to be heard.

Municipal Corporations -- Summary Revocation of Building Alteration Permit -- Improper Reasons

2. The buildings department's summary revocation of an alteration permit permitting the conversion of petitioner's single-room occupancy residence (SRO) to a class A multiple dwelling, is vacated. The revocation was arbitrary and contrary to law inasmuch as it was based on improper reasons, or proper reasons lacking a proper basis. Having been directed at the behest of the Mayor's Office of SRO Housing, an office which is independent from the buildings department, and not as a result of the independent judgment of the buildings department, the revocation was an abuse [***2] of discretion. It was improper for the Buildings Commissioner to use revocation of a building permit as punishment for activity outside the scope of his jurisdiction, of which he had no independent knowledge, as a means of effecting policies of other city agencies.

 

1797 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Whitby Operating Corp. v. Schleissner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


117 Misc. 2d 794; 459 N.Y.S.2d 203; 1982 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4087



October 20, 1982 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord filed a complaint for a permanent injunction and punitive damages against defendants, tenants and tenant association, to enjoin them from collecting and holding the tenants' rent during a rent strike and displaying rent strike signs on apartments inside and outside the building. The landlord filed an order to show cause that contained a temporary restraining order to prohibit the actions.


OVERVIEW: As to the collection of rent by the tenant association, the court found that the landlord failed to show a probability of success on the merits. The landlord failed to demonstrate any irreparable injury resulting from denial of the preliminary injunction. The court found that the equities favored the tenant association because of the burden on their First Amendment rights of association which would have resulted from an injunction and the undermining of their ability to organize effectively as protected by the legislature, under N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 230 (1975). As to the rent strike signs, the court found that the tenants sought to engage in a virtually pure form of speech. They wanted to inform other tenants of the rent strike and communicate to the community at large the deprivation of their right to decent housing conditions, to force the landlord to accord them those rights. The court found that the First Amendment protections extended to the signs in the windows and on the apartment doors, which were private areas used by the tenants. The court found that the protection did not extend to hanging banners out of the building's windows that involved public areas.


OUTCOME: The court dissolved the temporary restraining order staying the tenant association from continuing solicitation and collection of rents from individual tenants, and from releasing any rent moneys already on deposit. The court denied the landlord's motion for a preliminary injunction on the collection of rents by the tenants association. The court enjoined the tenants from displaying rent strike signs on areas outside the building.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and Tenant -- Tenants' Associations -- Right to Solicit Members and Display Signs during Rent Strike
